# CLAUDE SUMMARIES

# Week 1 — Foundations

# **Michel Foucault, *The Order of Things: An Archaeology of the Human Sciences* (1966/1970)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Foucault argues that Western knowledge is organized not by continuous progress toward truth but by discontinuous *epistemes*—deep structural configurations that determine what can be thought, said, and known in any given era. The book traces three such epistemes (Renaissance, Classical, Modern) and demonstrates that the "human sciences" (psychology, sociology, linguistics, economics) only became possible when "Man" emerged as both subject and object of knowledge in the late eighteenth century. In one sentence: **the human sciences are not timeless disciplines discovering eternal truths about human nature, but historically contingent formations made possible by a specific epistemic rupture around 1800—and "Man" as we know him may be a temporary figure, "like a face drawn in sand at the edge of the sea."**

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Foucault's intervention is aimed squarely at humanist and progressivist accounts of intellectual history—particularly the Enlightenment narrative that knowledge accumulates continuously toward greater rationality and human self-understanding. Against historians of ideas who trace the gradual refinement of concepts (think Arthur Lovejoy's "unit-ideas"), Foucault insists on radical discontinuity: epistemes do not evolve into one another but are separated by ruptures that make the previous order of knowledge literally unthinkable. He is also contesting phenomenology (Husserl, Merleau-Ponty) and existentialism (Sartre), which place human consciousness at the center of knowledge. For Foucault, "Man" is not the eternal ground of inquiry but a recent invention—a product of the modern episteme rather than its cause. The book engages implicitly with structuralism (Lévi-Strauss, Saussure) by adopting its synchronic, relational method, but Foucault historicizes structure itself, showing that the very rules governing representation shift across epochs. The work also anticipates his later break with structuralism by emphasizing contingency and transformation over static systems. Scholars in dialogue include Bachelard and Canguilhem (on epistemological breaks), Kant (whose *Critique* marks the threshold of the modern episteme), and Ricardo, Cuvier, and Bopp as exemplary figures of the 1800 rupture.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Foucault introduces the concept of the **episteme**: the underlying epistemological field that defines, in any given period, the conditions of possibility for knowledge—what statements can count as true, what objects can be studied, what methods are legitimate. This is not a "worldview" or "spirit of the age" but a structural a priori governing discourse. Key concepts include:

* **Resemblance** (Renaissance): Knowledge is organized by similitude; the world is a vast tissue of signs reflecting one another.  
* **Representation** (Classical age, 17th–18th c.): Knowledge operates through transparent representation, ordering, and taxonomy; language is a neutral grid for classifying nature.  
* **Man as empirico-transcendental doublet** (Modern age): "Man" becomes both an object of empirical study and the transcendental condition of knowledge itself—a paradoxical figure who is both finite (determined by life, labor, language) and foundational.  
* **The analytic of finitude**: Modern thought is haunted by the problem of human finitude—the recognition that Man is conditioned by forces (biology, economics, linguistics) that exceed his consciousness.

Foucault's method is **archaeological**: excavating the implicit rules that govern discourse, rather than tracing influences, intentions, or progress.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Foucault draws primarily on canonical texts from natural history, grammar, and political economy—treating them not as contributions to disciplines but as symptoms of underlying epistemic structures. His archive includes:

* Renaissance: Paracelsus, Aldrovandi, the Renaissance "prose of the world"  
* Classical age: Descartes, Port-Royal grammar, Linnaeus, Adam Smith (Wealth of Nations)  
* Modern: Kant, Cuvier (comparative anatomy), Ricardo (political economy), Bopp (philology), and later Marx, Nietzsche, Freud

The temporal scope spans roughly the sixteenth century to the mid-twentieth century, with the major ruptures located around 1650 and 1800\. The geographic focus is Western Europe, particularly France. Foucault does not use archival manuscripts or quantitative data; his method is close reading of published philosophical and scientific texts, analyzed for their formal structures rather than their explicit arguments.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Velázquez's *Las Meninas* (Preface):** Foucault's bravura opening analyzes this painting as an allegory of Classical representation—the artist, the royal subjects, and the viewer are caught in a hall of mirrors where representation represents itself, yet the central place (the subject who organizes the scene) remains structurally empty. This absence of "Man" epitomizes the Classical episteme.

**The shift from Natural History to Biology (c. 1800):** In the Classical age, natural history classified visible surfaces—organisms arranged on a taxonomic table. With Cuvier, life becomes an invisible organizing principle; anatomy reveals deep functional structures that cannot be directly observed. This marks the emergence of "Life" as an autonomous domain and the displacement of transparent representation.

**Ricardo and the emergence of "Labor":** Adam Smith's political economy still treated wealth as circulating representations (exchange value). Ricardo introduces labor as the hidden source of value—something that cannot be directly represented but conditions all economic relations. This shift parallels the emergence of "Man" as a finite, laboring being whose activity grounds but also limits knowledge.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

1. **On the episteme and discontinuity:** "In any given culture and at any given moment, there is always only one episteme that defines the conditions of possibility of all knowledge, whether expressed in a theory or silently invested in a practice." (p. 168\)

2. **On the invention of Man:** "Man is an invention of recent date. And one perhaps nearing its end. If those arrangements were to disappear as they appeared... then one can certainly wager that man would be erased, like a face drawn in sand at the edge of the sea." (p. 387\)

3. **On the Classical age's order:** "The Classical age gives knowledge a form that is neither hierarchical nor genealogical; it provides a tabulated space in which beings present themselves, one beside another, in a simultaneous arrangement." (p. 74\)

4. **On the analytic of finitude:** "Modern thought advances in that direction where man's Other must become the Same as himself... Man's finitude is heralded—and imperiously so—in the positivity of knowledge." (p. 314\)

5. **On the human sciences:** "The human sciences are not, then, an analysis of what man is by nature; but rather an analysis that extends from what man is in his positivity... to what enables this same being to know (or seek to know) what life is, what the essence of labour and its laws are, and in what way he is able to speak." (p. 353\)

6. **On resemblance in the Renaissance:** "Up to the end of the sixteenth century, resemblance played a constructive role in the knowledge of Western culture. It was resemblance that largely guided exegesis and the interpretation of texts." (p. 17\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:**

* Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison, *Objectivity*: Both examine historically shifting epistemic regimes, though Daston/Galison focus on scientific atlases and the changing ideal of the scientific self.  
* Thomas Kuhn, *The Structure of Scientific Revolutions*: Compare Kuhn's "paradigms" to Foucault's "epistemes"—both emphasize discontinuity, but Kuhn centers on scientific communities while Foucault excavates broader discursive conditions.  
* Ian Hacking, *The Emergence of Probability* or *Historical Ontology*: Hacking develops Foucault's archaeology into the history of concepts and "styles of reasoning."  
* Theodore Porter, *Trust in Numbers*: Both analyze how regimes of quantification and classification are historically contingent.

**Exam questions this book helps answer:**

* How have historians of science theorized periodization and discontinuity? (Compare Foucault's epistemes to Kuhn's paradigms, Daston/Galison's regimes of objectivity, etc.)  
* What is the relationship between knowledge and power in the history of the human sciences?  
* How did "the human" become an object of scientific inquiry? (Crucial for history of psychology, sociology, anthropology.)  
* What are the limits of progressivist narratives in intellectual history?

**Limitations:** Foucault's account is almost entirely Western European and textual; material practices, institutions, and non-European knowledge systems are absent. The concept of the episteme has been critiqued for its totalizing quality—does *everyone* in a given period really think within the same epistemological grid? Foucault later moved away from this model toward genealogy and power/knowledge. For exam purposes, it's important to note that *The Order of Things* is pre-*Discipline and Punish* and pre-*History of Sexuality*; the analytics of power and the body are not yet developed here.

# **Katharine Park, *Secrets of Women: Gender, Generation, and the Origins of Human Dissection* (Zone Books, 2006\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Park argues that human dissection in late medieval and Renaissance Italy (c. 1300–1550) emerged not primarily from university medical instruction on criminal corpses, but from a diverse set of cultural practices—embalming, autopsies, sectio in mortua (extraction of fetuses from women who died in childbirth), and the inspection of holy bodies—in which women's bodies played a central and generative role. The uterus, as the site of generation's mysteries, became the emblematic object of dissection, symbolizing the body's hidden interior and shaping how anatomical knowledge developed as a distinctly gendered enterprise. **In one sentence: The origins of human dissection lie in religious and familial concerns about women's bodies—particularly the uterus—which came to represent the hidden interior that anatomical inquiry promised to reveal.**

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Park directly challenges the longstanding historiographical narrative—dating to the nineteenth century—that medieval Christianity was hostile to dissection and that heroic Renaissance figures like Leonardo, Michelangelo, and Vesalius defied religious persecution to pursue anatomical science. This "warfare of science with theology" narrative, she argues, fundamentally misunderstands both medieval religious culture (which had no corpse-pollution taboos and actively encouraged relic veneration) and the actual origins of dissection in devotional and familial practices. Park also challenges the historiography of anatomy that focuses exclusively on university-based dissection of male criminal corpses, arguing that this anachronistic institutional focus has obscured the broader cultural phenomenon of opening bodies, in which women played a central role both as subjects and, in some cases, as practitioners. She is in dialogue with Caroline Walker Bynum's work on medieval corporeality and female religiosity, Joan Cadden's work on medieval sexuality and generation, and Nancy Siraisi's foundational scholarship on Italian medicine. Park also engages critically with Michel Foucault's *Birth of the Clinic* and its narrative of the "medical gaze," arguing that the process was neither as sudden nor as straightforwardly "desacralizing" as Foucault suggested.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Park draws on **cultural history of the body**, **history of gender**, and **history of science** approaches rather than adopting a single theoretical apparatus. Key conceptual moves include:

* **The social embeddedness of knowledge practices**: Dissection emerged from and remained connected to religious practices (saints' cults, embalming), family concerns (inheritance, paternity), and judicial procedures (criminal investigation), not just medical education.  
* **Gender as constitutive**: The female body was not simply one object among many; it served as the model for bodily interiority itself. The concept of "secrets of women" (*secreta mulierum*) shifted from meaning "knowledge women possess" to "knowledge about women's bodies that men must uncover."  
* **Material and visual culture analysis**: Park reads images, relics, and devotional objects alongside texts, treating material culture as evidence of cultural meaning.  
* **Critique of anachronism**: She refuses the modern distinction between "dissection" and "autopsy," showing that contemporaries used terms like *anatomia* and *sparare* interchangeably across medical, religious, and judicial contexts.

Key concepts Park mobilizes: the "secrets of women" (as both women's hidden knowledge and the hidden nature of women's bodies); corporeality and impressionability in medieval theories of vision; the "moral economy of personhood" that differentiated honorable from dishonoring procedures.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

**Types of evidence**:

* Canonization proceedings (particularly for Chiara of Montefalco and Margherita of Città di Castello)  
* Devotional texts and hagiographical *vitae*  
* Chronicles and diaries (*ricordanze* of Florentine patricians)  
* Medical treatises in Latin and vernacular (from Taddeo Alderotti through Jacopo Berengario to Vesalius)  
* Visual sources: frescoes, altarpieces, anatomical illustrations, woodcuts, bronze reliefs (Donatello)  
* Municipal statutes and judicial records  
* Vernacular "secrets of women" literature

**Geographic scope**: Northern Italy (Bologna, Florence, Padua, Montefalco, Venice, Cremona)

**Temporal scope**: c. 1280–1556, with emphasis on the period before Vesalius to demonstrate medieval continuities

**Methodological approach**: Case-study structure, beginning with the opening of a woman's body and moving outward to reconstruct cultural context. Park explicitly notes the limitations of her sources for recovering women's own voices and experiences.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Chiara of Montefalco (1308)**: An Umbrian abbess whose fellow nuns opened her body after death to preserve it for veneration. They found holy objects—a crucifix, the instruments of Christ's Passion—inside her heart and gallbladder. This case demonstrates how dissection emerged from religious devotion and female networks, not male medical institutions. The nuns performed the procedure themselves without calling a physician, though they later consulted one to confirm the miraculous nature of their findings.

**Florentine patrician women (late 15th century)**: Women like Fiametta Adimari and Nanna Strozzi were autopsied at the request of their families to determine causes of death, particularly reproductive complications. These cases reveal how elite families used dissection to investigate fertility problems, infant mortality, and hereditary illness—showing that concerns about generation, inheritance, and paternity drove anatomical investigation in domestic contexts.

**The female criminal on Vesalius's *Fabrica* title page (1542)**: An unnamed woman executed in Padua who had falsely claimed pregnancy to avoid execution. Park reads this iconic image against the tradition of the "miser's heart" (as depicted by Donatello) and the story of Nero opening Agrippina's womb. The criminal's splayed female body demonstrates both Vesalius's iconographic innovations and the deep continuities with medieval practices and meanings.

---

## **The Earlier Meaning: Knowledge Women Possess**

In the thirteenth and early fourteenth centuries, "secrets of women" (*secreta mulierum*) primarily referred to **knowledge that women held and transmitted among themselves**—practical wisdom about sexuality, conception, pregnancy, childbirth, and women's illnesses. This knowledge was understood as experiential and oral, passed between mothers and daughters, among neighbors, and through networks of midwives and female healers.

Park illustrates this with Salimbene's 1286 account of an epidemic: while male physicians performed autopsies and wrote formal *consilia*, the "wise women of Cremona" cured their chickens with horehound remedies derived from their own accumulated experience. The two knowledge systems coexisted, but they operated through different channels—one literate, public, and male; the other oral, domestic, and female.

The *secreta* in this framing carried a **dual valence of suspicion and respect**. Male writers often portrayed women as jealously guarding dangerous knowledge about sexuality and fertility—knowledge they might use for illicit purposes (contraception, abortion, love potions, determining paternity). The pseudo-Albertus Magnus *De secretis mulierum*, circulating widely from the late thirteenth century, exemplifies this anxious framing: women possess hidden knowledge about their own bodies that men cannot access and that women refuse to share.

## **The Transformation: Secrets Inherent in Female Anatomy**

Over the course of the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries, Park argues, Italian physicians increasingly **redefined the "secrets of women" as secrets located in the structure of women's bodies themselves**—not knowledge women possessed, but hidden anatomical truths that male experts could and should uncover through dissection.

This shift accompanied physicians' growing involvement in reproductive care. Where earlier they had largely left pregnancy, fertility, and "women's illnesses" to female practitioners, fifteenth-century Italian doctors began claiming authority over these domains. The "secrets" were no longer understood as experiential knowledge inaccessible to men because women wouldn't share it, but as **topological mysteries**—the hidden interior of the uterus, where generation occurred in darkness.

Park writes that this reframing transformed the problem from social (women won't tell us) to technical (we need better methods to see inside). Dissection became the solution: "anyone who could probe the complicated and mysterious workings of the uterus would have little trouble understanding the rest of the comparatively simple human frame" (p. 26).

## **The Epistemological Stakes**

What's crucial here is that this wasn't merely a change in who controlled knowledge—it was a change in **what kind of knowledge mattered**. The earlier framing acknowledged women's experiential authority, even while denigrating it. The later framing **delegitimized experiential knowledge entirely** in favor of anatomically-grounded, text-informed, visually-verified understanding.

By the time of Vesalius, the transformation is complete. In Park's reading of the *Fabrica* title page, the condemned woman's body had been examined by midwives who judged (correctly) that she was not pregnant. But Vesalius presents his anatomical demonstration as providing **certainty** where the midwives could offer only conjecture. The lone woman visible in the crowd of spectators—possibly a midwife herself—stands as a witness to her own displacement.

Park summarizes this as "the masculine birth of gynecology" (borrowing Monica Green's phrase): women's traditional authority over knowledge of female bodies was supplanted by a model of learned expertise that, by definition, excluded all women—since it required literacy, formal training, and access to institutions from which women were barred.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The opening and inspection of cadavers was a much more diverse cultural phenomenon in which women played a much larger part than I ever could have imagined." (p. 10\)

"Assuming anachronistically that opening the human body is in the first instance a medical procedure, historians have ignored the broader phenomenon of which it was a part—or reduced these other, related procedures to the status of 'background' or 'cultural context.'" (p. 18\)

"The female figure has come to illustrate internal anatomy in general, apparently by association with the uterus: where the male bodies are mostly surfaces, the woman is identified with a visualizable inside." (p. 33\)

"The men and women whose lives and work I describe in this book, the inhabitants of northern Italian cities from the mid-thirteenth to the mid-sixteenth century, understood their bodies primarily in terms of family and kinship, on the one hand, and religion, on the other. Medical models—even in this world of highly developed medical institutions and practices—came in a distant third." (p. 23\)

"The uterus acquired a special, symbolic weight as the organ that only dissection could truly reveal, and as a result, it came to stand for the body's hidden interior." (pp. 27–28)

"This new anatomically inflected ideal of self-knowledge and self-possession reinscribed humanity as male." (p. 267\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with**:

* Lorraine Daston and Katharine Park, *Wonders and the Order of Nature* (on early modern natural inquiry and wonder)  
* Caroline Walker Bynum, *Fragmentation and Redemption* and *The Resurrection of the Body* (on medieval corporeality)  
* Pamela H. Smith, *The Body of the Artisan* (on embodied knowledge and craft)  
* Steven Shapin, *The Scientific Revolution* (for contrasting interpretations of early modern science's emergence)  
* Harold Cook on the history of medicine and anatomy  
* For gender and technology: Ruth Oldenziel, *Making Technology Masculine*

**Generals exam questions this could help answer**:

* How did gender shape the development of early modern natural inquiry?  
* What role did religious culture play in the origins of scientific practices?  
* How should we periodize the "Scientific Revolution"? Was there meaningful continuity between medieval and early modern knowledge practices?  
* How have historians challenged the "warfare of science and religion" narrative?  
* What is the relationship between visual culture and scientific knowledge?  
* How did practices of knowing the body change between 1300 and 1600?

**Limitations/Critiques**:

* Park acknowledges the difficulty of recovering women's own voices and experiences; this remains a "history of the health care available to women" and male representations of female bodies more than a history of women themselves.  
* The Italian focus, while methodologically justified, raises questions about how generalizable these findings are to other European contexts.  
* Some readers may find the case-study structure somewhat episodic, though it allows for rich contextual analysis.  
* The book ends in 1556 and does not trace the later developments (witch trials, emergence of modern gynecology) that it gestures toward.

# **Carolyn Merchant, *The Death of Nature: Women, Ecology, and the Scientific Revolution* (1980)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Merchant argues that the Scientific Revolution of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries entailed a fundamental transformation in Western worldview: the replacement of an organic, female-centered conception of nature as a living, nurturing mother with a mechanistic conception of nature as dead, inert matter subject to domination and exploitation. This conceptual shift—sanctioned by figures like Francis Bacon, René Descartes, and Thomas Hobbes—removed ethical constraints against environmental exploitation while simultaneously reinforcing the subordination of women, whose symbolic and social roles were intimately bound to the older organic cosmology.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Merchant intervenes in several historiographic conversations simultaneously. Against triumphalist narratives of scientific progress that treat the Scientific Revolution as unambiguously liberating, she reveals its ecological and gendered costs. She challenges the internalist history of science that focuses on conceptual development in isolation from social context, instead demonstrating how metaphors, imagery, and language carried normative weight that legitimated new forms of exploitation. Her work engages critically with the "Whig history" of science while drawing on the emerging sociology of scientific knowledge (particularly the Edinburgh school's interest in how social factors shape scientific content).

**Merchant also participates in the nascent field of environmental history, predating William Cronon's *Nature's Metropolis* by over a decade, by tracing the material ecological transformations (deforestation, mining, fen drainage) that accompanied and enabled the conceptual shift.** She explicitly builds on and extends the work of Lewis Mumford (*Technics and Civilization*), Jacques Ellul (*The Technological Society*), and Theodore Roszak (*Where the Wasteland Ends*), while offering a distinctly feminist critique that was unprecedented in its scope. The book becomes a founding text of ecofeminism, connecting the domination of nature and the domination of women as parallel and mutually reinforcing processes.

Her framework also revises standard interpretations of canonical figures: Bacon is reread through the lens of witch trial rhetoric and gendered violence; Hobbes's mechanism is linked to the enclosure of commons and early capitalism; Newton's triumph is shown to have marginalized vitalist alternatives proposed by women philosophers like Anne Conway.

## **Francis Bacon**

Merchant's reading of Bacon is the most extensive and controversial. She argues that Bacon's program for experimental science drew directly on two contemporary discourses: **witch trials** and **mining**.

The witch trial connection is linguistic. Bacon's language for investigating nature—putting nature "to the question," extracting her "secrets," binding her to service—echoes the vocabulary of inquisition and torture. Nature must be "hounded in her wanderings," "bound into service," made a "slave." Merchant quotes Bacon's assertion that the secrets of nature "reveal themselves more readily under the vexations of art than when they go their own way" (p. 168). This is the language of coerced confession.

The mining connection works through the gendered imagery of penetration. Bacon explicitly compared experimental investigation to mining operations that delved into the earth's "womb" to extract hidden treasures. Where the older organic worldview had treated mining as a violation of Mother Earth (Merchant cites Roman and medieval writers who deplored mining as an assault on the earth's body), Bacon reframed it as improvement and progress.

The crucial move is that Bacon transformed a **constraint** into a **sanction**. The organic image of nature as nurturing mother had functioned as an ethical restraint—"one does not readily slay a mother." Bacon's rhetoric removed that constraint and replaced it with an ethic of mastery. His gendered violence wasn't incidental but constitutive of the new experimental program.

Merchant situates this in Bacon's immediate social context: his role as James I's attorney general, his participation in the "controversy over women," and his prosecution of the Countess of Somerset's accomplice. Bacon inhabited a world where female disorder (women wearing men's hats, queens ruling kingdoms, witches controlling nature) was being actively suppressed.

## **Thomas Hobbes**

Merchant reframes Hobbes as providing the **political and social theory** that complemented mechanism's view of nature. His move is to transform the organic metaphor of the body politic into a mechanical one.

In the older tradition (John of Salisbury's *Policraticus*), the state was imagined as an organism: the prince was the head, the senate the heart, the common people the feet. Each part had a natural function within a living whole, and the metaphor implied organic interdependence and hierarchy based on natural position.

Hobbes mechanizes this. In *Leviathan*, the state becomes an "artificial man"—"life is but a motion of limbs... why may we not say that all automata (engines that move themselves by springs and wheels as doth a watch) have an artificial life? For what is the heart, but a spring; and the nerves, but so many strings; and the joints, but so many wheels..." (p. 212).

This matters because the mechanical metaphor implies a different kind of order: not the organic interdependence of parts within a living whole, but the **predictable behavior of interchangeable components governed by external rules**. Order becomes something imposed from outside by a sovereign "technician" rather than emerging from the natural functions of parts.

Merchant connects this to the enclosure of the commons and the rise of market competition. Hobbes's state of nature, where everyone competes for resources, provides ideological justification for treating the commons not as a shared organic community resource but as a battleground requiring law and order. His political philosophy is "a French solution to an English problem"—the problem of disorder created by the Civil War, but also by the broader disruptions of early capitalism.

On gender, Merchant notes a telling inconsistency: Hobbes's logic of natural equality should have extended to women (in the state of nature, the mother has dominion over the child because paternity is uncertain), but in practice his family remains patriarchal. "Democratic" consent theory meant equality for property-holding males.

## **Isaac Newton**

Newton receives less direct attention than Bacon or Hobbes, but Merchant frames him in two key ways.

First, Newton represents the **triumph of mechanism over vitalism**. Merchant's Chapter 12 contrasts Newton with Leibniz, and Chapter 11 recovers the vitalist alternative represented by Anne Conway. Conway's philosophy—which influenced Leibniz—held that matter and spirit were unified, that nature was alive and active throughout. This vitalist worldview was "inherently antiexploitative" because it attributed inherent worth to all living things.

Newton's physics, by contrast, rendered nature passive. Matter became dead, inert, moved only by external forces. This wasn't just a technical claim about mechanics—it was a framework that removed any ethical barrier to treating nature as mere resource. Merchant relies here on work by her colleague David Kubrin, who argued that Newton actively suppressed the magical and alchemical elements in his own thought to promote a mechanical worldview compatible with social order.

Second, Newton's victory was also a **social and institutional victory**. The Royal Society, the "invisible college" of Baconian experimenters, the networks of merchants and improving landowners—all of these institutions carried forward a program that marginalized alternatives. Women philosophers like Conway and Margaret Cavendish were excluded from the Royal Society and their contributions forgotten (Conway's book was misattributed to a male editor for centuries).

---

The common thread across all three reframings is that Merchant refuses to treat scientific concepts as neutral descriptions of reality. Metaphors, language, and imagery carry normative weight. The "fathers of modern science" didn't just discover how nature worked—they articulated a conceptual framework that legitimated particular social relations: the domination of nature, the subordination of women, the replacement of organic community with market competition. Reading them through Merchant, you can't separate their science from their politics.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Merchant employs an **ecological-materialist** framework that integrates intellectual history with environmental history and feminist theory. Key conceptual moves include:

* **Controlling images**: Descriptive metaphors (nature as nurturing mother, nature as machine) carry tacit normative content that functions as ethical constraint or sanction. When controlling imagery changes, behavior changes.  
* **Organic vs. mechanistic worldviews**: The organic worldview assumed nature was alive, purposive, and interconnected; the mechanistic worldview rendered nature dead, passive, and composed of interchangeable parts subject to manipulation.  
* **Ecosystem history**: Historical change is ecological change; human institutions and natural environments form an interrelated system.  
* **Feminist standpoint epistemology**: Reading history "from the bottom up" and "from the ground up" reveals what mainstream narratives occlude—the perspectives of women, lower classes, and the natural environment itself.

Merchant introduces the concept of **"the death of nature"** as shorthand for the removal of animistic, organic assumptions from the cosmos—the most far-reaching effect of the Scientific Revolution.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Merchant draws on an unusually wide array of sources across multiple disciplines:

* **Literary and visual sources**: Renaissance poetry (John Donne, Edmund Spenser), emblem books, sculpture, and iconography depicting nature as female  
* **Scientific and philosophical texts**: Primary writings of Bacon, Descartes, Hobbes, Boyle, Newton, Leibniz, Anne Conway, Margaret Cavendish  
* **Economic and technological histories**: Records of mining, metallurgy, fen drainage, forest management, and early industrial development  
* **Legal and political documents**: Witch trial records, enclosure legislation, conservation ordinances  
* **Environmental data**: Timber surveys, coal production figures, ecological assessments

**Geographic scope**: Primarily England and Western Europe (especially the Netherlands, France, Italy) **Temporal scope**: 1500–1700, with attention to medieval antecedents and modern consequences

---

## **John Donne**

Donne gets the most extended treatment, particularly in Chapter 1\. Merchant uses him to show the **double-edged quality** of the female nature/mining metaphor.

In **"Love's Alchymie,"** Donne associates the female body with mining for gold in terms that link monetary greed with sexual lust:

"Men to such Gods, their sacrificing Coles, / Did not in Altars lay, but pits and holes... / So we her ayres contemplate, words and heart / And virtues; but we love the Centrique part."

The "pits and holes" of the female body parallel the mining of "Pluto's caverns" within the female earth. Love of the sexual "centrique part" corrupts just as avarice for gold corrupts. The poem ends with the image of women's "two purses"—linking female sexuality directly to monetary exchange.

But in **"Elegie XIX: Going to Bed,"** Donne flips the metaphor into a sanction rather than a warning:

"License my roaving hands, and let them go, / Before, behind, between, above, below. / O my America\! my new-found-land, / My kingdome, safliest when with one man man'd, / My Myne of precious stones, My Emperie, / How blest am I in this discovering thee\!"

Here the colonial explorer and the miner are celebrated. The female body is America, a kingdom, a mine of precious stones—and "discovering" her is a blessing. Merchant's point is that **the same imagery could function as constraint or sanction depending on whether the values attached to mining and exploration were negative or positive**. As commercial expansion made those activities seem progressive, the metaphor's ethical valence inverted.

Donne also appears as evidence of the perceived **disorder in nature** brought by the new science. Merchant quotes from **"An Anatomy of the World: The First Anniversary"** (1611):

"'Tis all in pieces, all coherence gone; / All just supply, and all Relation."

And:

"The new Philosophy calls all in doubt, / The Element of fire is quite put out; / The Sun is lost, and th'earth, and no mans wit / Can well direct him, where to looke for it."

This captures the anxiety that Copernican cosmology and the "new philosophy" were dissolving the organic coherence of the old worldview. The frame of nature was breaking.

## **Edmund Spenser**

Merchant uses Spenser's **Faerie Queene** to illustrate the Renaissance image of nature as a **nurturing, ordered female figure**—the organic worldview before its "death."

She quotes the figure of Dame Nature presiding over the "Mutability Cantos":

"Then forth issewed (great goddesse) great dame Nature, / With goodly port and gracious Majesty... / That well may seeme some heavenly shape, / Her face did seeme more clear than Christall glass, / And her broad eyes did burne with mighty light."

Nature here is authoritative, majestic, a judge who presides over cosmic disputes. This is nature as ordered, as normative—very different from the disorderly, chaotic nature that Bacon would seek to constrain.

## **Other Literary Sources**

Merchant references several other literary texts more briefly:

**Shakespeare's Troilus and Cressida** appears in Chapter 5, where Ulysses warns about the consequences of removing "degree" (hierarchy) from the cosmic order: "Take but degree away, untune that string, and hark what discord follows." This illustrates elite anxiety about disorder.

**Richard Hooker's Laws of Ecclesiastical Polity** (not poetry, but literary prose) expresses similar fears: if nature ceased to observe her own laws, "the celestial frame might dissolve, the moon wander from her orbit."

Merchant also draws on **emblem books** and visual sources—not poetry per se, but functioning similarly as cultural evidence for how nature was imagined. Sculptures like Barrias's "Nature Revealing Herself to Science" (showing a coy female figure removing her veil) demonstrate the later transformation: nature is no longer authoritative Dame Nature but a passive, sexualized body exposing herself to the male scientific gaze.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Francis Bacon and the rhetoric of domination**: Merchant provides a close reading of Bacon's language, demonstrating how his program for experimental science drew on imagery from witch trials (nature must be "put to the question," her secrets extracted through "inquisition") and mining (penetrating the earth's womb). Bacon's rhetoric transformed the organic constraint against violating Mother Earth into a sanction for scientific and commercial exploitation.

**The fen drainage controversy**: The drainage of English fens in the seventeenth century illustrates the conflict between organic and commercial worldviews. Local communities who depended on the fens for subsistence (fishing, fowling, grazing) resisted drainage schemes that would transform wetlands into profitable agricultural land. The conflict pitted communal organic economies against capitalist improvement ideology.

**Anne Conway and vitalist alternatives**: Conway's *Principles of the Most Ancient and Modern Philosophy* (1690) articulated a vitalist philosophy that rejected Cartesian dualism in favor of a monistic unity of matter and spirit. Her work influenced Leibniz and represented an alternative path not taken—one that preserved the life of nature and had inherently anti-exploitative ethical implications. Conway's marginalization (her book was misattributed to a male editor for centuries) exemplifies how women's intellectual contributions were erased.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The image of the earth as a living organism and nurturing mother had served as a cultural constraint restricting the actions of human beings. One does not readily slay a mother, dig into her entrails for gold or mutilate her body, although commercial mining would soon require that." (p. 3\)

"The removal of animistic, organic assumptions about the cosmos constituted the death of nature—the most far-reaching effect of the Scientific Revolution. Because nature was now viewed as a system of dead, inert particles moved by external, rather than inherent forces, the mechanical framework itself could legitimate the manipulation of nature." (p. 193\)

"Disorderly, active nature was soon forced to submit to the questions and experimental techniques of the new science. Francis Bacon (1561–1626), a celebrated 'father of modern science,' transformed tendencies already extant in his own society into a total program advocating the control of nature for human benefit." (p. 164\)

"The new image of nature as a female to be controlled and dissected through experiment legitimated the exploitation of natural resources. Although the image of the nurturing earth popular in the Renaissance did not vanish, it was superseded by new controlling imagery. The constraints against penetration associated with the earth-mother image were transformed into sanctions for denudation." (p. 189\)

"Both the women's movement and the ecology movement are sharply critical of the costs of competition, aggression, and domination arising from the market economy's *modus operandi* in nature and society." (p. xx)

"As a philosophy of nature, vitalism in its monistic form was inherently antiexploitative. Its emphasis on the life of all things as gradations of soul, its lack of a separate distinction between matter and spirit, its principle of an immanent activity permeating nature, and its reverence for the nurturing power of the earth endowed it with an ethic of the inherent worth of everything alive." (p. 253–254)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:**

* Steven Shapin, *The Scientific Revolution* (1996) — for a contrasting, more internalist account of the period  
* Lorraine Daston and Katharine Park, *Wonders and the Order of Nature* (1998) — for another cultural history of early modern nature  
* Pamela H. Smith, *The Body of the Artisan* (2004) — for artisanal knowledge and embodied practice  
* Leo Marx, *The Machine in the Garden* (1964) — for American continuations of the machine-nature tension  
* Theodore Steinberg, *Nature Incorporated* (1994) — for environmental history of industrialization  
* Lewis Mumford, *Technics and Civilization* (1934) — a key intellectual precursor

**Useful for exam questions on:**

* The relationship between scientific and economic change in early modernity  
* Gender and the history of science  
* Environmental history and the origins of ecological crisis  
* The social construction of scientific knowledge  
* Historiography of the Scientific Revolution  
* Alternative or "losing" traditions in science (vitalism, organicism)  
* How metaphors and imagery shape scientific practice and social norms

**Limitations/Critiques:**

* Some historians have questioned whether the organic worldview was as benign as Merchant suggests, or whether it actually constrained exploitation in practice  
* The causal relationship between metaphor and behavior remains somewhat undertheorized  
* Critics note the book occasionally romanticizes pre-modern organic communities  
* Later scholarship (e.g., Peter Dear, Margaret Jacob) has complicated the picture of mechanism's triumph  
* The "ecofeminist" frame has been critiqued for essentializing women's connection to nature  
* Merchant's reading of Bacon, while influential, has been contested by scholars who argue she takes his rhetoric too literally

Despite these critiques, *The Death of Nature* remains a foundational text for understanding how science, gender, and environment intersect historically, and its influence on subsequent environmental history, history of science, and feminist theory has been enormous.

# **The Body of the Artisan: Art and Experience in the Scientific Revolution**

**Pamela H. Smith (2004)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Smith argues that the Scientific Revolution was not solely a product of elite philosophical and mathematical reasoning, but emerged substantially from the bodily practices, material knowledge, and epistemological claims of artisans. Craftspeople—painters, sculptors, goldsmiths, potters, and other makers—developed a distinctive understanding of nature through hands-on engagement with materials, and this "artisanal epistemology" became central to the new experimental philosophy of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries. In one sentence: the body of the artisan, laboring with matter, was a crucial site for generating the empirical knowledge that would come to define modern science.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Smith intervenes in longstanding debates about the origins of the Scientific Revolution, pushing back against narratives that privilege the mathematical and theoretical contributions of figures like Galileo, Descartes, and Newton while marginalizing the role of craft knowledge. She engages critically with Edgar Zilsel's "Zilsel thesis," which posited that the Scientific Revolution arose from the merger of scholarly and artisanal traditions, but argues that Zilsel and subsequent scholars underestimated how artisans themselves articulated epistemological claims—they were not merely passive sources of technique waiting to be elevated by philosophers. Smith also builds on and revises the work of historians like Paolo Rossi (*Philosophy, Technology, and the Arts in the Early Modern Era*) and William Eamon (*Science and the Secrets of Nature*), who examined the relationship between craft secrets and natural philosophy. Her distinctive contribution is to foreground the *body* as the site of knowledge production: artisans knew nature through physical labor, sensory engagement, and the transformation of materials. This "bodily knowledge" was not inferior to or separate from intellectual knowledge but constituted its own legitimate epistemology that natural philosophers increasingly appropriated—often without full acknowledgment—as they developed experimental method. Smith positions her work within the broader turn toward material culture, practice-based history of science, and the rehabilitation of tacit and embodied knowledge as subjects of serious historical inquiry.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Smith mobilizes several interconnected theoretical frameworks. Most centrally, she develops the concept of **"artisanal epistemology"**—the idea that craftspeople possessed a coherent way of knowing nature rooted in hands-on manipulation of materials and close observation of natural processes. This draws on and extends Michael Polanyi's concept of "tacit knowledge" and anticipates recent work on "epistemic things" (Rheinberger) and material knowledge. She also engages with **embodied cognition** avant la lettre, arguing that the artisan's body was an instrument of knowing, not merely of doing. The book participates in **social constructionism** by demonstrating how the authority and credibility of knowledge claims depended on social status and rhetorical strategies, and it contributes to **labor history** by centering the work and worldview of craftspeople typically excluded from narratives of intellectual history. Smith draws on art historical methods, particularly attention to workshop practices and the material processes of image-making, while also engaging the history of alchemy and natural philosophy.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Smith's methodology is deeply archival and material, drawing on an extraordinary range of sources: **artisanal treatises** (such as Cennino Cennini's *Il Libro dell'Arte* and Vannoccio Biringuccio's *Pirotechnia*), **artists' writings** (Albrecht Dürer, Bernard Palissy, Leonardo da Vinci), **natural philosophical texts**, **guild records**, and **material objects** themselves. She reads paintings, sculptures, and decorative objects as evidence of epistemological claims—examining how artisans represented nature, labor, and the transformation of matter. The geographic scope centers on **northern Europe** (especially the German lands and the Low Countries) and **Italy**, with attention to France. Temporally, the book spans roughly **1400–1700**, with particular emphasis on the sixteenth century as a pivotal moment when artisanal claims to knowledge became most explicit and when natural philosophers began systematically appropriating craft techniques. Smith's approach is notable for treating artisanal texts not as mere technical manuals but as sources for intellectual and cultural history.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Bernard Palissy (c. 1510–1590):** The French potter and natural philosopher is Smith's most sustained example. Palissy explicitly articulated an artisanal epistemology, arguing that true knowledge of nature came from laboring with materials rather than from book learning. His ceramic work—especially his famous "rustic ware" featuring lifelike casts of animals and plants—was simultaneously artistic production and natural philosophical investigation. Palissy claimed that his bodily engagement with clay, fire, and organic matter gave him privileged access to nature's secrets, and he publicly debated university-trained scholars on these grounds.

**Albrecht Dürer (1471–1528):** Smith examines how Dürer, trained as a goldsmith before becoming a painter and printmaker, developed his famous naturalistic representations through artisanal practices of close observation and material manipulation. Dürer's watercolors of animals and plants, his treatises on measurement and proportion, and his self-portraits all reflect an artisanal epistemology that claimed the artist-craftsman could know and represent nature more truly than abstract theorists.

**Goldsmiths and alchemists:** Smith traces how the practical knowledge of metalworkers—their understanding of alloys, assaying, and material transformation—informed and overlapped with alchemical and natural philosophical inquiry. The goldsmith's workshop was a site where boundaries between art, craft, and science were porous, and where bodily skill and material expertise generated claims about the nature of matter itself.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Artisans came to view their ability to work with natural materials as giving them a particular claim to knowledge of nature." (p. 8\)

"The body of the artisan was the site of a union of hand and mind... knowledge was produced through bodily labor and accumulated in the body through repeated practice." (p. 24\)

"Palissy staked his entire claim to knowledge on his own experience and labor, in explicit opposition to that of the 'theoreticians' who read about nature in books." (p. 130\)

"The new philosophy of the seventeenth century took over the artisanal claim to productive knowledge of nature but effaced the body of the laborer and elevated the status of the practitioner to that of a 'philosopher.'" (p. 237\)

"The workshop was the primary site of learning, and knowledge resided in bodies—in the hands, eyes, and postures of makers." (p. 59\)

"Art and nature were not opposed; rather, human art imitated, completed, and competed with the productive activity of nature herself." (p. 82\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:** Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (on the social construction of experimental credibility), Lorraine Daston and Katharine Park's *Wonders and the Order of Nature* (on changing categories of natural knowledge), Mario Biagioli's *Galileo, Courtier* (on the social contexts of scientific authority), and Steven Shapin's *The Scientific Revolution* (as a synthetic counterpoint). Within Smith's own trajectory, this pairs with her later *From Lived Experience to the Written Word* (2022), which extends the methodological project through reconstruction experiments.

**Exam questions this could address:**

* How did the Scientific Revolution emerge, and what were the relative contributions of philosophical/mathematical and artisanal/practical traditions?  
* What is "tacit knowledge," and how has it been treated in the history of science?  
* How have historians understood the relationship between art, craft, and science in early modern Europe?  
* What counts as scientific knowledge, and how have the boundaries of legitimate knowledge-making been contested?  
* How does attention to material culture and practice change our understanding of intellectual history?

**Limitations/critiques:** Critics have noted that Smith may at times overstate the coherence and self-consciousness of "artisanal epistemology" as a unified worldview—artisans were a heterogeneous group with varying degrees of literacy and philosophical ambition. The focus on elite artisans who wrote treatises (Palissy, Dürer) may not fully represent the experiences of ordinary craftspeople. Additionally, the book's emphasis on northern Europe and Italy leaves room for further work on artisanal knowledge in other contexts (Iberian world, Islamic lands, Asia).

# **The Experiential Caribbean: Creating Knowledge and Healing in the Early Modern Atlantic**

## **Pablo F. Gómez (University of North Carolina Press, 2017\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Gómez argues that Black Caribbean ritual practitioners—not European natural philosophers—led an epistemological revolution in the seventeenth-century Caribbean, creating novel experientially-based ways of knowing the natural world that replaced dogma and first principles as the foundation for truth claims. These practitioners (whom Gómez calls *Mohanes*) developed transportable, localized practices for producing knowledge about bodies and nature that were predicated on sensorial phenomena they manufactured anew rather than on inherited traditions or universal principles. The Caribbean experiential—a variegated array of knowledge-making practices grounded in demonstrable, perceivable effects—allowed practitioners of African descent to claim authority over truth in volatile, multicultural communities where no single tradition predominated.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Gómez intervenes against two dominant historiographic traditions. First, he challenges the Eurocentric narrative of the Scientific Revolution that locates the rise of empiricism exclusively in European centers of learning—anatomical theaters, learned societies, and university halls—and treats colonial spaces as mere suppliers of raw specimens for European classification. Against scholars like Lorraine Daston, Peter Dear, and Steven Shapin who have mapped the rise of "facts" and experimental knowledge in Europe, Gómez argues that parallel (and interacting) experiential epistemologies emerged independently among Black Caribbean communities. Second, he pushes back against Atlantic World scholarship (including work on African diasporic healing by Sweet, Thornton, and others) that frames Black practitioners' activities primarily through lenses of cultural survival, resistance, or syncretism—what he calls "dialectical commitments" that assume bounded, identifiable African or European epistemes that then mix. Instead, Gómez argues for radical novelty: Caribbean Mohanes were not preserving or adapting traditions but creating entirely new ways of knowing suited to the unstable, multiethnic realities of the Caribbean. He explicitly distances his work from the Hispanic baroque tradition (studied by Maravall, Bolívar Echeverría) which he sees as nihilistic and backward-looking, contrasting it with the future-oriented, creative optimism of Black Caribbean knowledge-makers.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Gómez mobilizes a phenomenological approach to early modern bodies and sensation, drawing on work in sensory history while insisting on the "foreignness" of early modern flesh. Key theoretical moves include:

* **Criollo epistemologies**: Knowledge production characterized by incorporative, adaptive, transportable practices rather than fixed traditions  
* **The experiential** as epistemic foundation: Truth claims grounded in perceivable, sensorial phenomena rather than appeals to authority or dogma  
* **Wonder/marvel as evidence**: Following but extending European histories of wonder (Daston & Park), showing how astonishing acts established authority  
* **Numinous materialism**: Nature encompassed spiritual, moral, and physical dimensions simultaneously; the natural included entities Europeans would call supernatural  
* **Inspiration over origin**: Drawing on Édouard Glissant and Todd Ochoa, emphasizing search for the new rather than preservation of the old  
* **Rejection of mestizaje**: Refusing frameworks of hybridity or mixing that presuppose bounded, coherent source cultures

The book shares conceptual terrain with social construction of knowledge (Collins, Shapin & Schaffer) but locates knowledge production outside European institutional settings entirely.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Gómez's primary archive consists of **Inquisition trial records** from the tribunals of Cartagena de Indias and Havana, supplemented by ecclesiastical correspondence, administrative reports, hospital records, and contemporary travel accounts. He works with documentation on over **one hundred Black Caribbean ritual practitioners** across the long seventeenth century (c. 1580–1720). Geographic scope spans the Spanish Caribbean broadly: the northern New Kingdom of Granada (present-day Colombia), Panama, Cuba, Hispaniola, Puerto Rico, Venezuela, and their hinterlands. Gómez reads Inquisition records against the grain—not as documents of repression but as "grudging acknowledgments of power" that reveal competitive epistemological encounters. He is attentive to translation practices (many testimonies required African interpreters) and the materiality of ritual objects. Comparative evidence from West and West Central African sources (Capuchin missionary accounts, travel narratives) allows him to trace but not reduce Caribbean practices to African origins.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Bernardo Macaya (Congo, Panama, c. 1675–1705)**: A West Central African ritual practitioner who commanded hurricanes and controlled weather through whistling, maintained an altar with painted minkisi statues, and created protective rituals using blood and palm buds. His case opens the book and demonstrates the transportability of power over nature and the creation of ritual spaces that combined recognizable Congo elements with Caribbean innovations.

**Paula de Eguiluz (Cartagena, 1590s–1650s)**: Born to West African parents in Santo Domingo, this Mohana appears in multiple Inquisition trials across decades. Despite life sentences for "witchcraft," she treated Cartagena's bishop in his final illness (1647–48), illustrating how even elite ecclesiastics relied on Black practitioners. Her extensive wardrobe inventory reveals material success and transatlantic cosmopolitanism; her career trajectory shows the porosity of legal categories and the practical authority of experiential healing.

**Mateo Arará (Bight of Benin/Cartagena, 1640s–50s)**: A high-ranking priest from Allada sold into slavery, Mateo created a diagnostic tool—a "little mat" (*esterita*) animated by chicken blood—to test Caribbean herbs "for Christians" and identify social pollution causing epidemics. His case demonstrates the creation of new technologies for exploring New World nature and the link between physical and social diagnosis.

## **Bernardo Macaya (Congo, active c. 1675–1705)**

Bernardo was a West Central African slave of "Congo caste" who operated around Portobelo, Panama. He was so feared that the chief constable of Portobelo—worried he'd end up like his predecessor (dead)—hastily shipped Bernardo to Cartagena. According to witnesses, the ship couldn't depart for three days because Bernardo commanded the skies to unleash storms, declaring he would "break the ships of those taking him to Cartagena and kill them." Only when a shipmate held him at gunpoint did he relent and calm the sea.

His practice combined several elements:

* **Weather control**: Whistling and verbal commands to hurricanes and storms  
* **Diagnostic ceremonies**: In the free Black town of Nuestra Señora de La Consolación, he was called to treat the governor's wounded hand (suspected sorcery via poisonous plants). Bernardo appeared in the central plaza "dressed in skirts, with a macaw feather on his head," playing a deer antler and bell, dancing for six to eight hours. He identified seven people as witches by smearing their faces with charcoal.  
* **Ritual space**: He maintained a bohío (cane house) with an altar containing a painted tabernacle, seashells, and two clay statues dressed elaborately. When arrested, he ordered the statues placed face-down so they'd "get angry and destroy the land."  
* **Protective magic**: He sealed houses against harm by placing palm buds at entrances and burying chicken blood mixed with blood let from a seven-year-old boy.

Gómez identifies clear parallels to Kongolese *baganga* (ritual specialists) and *minkisi* (power objects), but emphasizes that Bernardo was creating something new—his practices worked for multiethnic Caribbean audiences who wouldn't have recognized specifically Kongolese meanings.

---

## **Paula de Eguiluz, "Alegría" / "Aleluya" (c. 1590s–1650s)**

Paula is perhaps the book's most compelling figure. Daughter of West African slaves (Bañon father, Languara mother), she was born enslaved in Santo Domingo, then lived in Puerto Rico, Santiago de Cuba, and Havana before being shipped to Cartagena for her first Inquisition trial in 1624\.

**Her reputation in Cuba** included:

* Healing and killing with herbs  
* Controlling weather  
* Flying and speaking with animals  
* Making her face appear to be on fire

**Her material success** is evident from the inventory taken at her arrest: silk dresses in green, blue, yellow, and purple; skirts embroidered with gold and silver; linen from Rouen and Holland. This wasn't the wardrobe of a marginal figure—she had thrived.

**Her institutional entanglement** is remarkable. After her first trial, she was sentenced to work at the Hospital of San Sebastián for two years. The friars praised her piety and charity—but she was also secretly preparing her own remedies in pharmacy flasks and conceived a daughter with one of the friars. After completing her sentence, she was freed (possibly manumitted by her owner) and quickly became one of Cartagena's most renowned healers.

**Her second trial** (1632) came partly because Inquisition officials were scandalized that a penitent wore silk and gold. She was condemned to death, but the sentence was reduced on appeal to perpetual imprisonment.

**The bishop episode**: Even while serving a life sentence in the Inquisition jail, Paula was regularly let out to treat patients—including Bishop Cristóbal Pérez de Lazarraga, one of the most powerful ecclesiastics in the Spanish Indies. She visited him almost daily for months, sometimes staying at his palace for twenty days at a time, arriving by litter bearers despite living only blocks away. She was his primary healer until his death in February 1648\. That a twice-condemned "witch" serving life imprisonment was treating the bishop tells you everything about the actual authority of Black practitioners in the healing marketplace.

---

## **Diego López (Cartagena, c. 1593–1658)**

Diego was born enslaved in Cartagena and began working at the San Sebastián hospital as a teenager. By 1611, he was already renowned enough that the Inquisition considered hiring him to treat prisoners—an official report noted he was "the only \[surgeon\] renowned in the city."

He eventually traveled to Santa Fé de Bogotá (high in the Andes) to be officially licensed and examined as a surgeon—a remarkable achievement for a formerly enslaved Black man. He proudly identified himself as a "licensed and examined surgeon" and owned a house in the Black neighborhood of Getsemaní.

**His dual practice**: Diego worked both as a conventional barber-surgeon (bloodletting, treating sword wounds, hemorrhoids, shingles) and as a member of a group of Black ritual specialists who gathered at night on Manzanillo beach and the Santa Catalina stronghold. This got him imprisoned by the Inquisition in the 1630s.

**His strategic maneuvering**: Diego was a fierce competitor who used Inquisition denunciations against rivals. He accused Paula de Eguiluz of witchcraft. He denounced the wealthy Portuguese Jewish slave trader Blas de Paz Pinto (also a surgeon and competitor in treating sick slaves), claiming Paz Pinto "menstruated like a woman"—a classic anti-Jewish trope. Paz Pinto was arrested, tortured, developed tetanus, and died unable to receive communion.

**His cosmopolitan pharmacopeia**: His inventory included preparations from Cartagena pharmacies, "liquids of the Indies" from Portuguese merchants, and an emerald (likely from Andean mines, where indigenous practitioners used them for snakebites).

**His final recorded act** shows his integration into colonial institutions: in 1658, he appeared as an expert witness to diagnose a prisoner with syphilis.

---

## **Mateo Arará (Bight of Benin / Cartagena, 1640s–1650s)**

Mateo may have been a high-ranking priest in Allada (in present-day Benin) before his capture. He described his initiation ceremony, held on the banks of a river "that did not flow," where initiates swore not to "do evil to the people, nor cause rain, or the worms to come and eat the fruits." A mule-like creature emerged from the river, grabbed each initiate, and dragged them to the middle—if they swore falsely, they stayed; if truthfully, they were returned to shore.

**His diagnostic technology**: In the Caribbean, Mateo created an *esterita* (little mat) from palm leaves, divided at the top into two arms, and a *congolón* (small gourd) tied to it. To activate these objects, he sprinkled them with chicken blood and packed them with powders of "counter herbs." When he asked questions, the mat would open (good herb, or innocent person) or close and move side to side (bad herb, or guilty person).

**The Morocí epidemic case**: Mateo's owner rented him to the lieutenant of Mompox to treat an epidemic among enslaved miners. At the gold mine, Mateo gathered the sick slaves in a circle, sat in the center with a pan of wine, had everyone drink, then consulted his mat three times. Each time it pointed to the same man—Ventura Anchico—as the *yerbatero* (sorcerer) causing the disease. Only after three repeated trials did Mateo confirm his diagnosis. He then treated the slaves with a root called *orejon* cooked with honey, water, and herbs. The patients vomited up "bones, hairs and feathers."

**His botanical exploration**: Mateo explicitly told Inquisitors he made the mat "from his wits" after arriving in the Caribbean, specifically to "recognize the good and bad herbs to cure Christians." He used it to test New World plants experimentally—if an herb was beneficial, the mat opened; if harmful, it closed.

---

## **Antonio Congo (West Central Africa / Cartagena, 1680s–1690s)**

Antonio was born in "Patara," which he said was located in the court of the King of Kongo, where his family lived among a Portuguese diaspora. He traveled through Holland, San Lúcar (Spain), New Spain, Santo Domingo, and Curaçao before settling in Cartagena for over twenty years.

**The hurricane**: In the 1680s, witnesses watched Antonio walk out into a tropical storm armed only with a string of tiger fangs and his leprosy-marked body. He whistled three times, and the storm retreated into the sea. He told a neighbor that if not for him, the hurricane would have destroyed their houses. When asked if he thought he was God, Antonio explained that a "giant" had come in the hurricane to destroy his sown field, and he had thrown it into the sea.

**His familial relationship with nature**: Antonio called himself "father" though he had no human children—plants and animals were his offspring. Before renting out his land plots, he blessed them by saying: "My sons who are there in the woodland, move aside and let this man work the land." He forbade anyone from sitting on his doorstep because that's where he kept his herbs—"his sons"—and they spoke to him in dreams, telling him which cures they would assist with.

**His healing practice**: He used the *limpiadientes* root for leg pain and *caraña* for stomach pain. He charged three pesos for cures. His power objects included a string of tiger fangs with a small black pebble someone had given him as a remedy.

---

## **Domingo de La Ascención (Cuba, 1660s)**

Domingo was a second-generation enslaved man born in San José del Cayo, Cuba, whose parents and grandparents came from Guinea. His Inquisition trial contains the most detailed record of any Black Caribbean healer's therapeutic practice—dozens of specific cases.

**His range of treatments**:

* *Hetico* (consumption) and colds with herbs and "natural remedies"  
* Dysentery with preparations placed on the belly while making crosses  
* Bloating with tobacco smoke blown around the room  
* Calentures (fevers) by rubbing arms and armpits with *culantrillo*  
* Abortifacients (an herb to be taken "at noon for it to have effect")  
* Diagnosis of sorcery using pebbles wrapped in cloth—if the ribbon broke, no cure was possible

**His wonder-working**: Domingo cultivated astonishment deliberately:

* He made water turn to blood by secretly cutting his own gums and mouth with a silver tack, then mixing blood into the gourd  
* He made knives "bleed" by squeezing them after cutting his gums—telling witnesses that anyone who stepped on the blood would be harmed  
* He called a "very large and terrible snake" from the woods by whistling, and it wrapped around his body  
* He applied white-hot knives to a patient's legs, who reported feeling only "tickling"

When Inquisitors asked about the bleeding water and knives, Domingo admitted the physical explanations—but emphasized that his goal was to "startle the people present." The astonishment *was* the point.

**His master's fear**: After his master Francisco de Pavia whipped Domingo so badly he was bedridden for eight days, de Pavia saw a feral boar bearing the same wounds lurking near the house. The dogs wouldn't attack it. Terrified, de Pavia threatened to kill Domingo "if he became a feral boar again." Yet this same master still called on Domingo to heal his wife.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Black ritual practitioners led an epistemological revolution in Caribbean intellectual spaces that existed both in parallel and in conversation with European-sponsored projects that explored the natural world." (p. 6\)

"Knowledge about the natural world in the black Caribbean emerged from particulars and did not lay claim to grand principles. It shaped a creative praxis for the production of local power that was transportable." (p. 6\)

"What traveled was not merely specific healing techniques and their meanings, but also people and the powerful creative ways of producing experiential knowledge." (p. 115\)

"He can only believe by virtue of the experience he had." —Juan de Alomera, testifying before the Inquisition, 1696 (p. 227\)

"Caribbean Mohanes were less interested in reinforcing particular paradigms and dogmas in a place in which they could not be ascertained or understood, and more interested in gaining power from the viscerally felt nature of the astonishing." (p. 202\)

"By creating detailed lists describing Caribbean techne and studying these objects under the rubric of a Eurocentric epistemological apparatus, Fernandez and his collaborators exemplified the process through which Europeans labeled the experiential a uniquely Western, scientific mode of knowing." (Conclusion, p. 235\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:**

* Lorraine Daston & Katharine Park, *Wonders and the Order of Nature* (for European wonder tradition; Gómez offers Caribbean parallel)  
* Lorraine Daston & Peter Galison, *Objectivity* (on the rise of evidence-based knowledge; Gómez decenters Europe)  
* Steven Shapin & Simon Schaffer, *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (experimental life and credibility; different actors, different spaces)  
* Pamela Smith, *The Body of the Artisan* (embodied/artisanal knowledge; Gómez extends to non-European practitioners)  
* Harold Cook, *Matters of Exchange* (empiricism and commerce; Gómez shifts from Dutch to Caribbean)  
* Daniella Bleichmar, *Visible Empire* (colonial knowledge production; both challenge Eurocentric epistemologies)  
* Ruth Rogaski, *Knowing Manchuria* (alternative epistemologies in colonial contexts)

**Useful for exam questions on:**

* The geography and social location of the Scientific Revolution  
* Non-European epistemologies and knowledge production  
* The relationship between experience, evidence, and authority in early modern thought  
* African diaspora intellectual history  
* Medical pluralism and healing marketplaces  
* Bodies, sensation, and phenomenology in history  
* Wonder, curiosity, and the marvelous  
* Race, slavery, and knowledge in the Atlantic world

**Limitations/critiques:**

* Reliance on Inquisition sources creates interpretive challenges—testimonies were coerced, translated, and scripted  
* The claim for Caribbean "revolution" independent of European developments may overstate separation  
* Limited engagement with gender beyond scattered examples (though women practitioners like Paula de Eguiluz are present)  
* Could more directly address how these practices related to survival, resistance, and community formation  
* Theoretical density occasionally obscures narrative flow

# **Steven Shapin, *The Scientific Revolution* (1996)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Shapin opens with the deliberately paradoxical assertion: "There was no such thing as the Scientific Revolution, and this is a book about it." His central thesis is that the changes in natural knowledge during the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries were neither singular, coherent, nor universally revolutionary, but rather comprised a heterogeneous array of contested practices, purposes, and beliefs. What we call "the Scientific Revolution" is best understood as an historically interested construction that selects certain "ancestors" and narratives to serve present-day purposes. The book demonstrates that these changes were deeply embedded in social, religious, and political contexts, and that the production of natural knowledge was always a collective, situated activity requiring the mobilization of trust, testimony, and social authority—not merely the passive observation of nature by autonomous individuals.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Shapin writes explicitly against the triumphalist, internalist histories of science exemplified by mid-twentieth-century historians like Alexandre Koyré, Herbert Butterfield, and A. Rupert Hall, who portrayed the Scientific Revolution as "the real origin both of the modern world and of the modern mentality"—a singular, conceptual revolution in which great minds put on "new spectacles" to see nature truly. Against this "Great Men" narrative of disembodied ideas floating free of context, Shapin draws on the sociology of scientific knowledge (SSK) tradition, particularly the work of the Edinburgh School (his colleague David Bloor, and his own collaborations with Simon Schaffer in *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*). He also engages with Bruno Latour's actor-network theory and Peter Dear's work on experience and experimentation.

The book revises several standard narratives: that the period saw a wholesale rejection of the past (continuities with medieval and ancient thought persisted); that "experience" was simply opposed to "authority" (the moderns relied heavily on testimony and social trust); that mechanical philosophy was uniformly anti-religious (it often served as religion's "handmaid"); and that the rejection of teleology was complete (final causes persisted in natural theology and elsewhere). Shapin positions the book as "historiographically up to date" while remaining accessible, aiming to show that "knowledge making and knowledge holding" are fundamentally social processes.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Shapin operates primarily within the **social construction of science** tradition, treating scientific knowledge as "a historically situated and social activity" to be understood in relation to its contexts. He rejects the old distinction between "internal" (ideas) and "external" (social) factors, insisting that "there is as much society inside the scientist's laboratory... as there is outside." Key concepts include:

* **Practices over beliefs**: Understanding what practitioners *did* to make, secure, and communicate knowledge, not just what they believed  
* **The social constitution of experience**: Experience reports required social validation through testimony, expertise, and gentlemanly codes of honor  
* **Boundary work**: The distinction between "science" and "society," or between the natural and the social, was itself a cultural product of this period  
* **Interests and purposes**: Natural knowledge served religious, political, and moral ends; its "disinterestedness" was itself a constructed and strategic resource  
* **Anti-essentialism**: There was no single "essence" of the Scientific Revolution; multiple legitimate stories can be told

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Shapin writes a synthetic, interpretive history drawing on extensive secondary literature rather than primary archival research. The book is organized around three questions—"What was known?" (Chapter 1), "How was it known?" (Chapter 2), and "What was the knowledge for?" (Chapter 3)—providing conceptual rather than chronological structure.

Sources and materials include: natural philosophical texts by Galileo, Descartes, Bacon, Boyle, Newton, Hobbes, Pascal, and others; visual evidence from period engravings and frontispieces (the Strasbourg clock, Bacon's *Instauratio magna*, anatomical illustrations); and extensive engagement with recent historiography. There is a moderate bias toward English materials and experimental/empirical sciences, which Shapin acknowledges as a corrective to previous overemphasis on mathematical physics and Continental settings.

**Temporal scope**: late sixteenth to early eighteenth century, with focus on c.1550–1700  
 **Geographic scope**: Western Europe, with emphasis on England, France, and Italy

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Galileo's sunspot observations (pp. 15-18)**: Shapin uses Galileo's interpretation of sunspots as contiguous with the solar surface to illustrate the challenge to Aristotelian physics (which held the heavens to be immutable) and the argument that "names and attributes must be accommodated to the essence of things, and not the essence to the names." This exemplifies the shift from a priori reasoning to observation-based inference, while also showing that the reliability of telescopic observation was itself deeply contested.

**Torricelli's barometer and Pascal's Puy de Dome experiment (pp. 38-42)**: The mercury barometer exemplifies the mechanical philosophy's replacement of anthropomorphic explanations (nature's "abhorrence" of a vacuum) with mechanical ones (the weight of atmospheric air). Yet Shapin notes that Pascal initially thought Torricelli had only proved the *finite* strength of abhorrence, and that the Puy de Dome experiment itself may not have been performed as described—raising questions about the status of "experience" versus "thought experiment."
