**Boyle's air pump and the making of experimental facts (pp. 96-118)**: The air pump serves as the Scientific Revolution's "greatest fact-making machine" and an emblem of how experimental philosophy constructed credible knowledge. Shapin shows that producing "matters of fact" required elaborate social technologies: multiple witnesses, public demonstrations, detailed circumstantial narratives, the invocation of gentlemanly honor, and the construction of "virtual witnessing" through prose that allowed readers to feel they had seen experiments themselves.

## **Galileo**

The standard Galileo story, as Koyré and Butterfield told it, portrays him as the paradigmatic modern: a man who looked through his telescope, saw the truth of nature, and thereby refuted centuries of Aristotelian dogma through the simple act of observation. The famous story of the Paduan professor who refused to look through the telescope serves as the morality tale—here is obscurantist authority refusing the evidence of the senses.

Shapin complicates this at every turn. First, he points out that Galileo's sunspot observations were not self-evidently meaningful. Other contemporary observers looked at the same phenomena and concluded the spots were small planets orbiting at considerable distance from the sun. The *interpretation* that the spots were "contiguous" with the solar surface—which carried the radical implication that the heavens were mutable and imperfect—required sophisticated calculations in mathematical optics, not naive observation. Seeing through a telescope, Shapin emphasizes, is "skilled seeing under special conditions" (p. 73). When Galileo assembled eminent practitioners to witness the moons of Jupiter, many of them allowed that the telescope worked "wonderfully" for terrestrial vision but "failed" or "deceived" in the celestial realm. One witness reported that "more than twenty learned men were present; yet nobody has seen the new \[moons\] distinctly" (p. 73).

This wasn't stupidity or obscurantism. Contemporary theories of vision raised legitimate questions about whether telescopic observation of the heavens was reliable. We use our familiarity with terrestrial objects to automatically correct for instrumental distortions; that familiarity isn't available for celestial bodies. Shapin notes that Galileo himself lacked a powerful theory of vision that could ground the reliability of his instrument. The resources that allow us as students to confidently see what Galileo claimed to see—"a culture that had already granted the reliability of these instruments... that had already decided for us what sorts of things authentically existed in the domains of the very distant and very small, and that had provided structures of authority within which we could learn what to see (and what to disregard)"—were precisely what Galileo had to "laboriously create and disseminate" (p. 73).

Second, Shapin dwells on Galileo's famous inclined-plane experiments, supposedly the empirical warrant for the mathematically expressed law of fall. Galileo claimed he did these experiments "often," even "a hundred times," with results fully agreeing with his theory. But historians have long debated whether these experiments were ever actually performed or whether they're better understood as "thought experiments"—imaginative rehearsals of what *would* happen given what we already securely know about the physical world. Shapin follows Peter Dear's analysis here: Galileo wasn't saying "'I did this and this, and this is what happened, from which we can conclude...' Rather, he was saying, 'This is what *happens*'" (p. 84). The "experience" being produced was closer to the Aristotelian sense of universal statements about how things behave in nature than to the modern sense of discrete, particular, witnessed events.

This reframing matters because it unsettles the tidy opposition between "modern experimental science" and "medieval book-learning." Galileo, that icon of empiricism, may have been operating with a notion of experience more continuous with Scholastic practice than the standard story admits.

## **Bacon**

Bacon gets similar treatment. The traditional narrative presents him as the prophet of induction, the man who told natural philosophers to put aside their books, go out into nature, observe, and let the facts speak for themselves. His *Novum Organum* promised a "total reconstruction of sciences, arts, and all human knowledge, raised upon the proper foundations" (p. 66).

Shapin doesn't dispute Bacon's historical importance, but he reframes what Bacon was actually doing. First, Bacon's "induction" wasn't mindless fact-collecting. It included "crucial instances" meant to decide unambiguously between alternative theories, and it required that the senses be "instructed by rational method" to yield reliable information. Neither sense nor reason alone sufficed. This is a more sophisticated picture than the caricature of Baconianism as anti-theoretical empiricism.

Second, and more importantly, Shapin emphasizes that Bacon's program was fundamentally about *social organization* and *state power*. The architectural metaphor—tear down the old edifice, build anew on proper foundations—wasn't just about epistemology. It was about bringing knowledge under administrative control. When Bacon announced he had "taken all knowledge to be my province," he was using the Elizabethan English for the Latin *provincia*—an administrative district of central government. Knowledge was to be effectively brought within the state's compass.

Shapin reads *New Atlantis* not as utopian fantasy but as a blueprint for bureaucratically organized, state-funded research in service of empire. Solomon's House in Bensalem is staffed by state officials on state salaries, and its purpose is twofold: "the knowledge of Causes" and "the enlarging of the bounds of Human Empire" (p. 130). The cure for natural philosophy's "distempers"—its notorious divisiveness and disputatiousness—was to be enforced collective method under state oversight. Individual "voluntaries" pursuing private belief were dangerous; disciplined collective labor was the answer.

This connects Bacon's methodological pronouncements to the "permanent crisis" of European institutions—the breakdown of unified religious authority after the Reformation, the wars of religion, the problem of skepticism as the solvent of all secure belief. Method, broadly construed, was proposed as the remedy for intellectual disorder, and intellectual disorder was understood to threaten social order. "Addressing the problem of knowledge, and the skepticism that corrodes belief, is what links philosophers' work to the concerns of the wider society" (p. 125).

Shapin also notes the *religious* framing of Bacon's program. The title page of the *Instauratio magna* shows a ship sailing beyond the Pillars of Hercules (the traditional limits of knowledge), with a quotation from Daniel: "Many shall pass to and fro, and science shall be increased." The expansion of knowledge was the fulfillment of biblical prophecy. The technical utility of new knowledge would restore humanity's dominion over nature lost at the Fall—a religious duty. Some practitioners even understood this in millenarian terms: only when humanity had restored its original dominion would Christ come again.

## **Boyle and the Air Pump**

The Boyle material is where Shapin's approach becomes most distinctive, drawing directly on his collaboration with Simon Schaffer in *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*. The traditional story presents Boyle's air pump experiments as straightforward empirical demonstrations of the weight of air, decisive refutations of Aristotelian horror vacui.

Shapin reframes the air pump as the Scientific Revolution's "greatest fact-making machine" (p. 96)—but emphasizes that "facts" had to be *made*, not simply discovered. The production of experimental "matters of fact" required elaborate social technologies:

**Witnessing and testimony**: Experiments were performed before multiple witnesses, preferably gentlemen whose honor would guarantee the reliability of their reports. The naming of witnesses, their social standing, their expertise—all this was essential to establishing credibility. "Some recent historical work has pointed to the importantly *gentlemanly* constitution of the new practice, and consequently to the importance of gentle codes of honor and truth telling" (p. 88).

**Virtual witnessing**: Since not everyone could be physically present at experiments, Boyle developed prose techniques—detailed circumstantial narratives, engravings of apparatus, specification of particular times and places—that allowed readers to feel they had witnessed experiments themselves. This extended the community of competent witnesses far beyond those actually present.

**Modesty and provisionalism**: Boyle cultivated a rhetoric of tentativeness, acknowledging the limitations of his apparatus and the possibility of error. This wasn't weakness but strategic humility—it made his factual claims more credible precisely by acknowledging uncertainty about their theoretical interpretation.

**The exclusion of controversy**: The experimental community explicitly banned discussion of religion and politics from its proceedings. "Matters of fact" were to be separated from contentious matters of interpretation. The Royal Society's charter prohibited fellows from speaking of religion or politics during scientific meetings. This wasn't neutral proceduralism; it was a deliberate strategy to create consensus in a culture riven by civil war and religious conflict.

Shapin's point is that Boyle's "experimental facts" weren't simply read off nature by passive observers. They were social achievements, requiring the mobilization of trust, the policing of testimony, the construction of credibility. The experimental community had to be constituted as a legitimate arbiter of natural knowledge—and this was a political as much as an epistemic project.

## **Descartes**

Descartes gets perhaps the most interesting treatment because he represents an alternative version of mechanical philosophy to the English experimental tradition. Where Boyle accumulated particular experimental facts and professed tentativeness about theoretical interpretation, Descartes sought demonstrative certainty through deductive method.

Shapin notes that Descartes's method was *individualistic* in a way that troubled even other moderns. He shut himself up alone "in a stove-heated room" and resolved to set aside all the philosophic texts he had ever read. This was not Bacon's collective bureaucratic labor but radical private meditation. Yet Descartes himself "explicitly worried about the effects his version of methodological modernism would have were it to be collectively adopted": he warned against the idea that everyone should overturn established institutions to start anew, claiming his maxims were meant for himself alone given his "own situation and his own idiosyncratic temperament" (p. 67, n.1).

More substantively, Shapin emphasizes that Descartes's mechanical explanations were *imaginary* in a precise technical sense. Descartes wasn't explaining the body or world that God *did* create but one he *might* have created—a hypothetically possible world wholly amenable to mechanical explanation. This imagined world was "judged to be rationally comprehensible and not to conflict significantly with what was reliably known about the workings of the real body and real natural world" (p. 158, n.7). Many readers set aside the caveat and took Descartes's distinction as merely a rhetorical device, but it mattered for how the explanatory program was supposed to work.

The Cartesian body-machine is another key example. Descartes explained digestion, circulation, respiration, and reflex action in purely mechanical terms—the human body was "a statue, an earthen machine," incomparably better arranged than human artifice but a machine nonetheless. Yet Shapin emphasizes that this mechanical program ended in mystery. Human beings have minds, and the phenomena of mind—will, purpose, consciousness, moral evaluation, language—are not ultimately accountable by matter in motion. The world contains two qualitatively different realms, matter and mind, and only in human beings do they meet. *Where* they meet (Descartes nominated the pineal gland, "the seat of the soul") remained "a primary, and therefore unexplicated, notion" (p. 160).

Shapin's point is that even the most aggressive mechanical program preserved a place for mystery and for the special status of human beings. Anthropocentrism was rejected in cosmology but retained in the philosophy of mind. "The special place of human beings in nature was at once a solution to a problem and a problem that might come to demand solution, a legacy of the Scientific Revolution to its cultural heirs" (p. 161).

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"There was no such thing as the Scientific Revolution, and this is a book about it." (p. 1\)

"I take for granted that science is a historically situated and social activity and that it is to be understood in relation to the contexts in which it occurs." (p. 9\)

g" (p. 10\)

"The very idea of the Scientific Revolution, therefore, is at least partly an expression of 'our' interest in our ancestors, where 'we' are late twentieth-century scientists and those for whom what they believe counts as truth about the natural world." (pp. 6-7)

"The condition for a proper natural philosophy was its foundation in a laboriously compiled factual register of natural history... 'Nothing duly investigated, nothing verified, nothing counted, weighed, or measured, is to be found in natural history; and what in observation is loose and vague, is in information deceptive and treacherous.'" (Bacon, quoted p. 87\)

"The more a body of knowledge is understood to be objective and disinterested, the more valuable it is as a tool in moral and political action... the most powerful storehouse of value in our modern culture is the body of knowledge we consider to have least to do with the discourse of moral value." (p. 164\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:**

* Daston & Galison, *Objectivity* (on the historical construction of scientific objectivity)  
* Shapin & Schaffer, *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (deeper dive into Boyle's experimental program)  
* Dear, *Discipline and Experience* (on the constitution of experience in early modern science)  
* Latour & Woolgar, *Laboratory Life* (sociological analysis of fact-making)  
* Smith, *The Body of the Artisan* (on practice and material culture in early modern knowledge)

**Contrasts with:**

* Koyré's internalist histories of ideas  
* Butterfield's *Origins of Modern Science* (the triumphalist narrative Shapin revises)  
* Hall's traditional *Scientific Revolution* (1954)

**Generals exam questions this book helps answer:**

* What was the "Scientific Revolution" and in what sense was it revolutionary?  
* How did early modern practitioners constitute "experience" as a basis for natural philosophy?  
* What was the relationship between science and religion in the seventeenth century?  
* How did social and political contexts shape the content and practices of natural knowledge?  
* What role did mechanical philosophy play in early modern thought?  
* How were trust, testimony, and authority mobilized in the production of scientific knowledge?  
* What purposes did natural knowledge serve in early modern Europe?

**Limitations/Critiques:**

* The focus on English materials and experimental philosophy underrepresents mathematical physics and Continental developments  
* The anti-essentialist framework, while historiographically productive, can make it difficult to characterize what *was* distinctive about the period  
* Critics have noted that Shapin's social constructionism can seem to dissolve the reality of natural knowledge into social negotiations  
* The book is explicitly introductory and synthetic; specialists will find many generalizations that require qualification

# **Visible Empire: Botanical Expeditions and Visual Culture in the Hispanic Enlightenment**

**Daniela Bleichmar (University of Chicago Press, 2012\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Bleichmar argues that the Spanish natural history expeditions of the late eighteenth century were fundamentally "visualization projects" in which the production of images—not text, specimens, or commodities—constituted both the primary method and the principal result of botanical exploration. Making imperial nature *visible* through carefully crafted illustrations was understood as the essential precondition for making it movable, knowable, and governable. The expeditions' twelve thousand botanical images thus reveal how "visual epistemology"—a way of knowing premised on expert observation and standardized pictorial representation—operated simultaneously within European natural history and the Hispanic imperial administrative apparatus.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Visible Empire* intervenes in several scholarly conversations at once. Against traditional histories of science that treat illustrations as decorative supplements to textual knowledge, Bleichmar insists that images were "much more than simple illustrations: they provided an entry point into the exploration of nature, functioned as a key instrument for producing knowledge, and constituted the foremost result of their investigations" (p. 8). She directly engages with Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison's concept of "collective empiricism" from *Objectivity* (2007), extending it to show how images enabled long-distance observation across the Spanish empire. Her analysis also revises center-periphery models of colonial knowledge production—particularly prevalent in earlier scholarship on Spanish imperial science—demonstrating instead that the empire "functioned as a network with multiple nodes and competing interests" (p. 14). Bleichmar draws on Ian Hacking's concept of "styles of reasoning" to frame visual epistemology as a distinct mode of knowing with its own techniques, materials, and standards. She positions her work against the catch-all category of "hybridity" in studies of colonial visual culture (following Carolyn Dean and Dana Leibsohn's critique), arguing instead that stylistic differences between European models and American productions reflect conscious choices rather than failed imitation.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

The book's central conceptual apparatus is **visual epistemology**: "a way of knowing based on visuality, encompassing both observation and representation" (p. 8). Bleichmar mobilizes this concept to bridge art history and history of science, treating images as historical sources rather than mere illustrations. She also employs the notion of **collective empiricism** (from Daston and Galison) to explain how naturalists across vast distances could collaborate through the exchange of images, specimens, and texts. The book draws on **visual history** as method—using images not merely as objects of analysis but as primary evidence equivalent to textual archives. Additional concepts include the distinction between **making visible** (the process of observation and representation) and **seeing** (the act of viewing finished images), and the tension between **global white space** (the decontextualized blank page of botanical illustration) and **local color** (the contextualized representations of casta paintings and regional visual traditions).

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Bleichmar works across three interlocking archives: a visual collection of over twelve thousand botanical illustrations housed primarily at Madrid's Royal Botanical Garden; a textual collection of manuscript journals, letters, scientific treatises, and administrative reports; and a material collection of natural history specimens. The geographic scope spans the entire Spanish empire—Chile, Peru, New Granada (Colombia), New Spain (Mexico), the Philippines—during the period 1777–1816. Her method involves close visual analysis of individual images, reconstruction of workshop practices through correspondence, and comparison of published European models with American productions. She treats images with the same hermeneutic rigor typically reserved for texts, attending to iconographic conventions, material properties (tempera vs. watercolor), and the social contexts of production and circulation.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The New Granada Expedition Workshop (1783–1816):** Under José Celestino Mutis's direction, this expedition assembled the largest artistic workshop of any scientific endeavor worldwide, producing approximately 6,500 botanical illustrations. Mutis hired painters from Quito's renowned religious workshops and retrained them as botanical draftsmen, preferring their "docility" and malleability to academically trained Spanish artists. The workshop developed a distinctive tempera style that Mutis considered superior to European models, demonstrating that stylistic departures from published norms were conscious innovations rather than colonial failures.

**The Cavanilles-Ruiz Controversy:** A dispute between the Madrid-based botanist Antonio José Cavanilles and the traveling naturalist Hipólito Ruiz over who deserved authorship credit for newly described plants illuminates the tension between cabinet and field naturalists. Cavanilles argued that "the true author of a plant is only the one who makes it known to the public"—that is, the one who correctly classifies it—while Ruiz claimed priority as the traveler who gathered specimens. This debate, conducted in visual terms, reveals how images could substitute for travel itself.

**Casta Paintings and the Quadro del Perú:** In contrast to the "global white space" of decontextualized botanical illustrations, Mexican casta paintings and Luis Thiebaut's encyclopedic *Quadro del Perú* embedded natural history within local social, racial, and geographic context. These works demonstrate that the blank-page convention of natural history illustration was a choice, not a necessity—and that contemporaneous American artists developed alternative visual epistemologies that insisted on the inseparability of nature, place, and human populations.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The Spanish eighteenth-century natural history expeditions, I argue, acted as visualization projects. One of their key goals was to make global nature visible across distances through images and collections. Visibility, in turn, would make imperial nature movable, knowable, and—ideally—governable." (p. 7\)

"For naturalists, images were much more than simple illustrations: they provided an entry point into the exploration of nature, functioned as a key instrument for producing knowledge, and constituted the foremost result of their investigations." (p. 8\)

"My central concept for explaining why images mattered so greatly in eighteenth-century natural history is what I term 'visual epistemology': a way of knowing based on visuality, encompassing both observation and representation." (p. 8\)

"The Hispanic world functioned as a visual machine, churning out a prodigious quantity of images that made this vast empire visible locally and across distances." (p. 10\)

"To be a traveler is not the same thing as to be a Botanist, nor to see plants as to be a competent Judge to determine their fructification, genus, and species." (Cavanilles, quoted p. 75\)

"I trained for this task several young men who already possessed at least the rudiments of drawing; I am present, I direct, I devote myself with such patience as it is not easy to grasp, and from this the result that here, where there was no idea of this kind of painting, where there were no models to imitate, we have opened new roads with intrepid daring, and with the happiest success." (Mutis, quoted p. 90\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:** Daston and Galison's *Objectivity* (collective empiricism, visual standards in science); Pamela H. Smith's *The Body of the Artisan* (material practice and knowledge production); Pablo Gómez's *The Experiential Caribbean* (colonial knowledge networks); Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (virtual witnessing); Bruno Latour's work on immutable mobiles and centers of calculation; David Arnold and Beth Fowkes Tobin on tropical visual culture and empire.

**Exam questions this could address:** How did visual practices shape scientific knowledge production in the early modern period? What role did images play in colonial governance and imperial information-gathering? How did non-European actors participate in and contest European knowledge systems? What is the relationship between art history and history of science as disciplines? How did classification systems (Linnaean and otherwise) operate through visual representation?

**Limitations:** The book focuses heavily on production rather than reception—Bleichmar acknowledges that most images never reached print and thus were rarely seen. The analysis centers on institutional and elite actors; indigenous knowledge systems and local informants appear primarily as resources extracted by expeditions rather than as agents in their own right. Economic botany's failures receive less sustained attention than taxonomic botany's visual successes.

# **Peter Burke, *What is the History of Knowledge?* (2015)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Burke argues that the history of knowledge has emerged as a distinct semi-discipline that extends beyond traditional history of science to encompass the full range of human "knowledges"—plural, situated, and socially embedded. The book serves as both a conceptual introduction and a methodological primer, contending that knowledge must be understood through its cultural production, dissemination, and employment across different "orders" and traditions. In one sentence: knowledge is always multiple, socially situated, and subject to processes of gathering, analysis, dissemination, and application that themselves have histories.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Burke positions the history of knowledge as an outgrowth of—and corrective to—the history of science, which he sees as constrained by anachronism (the term "science" itself being a nineteenth-century construction), ethnocentrism (the assumption of Western scientific superiority), and disciplinary narrowness. He draws heavily on the sociology of knowledge tradition stemming from Karl Mannheim and extended by Pierre Bourdieu, while also integrating insights from Foucault's archaeology of knowledge and the "strong programme" of the Edinburgh School (David Bloor). Burke explicitly engages with the "knowledge society" literature of Drucker, Castells, and Bell, historicizing their claims rather than accepting them as descriptions of unprecedented novelty. He challenges triumphalist narratives of knowledge accumulation, arguing alongside Kuhn and Bachelard for attention to ruptures, losses, and the "situated" character of all knowing. The intervention is essentially synthetic: Burke marshals an enormous range of scholarship to argue for a broader, more comparative, and more sociologically informed approach than the history of science alone has provided.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Burke operates within a loosely constructivist epistemology, though he favors the term "reconstruction" over "invention" to avoid implying creation ex nihilo. His key concepts include:

* **Orders of knowledge**: Systematic configurations of institutions, practices, and values that structure what counts as knowledge in particular cultures (cf. Foucault's "episteme")  
* **Situated knowledge**: Following Mannheim's "Seinsverbundenheit" and Donna Haraway's feminist epistemology, knowledge is always bound to social position, gender, place, and community  
* **Tacit knowledge**: Borrowing from Michael Polanyi, embodied knowing-how that resists textualization  
* **Knowledges (plural)**: Explicitly following Foucault's *savoirs*, Burke insists on multiplicity against any unified "Knowledge with a capital K"  
* **Information versus knowledge**: Information is "raw," knowledge is "cooked"—processed through classification, criticism, verification, and systematization

He draws on Bourdieu's concepts of "field," "habitus," and "cultural capital" for analyzing academic structures, and deploys Latour's "centres of calculation" for understanding the geopolitics of knowledge production.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

This is a work of synthetic historiography rather than archival research. Burke surveys scholarship across disciplines—history of science, sociology of knowledge, anthropology, economics, geography, and political science—to map the terrain of "knowledge studies." The geographic scope is genuinely global, with attention to Chinese, Islamic, Indian, and Latin American traditions of knowledge alongside European ones. Temporal scope ranges from antiquity to the present, with particular attention to early modern Europe (Burke's own area of expertise) and the institutional transformations of the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. The methodology is essentially taxonomic and comparative: Burke organizes the field through conceptual categories (authorities, curiosity, disciplines, innovation, intellectuals, etc.) and processual stages (gathering, analyzing, disseminating, employing).

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Captain Cook's First Voyage (1768-71)**: Burke uses this expedition to illustrate the entanglement of pure scientific inquiry with imperial knowledge-gathering. Joseph Banks's botanical collecting and anthropological observations of Tahiti were both driven by curiosity and shaped by classical education (he saw Tahiti as "arcadia"). The Admiralty's instructions to observe "the Nature of the Soil and the Products thereof" reveal how imperial interests structured ostensibly scientific research.

**British India and "Subjugated Knowledges"**: Drawing on Christopher Bayly and Bernard Cohn, Burke shows how British rule both depended on and transformed indigenous knowledge systems. The British "re-ordered" Indian knowledge through their "investigative modalities," yet this was a joint production involving Indian guides, translators, and clerks. The caste system, as Nicholas Dirks argues, was "reconstructed" through the colonial encounter rather than simply discovered.

**Ludwik Fleck and "Thought Collectives"**: Burke uses Fleck's 1930s work to illustrate how scientific communities develop distinctive "styles of thought" (Denkstil) that make their own assumptions seem natural while others appear arbitrary. This becomes a key example of how the sociology of knowledge challenges any simple notion of scientific objectivity.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"We can know more than we can tell." (p. 37, quoting Polanyi—illustrates the concept of tacit knowledge)

"Every accumulation of knowledge and especially such as is obtained by social communication with people over whom we exercise dominion … is useful to the state." (p. 35, quoting Warren Hastings—captures the power/knowledge nexus)

"The exercise of power perpetually creates knowledge and conversely, knowledge constantly induces effects of power." (p. 9, quoting Foucault—the book's theoretical touchstone)

"There are knowledges, not simply Knowledge with a capital K." (p. 6, quoting Peter Worsley—the book's methodological premise)

"Nothing is given. Everything is constructed." (p. 115, quoting Bachelard—the constructivist position Burke partly endorses)

"There was no such thing as the Scientific Revolution, and this is a book about it." (p. 109, quoting Steven Shapin—exemplifies the critique of revolutionary narratives)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:**

* **Daston and Galison, *Objectivity*** (2007): Both historicize scientific ideals; Burke draws explicitly on their work  
* **Shapin and Schaffer, *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*** (1985): Burke's discussion of situated knowledge and the social construction of scientific facts owes much to this text  
* **Foucault, *The Order of Things*** (1970): Burke extends Foucault's archaeology to a broader history of knowledges  
* **Latour and Woolgar, *Laboratory Life*** (1979): Burke's constructivism builds on their ethnography of science  
* **Cohn, *Colonialism and Its Forms of Knowledge*** (1996): Burke engages directly with Cohn's analysis of British India

**Useful for exam questions on:**

* The relationship between history of science and history of knowledge  
* Constructivism and relativism in science studies  
* Colonial knowledge production and "subjugated knowledges"  
* Tacit versus explicit knowledge  
* Disciplinary formation and professionalization  
* The "knowledge society" and its historical antecedents  
* Gender and the production of knowledge (especially vis-à-vis Londa Schiebinger, Donna Haraway)

**Limitations:** Burke's synthetic approach means the book is more useful as an orientation than as a source of deep empirical content. The conceptual glossary format (Chapter 2\) can feel like an inventory rather than an argument. His own position—somewhere between strong constructivism and realism, favoring "reconstruction" over "invention"—remains somewhat undertheorized. The book is more effective at mapping the field than at making a distinctive historiographical intervention of its own, functioning primarily as a sophisticated literature review for a rapidly expanding area of inquiry.

# **Wonders and the Order of Nature, 1150–1750**

## **Lorraine Daston and Katharine Park (1998)**

---

### **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Daston and Park argue that wonder and wonders—far from being marginal curiosities—were central to how European elites defined the natural order from the High Middle Ages through the Enlightenment. Wonders as objects marked the outermost limits of the natural, while wonder as a passion registered the boundary between the known and unknown. The disappearance of marvels from elite intellectual culture in the eighteenth century resulted not from a triumph of rationality or science, but from a transformation in the self-definition of intellectuals: marvels became vulgar, aesthetically distasteful, and politically suspect. In one sentence: the history of wonders is simultaneously a history of the orders of nature and the evolving collective sensibility of naturalists, revealing how objective order and subjective emotion were two sides of the same coin.

---

### **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Wonders and the Order of Nature* challenges several entrenched narratives in the history of science. Most directly, Daston and Park reject the familiar story of Enlightenment rationality displacing medieval superstition—the "disenchantment of the world" narrative associated with Max Weber. They demonstrate that leading Enlightenment intellectuals did not systematically debunk marvels through empirical testing or rational explanation; rather, they simply excluded wonders from respectable discourse on metaphysical, aesthetic, and political grounds. The book also pushes back against the periodization that sharply divides medieval from early modern science, showing instead that attitudes toward wonders were "undulatory, continuous, sometimes cyclical" rather than punctuated by clear epistemic breaks. Against Jacques Le Goff and others who define marvels anachronistically as "what we now call the fantastic," Daston and Park insist on attending to what historical actors themselves meant by wonder—a well-defined cognitive passion with specific objects, not a residual category of irrationality. Their emphasis on sensibility as historically variable engages with cultural history approaches (Caroline Bynum, Mary Douglas) while resisting the tendency to treat wonders as primarily "popular culture" or sites of resistance to elite power. Instead, they show how wonders were constitutive of elite identity from medieval abbots to seventeenth-century virtuosi.

---

### **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Daston and Park draw on cultural history of the emotions, treating wonder as a "cognitive passion"—simultaneously about knowing and feeling. They historicize the passions themselves, showing how wonder's relationships with other emotions (horror, curiosity, pleasure) shifted across contexts and centuries. The book employs a loosely ontological framework, tracking the category of the "preternatural" (phenomena suspended between the mundane natural and the miraculous supernatural) as it emerged, flourished, and disappeared. Their approach owes something to anthropological work on classification and category-transgression (Mary Douglas), but they resist reducing wonders to expressions of pollution anxiety. Key concepts introduced include "preternatural philosophy" (a sixteenth- and seventeenth-century mode of inquiry privileging empirical particulars and marvelous effects), the distinction between regularity of causes and regularity of effects in conceptions of natural law, and the notion that the Enlightenment order of nature was defined by the "anti-marvelous"—uniform, decorous, and verisimilar.

---

### **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

The authors synthesize an extraordinary range of sources across six centuries and much of western Europe: medieval encyclopedias and travel narratives (Gervase of Tilbury, Gerald of Wales, Marco Polo); scholastic natural philosophy (Albertus Magnus, Thomas Aquinas, Nicole Oresme); court inventories and Wunderkammer catalogues; medical treatises on hot springs and therapeutics; printed broadsides and monster pamphlets; learned society transactions (Royal Society, Académie Royale des Sciences); aesthetic theory and literary criticism; and Enlightenment philosophes. Visual evidence is central—manuscript illuminations, printed illustrations, portraits of collections—allowing analysis of how wonders were represented and displayed. Their geographic scope is primarily western European (with particular strength in Italy, France, Germany, and England), though they track the role of distant lands (India, Africa, the New World) as imagined sources of marvels. Temporal scope extends from roughly 1150 to 1750, with the sharpest rupture located in the late seventeenth and early eighteenth centuries.

---

### **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Monster of Ravenna (1512):** A conjoined twin born in Ravenna became a widely circulated portent across Italy and northern Europe, appearing in chronicles, broadsides, and humanist treatises. Daston and Park trace how commentators read the monster's deformities as an emblematic text—its horn signifying pride, wings indicating frivolity, raptor's foot marking avarice, double sex representing sodomy—presaging the subsequent sack of Ravenna. This case illustrates how monsters served as moral and political signs in an eschatological framework, while also showing the explosion of prodigy culture enabled by print technology.

**Robert Boyle's Glowing Veal (1672):** When Boyle's servant discovered luminescent meat in the larder, Boyle's response epitomizes the seventeenth-century marriage of wonder and curiosity. Despite illness and the late hour, he immediately began investigating, experiencing "both wonder and delight" at this strange fact. The episode exemplifies how Baconian natural philosophers rehabilitated wonder as a goad to inquiry—a striking contrast to both medieval scholastics who disdained wonder as ignorance and Enlightenment savants who would dismiss such phenomena as vulgar.

**Wunderkammern and the Art-Nature Boundary:** The princely cabinets of curiosity displayed objects that deliberately blurred or challenged the opposition between natural and artificial: nautilus shells mounted in gold, paintings on alabaster exploiting the stone's natural markings, automata that seemed to possess life's self-motion. These hybrid objects raised philosophical questions about whether nature could anticipate or imitate art—and vice versa—that Bacon and Descartes would later exploit in their programs to make natural philosophy operational.

## **What Counts as a "Wonder"**

Daston and Park insist on recovering what medieval and early modern Europeans themselves meant by *mirabilia* (Latin), *merveilles* (French), *meraviglia* (Italian), or *Wunder* (German). The key defining features are:

**1\. Rarity or unusualness** — Wonders had to be uncommon. As Gervase of Tilbury explained around 1210, we marvel at things that are "rare" or that deviate from "the course of nature." Something could cease to be wonderful simply by becoming familiar.

**2\. Ignorance of cause** — Even common phenomena could provoke wonder if their causes were unknown or counterintuitive. The lodestone's attraction of iron was a perennial wonder precisely because it seemed to violate ordinary mechanical causation.

**3\. The emotion they provoked** — Crucially, the objects and the passion were mutually defining. The Latin *mirabilia* and *admiratio* share the same root (connected, interestingly, to an Indo-European word for "smile"). A wonder was something that provoked wonder—that visceral, cognitive-emotional response of astonishment at boundaries being crossed.

## **The Canon of Wonders**

The book identifies a remarkably stable core of wondrous objects persisting from antiquity through the early modern period, with a shifting penumbra as items were added or debunked:

**Marvelous substances and objects:**

* The lodestone/magnet (mysterious attractive power)  
* The carbuncle (a gemstone said to glow in the dark)  
* Unicorn horns (actually narwhal tusks, valued for detecting poison)  
* Bezoar stones (supposedly formed in animal stomachs, used as antidotes)  
* Petrifying springs (waters that turned objects to stone)  
* Asbestos/salamander wool (fireproof material)  
* Coral, amber, and figured stones

**Marvelous creatures:**

* The phoenix (self-immolating and regenerating)  
* The basilisk (whose gaze could kill)  
* Dragons and sea serpents  
* The "monstrous races" at the edges of the known world—Cynocephali (dog-headed people), Blemmyae (headless people with faces in their chests), Sciapods (one-footed people who used their foot as a sunshade), pygmies, giants  
* Exotic but real animals that seemed almost as fabulous: elephants, rhinoceroses, ostriches, birds of paradise

**Monstrous births:**

* Conjoined twins  
* Infants with extra or missing limbs  
* Hermaphrodites  
* Human-animal hybrids (often reported, rarely verified)  
* Parasitic twins

**Geographical marvels:**

* Volcanic eruptions (Vesuvius, Etna)  
* Therapeutic hot springs  
* The "Burning Mountain" and other wonders of specific regions  
* Distant lands themselves—India, Ethiopia, the far East, and later the New World—as zones where nature's ordinary rules seemed suspended

**Celestial and meteorological marvels:**

* Comets  
* Eclipses  
* Rains of blood, frogs, or stones  
* Aurora borealis  
* Unusual cloud formations

**Artificial marvels:**

* Automata (mechanical figures that seemed to move on their own)  
* Elaborate clockwork  
* Ingenious fountains and grottos  
* Objects that blurred art and nature—nautilus shells mounted in gold, paintings on marble that exploited the stone's natural veining

## **The Preternatural Category**

One of Daston and Park's key contributions is recovering the ontological category of the **preternatural** (*praeter naturam*—"beyond nature" but not "above nature"). Medieval and early modern thinkers distinguished three domains:

1. **Natural** — ordinary phenomena operating according to nature's usual course  
2. **Preternatural** — extraordinary phenomena that exceeded nature's *usual* operations but were still produced by natural (not divine) causes—rare conjunctions of matter, occult qualities, celestial influences  
3. **Supernatural/Miraculous** — phenomena caused directly by God, suspending natural causation entirely

Wonders inhabited the preternatural zone. A two-headed calf was not a miracle (God didn't directly intervene to create it), but neither was it ordinary nature. It resulted from some unusual concatenation of natural causes—excess seed, maternal imagination, astral influence—that produced effects beyond nature's customary range.

This three-part ontology is crucial because it allowed natural philosophers to investigate wonders without either reducing them to the mundane or ceding them entirely to theology. The category largely disappeared in the eighteenth century, squeezed out as the conception of natural law shifted from "nature's customs" (which could have exceptions) to ironclad regularities that admitted no anomalies short of divine miracle.

## **What Gets Excluded**

Daston and Park deliberately restrict their study to *natural* wonders, setting aside miracles proper (direct divine interventions like the Resurrection or saints' miracles). They acknowledge this boundary was often blurred in practice—the vocabulary overlapped, and portentous monstrous births were read as divine signs even when their proximate causes were natural.

They also exclude purely fictional marvels—the wonders of romance literature that no one claimed were real (enchanted swords, fairy castles). Their wonders are things people actually believed existed and investigated, collected, or feared.

This is really the crux of the book's argument, and Daston and Park are quite insistent that the standard answer—that Enlightenment rationality or science "debunked" wonders—is wrong. Their explanation is more sociological and aesthetic than epistemological.

## **The Standard Story (Which They Reject)**

The familiar narrative, associated with Max Weber's "disenchantment of the world," goes something like this: medieval people believed in marvels because they were credulous and pre-scientific; the Scientific Revolution and Enlightenment introduced rational skepticism and natural laws; wonders were systematically tested, explained, or exposed as frauds; educated people stopped believing in them.

Daston and Park show this story doesn't hold up. Enlightenment savants didn't embark on any systematic program of empirical debunking. Many of the "strange facts" collected by seventeenth-century learned societies were simply ignored rather than refuted. Indeed, Emperor Frederick II in the thirteenth century was arguably *more* skeptical about specific marvels (he tested claims about barnacle geese) than many Royal Society fellows four centuries later. The philosophical project of *naturalizing* marvels—explaining them through natural causes—actually peaked in the early seventeenth century with figures like Bacon and Descartes, not during the Enlightenment.

## **What Actually Happened: A Triple Exclusion**

Daston and Park argue that wonders were excluded from respectable intellectual culture on three overlapping grounds:

### **1\. Metaphysical: The Order of Nature Became Uniform**

The seventeenth-century conception of natural law was compatible with marvels. God imposed laws on nature by fiat and could suspend them; even without miracles, rare concatenations of matter in motion might produce bizarre effects. Descartes explicitly claimed his mechanical philosophy could explain "the most wonderful effects."

But by the early eighteenth century, a further premise had been added: natural laws guaranteed that phenomena were **regular and uniform**. Nature became, in Fontenelle's phrase, "not capricious"—all her works were "equally serious." The shift was from regularity of *causes* to regularity of *effects*. If nature's laws produced uniform outcomes, then anomalies and singularities became not just unexplained but *implausible*. The preternatural category—that middle zone between the ordinary and the miraculous—simply collapsed.

This happened despite considerable experimental evidence to the contrary. Seventeenth-century experimenters constantly struggled with irreproducible results, capricious effects, and stubborn variability. But the metaphysical commitment to uniformity overrode empirical experience.

### **2\. Political: Wonders Were Dangerous**

Daston and Park repeatedly emphasize that wonders were condensations of **power**. Medieval princes collected marvels as symbols of their dominion over nature and distant lands. Monstrous births and portents mobilized popular anxiety and could legitimate or delegitimate political authority. The Reformation and Counter-Reformation weaponized prodigies—both Catholics and Protestants produced propaganda broadsides reading monsters as signs of divine judgment on their opponents.

For intellectuals writing after the Wars of Religion and the English Civil War, this was alarming. Wonders were associated with:

* **Enthusiasm**: the claim that God communicated directly through signs, visions, and wonders, bypassing established ecclesiastical and political authority  
* **Superstition**: the credulity of the ignorant masses, easily manipulated by demagogues and false prophets  
* **Popular disorder**: riots, millenarian movements, witch panics

Protestant and Catholic clergy joined natural philosophers in dampening the culture of portents. Demons quietly exited respectable theology at almost exactly the same time that the preternatural disappeared from respectable natural philosophy. Both were intermediate categories—between natural and supernatural, human and divine—that allowed unauthorized people to claim access to extraordinary power.

The strange facts collected by learned societies in the late seventeenth century were, in this sense, *safe* wonders—stripped of portentous meaning, domesticated into objects of polite curiosity rather than vehicles of divine wrath or political prophecy.

### **3\. Aesthetic: Wonders Became Distasteful**

This is perhaps the most distinctive part of Daston and Park's argument. They show that an **anti-marvelous aesthetic** emerged in parallel in natural philosophy and in art criticism, organized around the concept of *vraisemblance* (verisimilitude) and *decorum*.

In drama and literature, critics increasingly insisted that representations should be *probable*—conforming to audience expectations about what was possible and proper. The marvelous, the monstrous, and the fantastic violated proportion and decorum, risking "the debauchery of genius." Poets were warned against "events prodigious to excess" that "disgust readers whose judgment is formed."

This aesthetic sensibility migrated into natural philosophy. Nature herself was expected to be decorous—simple, parsimonious, serious, uniform. Fontenelle praised nature for achieving variety with "minimal means," like a bourgeois householder. When the naturalist de la Faille wrote to the Paris Académie in 1769 about the old belief that barnacles hatched geese, he was less concerned with specific evidence than with expressing **indignation** at such a "monstrous generation" that was "contrary to the natural order." The response was aesthetic and moral as much as epistemic.

Buffon gave monsters only three pages in his massive *Histoire naturelle*, explaining that they did not belong to "the ordinary facts of nature." They had become embarrassments.

## **The Social Logic: Wonders Became *Low***

All three exclusions converged on a single social judgment: **wonders became vulgar**. This was a profound reversal. From the twelfth through the seventeenth century, wonders had been partly constitutive of elite identity:

* Medieval abbots and princes displayed marvels as signs of wealth and power over nature  
* Physicians and natural philosophers possessed esoteric knowledge of preternatural causes  
* Virtuosi and collectors exercised refined taste and connoisseurship in assembling Wunderkammern

The vulgar were excluded from wonders—they couldn't afford them, didn't understand them, and couldn't distinguish true marvels from false.

By the mid-eighteenth century, this had completely inverted. Now the **love of the marvelous** was itself the mark of the vulgar—the credulous, the uneducated, the enthusiastic, the superstitious. Réaumur, reporting on regenerating crustacean limbs in 1712, insisted it was "ordinarily prudent to be on guard against the marvelous, which the vulgar always willingly credits." The Paris Académie dismissed reports of meteorite falls as smacking of popular credulity and "the love of the marvelous."

The enlightened defined themselves *against* wonder. As Daston and Park put it: "The order of nature was the anti-marvelous; the enlightened were the anti-vulgar; and, by a kind of contrapositive analogy, marvels were vulgar."

## **What This Means for the "Disenchantment" Narrative**

Daston and Park don't deny that something important changed—wonders really did disappear from elite natural philosophy and elite culture more broadly. But they insist the mechanism was social distinction and aesthetic taste, not the march of reason:

"It was neither rationality nor science nor even secularization that buried the wondrous for European elites.... Leading Enlightenment intellectuals did not so much debunk marvels as ignore them."

The "wistful Counter-Enlightenment"—Schiller, Weber, and countless subsequent writers mourning the disenchantment of the world—accepts the Enlightenment's own self-description, just with reversed valuation. Daston and Park suggest that both the Enlightenment and its critics have misunderstood what happened. Wonders didn't fail some test of rationality; they were expelled from polite company.

This argument has significant implications for how we understand the history of science. It suggests that the boundaries of legitimate scientific inquiry are shaped as much by social identity and aesthetic sensibility as by epistemology or method—a point that resonates with work by Shapin and Schaffer on credibility, though Daston and Park put more emphasis on emotion and taste than on political philosophy.

---

### **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Wonders as objects marked the outermost limits of the natural. Wonder as a passion registered the line between the known and the unknown." (p. 13\)

"To study how naturalists over some six centuries have used wonders to chart nature's farthest reaches reveals how variously they construed its heartland." (p. 14\)

"A history of wonders as objects of natural inquiry is therefore also a history of the orders of nature. A history of wonder as a passion of natural inquiry is also a history of the evolving collective sensibility of naturalists." (p. 14\)

"When marvels themselves became vulgar, an epoch had closed." (p. 19\)

"It was neither rationality nor science nor even secularization that buried the wondrous for European elites.... Leading Enlightenment intellectuals did not so much debunk marvels as ignore them. On metaphysical, aesthetic, and political grounds, they excluded wonders from the realm of the possible, the seemly, and the safe." (p. 361\)

"From the High Middle Ages to the Enlightenment, wonders had defined the order of nature by marking its limits; wonder had been the peculiarly cognitive passion that registered the breach of boundaries." (p. 363\)

---

### **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with:**

* Steven Shapin and Simon Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (both examine how credibility and social order shaped seventeenth-century natural philosophy, though Daston and Park emphasize affect and ontology rather than political philosophy)  
* Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison's *Objectivity* (extends the history of epistemic virtues and scientific sensibility into the nineteenth century)  
* Mary Poovey's *A History of the Modern Fact* (both historicize categories of knowledge, though Poovey focuses on quantification)  
* Paula Findlen's work on natural history collecting  
* Pamela Smith's *The Body of the Artisan* (both emphasize artisanal knowledge and the art-nature relationship in early modern science)

**Generals exam questions this book could help answer:**

* How did conceptions of natural order change between the medieval and early modern periods?  
* What role did emotion and sensibility play in the history of science?  
* How did the Scientific Revolution relate to changing epistemological categories?  
* What explains the decline of "preternatural philosophy" in the eighteenth century?  
* How did collections and material culture shape natural knowledge?  
* What was the relationship between elite and popular natural knowledge?

**Limitations:** The book's emphasis on elite intellectual culture means less attention to how ordinary people experienced wonders; the claim that wonders were primarily elite phenomena could be contested. The non-linear, thematic organization sometimes makes it difficult to follow specific arguments chronologically. The focus on western Europe limits attention to how colonial encounters shaped wonder—though recent scholarship (Stephen Greenblatt, Jorge Cañizares-Esguerra) has explored this territory.

# Week 2 — Subjectivities

# **Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison, *Objectivity* (Zone Books, 2007\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Scientific objectivity is not a timeless epistemic ideal coextensive with science itself but a historically specific "epistemic virtue" that emerged in the mid-nineteenth century. Objectivity names a particular way of knowing—"blind sight," knowledge unmarked by the knower's judgment, interpretation, or aesthetic preferences—achieved through mechanical procedures and fierce self-discipline. Crucially, each epistemic virtue **(truth-to-nature, mechanical objectivity, trained judgment)** corresponds to a particular kind of scientific self: the sage who synthesizes experience into ideal types, the indefatigable worker who subdues the will, and the intuitive expert who deploys unconscious pattern recognition. **Epistemology and ethos are inseparable; how one knows is bound up with what kind of person one becomes.**

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Daston and Galison reject the Husserlian-Koyrean narrative that locates scientific modernity's **decisive break in the seventeenth-century mathematization of nature**. They demonstrate instead that "objectivity" acquired its modern meaning only in the 1820s–1850s—prior to which the word meant nearly the opposite **(things as presented to consciousness rather than things as they exist independently).** The book thus challenges the widespread assumption that objectivity is simply what science has always pursued. Rather than a single epistemic virtue governing all of science, they reveal a succession of partially overlapping ideals: truth-to-nature (dominant from the Enlightenment through early nineteenth century), mechanical objectivity (emerging 1840s–1870s as a reaction against subjective intervention), structural objectivity **(a flight from images into mathematical structures among logicians and physicists, circa 1880s–1930s)**, and trained judgment **(early twentieth century onward)**. Each virtue arose to counter a specific perceived threat to knowledge: truth-to-nature combated the chaos of unprocessed sensation; mechanical objectivity targeted the scientist's willful projections; structural objectivity sought escape from the unreliability of all images; trained judgment responded to the impossibility of representing natural kinds through uninterpreted particulars. The authors engage implicitly with Shapin and Schaffer's work on the social construction of facts and explicitly with philosophical accounts from Kant through Carnap, Nagel, and Nozick that have treated objectivity as an ahistorical philosophical problem rather than a historical achievement.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

The book's central conceptual apparatus revolves around **epistemic virtues**: normative ideals for the acquisition of knowledge that are historically specific, culturally embedded, and accumulatively layered rather than sequentially replacing one another. Key concepts include:

* **Working objects**: The standardized images, diagrams, and type specimens that constitute the shared referents of scientific communities—not depictions of any single individual but normative representations that train practitioners to see.  
* **Scientific self/persona**: The characteristic habits, dispositions, and ethical commitments cultivated by practitioners of each epistemic regime. The sage exercises synthetic judgment; the worker disciplines the will; the expert trusts trained intuition.  
* **Technologies of the self** (drawing on Foucault): The concrete practices—journaling, drawing, instrument use, attention training—through which scientific selves are forged.  
* **Collective empiricism**: The distributed, trans-local character of scientific communities that necessitates standardized representations and shared epistemic norms.

The authors also introduce a crucial distinction between **aperspectival objectivity** (the view from nowhere, escape from all particular perspectives) and **mechanical objectivity** (the suppression of human intervention through automated procedures), while identifying **structural objectivity** as the attempt to preserve only formal relations stripped of all sensory content.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

The primary evidentiary base consists of **scientific atlases—systematic compilations of images that define and standardize the objects of inquiry for entire disciplines—spanning anatomy, botany, astronomy, crystallography, microscopy, radiology, and meteorology from roughly 1700 to the present.** The authors examine these atlases not merely as illustrations but as sites where epistemological commitments become materially instantiated. Additional sources include scientific autobiography and biography, advice manuals for aspiring scientists, philosophical treatises on scientific method, and the visual culture of science (photographs, drawings, diagrams). Geographic scope centers on Western Europe and North America; temporal scope runs from Enlightenment natural history through twentieth-century physics and contemporary nanotechnology.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

### **Worthington's Splash (1875–1894)**

Arthur Worthington's investigation of liquid drops striking surfaces provides the book's most vivid illustration of the transition from truth-to-nature to mechanical objectivity. Working in the 1870s–1880s, Worthington used an ingenious method: he illuminated splashes with millisecond electrical sparks and sketched the latent retinal images before they faded. His drawings depicted perfectly symmetrical "Auto-Splashes"—idealized forms that, he believed, captured the essential truth behind individual instances. In 1894, Worthington obtained high-speed photographs of the same phenomena and was startled to discover "shattered symmetry": the photographs revealed asymmetries "all the way down," irregularities that his earlier method had systematically smoothed away. Worthington's honest confession—"I have to confess that in looking over my original drawings I find records of many irregular or unsymmetrical figures, yet in compiling the history it has been inevitable that these should be rejected"—perfectly captures the epistemological shift. **Under truth-to-nature, such rejection was proper scientific practice; under mechanical objectivity, it became a form of falsification.**

### **The Haeckel-His Controversy (1870s)**

The bitter dispute between embryologists Ernst Haeckel and Wilhelm His dramatizes the collision between truth-to-nature and mechanical objectivity at the moment of transition. His accused Haeckel of smuggling theoretical prejudices into his embryological illustrations supporting the thesis that ontogeny recapitulates phylogeny. Haeckel defended his drawings as depicting "only the essentials of an object, leaving out inessentials"—a perfectly respectable position under truth-to-nature but increasingly suspect by the 1870s. His countered that drawings always contained "subjective elements" whereas photographs guaranteed "absolute fidelity." The controversy reveals how what counts as scientific integrity is historically variable: **Haeckel's practices, which would have been unremarkable a generation earlier, now seemed tantamount to fraud. Yet Haeckel understood the stakes perfectly, warning that if only "what has been objectively established" could be taught, "no idea, no thought, no theory, indeed no real 'science'" would survive.**

### **Hellmann/Neuhauss Snowflakes (1892)**

For centuries, naturalists depicted snowflakes as perfectly symmetrical hexagonal crystals—paradigmatic examples of nature's underlying geometric order. When Gustav Hellmann and Richard Neuhauss published photomicrographs of actual snowflakes in 1892, they deliberately preserved the asymmetries and imperfections that earlier observers had eliminated. Neuhauss went further, attacking the famous photographs of Wilson Bentley as fraudulent because Bentley had scraped, trimmed, and altered backgrounds to achieve aesthetic perfection. The controversy crystallizes competing regimes of objectivity: Bentley's "beautiful" snowflakes represented **truth-to-nature**'s commitment to revealing ideal forms; Hellmann and Neuhauss's "ugly" snowflakes embodied mechanical objectivity's prohibition against human improvement.

### **Golgi versus Cajal and the Neuron Doctrine (1906 Nobel)**

The shared 1906 Nobel Prize awarded to Camillo Golgi and Santiago Ramón y Cajal for their work on the nervous system masked a fundamental disagreement framed explicitly in terms of objectivity. Both scientists used Golgi's silver chromate staining method, yet reached opposite conclusions: Golgi saw neurons forming an interconnected "diffuse nervous net," while Cajal saw discrete cells with gaps (synapses) between them. Golgi openly simplified and schematized his drawings to reveal underlying patterns; Cajal accused him of producing "artificially distorted and falsified" images. **Cajal's rhetoric deployed the language of mechanical objectivity against Golgi's truth-to-nature approach.** The case demonstrates how **the same technique could yield radically different images depending on which epistemic virtue guided representation**—and how fraud accusations often masked conflicts between epistemic regimes rather than simple dishonesty.

### **Structural Objectivity: Helmholtz to Einstein**

Chapter Five traces an alternative response to the instability of mechanical objectivity: the flight from images altogether into mathematical structures. Helmholtz's 1869 argument that sensations are merely "signs" preserving only temporal sequences, Poincaré's conventionalism about geometry, Frege's anti-psychologism, Carnap's logical positivism, and Russell's structural realism all sought objectivity not in faithful images but in invariant formal relations. Einstein's theories of relativity provided the paradigmatic example: observers disagree about space and time separately, but agree on the space-time interval. The philosophical legacy—visible in Thomas Nagel's "view from nowhere" and Robert Nozick's equation of objectivity with invariance—treats structure as that which survives the stripping away of all subjective perspectives. **Yet Daston and Galison show this was a specifically historical response to perceived failures of image-based objectivity, not a timeless philosophical truth.**

### **The Gibbses' EEG Atlas and Trained Judgment (1941)**

Frederic and Erna Gibbs's *Atlas of Electroencephalography* (1941) exemplifies the twentieth-century emergence of trained judgment as an epistemic virtue. Faced with the impossibility of mechanical objectivity—no uninterpreted EEG trace could distinguish normal from pathological—the Gibbses embraced interpretation. **They promised readers that with twelve weeks of training, anyone could achieve 98% accuracy in reading electroencephalograms.** The scientific self here is neither the synthesizing sage nor the self-suppressing worker but the trained expert whose unconscious pattern recognition, schooled through repeated exposure to exemplars, can detect family resemblances invisible to explicit rules. The Gibbses explicitly invoked the analogy of distinguishing "Eskimos from Indians"—physiognomic judgment that cannot be reduced to mechanical procedures yet can be reliably taught.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"To be objective is to aspire to knowledge that bears no trace of the knower—knowledge unmarked by prejudice or skill, fantasy or judgment, wishing or striving. Objectivity is blind sight, seeing without inference, interpretation, or intelligence." (p. 17\)

"Mechanical objectivity was needed to protect images against subjective projections, but it threatened to undermine the primary aim of all scientific atlases, to provide the working objects of a discipline." (p. 19\)

"Objectivity and subjectivity are as inseparable as concave and convex; one defines the other. The emergence of scientific objectivity in the mid-nineteenth century necessarily goes hand in glove with the emergence of scientific subjectivity." (p. 197\)

"The will's work is in most cases practically ended when the bare presence to our thought of the naturally unwelcome object has been secured." —William James, quoted to illustrate the nineteenth-century reconceptualization of attention as willful effort rather than appetite (p. 238\)

Worthington on his splashes: "I have to confess that in looking over my original drawings I find records of many irregular or unsymmetrical figures, yet in compiling the history it has been inevitable that these should be rejected, if only because identical irregularities never recur. **Thus the mind of the observer is filled with an ideal splash—an Auto-Splash—whose perfection may never be actually realized."** (p. 11\)

"The same cultivated patterns of attention that single out certain objects in a certain way... also pattern a self. Perceptions, judgments, and, above all, values are calibrated and cemented by the incessant repetition of minute acts of seeing and paying heed." (p. 367\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs productively with:**

* Shapin and Schaffer, *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*: Both analyze the social and material conditions for producing credible scientific knowledge, though Daston/Galison focus on visual representation while Shapin/Schaffer emphasize witnessing and replication.  
* Theodore Porter, *Trust in Numbers*: Porter's account of quantification as a "technology of distance" that minimizes subjective judgment complements Daston/Galison's analysis of mechanical objectivity as self-suppression.  
* Steven Shapin, *A Social History of Truth*: The gentlemanly persona as guarantor of credibility provides a counterpoint to the scientific selves analyzed here.  
* Ken Alder, *The Measure of All Things*: The politics of standardization and precision measurement in revolutionary France connects to the emergence of objectivity as an epistemic virtue.

**Limitations:**

* The focus on atlases, while methodologically generative, may overstate the centrality of images to scientific epistemology in fields less dependent on visual representation.  
* The tripartite schema (truth-to-nature/mechanical objectivity/trained judgment) sometimes strains to accommodate hybrid or transitional cases.  
* Structural objectivity (Chapter 5\) feels somewhat disconnected from the atlas-based methodology of other chapters.  
* The book's scope is overwhelmingly Western European and North American; how these epistemic virtues traveled globally remains unexplored.

## **Truth-to-Nature**

**Core commitment**: The goal is to depict the *type*, *ideal*, or *characteristic form* that lies behind variable individual instances. Nature is understood to work according to underlying patterns, but any given specimen is a flawed, accidental instantiation of that pattern. The scientist's task is to see *through* particulars to the universal.

**Required practice**: The scientist must *actively intervene*—selecting the best specimens, synthesizing observations across many individuals, correcting accidental deformities, improving imperfect examples. This is not falsification but proper scientific method. **The raw data of sensation are chaos; only reason and judgment can extract truth.**

**Perceived threat**: The danger is being overwhelmed by the "blooming, buzzing confusion" of unprocessed sensation—mistaking an anomaly for the type, confusing accidental variation with essential form, drowning in detail without grasping the underlying pattern.

**Scientific self**: The ***sage***—someone with vast experience, prodigious memory, and refined judgment who has seen thousands of specimens and can therefore recognize the ideal that no single specimen perfectly embodies. Genius lies in synthesis and insight.

**Case study illustrations**:

* **Albinus's skeleton (1747)**: Bernhard Siegfried Albinus employed elaborate grids and measurements to ensure accuracy, but he openly stated that "those things which were less perfect, were mended in the figure." He selected the most perfect skeleton he could find, **posed it carefully, and then *improved* it further in the illustration. This wasn't deception—it was revealing nature's intention more clearly than nature herself managed in any single instance.**  
* **Worthington's drawings (1875–1894)**: Before photography, Worthington sketched splashes from retinal afterimages. **His drawings showed perfect radial symmetry—the "Auto-Splash" that he believed represented the essential form. He explicitly acknowledged rejecting asymmetrical observations: "identical irregularities never recur,"** so they must be accidental. Under truth-to-nature, this rejection was correct procedure.  
* **Haeckel's embryos**: Haeckel defended his embryological illustrations as showing "only the essentials of an object, leaving out inessentials." He was working within the truth-to-nature framework, where the scientist's job is precisely to distinguish essential from accidental and depict only the former.

---

## **Mechanical Objectivity**

**Core commitment**: The goal is to depict the *individual specimen* with all its particularities, warts and all, without human intervention. Nature should be allowed to "speak for itself" or even "draw itself" through mechanical procedures (photography, automatic recording instruments). Any individual, however imperfect, is more truthful than an idealized composite.

**Required practice**: The scientist must be *passive*—suppressing judgment, restraining the impulse to correct or improve, letting instruments record what is actually there rather than what should be there. **The scientist's role is to set up the apparatus and then get out of the way.**

**Perceived threat**: The danger is the scientist's own *subjectivity*—projections, pet theories, aesthetic preferences, wishful thinking. The post-Kantian self, organized around an active will, is understood as constantly threatening to impose its preconceptions on the data. The enemy is within.

**Scientific self**: The *worker* who disciplines the will against itself. This requires a strange psychological operation: the will must be strong enough to suppress the will. The heroism of mechanical objectivity lies in self-denial—refusing to intervene even when intervention seems scientifically justified.

**Case study illustrations**:

* **Worthington's photographs (1894)**: When Worthington obtained high-speed photographs of splashes, he discovered "shattered symmetry"—asymmetries all the way down that his earlier method had systematically eliminated. He treated this as a revelation: the photographs showed what was *really* there, freed from his unconscious idealization. His confession of earlier "rejection" of irregular figures now read as an admission of error.  
* **Hellmann/Neuhauss snowflakes (1892)**: Their photomicrographs deliberately preserved the asymmetries and imperfections that earlier naturalists had eliminated. They attacked Wilson Bentley's beautiful snowflake photographs as *fraudulent* because Bentley scraped, trimmed, and retouched them. Under mechanical objectivity, aesthetic improvement became scientific sin.  
* **Wilhelm His's criticism of Haeckel**: His accused Haeckel of "smuggling his theoretical prejudices into the illustrations." What Haeckel called "showing the essentials," His called distortion. His developed elaborate mechanical drawing apparatus with built-in "controls" to minimize subjective intervention, equating any "amendments or idealizations" with "conscious bungling." Same practice, opposite moral valence.

---

## **The Key Insight**

What makes Daston and Galison's argument powerful is showing that **what counts as proper scientific practice under one regime becomes fraud under the other**:

| Practice | Truth-to-Nature | Mechanical Objectivity |
| ----- | ----- | ----- |
| Correcting imperfections | Revealing nature's intention | Falsification |
| Depicting idealized type | Scientific insight | Subjective projection |
| Recording every irregularity | Drowning in noise | Scientific integrity |
| Smoothing away asymmetries | Extracting the essential | Imposing preconceptions |

Haeckel and His weren't disagreeing about facts—they were operating under different epistemic regimes with incompatible criteria for what counts as a faithful representation. Haeckel, working within truth-to-nature, thought His's demand for raw photographs would "drive all ideas out of science, leaving only facts." His, committed to mechanical objectivity, thought Haeckel's idealizations were tantamount to lying.

The transition wasn't driven by new technology (the camera obscura existed long before mechanical objectivity) or by scientific progress (truth-to-nature remained productive in many fields). It was driven by a reconceptualization of the *scientific self*—from a sage whose judgment reveals truth to a worker whose judgment threatens it.

This article is a fascinating companion piece to *Objectivity*—it extends, complicates, and in some ways troubles the book's framework. Let me trace the connections and tensions.

## **Direct Continuities**

**The atlas as epistemic site**: Both works treat scientific atlases as the place where epistemological commitments become visible. The *International Cloud Atlas* (1896–1987) is exactly the kind of source *Objectivity* analyzes—a standardized compilation of images meant to train observers worldwide to see in unison.

**Working objects**: The article explicitly addresses how cloud "types" function as what the book calls "working objects"—not depictions of any single cloud but normative representations that constitute the shared referents of a scientific community.

**The language of truth-to-nature**: Daston uses terminology straight from *Objectivity*: observers must learn to see the "type" behind variable particulars, to extract "truth to nature" (she even quotes Ruskin's phrase "truth of species"). Howard's cloud classification aimed to reveal characteristic forms underlying apparent chaos—classic truth-to-nature.

---

## **Complications and Tensions**

### **1\. The regimes don't sequence neatly**

*Objectivity* presents truth-to-nature and mechanical objectivity as partially sequential—mechanical objectivity emerging mid-nineteenth century as a reaction against the earlier regime. But the 1896 *International Cloud Atlas* combines them in ways that resist this periodization:
