He engages explicitly with sociolinguistic work by Ulrich Ammon on linguistic inequality, Scott Montgomery's *Science in Translation*, and the Whorfian hypothesis regarding linguistic relativity, positioning his historical approach as a necessary corrective to sociological accounts that lack temporal depth.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Gordin organizes his analysis around the conceptual dyad of **identity** (speaker-centric expression in one's native language) versus **communication** (audience-centric use of a vehicular language). He mobilizes this framework to demonstrate that scientific language choice is historically contingent rather than determined by any linguistic or scientific necessity. The book implicitly engages with science studies approaches that treat translation as constitutive rather than transparent, drawing on work in the history of scientific communication while avoiding strong commitments to either actor-network theory or social constructionism.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Gordin draws on an impressive multilingual archive spanning English, French, German, Russian, Latin, Esperanto, and Ido—a methodological choice he explicitly frames as both enabling and limiting his account. Sources include scientific journals (especially chemistry periodicals like *Zeitschrift für Chemie* and *Journal of the Russian Physico-Chemical Society*), correspondence among scientists (Ostwald, van't Hoff, Zamenhof), government reports on translation and language policy, records of international scientific congresses, archival materials from Georgetown University's machine translation project, CIA and NSF funding documents, and trade publications. The geographic scope centers on Western Europe and North America from roughly 1850 to the present, with necessary excursions into Latin's earlier history.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

### **The Chemical Revolution and Lavoisier's Linguistic Insularity (Chapter 1/Introduction)**

Gordin opens with the Chemical Revolution of the 1780s to illustrate how even transformative scientific developments were mediated through linguistic friction. Antoine Lavoisier, architect of the new oxygen theory, understood no English despite French's status as the "universal" language of scholarship. He learned of Joseph Priestley's experiments on "dephlogisticated air" (oxygen) only through intermediaries: Jean Hyacinthe de Magellan, an "intelligencer" who summarized English publications in French, and Lavoisier's wife Marie-Anne Paulze, who learned English specifically to translate scientific texts. When Richard Kirwan published his *Essay on Phlogiston* (1787) defending the older theory, Madame Lavoisier translated it into French with extensive refutatory footnotes by her husband and his colleagues. The example demonstrates that even at moments of supposed French linguistic hegemony, scientific communication depended on extensive human labor to bridge linguistic divides—and that speakers of "dominant" languages could afford a dangerous insularity.

### **Mendeleev, Meyer, and the Periodic Table Priority Dispute (Chapter 2\)**

The 1869 discovery of the periodic system of elements by Dmitrii Mendeleev (in Russian) and Lothar Meyer (in German) crystallizes Gordin's argument about how translation friction shapes scientific communication. Mendeleev's initial publication appeared in a Russian-language journal with extremely limited circulation, and the German abstract that appeared in the *Zeitschrift für Chemie* (the standard clearinghouse for chemical abstracts) fatally mistranslated his work. The abstract rendered Mendeleev's key term for atomic weight ambiguously, obscuring the theoretical basis of his periodic law. When Meyer published his independent version, a priority dispute erupted that turned substantially on the question of what Mendeleev had actually claimed. Gordin shows that the rise of the German abstract system—designed to solve the problem of too many languages—actually created new translation errors that had scientific consequences. The case illustrates that "the friction of translation between Russian and German powered the priority dispute over the periodic system."

### **The Esperanto-Ido Schism and Wilhelm Ostwald's Crusade (Chapters 4-5)**

Gordin devotes two chapters to the rise and fragmentation of constructed international auxiliary languages, focusing on Nobel laureate chemist Wilhelm Ostwald's advocacy for Ido (a reformed version of Esperanto). Zamenhof's Esperanto had attracted significant scientific support by 1900, with journals, congresses, and a growing community. But the 1907 Delegation for the Adoption of an International Auxiliary Language, chaired by Ostwald, recommended reforms that Zamenhof rejected, leading to a bitter schism. Ostwald devoted his Nobel Prize money (roughly $1 million in 2014 dollars) to Ido, attempted to require Ido abstracts in his journal *Zeitschrift für physikalische Chemie*, and developed an elaborate Ido chemical nomenclature. His coeditor Jacobus van't Hoff threatened to resign over the abstract proposal, and the two friends remained estranged until van't Hoff's death in 1911\. The schism demonstrated that the very features reformers valued—flexibility, perfectibility, "scientific" design—undermined the stability that made a language useful. Louis Couturat's death in an automobile collision with a military mobilization truck on August 3, 1914, as World War I began, symbolically ended the dream of a scientific auxiliary language. As Gordin notes, the war "eviscerated the quest for a scientific auxiliary."

### **The Georgetown-IBM Machine Translation Experiment (Chapters 8-9)**

On January 7, 1954, Léon Dostert of Georgetown University and IBM staged a public demonstration of machine translation from Russian to English. Sixty carefully selected Russian sentences—drawn from chemistry, politics, and general interest—were fed into an IBM 701 computer and rendered into English using a 250-word vocabulary and six grammatical rules. Headlines proclaimed the dawn of automatic translation. Gordin reveals the demonstration was essentially a fraud: the sentences were rigged for success, the vocabulary was limited to words the system could handle, and the "translation" was closer to word-by-word substitution than genuine linguistic processing. Nevertheless, the publicity secured massive funding—including covert CIA money funneled through the NSF (over $9.7 million in 2014 dollars went to Georgetown alone). Machine translation became a Cold War arms race, with American and Soviet programs playing each other off "in a kind of amiable conspiracy to extract money from their respective governments." The 1966 ALPAC report devastated the field by showing that human translation remained cheaper and better. Gordin uses this case to demonstrate how the "language barrier" became a national security problem amenable to technological solutions—solutions that consistently failed to deliver.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The collapse into monolingualism is, historically speaking, a very strange outcome, since most of humanity for most of its existence has been to a greater or lesser degree multilingual." (p. 2\)

"The story of the twentieth century, which from the point of view of the history of globalization is ever-rising English, from the perspective of scientific languages might be better reformulated as the decline of German." (p. 7\)

"Knowledge and power are bedfellows; they are not twins." (p. 8\)

"Every history has those flaws, but I want to expose the reader to the friction caused by languages one knows imperfectly, the alienating quality of other people's words, to make the active translation visible. The past did not happen exclusively in English, though many histories make it seem as though it did." (p. 2\)

"English has attained its current position owing to a series of historical transformations that it also in turn shaped, exploiting a perception of neutrality that it gained through being distinctly non-neutral in either its British or American guise." (p. 315\)

"The 'English' that is used in scientific communication—particularly in written form, but also quite often in oral interchange—is simplified, reduced, stereotyped to highlight communication and minimize stylistic nuance." (p. 314\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Pairs well with:**

* Steven Shapin and Simon Schaffer, *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (on the social construction of scientific communication and credibility)  
* Bruno Latour, *Science in Action* (on translation, inscription, and the circulation of scientific claims)  
* Mary Terrall, *The Man Who Flattened the Earth* (on eighteenth-century scientific correspondence networks and linguistic mediation)  
* Fa-ti Fan's work on science in East Asia (for comparative perspectives Gordin acknowledges neglecting)  
* David Edgerton, *The Shock of the Old* (for skepticism about technological solutions to social problems, paralleling Gordin's treatment of machine translation)

**Limitations:**

* Gordin explicitly acknowledges his limited coverage of East Asia (Japanese scientific publishing, Chinese scientific modernization) due to his own linguistic capacities—a significant gap given these regions' importance in twentieth-century science.  
* The book's focus on elite, professional natural scientists excludes medicine, applied sciences, and the humanities/social sciences, where different linguistic dynamics may have operated.  
* The analytical framework of identity vs. communication, while illuminating, sometimes flattens the political and institutional dimensions of language choice into individual-level decisions.

# **Daniel J. Kevles, *The Physicists: The History of a Scientific Community in Modern America* (1978)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Kevles argues that from the post-Civil War period through the 1970s, American physicists transformed from a small, marginalized group of practitioners into a powerful professional elite whose relationship with the state fundamentally reshaped both science and democracy. This transformation was driven by **physicists' persistent pursuit of autonomy, resources, and recognition,** achieved through strategic appeals to utility, national defense, and cultural prestige—culminating in their "establishment" after World War II but generating enduring tensions between **scientific elitism and democratic accountability**.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Kevles intervenes against a triumphalist narrative of American science as the inevitable product of democratic values and practical genius. Writing in the wake of **Vietnam-era critiques of the military-industrial complex**, he demonstrates that the rise of American physics was neither natural nor purely meritocratic but rather the result of **sustained political maneuvering, ideological work, and institutional entrepreneurship** by scientists themselves.

The book challenges the internalist history of science dominant in earlier decades by treating physicists "as human beings"—shaped by social circumstances, professional ambitions, and political contexts—rather than as disembodied minds pursuing truth. Kevles explicitly engages with the tradition of Henry Rowland's "best-science elitism" (the idea that resources should flow only to the most capable researchers) and traces how this ideology competed with democratic demands for geographical distribution and social accountability. He is in dialogue with A. Hunter Dupree's *Science in the Federal Government* (1957) but extends the analysis to encompass professional community formation, cultural authority, and Cold War politics. The book also anticipates later STS scholarship on the social construction of expertise by showing how **physicists actively constructed their authority** and negotiated their position within American democracy.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Kevles employs a framework combining **professionalization theory**, **political history of expertise**, and **sociology of scientific communities**. Central concepts include "best-science elitism" (allocating resources to the most capable regardless of geography or social purpose) versus "political elitism" (scientists' desire for autonomy from democratic oversight while receiving public funds). He analyzes physics as a professional community with its own internal hierarchies, reward systems, and strategies for legitimation—treating the discipline as both an intellectual enterprise and a social institution embedded in broader structures of power.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Kevles draws on an extraordinary range of manuscript collections from presidential libraries, the National Archives, university archives, and personal papers of major figures (Bush, Conant, Hale, Millikan, Oppenheimer, and dozens of others). He combines this archival research with published scientific papers, congressional hearings, contemporary journalism, and oral histories. The scope spans from the 1860s through the mid-1970s, focusing primarily on American physics but with attention to transatlantic scientific exchanges. The book integrates intellectual, institutional, political, and social history.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

### **The Allison Commission and the Politics of Federal Science (1884-1886)**

In the mid-1880s, a joint congressional commission investigated federal scientific bureaus amid charges of corruption and overspending at agencies like the Coast Survey and Geological Survey. Kevles uses this episode to illuminate the fundamental tension between **scientific autonomy and democratic accountability** that would persist for a century. Scientists like John Wesley Powell argued that research should be judged only by scientific men, not political appointees—revealing what Kevles terms "political elitism," the assumption that scientists "deserved to exercise power with only limited accountability." Secretary of the Navy William Chandler countered that **no agency of a democratic government could "set itself above examination."** The commission's resolution—keeping scientific bureaus under political departments while affirming their general scope—established a template for federal science that persisted until World War II. The episode demonstrates how early American scientists sought insulation from "philistines in and out of the government" while operating within a political system inherently suspicious of unaccountable expertise.

### **George Ellery Hale and the Creation of the National Research Council (1916)**

The astronomer George Ellery Hale, foreign secretary of the National Academy of Sciences, orchestrated the creation of the National Research Council as a mechanism for mobilizing science for World War I preparedness. Frustrated that Josephus Daniels had created the Naval Consulting Board under Thomas Edison—**composed of practical inventors and engineers who dismissed physicists as mere talkers**—Hale maneuvered to position the Academy as the government's preferred scientific adviser. He **cultivated relationships with industrialists, government officials, and university presidents while promoting a vision of interdisciplinary, large-scale research projects.** The NRC brought **academic physicists into defense** work on submarine detection and other problems while establishing the principle that fundamental research—not just tinkering—was essential to military technology. Hale's entrepreneurship reveals how scientific leaders actively constructed their political relevance, **packaging pure research as serving national interests.** His success depended on exploiting the crisis of war to gain access to state resources while maintaining professional autonomy—a pattern that would repeat in World War II.

### **Millikan's Oil-Drop Experiment and the Social Meaning of American Physics (1909-1910)**

Robert Millikan's precise measurement of the electronic charge through his oil-drop experiment represents a turning point in American physics' international standing. Kevles uses this case to illustrate how the "new physics" of electrons, radioactivity, and quantum theory **offered American researchers opportunities to achieve world-class results even without Europe's theoretical traditions.** Millikan's work combined experimental ingenuity with the kind of precision measurement at which Americans excelled, and his success earned accolades from both sides of the Atlantic. More significantly, Millikan became a tireless promoter of physics' utility, combining Rowland's best-science elitism with appeals to practical benefits. He represented a new type of **physicist-entrepreneur who could speak to industrialists, politicians, and the public about why pure research deserved support**. The case demonstrates how individual scientific achievements were leveraged into broader claims for professional resources and prestige.

### **The Bush-Kilgore Debate and the Creation of the National Science Foundation (1944-1950)**

The protracted battle over postwar science policy between Vannevar Bush and Senator Harley Kilgore crystallized competing visions of the relationship between science and democracy. Kilgore, a New Deal liberal from West Virginia, proposed a foundation under presidential control that would support socially purposeful research, distribute funds geographically to develop science in "have-not" regions, and vest patents from federally funded research in the government. Bush, the OSRD director, countered with *Science—The Endless Frontier*, calling for **a foundation governed by part-time scientists insulated from political control**, focused on best-science allocation regardless of geography, and leaving patents with contractors. The debate exposed fundamental questions: Should public money for science be subject to democratic priorities? Could scientists claim disinterestedness while benefiting from government largess? Bush's vision largely prevailed—the 1950 NSF act created an agency governed substantially by scientific leaders—but the delay meant that military agencies (especially the Office of Naval Research) filled the gap, making defense the dominant patron of physics. Kevles shows this outcome was "produced not by design but by a combination of demands and defaults," including scientists' own **preference for military patronage that came with fewer strings attached.**

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"From these strategic positions Henry and his allies politicked, maneuvered, cajoled, and exhorted to strengthen science in America. Their chief goals: to exclude amateurs from the control of science, or, in the terms of a later generation, to professionalize research; to improve the condition of science in institutions of higher learning; and to enlarge its role in the federal government." (p. 4\)

"Distinct from the best-science elitism of Rowland, this political elitism was widespread among American scientists. It was not in any respect ideological. It bespoke neither a cavalier disregard for the processes of democratic government nor a bald-faced bid for power. **Rather, it revealed the scientific community's self-conception as an aristocracy of intellect and ideals.**" (p. 53\)

"Not only had they changed the world, but the world had changed them—their opportunities and institutions, responsibilities and attitudes, their power, status, and expectations." (p. ix)

"Almost overnight, physicists have been promoted from semi-obscurity to membership in that select group of rarities which include rubber, sugar and coffee." (p. 320, quoting J. Hammon McMillen, 1943\)

**"There is no democracy in physics. We can't say that some second-rate guy has as much right to \[an\] opinion as Fermi."** (p. 425, quoting Luis Alvarez)

"However far the nation's physicists had come in status, power, prestige, and professional accomplishment since Joseph Henry's day, as the republic moved ahead into its third century they remained a special establishment in American life, destined to function in uneasy tension with the democracy from which they derived vitality, sustenance, and purpose." (p. 426\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Pairs well with:**

* Steven Shapin's *The Scientific Revolution* (for contrasting approaches to science as social activity; Shapin's focus on trust and gentlemanly culture vs. Kevles's American professionalization narrative)  
* Margaret O'Mara's *Cities of Knowledge* (extends the story of science-state relations into Cold War university development and geographical politics of research funding)  
* Stuart Leslie's *The Cold War and American Science* (complements with deeper focus on MIT and Stanford during the period Kevles treats more broadly)  
* David Hollinger's work on scientific liberalism and the "culture of science" as a democratic ideology

**Limitations:**

* The book's focus on elite physicists at major institutions means it says relatively little about physics education, community colleges, or the everyday practice of science outside premier research universities  
* Published in 1978, it naturally cannot address developments in science policy, the rise of biotechnology, or shifts in federal R\&D priorities after the Carter administration; the "revolt against science" narrative may now seem dated given subsequent fluctuations in physics' public standing

## **Best-Science Elitism**

This concept originates with Henry Rowland, the Johns Hopkins physicist who delivered a famous 1883 address lamenting that America produced mostly "mediocre" science. Rowland's solution was a pyramid model: concentrate resources at the apex among the truly capable researchers, rather than distributing them broadly. The underlying logic is that scientific progress depends on exceptional individuals, and spreading resources thinly across many practitioners merely produces more mediocrity.

Kevles traces this ideology through figures like Millikan, Lawrence, and eventually Vannevar Bush. In the postwar debates, best-science elitism manifested as opposition to Senator Kilgore's proposal for geographical distribution of NSF funds. Bush and his allies argued that **money should follow talent wherever it resided—which meant, in practice, that it would flow to Harvard, MIT, Berkeley, Caltech, and a handful of other elite institutions**. When critics from "have-not" states like West Virginia complained that this perpetuated existing inequalities, **best-science advocates responded that diluting funds among lesser institutions would only retard scientific progress.**

The key passage on Rowland's vision:

"All you have done is raise the general level of the country, which is a good thing, but the general level is still so far behind the outposts of the scientific army that very little influence has been experienced."

Best-science elitism is fundamentally **internal** to science—it concerns how scientific resources should be allocated among scientists and institutions. It assumes that scientific merit is objectively identifiable and that the most capable researchers can be distinguished from the merely competent.

## **Political Elitism**

This concept describes scientists' relationship to the **external** political system. **Political elitism is the assumption that scientists constitute a special class whose work should be substantially insulated from democratic oversight,** even when funded by public money.

Kevles locates this disposition in the earliest federal scientists. During the Allison Commission hearings, John Wesley Powell argued that scientists "spurn authority" and would not tolerate having their research programs determined by "officers or functionaires." The key passage:

"Distinct from the best-science elitism of Rowland, this political elitism was widespread among American scientists. It was not in any respect ideological. It bespoke neither a cavalier disregard for the processes of democratic government nor a bald-faced bid for power. **Rather, it revealed the scientific community's self-conception as an aristocracy of intellect and ideals**."

Political elitism rests on several assumptions:

* Scientists possess specialized knowledge that laypeople cannot evaluate  
* The disinterested pursuit of truth makes scientists morally superior to ordinary political actors  
* Democratic processes are too slow, uninformed, or corrupt to direct research effectively  
* Scientific progress ultimately benefits everyone, so scientists' judgment about research priorities can be trusted

In practice, political elitism meant scientists wanted public funding without public control. They sought advisory relationships where they could shape policy while remaining unaccountable to voters. The National Academy of Sciences embodied this—a private, self-perpetuating body that served as the government's official scientific adviser.

## **How They Interact**

The concepts are analytically separate but historically intertwined. Best-science elitism concerns allocation **within** the scientific community; political elitism concerns the community's relationship to **external** authority. But they reinforced each other:

**During the NSF debates:** Bush combined both forms. He argued that funds should go to the best scientists (best-science elitism) AND that decisions about who qualified as "best" should be made by scientists themselves through peer review, insulated from presidential or congressional control (political elitism). Kilgore's alternative would have subjected both allocation decisions and research priorities to democratic processes.

**The Office of Naval Research example:** Scientists tolerated—even preferred—military patronage because ONR practiced a form of political elitism favorable to them. Navy program officers deferred to scientific judgment about what research to fund, imposed few restrictions, and asked few questions about social relevance. This was political elitism with a military patron rather than democratic oversight.

**The Cold War settlement:** Kevles argues that **physicists achieved a "victory for elitism"** on both fronts. The NSF was governed substantially by scientists (political elitism), and its funds were allocated largely by peer review favoring established institutions (best-science elitism). The result was "a system of federal support for basic research and training insulated from political control and focused on the advancement of the best possible physics."

## **The Democratic Critique**

Kevles doesn't simply endorse or condemn these elitisms—he presents them as generating genuine dilemmas. The democratic critique of each:

**Against best-science elitism:** If public funds flow only to elite institutions, taxpayers in most states subsidize research that happens elsewhere and trains students who leave for the coasts. Geographic concentration perpetuates inequality and may exclude capable researchers who lack access to elite networks.

**Against political elitism:** If scientists claim disinterestedness while lobbying for their own budgets and resisting oversight, they are merely another interest group cloaking self-interest in neutral language. **Democratic accountability is the price of public funding.**

But Kevles also takes seriously the scientific response: spreading funds thinly might genuinely retard progress, and subjecting research priorities to congressional horse-trading might distort inquiry toward short-term political payoffs.

# **Alice Lovejoy, *Tales of Militant Chemistry* (2025)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Lovejoy argues that photographic film is best understood not through its images but through its chemistry—as an industrial product whose raw materials (cotton, trees, silver, gelatin, petroleum) and manufacturing processes were inseparable from twentieth-century military violence, colonial extraction, and environmental devastation. By tracing the histories of Eastman Kodak's Tennessee Eastman subsidiary and Germany's Agfa Wolfen, she demonstrates that **the chemical knowledge and industrial capacity required to make "safe" (nonflammable cellulose acetate) film led directly to weapons manufacturing, including poison gas, high explosives (RDX), and the atomic bomb**, revealing film as both a product of and participant in the chemical industry's "militant chemistry."

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Lovejoy intervenes in multiple fields simultaneously. Against cinema and media history's traditional focus on film as text, spectacle, or apparatus, **she insists on film as material**—situating it **alongside textiles, plastics, and weapons** rather than television or radio. This aligns with recent "infrastructural" turns in media studies (Haidee Wasson's work on 16mm, Siobhan Angus and Nadia Bozak on extraction and media) while pushing toward a more explicitly chemical materialism. Against triumphalist narratives of technological progress, she shows how "safety film"—marketed as a humanitarian improvement over explosive cellulose nitrate—created the industrial foundation for weapons production. She engages with histories of the Manhattan Project (following scholars like Gabrielle Hecht on uranium colonialism and Kate Brown on nuclear secrecy) while **reframing the bomb's production through the film industry** rather than physics or military history. The book also contributes to environmental history and Anthropocene studies by arguing that **film factories functioned as unwitting "sentinels" of nuclear fallout, with black spots on film emulsion providing early evidence of atmospheric contamination that prefigured later health crises.** Against corporate histories that separate "yellow boxes" from chemical production, Lovejoy demonstrates that **companies like Kodak were always chemical companies**, whose diversification strategies (vertical integration to avoid antitrust rulings) entangled entertainment media with military-industrial production.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Lovejoy draws on material culture studies and infrastructural analysis, treating film's chemistry as constitutive rather than incidental to cinema's history. She mobilizes the concept of "militant chemistry" from Primo Levi's *The Periodic Table* to describe the dual-use nature of industrial chemistry during wartime. Her approach echoes **Frankfurt School critiques (Adorno and Horkheimer's "culture industry" thesis) while extending them to the literal industrial base of media production**. Key concepts include: diversification (how chemical companies' multiple product lines created pathways from film to weapons); vertical integration (corporate control of raw materials as a strategy of market dominance); and the sentinel function of film (its capacity to register environmental contamination through material sensitivity).

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Lovejoy employs multi-archival transnational history, drawing on materials from two dozen archives across the United States, Germany, Belgium, and elsewhere, including corporate records from Kodak and Agfa, personal papers, government documents (AEC, Manhattan Project), concentration camp records, and oral histories. The book's temporal scope extends from the late nineteenth century to the present, with primary focus on 1915–1965. Geographic scope is genuinely global, tracking materials from the Belgian Congo (uranium) to Tennessee, Germany to Ukraine, Nevada to the Marshall Islands.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Cleveland Clinic Fire (1929)**: On May 15, 1929, a gas leak ignited X-ray films stored in the Cleveland Clinic basement. The cellulose nitrate film created poisonous fumes comparable to World War I chemical weapons. **123 people died—the largest hospital accident in US history**. Doctors who had served in France recognized the symptoms: "They're all gassed. It's a film gas." The Chemical Warfare Service investigated, warning that "conditions of this sort could not be duplicated in wartime, except by accident" and that even Army gas masks were useless against burning celluloid. The disaster accelerated Kodak's turn to cellulose acetate "safety" film, which paradoxically created the industrial capacity for weapons production. This case study exemplifies how the danger of nitrate film—its kinship with explosives and poison gas—drove the very innovation that would lead Tennessee Eastman to the Manhattan Project.

**Forced Labor at Agfa Wolfen**: During World War II, Agfa Wolfen's film and rayon production depended extensively on forced laborers, including concentration camp prisoners. Oleksandra Lawrik, a Ukrainian accountant and Communist Party member, was arrested in Dnipropetrovsk in 1943, passed through Buchenwald and Ravensbrück, and was selected for factory work when Agfa managers inspected prisoners' hands and arms. She worked with caustic soda (sodium hydroxide) making Vistra rayon—the same cellulose-based fiber that Hans Dominik celebrated in his 1936 autarky propaganda book as Germany's "white gold." Prisoners were beaten, worked without shelter during air raids, and sent back to death camps when illness made them unfit for labor. The French art historian Agnès Humbert, also a political prisoner at an IG Farben fiber factory, recalled the work as "burns and blinding fumes" where the metal spinnerets were valued more than human lives. This case study demonstrates how the same **factories producing film for Nazi propaganda were simultaneously sites of enslaved labor, collapsing any distinction between cinema as culture and chemistry as violence.**

**The "Cauvin Mission" and Congo (1942-1944)**: Belgian filmmaker André Cauvin led a propaganda expedition to document the Belgian Congo's "riches" for Allied audiences. The resulting film *Congo* (1944) emphasized Katanga's copper, tin, and (unnamed) uranium—the materials fueling the war effort—while depicting forced Congolese labor as a continuation of ancestral rhythms: "The song of work, the song of the ancestors." The film's elaborate US release, including a White House screening for FDR, a Warner Brothers distribution deal, and a fabricated publisher (Willow, White & Co.) for an accompanying book by John Latouche, reveals how desperately Belgium sought to maintain its colonial claims. Meanwhile, the Office of Strategic Services monitored uranium supplies with agents carrying Kodak cameras. **The mines at Shinkolobwe exposed workers to "a year's worth of radiation in about two weeks."** This case demonstrates how film production (the documentary), film's raw materials (uranium from the same region), and colonial violence converged in a single geographic and political space.

**Black Spots and Nuclear Fallout (1945-1963)**: When Trinity's fallout contaminated strawboard packaging in Indiana and Iowa in August 1945, Julian Webb—a Kodak physicist who had worked at Y-12—traced the damage to river water carrying human-made radioactive particles. After Nevada testing began in 1951, Rochester became a "radiation sewer" where lake-effect weather deposited fallout on Kodak factories. The company leveraged its importance to secure advance warning of tests and maps of predicted fallout patterns—information withheld from the public. When Kodak president Thomas Hargrave threatened litigation, he called radiation "insidious"—echoing Cold War rhetoric about Communism while describing a hazard his company had helped create. Meanwhile, film served as a sentinel: X-ray film's sensitivity revealed contamination before health monitoring caught up, and film badges measured workers' radiation exposure at nuclear facilities. The case demonstrates how the same company that produced fuel for atomic bombs found its products registering the consequences, creating an uncomfortable feedback loop between film's military applications and its documentary function.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"This is a book about film as a weapon—not in its images or sounds, but in its chemistry. It asks how Kodak, a company synonymous with photography, came to the Manhattan Project, and it traces the aftermath of Kodak's work at Oak Ridge." (Introduction)

"Seen in the factories' light, film looks different than it does in a photo shop or on a cinema screen... We can compare film to television or radio, but in the factory, it sits just as comfortably alongside synthetic fibers, plastics, pesticides, painkillers, and weapons." (Introduction)

"As Esther Leslie puts it plainly: 'The chemical industry had it all covered: it produced the bombs and gases and the photographic materials.'" (Chapter 1\)

"Kodak—the US government's microfilm contractor—stood, above all, to profit" from postwar reparations that seized Agfacolor technology, demonstrating how "reels of nonflammable film traveled from the United States to Germany and back again, carrying Agfa's trade secrets." (Chapter 3\)

"The black spots that conveniently confined fallout to an industrial problem also eventually helped reveal the extent of fallout's danger to public health. As Eisenbud recalled years later, his office... 'might not have been involved in further fallout studies were it not for the fact that Eastman Kodak needed information to protect its processes.'" (Chapter 4\)

"'We've never been thought of as a chemical company,' the spokesman remarked coolly, 'we just made the yellow boxes, so there's an element of surprise and unfamiliarity.'" —Kodak spokesman at School 41 meeting about methylene chloride contamination (Chapter 5\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Pairs well with:**

* **Gabrielle Hecht, *Being Nuclear* (2012) — on uranium colonialism and the global politics of nuclear materials**  
* **Kate Brown, *Plutopia* (2013) — on secrecy and health consequences in nuclear cities**  
* Timothy Mitchell, *Carbon Democracy* (2011) — on the political economy of energy extraction  
* Jennifer Fay, *Inhospitable World* (2018) — on cinema and Anthropocene thinking  
* Ruth Schwartz Cowan, *More Work for Mother* (1983) — for comparative approach to how "labor-saving" technologies create new dependencies

**Limitations:**

* The book's ambitious global scope sometimes sacrifices depth for breadth; the Belgian Congo material, for instance, relies heavily on secondary sources and the Cauvin film rather than Congolese archives or perspectives.  
* Workers' voices (beyond Lawrik's documented story) remain relatively muted compared to corporate and scientific sources, though this reflects archival constraints rather than analytical choice.  
* The post-1963 narrative accelerates significantly, leaving contemporary connections (digital photography's material dependencies, lithium mining at Wolfen) suggestive rather than fully developed.

**Key ideas in this book**:

* This is a book about the relationship between film and war, in two senses: the way that film is used to document and propagandize during times of war, but even more importantly, the way the manufacture of film—the chemical engineering techniques, the industrial processes—resemble and enable the manufacture of weapons and explosives.  
  * In this book, film is less like television, and more like weapons and explosives.  
* Primarily follows Eastman Kodak, which has been a leader in film production since its founding in 1892\. Film production is a mass chemical manufacturing process, much like plastics, synthetic fibers, pesticides, painkillers, and poisonous gasses.  
* During WWII, private companies were mobilized to support the Manhattan Project and contribute their industrial expertise to the atomic bomb, with the employees often unaware of what they were working on. Kodak’s chemical engineering division, Eastman Tennessee, managed one of the main uranium enrichment plants in Oak Ridge.  
* Across the Atlantic, Germany’s major film production company, Agfa, employed concentration camp prisoners. Nazi propaganda was filmed on celulite 

* Kodak Eastman made film; it was dangerous and flammable. Huge incident in Cleveland hospital 

# Week 3 — Lab

# ***Image and Logic: A Material Culture of Microphysics* — Peter Galison (1997)**

### **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Galison argues that experimental physics in the twentieth century developed through two distinct and partially autonomous traditions of knowledge-making machines: the **image tradition** (cloud chambers, nuclear emulsions, bubble chambers), which produces pictures and seeks homomorphic representations of nature that preserve form, and the **logic tradition** (Geiger counters, spark chambers, wire chambers), which produces counts and aggregates statistical demonstrations of particle existence. Neither tradition has epistemic priority; each embodies fundamentally different epistemological commitments—the image tradition pursues "world→head reliability" (if an event occurs, the passive detector records it), while the logic tradition pursues "head→world reliability" (if we believe an event occurred, our selective detection confirms it). Physics coheres not through unified paradigms but through **trading zones** where practitioners from different subcultures coordinate action and belief through local exchanges, even without global agreement.

### **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Galison writes directly against the Kuhnian model of unified paradigms and revolutionary ruptures. Where Kuhn and his interpreters (including strong programme sociologists like Barnes, Bloor, and Collins) posited science as divided into self-validating "island empires" that undergo wholesale replacement during revolutions, Galison demonstrates that the subcultures of physics—theory, experiment, and instrument-making—develop on **different temporal rhythms** and rarely experience simultaneous breaks. This "intercalation" model envisions physics as a brick wall rather than periodic stacks: staggered breaks in different subcultures provide structural continuity even as individual traditions undergo transformation.

Galison also challenges Bachelard's influential claim that instruments are "reified theorems"—that apparatus is wholly determined by theoretical commitments. Against this theory-ladenness thesis, he shows how cloud chambers were deployed across wildly divergent theoretical frameworks (from condensation physics to quantum electrodynamics) with substantial autonomy from specific theoretical agendas. The book equally rejects naive empiricism: instruments are neither transparent windows onto nature nor mere expressions of theory.

Galison engages explicitly with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*, accepting their insight that "experiment" was a contested category in the seventeenth century but arguing against any assumption that the Boyle-Hobbes settlement fixed the meaning of experimentation permanently. For Galison, "the dispute over what empirical knowledge ought to be like has continued, productively, for three hundred years."

### **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Galison develops several interlocking concepts:

**Material Culture**: Drawing from anthropology, Galison treats instruments not as passive tools but as "entangled objects" carrying cultural meanings, labor relations, and epistemic commitments. The "inner laboratory"—the immediate working environment of instruments and practices—is the primary site of inquiry.

**Trading Zones**: Borrowed from anthropological studies of cross-cultural contact, trading zones are local spaces where practitioners from distinct subcultures can coordinate activity without sharing global meanings. Physicists, engineers, and theorists may disagree about the ultimate nature of particles while successfully exchanging data, building detectors, and publishing papers.

**Pidgins and Creoles**: Contact languages that arise in trading zones can stabilize and expand. Galison identifies "FORTRAN creoles" and "wordless creoles" (regularized laboratory practices) that enable coordination across subcultures. When pidgins expand sufficiently, they become full creoles—autonomous practices like "particle phenomenology" that bridge theory and experiment.

**Intercalation**: The temporal structure of physics resembles offset bricks rather than stacked blocks. Theory, experiment, and instrumentation each develop on their own timelines; their staggered breaks give physics continuity as a discipline even when individual subcultures undergo transformation.

### **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Galison draws on an extraordinary range of sources across a century of physics: archival materials from major laboratories (MIT Radiation Laboratory, Los Alamos, Lawrence Berkeley Laboratory, CERN, Bristol); laboratory notebooks (especially C.T.R. Wilson's); private and published correspondence; technical conference proceedings; instrument manuals and trade publications; oral histories; patent records; and industrial archives (Ilford, Kodak). The geographic scope spans Britain, Austria, the United States, France, Germany, Japan, and transnational collaborations. The temporal range extends from the 1890s (Wilson's first cloud experiments at Ben Nevis) through the 1990s (the cancelled Superconducting Supercollider).

Quantitative data supplements the qualitative narrative: Galison analyzes publication patterns showing that physicists exhibit 94% correlation between sequential papers within their instrumental tradition—confirming that image and logic practitioners rarely cross over.

### **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**C.T.R. Wilson and the Cloud Chamber (Chapter 2\)**: Wilson's invention emerged not from particle physics but from Victorian meteorological obsessions—he sought to recreate "artificial glories" witnessed at the Ben Nevis Observatory in Scotland. The cloud chamber's epistemic identity was bound to a "condensation physics" that was simultaneously meteorological and ionic. Wilson's work exemplifies how the image tradition valorized mimetic representation and the "golden event"—a single photograph compelling enough to demonstrate a particle's existence. The tradition institutionalized itself through cloud chamber "atlases" modeled on medical atlases, establishing conventions of visual objectivity.

**Marietta Blau and Nuclear Emulsions (Chapter 3\)**: Blau, a Jewish woman physicist working marginally at Vienna's Institut für Radiumforschung, developed the nuclear emulsion technique using ordinary dental X-ray film modified with pinakryptol yellow. Her work exemplifies the emulsion as a "laboratory-in-a-box" for marginalized practitioners. When the Nazis arrived, Blau fled, and Cecil Powell at Bristol continued her work. Powell transformed the emulsion laboratory into a "cottage industry" employing women scanners who searched exposed plates for interesting tracks. This introduced what Galison calls the **"anxiety of the experimenter"**: control over the experiment fragmented across chemists (at Ilford/Kodak), scanners (who found the events), and theorists (who interpreted them). The category of individual "experimenter-author" began dissolving.

**Luis Alvarez's Bubble Chamber Factory (Chapter 5\)**: Alvarez industrialized the bubble chamber at Lawrence Berkeley Laboratory, creating a "physics factory" that institutionalized image production on an unprecedented scale. Where Donald Glaser invented the bubble chamber as tabletop craft science (his first device used beer-bottle glass), Alvarez mobilized the organizational structures, safety protocols, and engineering resources of the wartime nuclear weapons program. The 72-inch hydrogen bubble chamber required coordination among physicists, engineers, cryogenics specialists, safety officers, and teams of women scanners analyzing photographs. This chapter tracks how danger—the constant threat of explosion from superheated hydrogen—shaped laboratory hierarchies, insurance negotiations, and the very definition of "responsible" physics.

**Monte Carlo Simulations (Chapter 8\)**: Emerging from Los Alamos nuclear weapons calculations, Monte Carlo methods created a "tertium quid" that was neither traditional theory nor experiment. Ulam and von Neumann developed the technique to simulate neutron transport in fission bombs when analytic solutions were impossible. By tracking pseudorandom "particles" through simulated geometries, physicists gained what they called "artificial data" requiring error analysis, variance reduction, and replication—practices borrowed from experimental physics. The Monte Carlo became a trading zone linking mathematicians, statisticians, nuclear engineers, and physicists. This chapter demonstrates how computational practices blurred the theory/experiment boundary and raised persistent questions about whether simulations could constitute genuine "experiments."

### **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Physics is a complicated patchwork of highly structured pieces: instrument makers thoroughly versed in the manipulation of gases, liquids, and circuitry; theorists concerned with the coherence, self-consistency, and calculability of the behavior of matter in their representation of matter most finely divided; and experimenters drawing together instruments into combinations in pursuit of novel effects." (p. xix)

"One tradition of instrument making—the image tradition—has had as its goal the representation of subvisible nature in all its fullness and complexity—the production of images. Such mimetic pictures can serve as evidence for a claim because they are presented, interpreted, and defended, as mimetic—as direct representations of nature as they occur in the world." (p. 19\)

"\[T\]he mimetic representation is well girded against the charge that something is missing from its account but vulnerable to the accusation that it has located a fluke or oddity. The statistical approach, by contrast, consciously sacrifices the detail of the one for the stability of the many." (p. 20\)

"Far from anticipating that coperiodization is the rule, we might well expect the histories of the different subcultures each to ring only rarely. This intercalated structure could give the discipline as a whole as an irregular... wall of adjacent columns of stacked bricks, each column of which is staggered with respect to its neighbor. Just as breaks in a brick wall give the wall much of its strength, so too these multiple series of practices (instrument making, theorizing, experimenting) give physics its sense of continuity as a discipline despite the periodic breakups of each subculture separately considered." (p. 19\)

"\[The experimenters'\] covenant with the industrial chemists left the particle hunters on shaky ground... The shock of exclusion was great. And nowhere was the abruptness of the boundary so visible as in the contractual isolation of the emulsion chemists from the physicists." (p. 231\)

"It is the culturally meaningful...things close at hand that I am after—read in the fine rain of C.T.R. Wilson's cloud chamber, in Robert Hofstadter's heavy gun-mounted spectrometer, in the quasi-real computer space of the simulation, and in the Time Projection Chamber's delicate array of image-making charged coupled sensors." (p. 7\)

### **7\. CONNECTIONS & EXAM UTILITY**

**Pairs well with**: Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (on contested meanings of experiment); Hughes's *Networks of Power* (on large technical systems); Kuhn's *Structure of Scientific Revolutions* (as direct counterpoint); Latour's *Science in Action* (alternative view of heterogeneous networks, though Galison emphasizes constraints rather than alliances); Collins's *Changing Order* (on tacit knowledge and replication, which Galison complicates); Daston and Galison's *Objectivity* (on visual cultures of science).

**Generals exam utility**: This book is essential for any question on the nature of experiment, laboratory studies, the theory-experiment relationship, or the social organization of twentieth-century physics. It provides a powerful alternative to Kuhnian periodization for questions about scientific change. The "trading zone" concept has become influential across STS for discussing interdisciplinary coordination. The detailed treatment of WWII's impact on physics (Chapter 4\) connects to questions about the military-industrial-academic complex. The emulsion chapter offers material on gender and labor in science. The Monte Carlo chapter addresses the epistemology of simulation—increasingly relevant for digital history.

**Limitations**: The book's length (900+ pages) reflects exhaustive archival work but can obscure the theoretical architecture. Galison's focus on high-energy physics limits generalizability to other sciences. Some critics find the image/logic distinction too binary, though Galison himself tracks hybrid devices. The trading zone metaphor has been criticized for underspecifying power differentials between exchanging parties.

# **Hasok Chang, *Inventing Temperature: Measurement and Scientific Progress* (2004)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Chang argues that the measurement of temperature—seemingly the most basic and uncontroversial of scientific operations—required solving profound epistemological problems that have no indubitable foundation. Scientific knowledge advances not through reference to self-evident truths but through *epistemic iteration*: a self-correcting process in which each stage builds on the previous one, gradually refining standards without ever possessing certainty that the starting point was correct. The book demonstrates that what we now take for granted about temperature (fixed points, scales, absolute zero) emerged from contentious debates, pragmatic workarounds, and serendipitous discoveries that later generations forgot or suppressed.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Chang intervenes against two dominant tendencies in the philosophy and history of science. First, he challenges epistemological foundationalism—the view that scientific knowledge rests on self-justifying bedrock beliefs. By showing that even something as basic as establishing that water boils at 100°C required centuries of innovative work, contested judgments, and pragmatic compromises, Chang demonstrates that there are no unshakeable foundations on which measurement is built. Second, he pushes against the tendency in history of science (associated with Kuhn, Shapin, and Schaffer, and the sociology of scientific knowledge more broadly) to treat recovered past knowledge as material for historical contextualization without pursuing its further scientific development. Chang insists that HPS can and should be a *knowledge-generating* enterprise, not merely a descriptive or prescriptive one. He is in explicit dialogue with Kuhn's account of normal science, Popper's insistence on the value of criticism, Bridgman's operationalism, Duhem's holism, and the coherentist epistemologies of Quine and Neurath. Against Kuhn, he argues that the demonstrated effectiveness of paradigm-bound science comes at the cost of suppressing legitimate questions and losing viable knowledge. Against Bridgman, he argues that operationalism is too restrictive because it fractures unified concepts unnecessarily. His proposed alternative—epistemic iteration within a "round-earth coherentism"—offers a middle path that acknowledges the historical contingency of scientific knowledge while defending the reality of progressive knowledge-building.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Chang mobilizes a coherentist epistemology anchored by his concept of "epistemic iteration"—a process modeled on mathematical iteration in which successive approximations build upon one another without any guarantee of convergence toward a pre-known correct answer. He also introduces the concept of "complementary science" as a mode of inquiry in which HPS addresses scientific questions neglected by specialist science, operating as what he analogizes to a welfare state offsetting the inevitable losses of the capitalist knowledge-production system.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Chang combines detailed historical reconstruction of thermometric practices from the seventeenth through nineteenth centuries with philosophical analysis of the epistemological problems those practices reveal. His sources include published scientific papers and treatises (De Luc, Cavendish, Regnault, Wedgwood, William Thomson), instrument collections at the Science Museum and National Maritime Museum, encyclopedias and textbooks of the period, and correspondence. The temporal scope runs from Galileo's thermoscopes (c. 1600\) through Thomson's operationalization of absolute temperature (1850s), with geographic focus on Britain, France, the Netherlands, and peripheral sites of imperial science (Hudson's Bay, Siberia).

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Vexatious Boiling Point and the Steam Point (Chapter 1).** Chang's opening case study devastates the commonsense assumption that the boiling point of water is a straightforward, natural "fixed point" for calibrating thermometers. He narrates how early thermometrists discovered that water could be heated well above 100°C without boiling (superheating), that the boiling temperature varied with dissolved impurities, atmospheric pressure, the material of the container, the depth at which the thermometer was immersed, and even the "degree of boiling." The solution eventually adopted—replacing the boiling point of liquid water with the "steam point" (the temperature of steam at standard atmospheric pressure)—was itself contingent on work by Cavendish and the Royal Society committee in the 1770s–1780s. Chang then traces John Aitken's late-nineteenth-century discovery that steam could be supersaturated (cooled below the steam point without condensing) in the absence of dust particles, meaning that even the steam point's fixity depended on the serendipitous impurity of ordinary air. The episode shows that what appears as a naturally fixed phenomenon was in fact manufactured through pragmatic strategies: eliminating causes of variation where possible, developing correction formulas for quantifiable variables, and simply ignoring small inexplicable variations in the hope they would disappear.

**The Problem of Nomic Measurement and the Search for the "Real" Scale (Chapter 2).** Chang identifies the "problem of nomic measurement" as the fundamental circularity in thermometry: to test whether mercury expands uniformly with temperature, one needs an independent measure of temperature—but the mercury thermometer is the very instrument one is trying to validate. He traces how Jean-André De Luc attempted to break this circle through the "method of mixtures" (mixing equal quantities of hot and cold water and checking whether the thermometer reads the calculated midpoint), demonstrating that mercury came closest to indicating "real" temperatures. He then follows the competing claims of caloric theorists who argued for the air thermometer's superiority on theoretical grounds—arguments Chang reveals as circular, since they relied on the very gas laws that assumed linear gas expansion. The resolution came through Victor Regnault's radical empiricism in the 1840s: Regnault abandoned all theoretical claims about which fluid expanded "truly" and instead adopted a criterion of "comparability"—the best thermometer was the one whose readings were most reproducible across different instruments and conditions. Chang uses Regnault's approach to illustrate a "minimalist" empiricism that refused to take on more theoretical commitments than were strictly necessary.

**Wedgwood's Pyrometer and the Problem of Metrological Extension (Chapter 3).** Josiah Wedgwood, the great potter, developed a clay-based pyrometer in the 1780s to measure the extreme temperatures in his kilns where mercury thermometers were useless. His device measured the contraction of small clay cylinders pushed through a tapered brass gauge after firing. Wedgwood's pyrometer was widely adopted—his temperatures appeared in standard references including the *Encyclopaedia Britannica*—but his numbers were wildly wrong: he reported his furnace temperatures at nearly 22,000°F, far hotter than the surface of the sun. The problem was that there was no reliable way to connect the Wedgwood scale to the Fahrenheit scale, since the domains of the two instruments did not overlap sufficiently. Chang uses this episode to explore the philosophical problem of extending a concept (temperature) beyond its "birth domain." He engages critically with Bridgman's operationalism (which would simply declare Wedgwood's measurements a different concept from Fahrenheit temperatures) and develops the alternative idea of "metrological extension" through "mutual grounding"—building overlapping chains of measurement methods that validate one another across domains, as Claude Pouillet later achieved by linking air thermometers, thermocouples, and alcohol thermometers in an interlocking network of agreement.

**Thomson's Absolute Temperature and the Challenge of Operationalization (Chapter 4).** William Thomson (Lord Kelvin) sought to define temperature on a purely theoretical basis, independent of the properties of any particular substance, using Carnot's theory of heat engines. His first definition (1848) defined equal temperature intervals as those producing equal amounts of work in an ideal Carnot engine. But this definition was entirely abstract—it specified no way to actually measure absolute temperature. The operationalization required the Joule-Thomson "porous plug" experiment and an elaborate chain of approximations linking theoretical temperature to readings of actual gas thermometers, using Regnault's painstaking data on steam behavior. Chang shows that this process was itself iterative: Thomson had to assume that the gas thermometer approximately measured absolute temperature in order to calculate the corrections needed to make it measure absolute temperature more precisely. The episode demonstrates that the relationship between theoretical concepts and empirical measurement is not a simple one-way deduction but a mutual, iterative adjustment.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"How do we know that our thermometers tell us the temperature correctly, especially when they disagree with each other? How can we test whether the fluid in our thermometer expands regularly with increasing temperature, without a circular reliance on the temperature readings provided by the thermometer itself?" (p. 4\)

"This circularity is probably the most crippling form of the theory-ladenness of observation." (p. 60\)

"Epistemic iteration is a process in which successive stages of knowledge, each building on the preceding one, are created in order to enhance the achievement of certain epistemic goals. It differs crucially from mathematical iteration in that the latter is used to approach the correct answer that is known, or at least in principle knowable, by other means. In epistemic iteration that is not so clearly the case." (pp. 45–46)

"If defensible fixed points do not occur naturally, they must be manufactured. I do not, of course, mean that we should simply pretend that certain points are fixed when they are not. What we need to do is find, or create, clearly identifiable material circumstances under which the fixity does hold." (p. 49\)

"History and philosophy of science can seek to generate scientific knowledge in places where science itself fails to do so; I will call this the complementary function of history and philosophy of science." (p. 236\)

"We build structures outward on the round earth, not upward on a flat earth. We build on the earth because we happen to live on it, not because the earth is fundamental or secure in some ultimate sense.… In science, too, we build structures around what we are first given, and that does not require the starting points to be absolutely secure." (pp. 223–224)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Chang pairs productively with several works across your lists. Theodore Porter's *Trust in Numbers* shares Chang's concern with how quantification achieves authority, but where Porter emphasizes the social and political functions of quantitative objectivity, Chang asks the prior epistemological question of how measurement standards acquire validity in the first place. Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison's *Objectivity* similarly complements Chang by tracing how epistemic virtues (including mechanical objectivity) were historically constructed, and Chang's account of Regnault's austere empiricism reads as a case study in their framework of objectivity-as-impersonality. Steven Shapin and Simon Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* shares Chang's interest in how experimental knowledge is constructed and stabilized, but Chang explicitly critiques the SSK tradition for failing to develop the scientific potential of the alternative knowledge systems it recovers.

One limitation is that Chang's focus on the internal epistemology of measurement gives little attention to the social, economic, and imperial contexts that shaped thermometric practices—the role of Hudson's Bay Company imperialism in enabling mercury-freezing experiments, or Wedgwood's industrial interests in pyrometry, receive narrative mention but not sustained analysis. Additionally, his concept of "complementary science" remains programmatic rather than fully realized: the book is more successful as a demonstration of how historical investigation reveals epistemological puzzles than as an example of HPS actually generating new scientific knowledge.

# **Hasok Chang, *Inventing Temperature: Measurement and Scientific Progress* (2004)**

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Chang intervenes against two dominant traditions. First, he challenges **standard philosophy of science** that treats measurement as epistemologically unproblematic—a mere backdrop to theory-testing rather than a site of profound foundationalist dilemmas. Against philosophers who assume thermometers simply "read off" temperature values, Chang reveals the circular reasoning involved: How can we test whether a thermometer expands linearly with temperature without already having a trusted thermometer? How can we establish fixed points without knowing they are fixed? This circularity problem, he argues, undermines classical foundationalism.

Second, Chang challenges the **internalist/externalist divide** in history of science by practicing what he calls "complementary science"—an approach that treats historical and philosophical inquiry as a legitimate form of knowledge production, not merely descriptive scholarship. He positions himself against the post-Kuhnian sociology of scientific knowledge (SSK), which reduces scientific beliefs to social causes, while also departing from triumphalist internalist histories that treat past debates as mere stepping stones to current truth. Chang suggests that some rejected scientific approaches may have been abandoned for inadequate reasons and deserve reconsideration.

His key interlocutors include Percy Bridgman (whose operationalism he both draws upon and revises), Pierre Duhem (whose holism about theory-testing he addresses), and Charles Sanders Peirce (whose ideas about self-correction inform the concept of epistemic iteration). He engages extensively with the Laplacian tradition in French physics, particularly the caloric theory of heat, and shows how Regnault's "post-Laplacian empiricism" represented a distinctive methodological stance that privileged precision measurement over theoretical speculation.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Chang develops a **coherentist epistemology** buttressed by the method of epistemic iteration. Rejecting both strict foundationalism and the relativism of SSK, he argues that knowledge systems gain legitimacy through their capacity for self-improvement rather than their derivation from indubitable premises. He introduces the **principle of respect**: scientists begin by accepting existing systems of knowledge provisionally, treating them as achievements worthy of deference while also subjecting them to correction. This is stronger than simple credulity—one continues working within a framework even while recognizing its imperfection, because abandoning it would mean losing embodied achievements difficult to reconstruct.

Key concepts include:

* **Epistemic iteration**: Successive stages of knowledge building on prior stages, each improving without being deducible from the last  
* **Metrological extension**: The problem of extending measurement concepts beyond their original domain (via Bridgman's operationalism)  
* **Comparability**: The requirement that different instruments measuring the same quantity agree in their overlapping domains  
* **Serendipitous robustness**: The fortunate circumstance that certain natural facts (dissolved air in water, dust in atmosphere) stabilized measurement standards without scientists understanding why

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Chang combines **archival research** with **philosophical analysis**, drawing on primary sources from 18th- and 19th-century physics and chemistry. Key sources include the Royal Society of London's committee reports on thermometry (1777), Jean-André De Luc's *Inquiries on the Modifications of the Atmosphere* (1772), Henri Victor Regnault's precision experiments (1840s-1860s), and Josiah Wedgwood's papers on pyrometry. He examines scientific correspondence (Cavendish, Fahrenheit, Thomson/Kelvin), instrument collections at the Science Museum London and National Maritime Museum, and scientific periodicals. The temporal scope spans roughly 1700-1900, with primary focus on Western European science (Britain, France, Geneva, Germany).

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

### **Case Study 1: Superheating and the Problem of the Boiling Point (Chapter 1\)**

Jean-André De Luc's four-week obsessive experiment (1772) forms the dramatic heart of Chapter 1\. Convinced that the "true ebullition" of water required knowing the temperature of the "first layer" of water in contact with the heat source, De Luc discovered that pure water—freed of dissolved air—could be heated far beyond 100°C without boiling. After spending four continuous weeks shaking a flask of water to purge dissolved air ("I ate, I read, I wrote, I saw my friends, I took my walks, all the while shaking my water"), De Luc obtained water that reached 112°C before explosive boiling. This "superheating" threatened to undermine the boiling point as a fixed reference for thermometry.

Henry Cavendish responded by proposing the *steam point* rather than the boiling point as the true fixed point, arguing that steam emerging from water would always be at a constant temperature regardless of superheating in the water itself. The Royal Society committee of 1777, which included both De Luc and Cavendish, adopted this compromise despite unresolved theoretical disagreements. Chang shows that the stability of the steam point was ultimately secured by "serendipitous robustness"—the fact that ordinary air contains sufficient dust particles to prevent supersaturation of steam, a phenomenon only understood a century later through John Aitken's experiments on condensation nuclei (1880s). The fixed points were thus established through pragmatic success rather than theoretical understanding.

### **Case Study 2: The Air vs. Mercury Thermometer Debate (Chapter 2\)**

The "problem of nomic measurement" structures Chapter 2: How can we determine which thermometric fluid expands linearly with "real" temperature, when we can only measure temperature using thermometers whose linearity is precisely what we seek to establish? De Luc's solution was the **method of mixtures**—if equal amounts of water at 0°C and 100°C are mixed, a correct thermometer should read 50°C. His extensive testing appeared to vindicate mercury.

However, the caloric theory (particularly Laplace's sophisticated mathematical version from the 1820s) provided theoretical reasons to prefer air thermometers, since gases were believed to be "caloric machines" whose volume directly reflected heat content. Chang shows that Laplace's derivations—despite their mathematical elegance—rested on untestable assumptions about intermolecular forces and contributed nothing concrete to thermometric practice.

Victor Regnault's meticulous experiments (1840s-1860s) delivered the decisive verdict against mercury, showing that different types of glass expanded differently, making "the mercury thermometer" an inconsistent standard. Regnault's broader significance lies in his **post-Laplacian empiricism**: having found that even supposedly fundamental gas laws were only approximately true, he became deeply skeptical of all theoretical claims and devoted himself to accumulating precise data. Chang argues that Regnault's work represented a "true revolution" in physics, though one whose methodological character—endless circles of experimental virtuosity without theoretical closure—frustrated contemporaries and historians alike.

### **Case Study 3: Wedgwood's Clay Pyrometer (Chapter 3\)**

Josiah Wedgwood, the celebrated potter, invented a pyrometer in the 1780s to measure kiln temperatures far beyond where mercury boiled. His ingenious device measured the contraction of standardized clay pieces when heated. The original Wedgwood scale worked well for ceramic practice—enabling Wedgwood to achieve greater consistency in his famous products—but the attempt to convert Wedgwood degrees to Fahrenheit degrees produced wildly inflated numbers. Wedgwood reported kiln temperatures of 21,877°F (far hotter than the surface of the sun, as we now know).

The "ganging up on Wedgwood" that followed his death in 1795 is epistemologically instructive. Critics attacked his scale using alternative pyrometric methods—platinum expansion, ice calorimetry, cooling rates. Chang demonstrates that **none of these alternatives was better established than Wedgwood's own method**; each rested on equally unverified assumptions about linearity and material behavior. Their collective strength lay only in their mutual agreement. Chang develops a taxonomy of **metrological extension strategies**: disconnected extension (Wedgwood's original approach), patching (using intermediate standards to bridge gaps), whole-range standards (finding materials usable across the full temperature range), and leapfrogging (extrapolating established laws into new domains). The International Practical Temperature Scale adopted in the 20th century is essentially a sophisticated form of patching.

### **Case Study 4: Thomson's Absolute Temperature (Chapter 4\)**

William Thomson (Lord Kelvin) sought to define temperature independent of any particular thermometric substance by grounding it in Carnot's theory of heat engines. His **abstract concept** of absolute temperature was elegant: the ratio of temperatures equals the ratio of heat absorbed to heat rejected by an ideal Carnot engine operating between two reservoirs. But this created a new problem—how to **operationalize** an abstract theoretical concept.

Chang shows that Thomson's operationalization required the **Joule-Thomson experiment** (measuring the cooling of gas forced through a porous plug) and complex iterative corrections. Starting from the assumption that real gases approximately obey the ideal gas law, experimenters could measure deviations and calculate corrections, then use corrected values to obtain better deviation measurements, iterating toward (hoped-for) convergence. Chang reveals that Thomson's published justifications for these procedures contained serious gaps—he assumed mercury thermometers were accurate for the Joule-Thomson experiments while simultaneously arguing that absolute temperature was needed because mercury thermometers were unreliable. The procedure worked despite rather than because of its theoretical justification, through epistemic iteration that gradually aligned the abstract concept with concrete measurements.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

**On the foundationalist dilemma:**

"How do we know that our thermometers tell us the temperature correctly, especially when they disagree with each other? How can we test whether the fluid in our thermometer expands regularly with increasing temperature, without a circular reliance on the temperature readings provided by the thermometer itself?" (p. 4\)

**On epistemic iteration:**

"Epistemic iteration is a process in which successive stages of knowledge, each building on the preceding one, are created in order to enhance the achievement of certain epistemic goals.… In each step, the later stage is based on the earlier stage, but cannot be deduced from it in any straightforward sense. Each link is based on the principle of respect and the imperative of progress, and the whole chain exhibits innovative progress within a continuous tradition." (p. 226\)

**On the principle of respect:**

"Those who respect the affirmed system may have quite strong reasons for rejecting it, but will continue to work with it because they recognize that it embodies considerable achievement that may be very difficult to match if one starts from another basis." (p. 225\)

**On Regnault's post-Laplacian empiricism:**

"For Regnault, to search for truth meant 'to replace the axioms of theoreticians by precise data.'… The task of obtaining indisputable data turned out to have no end, which meant that theoretical activity had to be postponed indefinitely." (p. 99\)

**On serendipitous robustness:**

"The most important serendipitous factor for the fixity of the boiling point is the fact that water on earth normally contains a good deal of dissolved air. For the steam point, it is the fact that air on earth normally contains a good deal of suspended dust." (p. 50\)

**On complementary science:**

"The primary aim of complementary science is not to tell specialist science what to do, but to do what specialist science is presently unable to do. It is a shadow discipline, whose boundaries change exactly so as to encompass whatever gets excluded in specialist science." (pp. 249-250)

# **Angela N. H. Creager, *Life Atomic: A History of Radioisotopes in Science and Medicine* (2013)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Creager argues that the U.S. Atomic Energy Commission's radioisotope distribution program profoundly shaped postwar biology and medicine, serving simultaneously as a material foundation for new experimental practices and as a political instrument to demonstrate the peaceful benefits of atomic energy. By using radioisotopes as "historical tracers," she reveals how the civilian-military boundary in Cold War science was constitutively blurred rather than clearly demarcated, with the same Oak Ridge reactor that produced materials for radiological warfare experiments also supplying isotopes for cancer research and ecological studies.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Creager intervenes in historiography on the atomic age by shifting focus from physics and weaponry to biology and medicine, demonstrating that the Manhattan Project shaped life sciences as profoundly as it did the physical sciences. She builds on but extends the work of scholars like Paul Forman on military patronage's effects on physics, arguing against both a reductionist view that postwar science was simply militarized and an idealized vision of autonomous civilian science. Her focus on radioisotopes as "dual-use" technology complicates the standard narrative of a sharp divide between peaceful and military applications of atomic energy.

The book engages with the ACHRE (Advisory Committee on Human Radiation Experiments) reports from the 1990s, recontextualizing human radiation experiments within the broader culture of postwar medical research and the government's promotional agenda.
