Against triumphalist accounts of nuclear medicine, Creager shows how the AEC's conflict of interest between promotion and regulation shaped both scientific practice and public health outcomes. She follows Hans-Jörg Rheinberger's characterization of the radioisotope program as "big science coming in small pieces," demonstrating how decentralized distribution of government-produced materials transformed research across thousands of institutions.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Creager employs an infrastructural and material approach to the history of science, treating radioisotopes as objects whose circulation reveals larger transformations in politics, epistemology, and scientific practice. She draws on scholarship concerning scientific instruments and materials (Clarke and Fujimura, Kohler, Rheinberger, Galison) while also engaging with the literature on "drug trajectories" and commodity circulation. Key concepts include the "dual-use" nature of nuclear technologies, the co-production of civilian and military science, and the idea of radioisotopes as tools that illuminated the temporality of biological systems through "tracing" molecular pathways.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Creager draws extensively on declassified government documents (many released following Clinton's 1994 ACHRE initiative), AEC administrative records at the National Archives, personal papers of key figures (Ernest Lawrence, Paul Aebersold, Gunther Stent), and scientific publications. She combines institutional and policy history with detailed case studies of laboratory practice. The geographic scope centers on the United States but includes attention to international exports (especially through Atoms for Peace); temporal scope spans from 1930s cyclotron-era experiments through the dissolution of the AEC in 1975, with an epilogue on subsequent developments.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Photosynthesis Research with Carbon-14 (Berkeley, 1940s-1950s)** Martin Kamen and Sam Ruben discovered carbon-14 in 1940, offering a long-lived radiocarbon tracer superior to carbon-11's 21-minute half-life. After Ruben's tragic death in 1943 and Kamen's dismissal over security concerns, Melvin Calvin and Andrew Benson took up photosynthesis research using carbon-14 to trace the sequence of chemical reactions whereby plants convert carbon dioxide into sugars. Working in the old cyclotron building (later renamed the "Old Radiation Laboratory"), they exposed *Chlorella* algae to 14C-labeled CO2 for varying durations, then used paper chromatography to separate and identify radioactive intermediates. This "lollipop" technique—killing cells at precise intervals—revealed that the first stable product of carbon fixation was phosphoglyceric acid (PGA), not formaldehyde as previously assumed. By 1954, Calvin's group had mapped the complete photosynthetic carbon cycle (later called the Calvin-Benson cycle), work that earned Calvin the 1961 Nobel Prize in Chemistry. This case exemplifies how reactor-produced carbon-14 (far cheaper than cyclotron production—$10,000 from Oak Ridge versus $100 million equivalent from cyclotrons) made previously impossible experiments routine, fundamentally reshaping biochemistry.

**Hershey-Chase Experiment and Bacteriophage Research (1940s-1950s)** Alfred Hershey, working initially at Washington University with Martin Kamen and graduate student Howard Gest, used phosphorus-32 to investigate bacteriophage reproduction. They accidentally discovered "phage suicide"—the phenomenon whereby radioactive decay of incorporated phosphorus-32 inactivated viruses—which became a tool for studying the genetic material's role in reproduction. In the famous 1952 experiment with Martha Chase, Hershey used phosphorus-32 (labeling DNA) and sulfur-35 (labeling protein) to demonstrate that DNA, not protein, entered bacteria during infection and directed viral reproduction. This "blender experiment" (using a kitchen blender to separate phage coats from bacteria) became a landmark demonstration that genes are made of DNA. Creager shows how this celebrated experiment depended on the AEC's supply of high-specific-activity phosphorus-32 from Oak Ridge, and how the half-life of the radioisotope (14 days) had to be coordinated with the timing of biological processes (the phage eclipse period lasted only 13 minutes, requiring researchers to freeze samples in liquid nitrogen to allow radioactive decay to catch up with biology).

**Vanderbilt Radioiron Studies on Pregnant Women (1945-1947)** Paul Hahn, building on George Whipple's pioneering work with cyclotron-produced iron-59 at Rochester, designed a study at Vanderbilt Medical School in which over 800 pregnant women received oral doses of radioactive iron to investigate maternal iron absorption. The women, mostly poor clinic patients, received the radioisotope with therapeutic iron supplements during prenatal visits; blood samples were then drawn to measure absorption. Results showed that iron absorption increased as pregnancy progressed (from 17% in the first trimester to 36% in the last) and that radioiron crossed the placenta to the fetus. Critically, the reactor-produced radioiron from Oak Ridge contained about 10% iron-55 (with its longer half-life), creating greater radiation exposure than pure cyclotron-produced iron-59. Many women did not seem to understand they were research subjects, and the study provided them no direct benefit. When follow-up studies in the 1960s revealed elevated cancer rates among exposed children, the Vanderbilt experiment became emblematic of the ethical problems with Cold War human experimentation—though Creager emphasizes that at the time, researchers considered tracer doses safe and the work proceeded alongside Nobel Prize-winning research using identical methods.

**Rosalyn Yalow and Radioimmunoassay Development (1950s)** At the Veterans Administration Hospital in the Bronx, nuclear physicist Rosalyn Yalow and physician Solomon Berson used iodine-131-labeled insulin to study diabetes in veterans. Their unexpected finding—that diabetic patients who had received insulin injections produced antibodies against the hormone—contradicted prevailing immunological theory that small molecules like insulin could not be antigenic. More significantly, they developed radioimmunoassay (RIA), a technique using radiolabeled antigens and antibodies to measure minute quantities of hormones, drugs, and other substances in blood. RIA transformed clinical diagnostics by enabling detection of substances at concentrations a million times lower than previous methods. Yalow received the 1977 Nobel Prize for this work. Creager uses this case to show how radioisotope research at military-funded facilities (the VA hospital also conducted classified research) could produce medical breakthroughs with far-reaching civilian applications.

**Radioecology at Oak Ridge, Hanford, and Savannah River (1950s-1960s)** G. Evelyn Hutchinson's pioneering use of phosphorus-32 to trace nutrient cycling in Linsley Pond, Connecticut, provided the template for ecosystem studies at the AEC's major installations. At Hanford Works in Washington, scientists tracked radioactive effluents from plutonium production through the Columbia River ecosystem, documenting alarming bioconcentration—some organisms accumulated radioactivity thousands of times above ambient levels. At Oak Ridge, Stanley Auerbach established a radiation ecology program studying cesium-137 and strontium-90 cycling through forest ecosystems around the White Oak Creek waste disposal area. Eugene and Howard Odum at the Savannah River Plant conducted whole-ecosystem experiments, introducing phosphorus-32 into ponds to trace energy flow and developing the energy flow diagrams that became iconic representations of ecosystem function. These AEC-funded studies gave concrete empirical substance to Tansley's abstract concept of "ecosystem," transforming ecology from natural history into quantitative systems science. The same tracer methodology developed to track radioactive contamination proved applicable to understanding the environmental fate of pesticides, heavy metals, and other pollutants—a legacy that shaped environmental science and regulation.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"This book is an account of the uses of radioisotopes as a way to shed new light on the consequences of the 'physicists' war' for postwar biology and medicine." (p. 2\)

"As tracers, \[radioisotopes\] are proving themselves the most useful new research tool since the invention of the microscope in the 17th Century; in fact, they represent that rarest of all scientific advances, a new mode of perception." (AEC, 1948, quoted p. 3\)

"Radioisotopes, as part of a classic 'dual-use' technology, exemplify the blurriness of the civilian-military divide. The postwar growth of the federal government's defense organizations and programs, with an increasingly elaborate security apparatus, implied a civilian counterpart, and vice versa." (p. 19\)

"The radioisotope program was notable for its early origin—the program was established by the Manhattan Project in advance of the Atomic Energy Act of 1946—and the sheer number of research sites that it affected." (p. 7\)

"Through radioisotopes we can see traces of the Manhattan Project across the postwar life sciences and medicine: in the bodies of patients and healthy human subjects, in experimental bacteria, plants, and animals, in the schematic diagrams and theories that represented life in molecular terms, and in pristine and contaminated Cold War landscapes." (p. 406\)

"To follow radioisotopes is not only to see how their availability as by-products of the atomic age enabled a new sensitivity to temporal processes in the living world, but also to glimpse the evanescent materiality that makes scientific knowledge possible in the first place." (p. 407\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Paired works:** This book complements Paul Edwards's *The Closed World* (on computers and Cold War discourse) and John Krige's *American Hegemony and the Postwar Reconstruction of Science in Europe* (on science diplomacy). Creager's attention to materials and instruments pairs productively with Rheinberger's *Toward a History of Epistemic Things*, while her analysis of human experimentation engages with the historiography addressed in Stark's *Behind Closed Doors* and the ACHRE reports. For connections between science and the state, Jasanoff's *States of Knowledge* provides theoretical grounding.

# **Laboratory Life: The Construction of Scientific Facts**

## **Bruno Latour & Steve Woolgar (1979; 2nd ed. 1986\)**

---

### **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Laboratory Life argues that scientific facts are not discovered but *constructed* through material practices, literary inscription, and agonistic social processes within the specific material setting of the laboratory. The central thesis holds that the difference between "fact" and "artefact" is not given but rather *produced* through operations that systematically erase the traces of their own construction—such that what emerges as "reality" is the consequence, not the cause, of the settlement of disputes. Facts achieve their apparent solidity precisely through the accumulation of investments that make alternative statements prohibitively costly to raise.

---

### **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Latour and Woolgar intervene against two dominant traditions in the study of science. First, they reject the Mertonian sociology of science, which treated "social factors" (norms, reward systems, institutional affiliations) as entirely separate from "technical content," effectively leaving the substance of scientific knowledge as a black box beyond sociological analysis. Second, they challenge the epistemological tradition that treats scientific reasoning as fundamentally different from everyday reasoning—a tradition sustained by philosophers from Popper to Kuhn who maintained a conceptual firewall between "context of discovery" and "context of justification."

The book proposes instead an "anthropology of science" that refuses to take the distinction between "social" and "technical" as a starting point, treating it instead as a resource that scientists themselves deploy strategically in the agonistic field. Drawing on Bachelard's concept of "phenomenotechnique" and anticipating what would become Actor-Network Theory, they argue that facts, artifacts, instruments, careers, and arguments form a seamless web that cannot be analytically separated into "internal" (cognitive) and "external" (social) factors. The work directly engages with and extends the Edinburgh school's "strong programme" (Barnes, Bloor), while offering a methodological alternative to the interview-based and archival approaches that had dominated the field.

---

### **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

The book develops a **constructivist** approach that synthesizes elements from science and technology studies, anthropology (particularly ethnomethodology and the stranger device from Schutz), semiotics (the analysis of statement types and modalities from Greimas), and information theory (Brillouin's signal-to-noise ratio). Key concepts introduced or mobilized include: **inscription devices** (apparatus that transforms material substances into written documents usable in arguments), the **agonistic field** (the competitive arena of statement-making toward which scientific activity is oriented, rather than "nature"), **credibility cycles** (the conversion between different forms of credit—money, data, prestige, arguments, papers), and the **splitting and inversion** process whereby constructed statements become separated from their conditions of production and appear as pre-existing objects "out there."

---

### **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

The study is based on two years of participant observation (October 1975–August 1977\) at Roger Guillemin's neuroendocrinology laboratory at the Salk Institute in San Diego. Latour, a French philosopher with no scientific training, deliberately adopted the position of an anthropological "stranger" to avoid taking scientific categories for granted. Sources include: extensive field notes from daily observation, formal interviews with all laboratory members, analysis of the laboratory's complete publication output and citation patterns, examination of drafts, letters, memoranda, and protocol books, and Latour's own experience working as a (notably incompetent) laboratory technician. The geographic scope is primarily the Salk Institute, with the historical case study of TRF extending to competing laboratories (Schally's group in New Orleans, Harris's group in England, Schibuzawa in Japan, Schreiber in Czechoslovakia).

---

### **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The TRF(H) Controversy (Chapter 3):** The book's most sustained historical analysis traces the eight-year construction of Thyrotropin Releasing Factor from 1962–1969. This case demonstrates how a "hard fact"—the molecular structure Pyro-Glu-His-Pro-NH₂—was produced through material and social processes rather than "discovered." Guillemin's crucial 1962 decision to reshape the subspecialty around one goal ("obtain the structure at all costs") established fourteen stringent new criteria that effectively eliminated competitors like Schibuzawa (Japan) and Schreiber (Czechoslovakia), whose earlier claims to have characterized TRF were retrospectively dismissed as "artefacts." The case shows how the substance TRF did not exist prior to the imposition of these constraints; it was constructed through the superimposition of two sets of inscriptions (bioassays and purification cycles) and only stabilized when the cost of raising alternatives became prohibitive. The 1966 enzymatic test crisis—when results suggested TRF might not be a peptide, contradicting the fundamental assumption—demonstrates that "logical" deductions depended entirely on social context: Schally found the correct amino acids in 1968 but didn't believe his own results because he believed Guillemin's (incorrect) hypothesis. Only the September 1969 use of mass spectrometry provided "unambiguous" proof, at which point the statement split: it became both a statement AND an object "out there," and history was inverted so that TRF appeared to have been waiting "there all along" to be discovered.

**The Brunick Episode (Chapter 4):** A brief but illuminating conversational exchange demonstrates how facts are constructed and dismantled in daily laboratory talk. Wilson discusses his ACTH assay results with Flower, building credibility for his claim that ACTH and endorphin are the same by citing a 0.8 correlation ("Wooh\!") that will improve to 0.9 "which is perfectly legal." But then Wilson pivots to discussing colleague Brunick's recent claim to have characterized CRF. Using a computer program that found 93% homology between Brunick's "CRF" and haemoglobin, Wilson dismisses the claim by linking it to Brunick's previous well-known blunder (claiming to have found a releasing factor that turned out to be haemoglobin contamination). The episode shows how the same rhetorical operations—superimposition of curves, correlation statistics, invocation of past credibility—can either establish or destroy a claim depending on the agonistic positioning of the speakers.

**The Observer's Incompetent Bioassay (Chapter 6):** Latour's experience as a failing laboratory technician provides crucial insight into the "practical miracle" that produces stable scientific inscriptions. Attempting to run an MSH bioassay, the observer found himself unable to keep track of which dose went in which beaker, whether he had made notes before or after actions, forgetting to record time intervals, and ultimately producing a useless scatter of points. The episode reveals the "wealth of invisible skills" underlying material inscription—the routinized training, protocol books, and manic attention to coding and labeling that prevent laboratory work from dissolving into chaos. It also establishes the essential similarity between the observer's methods and those of his informants: both constructed ordered accounts from disorder using inscription devices, both operated in agonistic fields, and the only difference was in resources. The laboratory had millions of dollars and tons of equipment; the observer had only a notebook and, eventually, a text.

**Dietrich's Career Trajectory (Chapter 5):** The detailed reconstruction of one scientist's career path demonstrates the credibility cycle in action. Dietrich's decisions—choosing Munich over Bern for graduate training, attending the Eilat congress where he assessed neurophysiology as "a good field, not crowded, bound to be more and more important," approaching X (who rejected him) then Y (who offered support and tenure), moving to Houston to collaborate with Z (whose claims proved unsupported by data), finally securing a fellowship to work with Flower at the Salk Institute—reveal science as a series of quasi-economic investments and conversions between forms of capital. Each move involved precise calculations of opportunity, return, and risk. The case shows that scientists are not motivated primarily by "credit as reward" (which would make Dietrich's career incomprehensible, since he received few citations and no awards) but by "credit as credibility"—the accumulation of credentials, reliable data, access to resources, and positioning in active markets for information.

---

### **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Scientific activity is not 'about nature,' it is a fierce fight to construct reality. The laboratory is the workplace and the set of productive forces, which makes construction possible." (p. 243\)

"The result of the construction of a fact is that it appears unconstructed by anyone; the result of rhetorical persuasion in the agonistic field is that participants are convinced that they have not been convinced." (p. 240\)

"The set of statements considered too costly to modify constitute what is referred to as reality. Scientific activity is not 'about nature,' it is a fierce fight to construct reality." (p. 243\)

"Without a bioassay, for example, a substance could not be said to exist. The bioassay is not merely a means of obtaining some independently given entity; the bioassay constitutes the construction of the substance." (p. 64\)

"There is, then, an essential congruence between a 'fact' and the successful operation of various processes of literary inscription. A text or statement can thus be read as 'containing' or 'being about a fact' when readers are sufficiently convinced that there is no debate about it and the processes of literary inscription are forgotten." (p. 76\)

"Our account is no more than fiction. But this does not make it inferior to the activity of laboratory members: they too were busy constructing accounts to be launched in the agonistic field." (p. 257\)

---

### **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Pairs well with:** Steven Shapin and Simon Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (1985), which similarly demonstrates the social construction of experimental facts but through historical rather than ethnographic methods, and which shares the commitment to showing that epistemological categories (fact/artifact, nature/society) are outcomes rather than starting points of analysis. Also pairs productively with Harry Collins's *Changing Order* (1985), which developed the "experimenter's regress" argument for the interpretive flexibility of experimental results, though Collins relies more on interviews than participant observation. For the history of technology specifically, Thomas Hughes's *Networks of Power* (1983) offers a complementary analysis of how large technical systems stabilize through heterogeneous network-building, though Hughes retains more faith in the causal efficacy of technical factors.

**Limitations:** The study examines only one laboratory in one subdiscipline (neuroendocrinology) during a relatively brief period, raising questions about generalizability. The book's deliberate refusal to engage with macro-social analysis (class relations, political economy, institutional structures) was criticized by Marxist scholars as "idealist relativism." Additionally, the "stranger" methodology, while productive for defamiliarization, may systematically miss aspects of technical practice that only become visible through prolonged competent participation—as Lynch (1985) argued, the observer's incompetence became a feature rather than a bug, but this means certain dimensions of scientific work remained inaccessible.

---

# **Robert E. Kohler, *Lords of the Fly: Drosophila Genetics and the Experimental Life* (1994)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Kohler argues that the fruit fly *Drosophila melanogaster* was not simply discovered but actively *constructed* into a standardized laboratory instrument through deliberate inbreeding, selection, and the creation of elaborate technological systems for genetic mapping. The success of Morgan's fly group depended not merely on intellectual breakthroughs but on the symbiotic relationship between the fly's biological capacities (rapid reproduction, visible mutations, and tractable chromosomes) and the social organization of the research community—its "moral economy" of shared tools, collaborative norms, and exchange networks that enabled unprecedented productivity in experimental genetics.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Kohler positions this work against internalist histories of genetics that focus primarily on conceptual developments and intellectual discoveries. He critiques the tendency to treat experimental organisms as passive, transparent windows onto nature rather than as actively constructed technologies embedded in social systems of production. The book engages directly with the emerging science studies literature of the 1980s and early 1990s—particularly the work of Latour and Woolgar on laboratory life, Shapin and Schaffer on experimental credibility, and the sociology of scientific knowledge more broadly—but departs from their primary focus on epistemology and "social construction" to emphasize production processes themselves.

Kohler explicitly refuses the strong program's emphasis on deconstructing scientific facts; instead, he treats the "material culture and craft practices of science" as fundamental categories that can transform traditional histories of science without replacing intellectual or institutional frameworks. He draws on historians of technology (particularly Thomas Hughes's work on large technical systems) to argue that experimental organisms are best understood as components of complex sociotechnical systems. Against triumphalist narratives of genetic discoveries, Kohler shows how the fly's construction for mapping purposes actually *constrained* the development of developmental and evolutionary genetics for decades, requiring new hybrid practices in the 1930s to overcome these built-in limitations.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Kohler deploys three complementary analytical frameworks: (1) experimental organisms as *technology*—artifacts constructed and embedded in systems of production analogous to industrial machinery; (2) laboratories as *ecosystems* with distinctive natural histories where creatures and humans coevolve in symbiotic relationships; and (3) *moral economy*—the unwritten rules regulating access to tools, distribution of credit, and authority relations within and between research groups. Key concepts include "breeder reactor" (the autocatalytic capacity of Drosophila to produce ever more mutants), "threshold" (the boundary zone between laboratory and field that organisms and practices must cross), and "standard organism" (a highly constructed creature that enables precise, reproducible experimental production).

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Kohler draws primarily on archival sources including the papers of T.H. Morgan, Calvin Bridges, Alfred Sturtevant, H.J. Muller, Curt Stern, Milislav Demerec, and the Carnegie Institution of Washington. He supplements these with published scientific literature, oral histories (particularly Theodosius Dobzhansky's), and contemporary accounts from the *Drosophila Information Service*. The geographic scope centers on American institutions (Columbia, Caltech, Cold Spring Harbor, University of Texas) with attention to international networks, spanning roughly 1900–1945.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

### **The Domestication and "Breeder Reactor" Effect (Chapters 2–3)**

Kohler traces how *Drosophila melanogaster* moved from being one experimental organism among many to becoming *the* standard genetic creature. The fly's wild ancestors were already "specialists adapted to the domestic economy of humankind"—cosmopolitan hitchhikers that bred in fermenting fruits and human garbage, making them unusually likely to cross the threshold into laboratories. But what transformed Drosophila into a scientific powerhouse was an unexpected autocatalytic effect: Mendelian breeding experiments, scaled up to thousands of flies, began producing new mutants at an unprecedented rate. **Morgan's** group discovered that the very act of doing genetics with Drosophila *created new tools for doing more genetics*—a "breeder reactor" that generated an ever-expanding supply of genetic markers, mapping stocks, and experimental possibilities. This was not planned; Morgan initially saw flies as just one organism among many. The transition happened when the neo-Mendelian classification system (organizing genes by organ series) collapsed under the weight of new mutants in 1912, forcing the group to adopt chromosome mapping as their organizing principle—a system with "infinite capacity to accommodate as many mutant forms as the breeder reactor could produce."

### **Construction of Standard Mapping Technology (Chapter 3\)**

The transformation of wild, variable flies into precision instruments required intensive genetic engineering avant la lettre. Calvin Bridges emerged as the master craftsman of this process, creating "mapping stocks" with multiple genetic markers evenly spaced along chromosomes—reagents like "Xple" (for the X chromosome) that balanced elegance and practicality. Bridges systematically eliminated genetic "junk"—modifiers, suppressors, lethals—that caused variability, replacing naturally evolved genetic assemblages with carefully constructed ones optimized for consistent crossover frequencies. The construction of these standard stocks exemplifies what Kohler calls Drosophila's "second nature": creatures that "could only exist in the domestic ecology of the laboratory, serving its human symbionts' desire for quick, convenient, and cost-effective experimental production." The contrast between Bridges's pragmatic approach and Muller's pursuit of ever-more-complex twenty-marker stocks illuminates different engineering philosophies—"good enough" technology versus technical perfectionism.

### **The Moral Economy of the Fly Group (Chapters 4–5)**

The Columbia fly room—a cramped sixteen-by-twenty-three-foot space crammed with eight desks and hundreds of milk bottles—became the crucible for a distinctive research culture. Kohler reconstructs the group's internal dynamics: Morgan as the "Boss" who controlled Carnegie Institution funds but deferred to Bridges and Sturtevant on experimental matters; the tensions created when brilliant students remained perpetually "boys" dependent on their mentor for salaries; the gender dynamics that marginalized women after 1915 despite their early contributions. The system of free exchange of stocks and information—the "Drosophila exchange network"—expressed and enforced this moral economy. Stocks were shared freely, but unwritten rules governed proprietary claims: one could not work on problems that others had staked out; constructed stocks remained "personal" until the constructor released them; etiquette required disclosure of intentions before requesting materials. When the Swedish geneticist Gert Bonnier unwittingly violated these norms by publishing on problems Otto Mohr had claimed, Morgan delivered a sharp "paternal lecture" on cooperation. The system worked because everyone depended on access to tools that no single lab could maintain.

### **The Beadle-Ephrussi Transplantation Experiments (Chapter 7\)**

George Beadle and Boris Ephrussi's collaboration in the mid-1930s exemplifies the cross-disciplinary innovations required to escape the constraints built into the standard fly. Trained in classical genetics, Beadle was frustrated by the disconnect between chromosomal mechanics and developmental biology. Ephrussi brought embryological techniques from the Rothschild Institute in Paris. Together they invented a method of transplanting eye-color disks between larvae of different genetic backgrounds—a true hybrid of genetic and embryological practice. Their discovery that transplanted disks developed eye color according to a combination of host and donor genetics revealed diffusible "hormones" (later identified as chemical intermediates in pigment biosynthesis). This work pioneered what would become biochemical genetics. But Kohler shows the irony: the very success of the transplantation system pulled them away from developmental questions toward biochemistry, and when Beadle and Tatum shifted to *Neurospora* for mapping metabolic pathways, Drosophila's role in developmental genetics was again deferred. *Neurospora* displaced Drosophila in Beadle's lab in precisely the way Drosophila had displaced mice in Morgan's—by revealing unexpected capacities for mass production.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Experimental creatures are a special kind of technology in that they are altered environmentally or physically to do things that humans value but that they might not have done in nature... The extent of construction varies a good deal, however." (p. 6\)

"The Morgan group's mapping project... had outgrown small-scale, improvised academic funding. The impending breakup of the mapping team drove Morgan to accept outside support, with its uncertain moral obligations." (p. 107\)

"Laboratories of experimental biology are a distinctive kind of ecosystem, in which creatures live and evolve in symbiotic and commensal relations with humankind. They have natural histories, no less than fields and forests do, and the boundaries between lab and field are active places that may be traversed or occupied in a variety of imaginative ways." (p. 10\)

"The decisive advantage of the new system of naming and classifying genes by location was its infinite capacity to accommodate as many mutant forms as the breeder reactor could produce." (p. 60\)

"The fundamental distinction between tool and problem, so simple in principle, was in practice often hard to make. Trick stocks could be applied to various problems, but they were also objects of study in themselves, and tool could easily and unexpectedly become object." (p. 147\)

"It is useful to regard the construction of standard laboratory creatures as a special kind of domestication. The resemblance is obvious between standard laboratory creatures and the highly engineered, standard biological machines of large-scale agricultural technology." (p. 9\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Pairs well with:** Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life* (1979/1986) for its treatment of scientific work as a cycle of investment and credibility, though Kohler's production-oriented approach differs from their epistemological focus. Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (1985) provides the concept of "moral economy" in experimental communities that Kohler extends. Hans-Jörg Rheinberger's *Toward a History of Epistemic Things* (1997) offers a complementary analysis of how experimental systems constrain and enable knowledge production.

**Limitations:** The focus on the Morgan group and its networks means that peripheral practitioners, women scientists, and non-American drosophilists receive less attention—Kohler acknowledges setting aside a planned chapter on "underprivileged drosophilists." The emphasis on production processes, while analytically powerful, occasionally downplays the intellectual content of genetic discoveries that contemporaries found so compelling.

## **The Two Classification Systems**

**Neo-Mendelian "Organ Series" System (pre-1912)**

This was inherited from work on mice and fowl. Genes were organized by the *organ they affected*—so you'd have an "eye color series," a "body color series," a "wing series," etc. Each mutant was described by a formula showing which "factors" were present or absent.

The logic: if you have red eyes, white eyes, and vermilion eyes, you explain this by positing multiple factors (A, B, C...) where different combinations produce different colors. White might be the absence of A; vermilion the absence of B; and so on.

**The problem**: Every time a *new* mutant appeared in a series, you had to completely rebuild *all* the formulas for that entire series. The mathematics of "presence and absence" required that each formula include every factor in the series, whether present or absent. Adding a fifth eye-color mutant meant rewriting the formulas for all five.

Worse, the system predicted "phantom mutants"—mathematical combinations that *should* exist but never appeared, even in massive experiments designed to find them.

**Chromosome Mapping System (post-1912)**

Genes are organized by their *physical location* on chromosomes. Each gene gets a number representing its position (measured by crossover frequency from a reference point).

The key advantage: **adding new mutants changes nothing about existing entries**. You just slot the new gene into its location on the map. The system has "infinite capacity to accommodate as many mutant forms as the breeder reactor could produce."

## **The "Breeder Reactor" Effect**

This is Kohler's term for an unexpected autocatalytic phenomenon:

**What happened**: When Morgan scaled up Mendelian crosses from dozens to *thousands* of flies, new mutants started appearing at rates no one anticipated. Wild Drosophila populations harbored enormous hidden genetic variation—recessive mutations carried silently for generations—that only became visible through intensive inbreeding.

**The autocatalysis**: Each new mutant became a *tool* for further experiments. More mutants meant more markers for mapping, which meant more precise experiments, which meant more flies examined, which meant more new mutants discovered. The process fed itself.

**Why this matters**: Morgan didn't *plan* to become a Drosophila geneticist. In 1908-1910 he was working on dozens of organisms—mice, rats, pigeons, Phylloxera, marine invertebrates. Drosophila was just one creature among many. But once the breeder reactor effect kicked in, Drosophila became qualitatively different from other organisms. It was producing tools faster than Morgan's group could use them.

## **The 1912 Crisis**

The collision happened in spring 1912\. Morgan was "head over ears" in flies, confident he was about to produce *definitive* formulas for the eye-color series. Then two more eye-color mutants (purple and maroon) appeared, and the whole edifice collapsed:

"Each fresh appearance required that the entire edifice be rebuilt. The system finally became so unwieldy that it had to be abandoned in toto."

The organ-series system had worked fine for mice and fowl, which produced new mutants rarely if ever. It couldn't survive contact with Drosophila's breeder reactor.

## **The Historiographic Point**

This is a case where **the organism forced conceptual change**. The shift from organ-series to chromosome mapping wasn't primarily driven by new theoretical insights—it was driven by the *practical impossibility* of doing genetics with the old system once Drosophila started producing mutants faster than formulas could be rebuilt.

It's a beautiful example of Kohler's larger argument: the material culture of experimental production shapes the intellectual content of science in ways that internalist histories of ideas miss entirely.

# **Dan Healey, *The Gulag Doctors: Life, Death, and Medicine in Stalin's Labour Camps* (Yale University Press, 2024\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Healey argues that medical care in Stalin's Gulag constituted a crucial "grey zone" where two fundamentally incompatible forms of power collided: the penal authority of camp commandants demanding maximum labor extraction from prisoners, and the medical knowledge of doctors, nurses, and feldshers attempting to heal individual patients. This "dual loyalty"—to professional medical discipline and to penal supervisors—was "the essence of Gulag medicine." Rather than dismissing camp medicine as purely instrumental or malign, Healey contends that understanding the biographies of Gulag medical workers reveals how some individuals navigated this tension, occasionally achieving humane outcomes within a system designed for exploitation and death.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Healey directly challenges Alexander Solzhenitsyn's influential dismissal of Gulag medicine as "born of the devil and filled with the devil's blood." For generations, Solzhenitsyn's *Gulag Archipelago* cast a "long shadow" over scholarly engagement with camp medical services, leading historians to either ignore this topic or approach it with unease, finding it "confusing and contradictory." Healey also critiques the tendency of archival historians like Boris Nakhapetov and Golfo Alexopoulos to mine central Sanotdel (Sanitary Department) records for institutional facts while "skirting uneasily past the quandary of its historical meaning."

The book intervenes in three historiographic conversations. First, it challenges the metropolitan Russian narrative of the Gulag as purely a producer of "roads to nowhere" and destroyer of human life by attending to regional commemoration in Gulag-built cities like Ukhta, Pechora, and Magadan, where local museums present more complex stories of construction alongside destruction. Second, Healey engages with scholarship on Soviet medicine (drawing on Susan Grant, Tricia Starks, and Paula Michaels) to situate Gulag healthcare within broader patterns of "embedded" or "departmental" medical systems that privileged certain populations over others. Third, the book contributes to the "memory studies" turn in Gulag historiography by examining how local historians, Memorial activists, and museum curators have processed the Stalinist experience through narratives of medical heroism.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Healey deploys Foucauldian concepts of "sovereign power" (the penal authority to take life or let it continue) and "disciplinary power" (medical knowledge's capacity to save and sustain lives) to theorize the fundamental tension animating Gulag medicine. He also draws on the concept of "grey zones" from Holocaust scholarship to describe spaces where categories of perpetrator and victim became blurred. The book employs biographical method as a deliberate analytical strategy, using individual life histories to illuminate systemic patterns while attending to agency and contingency within totalitarian structures.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Healey combines central archival records from the State Archive of the Russian Federation (GARF) with extensive regional museum collections from Ukhta's Museum of the History of Public Health, the Pechora Historical-Regional Museum, and the Magadan Province Regional Museum. He conducted interviews with Gulag survivors, free medical workers, and descendants of prisoner-doctors between 2009 and 2012\. The memoir sources include both prisoner-doctors' reminiscences and the rarer narratives of "freely hired" Gulag medical employees. Geographic scope spans northern European Russia (Komi Republic), Siberia (Norilsk), and the Russian Far East (Kolyma/Magadan), covering the period from the Gulag's formal establishment in 1930 through Stalin's death in 1953\.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Lev Sokolovsky and the Simulation of Mental Illness**: Perhaps the book's most revealing case study centers on prisoner-psychiatrist Lev Grigorevich Sokolovsky, who in 1944 wrote an extraordinary handwritten research paper "for service use only" on prisoners simulating psychoses and neuroses. The paper, presented at a medical conference at Vetlosian Central Camp Hospital, survives in Moscow's central Gulag Sanotdel archives. Unlike the ideologically conformist, production-focused research typical of Gulag doctors, Sokolovsky's study demonstrated remarkable "humanity and truthfulness." His case histories reveal how prisoners driven to "unbearable situations" by camp conditions developed genuine mental illness while appearing to malinger. The case of a Chinese prisoner "L.F.S." who could not answer simple questions about his origins exemplifies how Sokolovsky distinguished authentic psychiatric breakdown from simulation, recognizing that the Gulag itself produced the conditions that drove prisoners mad. Sokolovsky's military psychiatric background and Jewish intelligentsia formation enabled him to maintain professional standards and even embed implicit critiques of the labor-camp system within ostensibly technical medical documentation.

**Eufrosinia Kersnovskaya in the Norilsk Morgue**: Kersnovskaya, a Moldovan woman from a dispossessed Bessarabian family, served as a self-taught anatomist in the morgue of Norilsk's Central Camp Hospital from 1946-1947, conducting 1,640 autopsies. Her extraordinary illustrated memoir, completed in the 1960s, provides unique insight into the "place where Death delights in helping Life." Kersnovskaya's perspective reveals the moral universe that prisoner-doctors navigated: she witnessed the surgeon Viktor Kuznetsov conducting experimental rectal surgeries on emaciated prisoners ("a man lacking all human morality") while also learning diagnostic skills from the Estonian prisoner-doctor Leonard Mardna, who had studied at the Sorbonne. Her refusal to falsify death certificates when doctors made fatal errors brought her into conflict with hospital authorities. The autopsy practice itself embodied the paradox of Gulag medicine: a scientific procedure designed to improve medical knowledge conducted on bodies destroyed by a system of deliberate malnutrition and overwork.

**Yakov Kaminsky and the Radium-Water Spa**: Ukrainian-Jewish radiologist Yakov Kaminsky, arrested in 1937 during the Great Terror, became chief doctor of Vetlosian Central Camp Hospital and dreamed of establishing a "Northern Resort" using the radioactive waters near Ukhta. The Gulag had secretly established a radium-water bathing facility at Vodny in the 1930s, where both prisoners and free staff received therapeutic treatments for conditions ranging from rheumatism to "neurasthenia." Kaminsky's 1944 handwritten pamphlet advocating for expansion of this facility reveals the strange intersection of Soviet resort medicine (kurortologiia), atomic-age enthusiasm for radiation, and Gulag exploitation. Prisoner-workers in the radium distillation factory served as unwitting experimental subjects, monitored through "dispensarisation" while receiving minimal safety protections. The case illuminates how prisoner-doctors could simultaneously advocate for therapeutic innovations and remain complicit in systematic poisoning of a workforce—the "poisoning of a community thanks to Gulag planners' negligence and the weakness of experts in the face of supreme power."

**Nina Savoeva and Sofia Guzikova: Freely Hired Doctors**: These two young women represent the thousands of medical school graduates assigned to Gulag service through the annual graduate commission system. Savoeva's memoir *I Chose Kolyma* (1996) presents her decision in romantic terms, echoing the intelligentsia tradition of service in remote locations depicted in Chekhov's medical stories and Bulgakov's *A Doctor's Notebook*. Yet Healey's analysis reveals how such narratives serve as retrospective "shielding" strategies, masking the degree of complicity with a repressive system. The Gulag recruited 5,359 new doctors between 1939 and 1952 through this system. These freely hired doctors, often young women from "new" Soviet backgrounds with crash-course Stalinist medical training, frequently supervised older, more experienced prisoner-physicians, creating an inverted hierarchy where penal status trumped professional expertise. Their memoirs demonstrate strategies of self-presentation that emphasize naïveté, youth, and circumstances beyond their control—what Healey calls being "playthings of circumstance."

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The Gulag's medical personnel answered to two types of authority, each expressing objectives for this penal medical system that were paradoxical, contradictory, and challenging to reconcile. This dual loyalty was the essence of Gulag medicine." (p. 29\)

"In the medical 'grey zone' the penal authority to take life or let life continue—Michel Foucault's 'sovereign power'—confronted the power of medical knowledge—Foucault's 'disciplinary power'—to save and sustain lives. That confrontation, which is the essence of Gulag medicine, animates the chapters that follow." (p. 53\)

"The Gulag hospital could be a place of healing, compassion, and learning, while often simultaneously a portal to death after penal malnutrition, exhaustion, and systemic neglect. How can we comprehend the meaning of medical care in the forced-labour camps? It was a troubling, and extremely differentiated, feature of the Gulag's organisation and of prisoners' experience, encompassing darkness and light." (p. 270\)

"Historians should not dismiss medical care in the Gulag as 'born of the devil', as Solzhenitsyn did; nor should they mine the archive of the Sanotdel for facts but skirt uneasily past the quandary of its historical meaning as too sensitive, or too confusing, to address." (p. 52\)

"Medical care for Stalin's Gulag \[...\] Readers should keep the estimated death toll in their minds as they read these stories of prisoner lives 'saved' and conscientious medical duties fulfilled." (p. 5\)

"The 'medical knowledge' that was given some authority by Gulag bosses was intended to be Soviet, collectivist, and production-focused: it diverged substantially from the Hippocratic values that some doctors still understood and tried to honour in their Gulag healing." (p. 53\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Paired Works**: This book pairs productively with Golfo Alexopoulos's *Illness and Inhumanity in Stalin's Gulag* (2017), which examines Gulag medicine from the perspective of central institutional archives and the concept of "hygienic violence." Where Alexopoulos emphasizes systemic dehumanization, Healey foregrounds individual agency and the possibilities for humane practice within repressive structures. The book also connects to Nikolai Krementsov's *Stalinist Science* (1997) through its attention to how scientific knowledge production operated under totalitarian constraints, and to Paul Erickson et al.'s *How Reason Almost Lost Its Mind* (2013) through its analysis of expertise deployed within Cold War-era state systems.

**Limitations**: The book's reliance on survivor memoirs and regional museum collections, while enabling rich biographical analysis, means that the perspectives of the millions who died without leaving testimony remain largely inaccessible. Healey acknowledges this limitation but cannot fully overcome it. Additionally, the focus on medical workers who achieved some professional distinction within the system may inadvertently reproduce a narrative of exceptional individuals rather than capturing the more typical experience of anonymous suffering and medical neglect that characterized most prisoners' encounters with Gulag healthcare.

# **Zehra Hashmi, "Making Reliable Persons: Managing Descent and Genealogical Computation in Pakistan" (2021)**

*Comparative Studies in Society and History* 63, no. 4 (2021): 948–978.

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Hashmi argues that Pakistan's biometric national identity database (NADRA) embeds colonial-era genealogical logics within its verification procedures, producing differential outcomes for citizens based on historically uneven governance practices. The capacity to prove one's identity—and thus be recognized as a "reliable person"—depends not on biometric data alone but on one's ability to provide documentary evidence of genealogical descent rooted in specific territorial configurations, a capacity that colonial governance patterns systematically distributed unevenly between "settled" and "tribal" areas.

---

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Hashmi intervenes in multiple scholarly conversations simultaneously. Against scholarship that treats kinship as a residual "traditional" category superseded by modern state formations (drawing from nineteenth-century theorists like Maine, Tönnies, and Spencer), she demonstrates how genealogical reckoning remains central to contemporary governance—not as a holdover but as something actively embedded in state technologies. She engages critically with biometrics scholarship (Gates, Magnet, Abraham) that centers the body as evidentiary truth, showing that even within one of the world's largest biometric databases, bodily authentication proves insufficient for establishing citizenship; descent-based documentary evidence remains essential.

Drawing on kinship studies' turn away from biological determinism (Schneider, Franklin and McKinnon), Hashmi shows how genealogical "computation"—the practice of calculating kin ties to produce equivalences—extends beyond biological descent into domains of status, reputation, and political legitimacy. She builds on South Asian scholarship regarding colonial documentation (Matthew Hull on documents in Pakistan, Neeladri Bhattacharya on agrarian governance) to trace how colonial revenue administration produced genealogical records (the *shajarah-yi-nasab*) that now directly condition postcolonial citizenship verification. Her comparison between settled and tribal areas draws on frontier studies (Sana Haroon, Benjamin Hopkins) to argue that colonial "sovereign pluralism" created durable administrative distinctions whose effects persist into the present.

---

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Hashmi mobilizes a framework that bridges kinship studies, science and technology studies (particularly infrastructure studies), and postcolonial governance studies. Her central analytic concept—"genealogical computation"—describes the practices through which kin connections are calculated, documented, and deployed to establish identity and status. She treats the *shajarah-yi-nasab* as what she terms a "scaffolding" document that seeks to officialize inherently slippery kinship relations. The concept of "reliable persons" captures how genealogical success or failure maps onto trustworthiness in bureaucratic encounters.

---

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

The article combines ethnographic fieldwork at NADRA registration centers in Islamabad (including observation of registration encounters and interviews with citizens undergoing re-verification) with historical analysis of colonial administrative documents, particularly Baden-Powell's *Land Systems of British India* (1892), Tupper's *Punjab Customary Law* (1881), and India Office Records. Hashmi also uses Asad Muhammad Khan's Urdu short story "Ma'i Dada" (1982) as a literary-ethnographic entry point for understanding genealogical practice. The temporal scope spans from colonial revenue administration through the 2016 NADRA re-verification campaign; the geographic focus is Punjab, the Peshawar Valley, and the Federally Administered Tribal Areas.

---

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Ma'i Dada and the Labor of Genealogical Maintenance**

Hashmi opens with Asad Muhammad Khan's short story depicting Ma'i Dada, a servant in a diasporic Pashtun household in colonial India who serves as the family's genealogical expert. Though officially registered in "police papers, ration cards, state hospital records" as Abdul Majid Khan Yusufzai—a Pashtun Muslim identity—neighborhood rumors insist he is actually from a low Hindu caste of *telis* (oil pressers). Ma'i Dada supervises children copying genealogical tables, a "torment" described as "mandatory as circumcision." The story illustrates several key analytical points: genealogical knowledge requires precision and embodied practice; expertise often resides in insider-outsider figures whose own status is precarious; and maintaining genealogical integrity involves constant vigilance against "slippage" that could transform a grandfather into an uncle or make "a grandparent of the purest pedigree into a bastard." Ma'i Dada's simultaneously secure insider status and contested origins demonstrates how genealogical computation is as much about exclusion as belonging.

**Gulnar Bibi: Genealogical Success in a Settled Area**

Gulnar Bibi, a Pashtun woman living in an Islamabad informal settlement, receives notice that her NADRA card has been "blocked" pending citizenship re-verification. During her verification interview, NADRA officials request documentary evidence including the *shajarah-yi-nasab* linking her to ancestral land. Gulnar cannot obtain this record (as a woman, she was excluded from inheritance), but she successfully deploys multiple alternative genealogical strategies. First, she declares herself Sayyid—a descendant of the Prophet Muhammad—within the interview, attempting to establish herself as an unquestionable, reliable identity through religious genealogical computation. Second, she travels to Peshawar, obtains a property deed from relatives who had purchased her grandfather's house, and secures letters from the deputy superintendent of police and the local union councilor by invoking personal connections spanning generations: "I do not know you, but my father knew your senior Akhtar Gul." Her success demonstrates that governmental verification systems rely on both documentary infrastructure and social relations necessary to produce documents. NADRA officials treat her ability to navigate these networks as itself evidence of authentication. Genealogical computation thus extends beyond the strictly textual into networks of reputation and memory.

**Hassan and His Father: Genealogical Failure from the Tribal Areas**

Hassan, a young man from the Mohmand tribal agency whose identity card application has been repeatedly rejected, arrives at the NADRA registration center with his elderly father. The father immediately begins reciting his lineage: "He is a Musa, from the line of...," narrating patrilineal ancestors stretching back generations. When asked for documentary proof, the father explains they have "plenty of land" in Mohmand, but it is jointly owned with no record of transfer through generations—because the tribal areas were never subject to colonial revenue settlement. He insists: "I could go to Mohmand and ask anyone who \[Musakhels\] were, and they would tell me." The father emphasizes that "Musakhel is a place but it is also who we are," simultaneously making a genealogical claim and territorial affiliation.

Yet this oral genealogical computation, however deep and detailed, fails to meet NADRA's evidentiary requirements. Colonial governance treated frontier tribes not through revenue administration and property documentation but through political officers managing tribes as security concerns. The same genealogical knowledge that functioned effectively within colonial frontier governance—establishing reputation, alliance, and political stakes—cannot be translated into the documentary forms NADRA requires. Hassan's "failure" reveals how the postcolonial state inherited and now perpetuates colonial administrative fractures. The dissonance is not a misunderstanding but a structural effect of historically constituted power differentials that continue to exclude tribal populations from full citizenship recognition.

**Colonial Revenue Settlement and the Shajarah-yi-nasab**

Hashmi traces how the colonial state in Punjab deployed the *shajarah-yi-nasab* for dual purposes: determining who owed revenue on assessed land and revealing what officials considered the "authentic" form of village India organized through kinship. Baden-Powell described it as a document of "utmost importance" that showed "the brief history and constitution and form of the village, and how it is divided." The genealogical table mapped lineage branches onto land divisions, producing a mimetic relation between family tree and territorial organization. This documentary technology was directed at managing property relations and extracting revenue—concerns largely absent in the frontier tribal areas, which were non-agrarian and considered a potential financial "burden." On the frontier, colonial governance instead deployed tribal genealogy for security purposes: managing tribes as political entities, assessing "fighting strength," and maintaining a buffer zone against Russian and Afghan interests. This differential use of genealogical knowledge produced divergent documentary regimes whose effects persist into NADRA's contemporary identification requirements.

---

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The capacity to verify an identity and present oneself as a reliable and trustworthy person—especially in order to meet the expectations of an ethno-securitized state—continues to hinge, even in the age of biometric technology, on genealogical success or tragedy: an outcome dependent on the ability to produce evidence of genealogical claims." (p. 949\)

"Genealogical computation is the practice of 'calculating' kin ties to produce a relation. For instance, the calculation 'my mother's brother is my maternal uncle' is seemingly simple but requires the production of an equivalence that we normalize because we do it all the time." (p. 950\)

"Genealogical records serve a scaffolding function and seek to officialize that which is often slippery and contradictory in the realm of practice. Governments, especially colonial states seeking legitimation, thus lean on genealogical sources for the purpose of tracking, governing, and distinguishing between heterogeneous groups." (p. 955\)

"From this document we can at once tell the brief history and constitution and form of the village, and how it is divided; its total revenue; the area of each co-sharer's holding.... This document must be seen to be appreciated." —Baden-Powell on the *shajarah-yi-nasab* (p. 956\)

"Musakhel is a place but it is also who we are." —Hassan's father (p. 967\)

"In the absence of documents that could authenticate genealogical ties—authorized or even produced by the state—Hassan's father was unable to 'show the work' of genealogical computation, not because it did not exist but because it did not match the requisite form." (p. 968\)

---

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

**Pairings**: This article pairs exceptionally well with Theodore Porter's *Trust in Numbers* (1995), which examines how quantification and standardized documentation substitute for trust and personal knowledge in governance—Hashmi shows the inverse, where documentary failures force reliance on personal networks. Geoffrey Bowker and Susan Leigh Star's *Sorting Things Out* (1999) provides a complementary framework for understanding how classification systems (here, citizen/alien) produce structural consequences that map onto prior inequalities. Matthew Hull's work on documents in Pakistan bureaucracy offers essential context for the material culture of Pakistani governance. Sarah Igo's *The Averaged American* (2007) similarly traces how identification technologies construct rather than simply register citizen identities.

**Limitations**: The article's ethnographic evidence, while richly detailed, draws from a limited number of cases (primarily Gulnar Bibi and Hassan) that may not capture the full range of verification experiences. The focus on Pashtun populations, while analytically productive, leaves unexplored how other ethnic groups (Bengalis, Burmese-Rohingya mentioned briefly) experience parallel processes of exclusion.

# **Gregg Mitman, *Empire of Rubber: Firestone's Scramble for Land and Power in Liberia* (The New Press, 2021\)**

---

### **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Mitman argues that the Firestone Tire and Rubber Company's establishment of a vast rubber plantation in Liberia beginning in 1926 constituted a form of American corporate empire that replicated and extended structures of racial capitalism, land dispossession, and plantation violence rooted in the Atlantic slave trade. Rather than delivering on its promises of development and modernization, Firestone imported Jim Crow segregation to a sovereign Black republic, wielded American science and medicine as instruments of corporate power, and reshaped ecological relationships between land, labor, and nature in ways that concentrated wealth among American industrialists and Liberian elites while impoverishing the indigenous majority. The plantation—in all its historical meanings as colonial settlement, extractive agricultural system, and site of racialized labor exploitation—is the central organizing concept through which Mitman traces how the deep structures of racial capitalism persisted from the antebellum South through twentieth-century corporate globalization.

### **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Empire of Rubber* intervenes in multiple overlapping historiographic conversations. Most fundamentally, Mitman challenges narratives that treat American corporate expansion abroad as distinct from the domestic history of racial capitalism and plantation slavery. By showing how Firestone literally transplanted Jim Crow labor practices, residential segregation, and racialized medical experimentation from Akron, Ohio, to Harbel, Liberia, Mitman collapses the analytical boundary between American domestic racial history and the history of American empire. He positions his work against celebratory narratives of corporate development and benevolent modernization, demonstrating that the rhetoric of scientific progress and humanitarian uplift served as ideological cover for extractive accumulation. The book also intervenes in the historiography of Pan-Africanism and Black internationalism by revealing the agonizing contradictions facing figures like W.E.B. Du Bois and diplomat Lester Walton, who were caught between defending Liberia's sovereignty as a Black republic and recognizing the exploitative character of American corporate investment. Mitman draws on and extends the "Plantationocene" framework emerging from environmental humanities, treating the plantation not merely as an agricultural system but as an ecology that reconfigured relationships among humans, plants, parasites, chemicals, and machines. He is in dialogue with scholars of racial capitalism (Cedric Robinson, Sven Beckert), commodity histories (Sidney Mintz), histories of American empire (Daniel Immerwahr, Julie Livingston), and environmental history of extractive economies. The book also responds to the corporate history tradition by centering Liberian agency, resistance, and ecological knowledge against the typically one-sided narrative of American industrial triumph.

### **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Mitman deploys a framework combining environmental history, racial capitalism, and the history of science and medicine, organized around the concept of "industrial ecology"—the totality of relationships among people, land, organisms, chemicals, and capital that the plantation system produced. His key analytical move is treating the plantation as a transhistorical form whose successive iterations (colonial settlement, slave agriculture, corporate monoculture) share structural features of land dispossession, racialized labor coercion, and ecological transformation, even as the specific technologies and justifications shift.

### **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Denied access to the Firestone company archives (removed from the University of Akron after a 2005 labor rights lawsuit), Mitman assembled an archival mosaic from U.S. State Department records, Liberian government documents at the Center for National Documents and Records Agency, the papers of W.E.B. Du Bois, Claude Barnett's Associated Negro Press archives, Harvard University expedition records, oral histories with former plantation workers and their descendants, Liberian and American newspapers (including the Black press), diplomatic correspondence, promotional films, and photographs from the Loring Whitman collection. The book spans from Liberia's founding in the 1820s through the 2018 Land Rights Act, with primary focus on the period from 1924 to the 1960s. Mitman also conducted extensive fieldwork in Liberia over eight years, including a documentary film project.

### **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Du Bois–Ross–Hood reconnaissance at Mount Barclay Plantation (January 1924).** When W.E.B. Du Bois, serving as President Coolidge's envoy extraordinary to Liberia, visited an abandoned British rubber plantation on Monrovia's outskirts with Firestone scout Donald Ross and U.S. minister Solomon Porter Hood, three men stood in the same landscape and imagined radically different futures. Ross saw cheap land, cheap labor, and favorable growing conditions for a corporate rubber empire to rival British holdings in Southeast Asia. Du Bois and Hood saw Firestone's potential capital investment as a vehicle for Black self-determination—American money channeled through Black expertise to build the economic foundation of an independent African republic. This scene crystallizes the book's central tension: competing visions of development that temporarily aligned the interests of white capital and Black self-determination, masking the fundamental incompatibility of corporate extraction and genuine sovereignty. Mitman notes that Hood, who first alerted Firestone to Liberia's potential, was never credited by the company for the idea that would generate enormous Firestone family wealth.

**The forced labor scandal and League of Nations investigation (1928–1934).** Mitman traces the intricate political maneuvering surrounding revelations that Liberian government officials were complicit in a system of coerced labor export, particularly the shipment of indigenous workers to Spanish cocoa plantations on Fernando Po. U.S. diplomat Henry Francis spent nine months undercover gathering evidence that officials were making fortunes from what amounted to organized slave trafficking. The ensuing League of Nations investigation forced President King's resignation but also became a vehicle through which Firestone and the U.S. State Department attempted to transform Liberia into an American protectorate—installing an American "chief adviser" with sweeping powers over Liberian governance. Mitman reveals the cynical calculation at work: Firestone needed to distance itself from forced labor allegations while simultaneously exploiting them to gain greater control over Liberian affairs. President Edwin Barclay's skillful resistance—ultimately outmaneuvering both Firestone and the League of Nations to preserve Liberian sovereignty while renegotiating more favorable concession terms—demonstrates that Liberian political agency operated within but was not wholly determined by the structural constraints of racial capitalism.

**Medical experimentation and the Liberian Institute for Biomedical Research.** Mitman documents a disturbing pattern of racialized medical experimentation extending from the plantations' founding through the postwar period. Harvard tropical medicine professor Richard P. Strong—who had previously killed thirteen Filipino prisoners in an unauthorized cholera vaccine trial in 1906—led a 1926 expedition to Liberia that combined genuine scientific survey work with explicit service to Firestone's corporate interests. The $250,000 Firestone-funded Liberian Institute for Biomedical Research, established in 1952 as a memorial to Harvey Firestone Sr., became an enclave of white scientists who conducted experiments on indigenous workers and surrounding communities without meaningful consent. Parasitologist R.S. Bray intentionally infected thirty Liberian staff and residents with *Plasmodium vivax*, a malaria strain not present in Liberia, to test whether "Negroes" were susceptible. Workers were also enrolled in trials of experimental arsenic compounds for sleeping sickness. Mitman frames these experiments as continuous with the logic of plantation slavery: Black bodies as raw material for both commodity extraction and knowledge production, with scientific "beneficence" serving as moral cover for exploitation.

**The industrial ecology of the plantation and its health consequences.** Mitman's environmental history analysis is most vivid in his account of how Firestone's monoculture landscape created new disease ecologies. The clearing of land and concentration of human labor created ideal habitat for the black fly *Simulium yahense*, vector for the parasitic worm *Onchocerca volvulus* that causes river blindness. Along the Du River divisions—where the Harvard expedition originally camped—onchocerciasis infection rates among male laborers eventually reached 80 percent. Chemical exposures compounded biological hazards: tappers handled ammonia without protective equipment, destroying fingertips and nails and sometimes causing blindness. The herbicide 2,4,5-T (containing dioxin) was used to clear old rubber stands, and the carcinogenic fungicide captafol was smeared by hand onto tapping panels. For decades, the Farmington River served as a dumping ground for industrial waste, contaminating water used by nearby communities for bathing, washing, and fishing. A wastewater treatment plant was not built until 2008—nearly seventy years after the processing factory began operations. Mitman uses these interconnected environmental and health harms to demonstrate that the "natural" in natural rubber was anything but, and that the risks of industrial production were systematically distributed along racial lines.

### **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"This book is a history of America through the lens of Firestone in Liberia. It is the story of how a behemoth American company on a nationalist mission of profit, robed in beneficence, negotiated, maneuvered, and bullied its way into what was then one of only two sovereign Black nations on the African continent." (p. xiii)

"Could the plantation free itself from the violence of land dispossession, racial exploitation, and unfree labor that shaped its history in the Atlantic world? Could American capital be trusted to respect the rights of a sovereign Black nation and its people in an imperial and colonial world?" (p. xiii)

"The first act in the making of a plantation world is always land dispossession." (p. xii)

"In the calculus of worker's compensation, Firestone disclosed the value it placed on the lives of Black laborers in the Jim Crow enclave it had imported to Liberia. It was a valuation founded upon structures of racial capitalism rooted in the violent and bloody soil of plantation slavery." (p. 208\)

"We have found the Negro particularly adapted to handling our raw materials." —Harvey Firestone Sr. (p. 19\)

"Land is life in Liberia. Access to it will not be, nor has it ever been, surrendered easily." (p. 247\)

### **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*Empire of Rubber* pairs productively with Sven Beckert's *Empire of Cotton* as a parallel study of how a single global commodity organized systems of land, labor, and capital across continents, though Mitman's focus on American corporate empire in Africa and his centering of Black political agency distinguish his approach. Cronon's *Nature's Metropolis* offers a complementary framework for understanding how commodity networks reshape hinterland ecologies, while Sugrue's *Origins of the Urban Crisis* illuminates the domestic racial capitalism that Firestone exported to Liberia. The book also connects to the history of science list through its treatment of medical experimentation and the co-production of scientific authority and corporate power, resonating with Jasanoff's work on science and the state.

One limitation is that the Firestone company archives remain inaccessible, meaning that internal corporate decision-making must be reconstructed indirectly through diplomatic correspondence, public records, and secondhand accounts. Additionally, while Mitman's narrative is rich with individual Liberian voices—particularly from oral histories with former plantation workers—the book's chronological structure and its necessary focus on elite diplomatic maneuvering sometimes constrains the depth of attention given to indigenous communities' own economic systems and forms of resistance in the pre-Firestone and early concession periods.

# **Hans-Jörg Rheinberger, *Toward a History of Epistemic Things: Synthesizing Proteins in the Test Tube* (1997)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Rheinberger argues that the fundamental units of modern scientific research are not theories, disciplines, or institutions, but *experimental systems*—hybrid material arrangements within which "epistemic things" (the objects of scientific inquiry) and "technical things" (the stabilized conditions of their investigation) are inextricably entangled. Through a microscopic reconstruction of Paul Zamecnik's in vitro protein synthesis system at Massachusetts General Hospital (1947–1962), he demonstrates that scientific novelty emerges not from the rational application of theory but from the *differential reproduction* of experimental systems—a process in which the iterative recombination of material elements generates unprecedented events that cannot be anticipated by the researchers themselves.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Rheinberger positions his work as a "post-Kuhnian" departure from theory-centered accounts of scientific change. Where Kuhn's paradigm model privileged conceptual revolutions and the replacement of one theoretical framework by another, Rheinberger insists that the relevant unit of analysis lies below the level of theory, discipline, or paradigm: it is the experimental system itself, understood as a materially constituted "machine for making the future." He explicitly rejects the positivistic vocabulary of "discovery" in favor of "unprecedented events," and he refuses the language of "anomaly" and "paradigm shift" in favor of "conjunctures" and "bifurcations"—terms that capture the nonlinear, nonteleological dynamics of laboratory research. The book is in direct dialogue with Bruno Latour's actor-network theory (particularly the concept of "immutable mobiles" and the insistence on following things rather than theories), with the sociology of scientific knowledge tradition of Harry Collins and the Bath school, and with Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life*. But Rheinberger also draws on a Continental philosophical lineage that distinguishes him from anglophone STS: Gaston Bachelard's historical epistemology of the "scientific real," Derrida's concepts of *différance* and iterability, Heidegger's account of modern research as "opening a sphere" and "establishing a procedure," and Lacan's notion of the subject "sutured" to its object. Against both naive realism and strong social constructivism, Rheinberger proposes that experimental systems are semiotic-material arrangements in which "traces" generate further traces without a stable referent—a position he characterizes as neither platonistic approximation nor blunt social construction, but "constrained" construction governed by material resistance. His intervention also challenges triumphalist narratives of molecular biology's emergence, arguing against the "myth" that it sprang from a few heroic research schools (the phage group, the Cavendish crew, the Pasteur équipe) and instead showing how it arose from "a multiplicity of widely scattered, differently embedded, and loosely connected" experimental systems.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Rheinberger develops a distinctive epistemological vocabulary: *experimental systems* as the working units of research, composed of *epistemic things* (vaguely defined objects of inquiry) and *technical things* (stabilized instruments and conditions); *differential reproduction* (drawn from evolutionary thinking and Derrida's *différance*) as the mechanism of experimental change; *spaces of representation* as the material-semiotic coordinates within which graphematic traces are produced and concatenated; and *conjunctures* and *bifurcations* as the nodes where experimental systems link into broader *experimental cultures*. His approach is self-described as "pragmatogonic"—focused on the material coming-into-being of scientific objects rather than on theoretical frameworks or social interests.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Rheinberger draws on published scientific papers, laboratory notebooks (particularly Zamecnik's), personal recollections and interviews with surviving participants (Zamecnik, Loftfield, Hoagland, Littlefield), archival materials from MGH and the Huntington Laboratories, grant applications, and committee minutes. The temporal scope covers 1947–1962 at a single institutional site (Huntington Memorial Hospital at MGH, Boston), though the later chapters expand to trace conjunctures with the Pasteur Institute, Cambridge, and other laboratories.
