The book alternates between "reflective" theoretical chapters and "narrative" historical chapters—a deliberate formal strategy meant to mirror the recursive relationship between epistemology and case study.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**From Cancer Research to Protein Synthesis (1947–1952).** The book's opening historical chapters trace how the in vitro protein synthesis system emerged not from a deliberate program in molecular biology but from cancer research. Zamecnik, a Harvard-trained oncologist working in Joseph Aub's cancer research laboratory, initially sought to identify "the most promising point at which to begin a search for a metabolic distinction between normal and neoplastic tissue." Because malignant cells displayed deregulated growth tied to protein production, Zamecnik began studying protein metabolism using newly available radioactive ¹⁴C-labeled amino acids as tracers. The radioactive amino acids themselves arrived through a structural coupling between Cold War atomic energy funding (AEC grants), wartime cyclotron technology at MIT, and the organic chemistry expertise of Robert Loftfield. The experimental system was assembled from this convergence of political opportunity, institutional collaboration, and craft skill—not from theoretical foresight. By 1952–53, cancer had shifted from being the research target to becoming what Rheinberger calls a "background screen" providing institutional legitimacy, while the protein synthesis system had acquired its own experimental momentum. As Loftfield recalled: "In 1950 protein synthesis was important to us because of its possible impact in cancer. Around 1953 cancer was important to us because it provided the biological systems to study protein synthesis."

**The Emergence of Soluble RNA (1955–1958).** This is the book's pivotal case study and its most detailed illustration of an "unprecedented event." In November 1955, Zamecnik was investigating the activation of amino acids by ATP and enzymes. Following what Rheinberger calls a "symmetry principle" of experimental reasoning—testing all possible combinations within a system—Zamecnik ran a parallel experiment in which he incubated radioactive leucine (rather than radioactive ATP) with RNA fractions. The result was "totally unexpected": the radioactive amino acid became attached to the RNA itself. This was not the result of a theoretical prediction but of a material practice of systematic variation. For months, the group had noticed small amounts of RNA in their enzyme supernatant fraction and dismissed it as contamination. At an Oak Ridge meeting earlier that year, Erwin Chargaff had asked Zamecnik whether this supernatant RNA might play a functional role; Zamecnik acknowledged the possibility but "forgot about Chargaff's suggestion." It was only through the symmetry principle of the experiment—not through the conceptual framework of the information metaphor—that soluble RNA (later transfer RNA) materialized as an epistemic thing. Rheinberger emphasizes that the language of "code" and "information" was entirely absent from Zamecnik's vernacular in 1954–55 and entered the discourse only retrospectively, through supplementation rather than governance.

**Zamecnik and Watson's Non-Meeting of Minds (1954).** In a striking vignette drawn from a 1979 retrospective account, Zamecnik describes meeting James Watson in 1954\. Zamecnik had not even heard of the DNA double helix model. Watson came to his lab, Zamecnik showed him the in vitro protein synthesis evidence and asked whether RNA might fit into a groove in Watson's DNA model. "Watson just startled, shrugged his shoulders, lifted his hands, and went off to look at birds." Rheinberger uses this episode to demonstrate three things: the DNA double helix had no immediate impact on biochemical thinking about protein synthesis; the possibility of in vitro protein synthesis had no impact on Watson's thinking about information transfer; and twenty-five years later, Zamecnik inadvertently recast the 1954 conversation in the language of "message" and "code" that was completely absent from his vocabulary at the time. The case illustrates how experimental systems belonging to different "experimental cultures" can coexist without mutual intelligibility, and how retrospective narration imposes teleological coherence on contingent processes.

**The Genetic Code and Bacterial In Vitro Systems (1960–1962).** The book's final historical chapters trace how the rat liver protein synthesis system, having been "stabilized to such a degree that it could serve as a technical package, or kit," was displaced by a bacterial (E. coli) in vitro system that proved more amenable to "minimal representation"—the strategy required for code-breaking. Nirenberg and Matthaei's 1961 experiment, in which synthetic polyuridylic acid directed the synthesis of polyphenylalanine, cracked the genetic code using an experimental system that descended from but fundamentally differed from Zamecnik's. The transition required that microsomes lose their presumed "intrinsic template specificity" and be reconceived as "simple machines for assembling amino acids to form proteins of any kind"—a conceptual shift that met deep resistance from researchers embedded in the eukaryotic experimental culture. The concepts of information and communication (transfer, message, transcription, translation, coding) had "subreptitiously crept into the experimental discourse of biochemistry rather than governing it from the beginning."

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Experimental systems are the genuine working units of contemporary research in which the scientific objects and the technical conditions of their production are inextricably interconnected. They are, inseparably and at one and the same time, local, individual, social, institutional, technical, instrumental, and, above all, epistemic units." (p. 28\)

"Experimental systems grow slowly into a kind of scientific hardware within which the more fragile software of epistemic things—this amalgam of halfway-concepts, no-longer-techniques, and not-yet-values-and-standards—is articulated, connected, disconnected, placed, and displaced." (p. 36\)

"He who does research, does not know. He gropes about, he tinkers, lingers, keeps his options open. No, he does not construct, thirty years before its realization, the calculator of the day after tomorrow." (Serres, quoted approvingly, p. 15\)

"In configuring and reconfiguring epistemic things, scientists meet with resistance, resilience, recalcitrance. Not anything goes. If there is construction, it is constrained." (p. 226\)

"The concepts accompanying this turn in the experimental history of molecular biology were concepts of information and communication: transfer, message, transcription, translation, and coding. They subreptitiously had crept into the experimental discourse of biochemistry rather than governing it from the beginning." (p. 221\)

"The temporal coherence of an experimental system is granted by recurrence, by repetition, not by anticipation and forestalling. Its future development, on the other hand, if it is not to end in idling, depends upon groping and grasping for differences. Together, this adds up to what can be called differential reproduction." (p. 75\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Rheinberger's account pairs productively with Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life* (1979/1986), which similarly follows the material construction of scientific facts in a single laboratory, though Rheinberger is more philosophically ambitious and less sociologically programmatic. Peter Galison's *Image and Logic* (1997)—which appears on the same reading list—shares Rheinberger's attention to the material culture of experimentation and the heterogeneous "trading zones" between different experimental traditions, though Galison works at a much larger institutional scale in particle physics. The book also speaks directly to Hasok Chang's *Inventing Temperature* (2004), which similarly shows how measurement practices and epistemic objects co-constitute one another through iterative refinement rather than theoretical deduction.

**Limitations.** Rheinberger's single-laboratory focus, while enabling extraordinary detail, makes it difficult to assess how generalizable the "experimental system" framework is beyond the specific conditions of mid-twentieth-century biochemistry—a limitation he acknowledges but does not resolve. The heavy reliance on Derridean vocabulary (*différance*, graphemes, supplementation) can obscure rather than illuminate the historical analysis for readers outside the Continental philosophical tradition, and at times the theoretical chapters feel disconnected from the empirical richness of the case study despite the book's explicit effort to integrate them.

# Week 4 — Social Construction

# **Mara Beller, *Quantum Dialogue: The Making of a Revolution* (1999)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Beller argues that the quantum revolution was fundamentally a product of ongoing, multidirectional dialogues among physicists—not the result of individual genius or paradigmatic consensus—and that the Copenhagen interpretation achieved its dominance not through logical superiority but through rhetorical strategies that disguised contingent arguments of consistency as arguments of inevitability. The book simultaneously revises the standard narrative of the quantum revolution and proposes "dialogism" as an alternative to Kuhnian paradigm theory for understanding the growth of scientific knowledge.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Beller takes aim at the orthodox historical narrative of the quantum revolution, which had been reproduced largely unchallenged by historians (Jammer 1966; Mehra and Rechenberg 1982; Pais 1991\) who relied on the reminiscences of the "winners"—Bohr, Heisenberg, Born—without critically examining how those recollections served ideological purposes. She argues that these standard accounts are "Whiggish," constructed after the fact to make the Copenhagen interpretation appear as the natural and inevitable outcome of the development of quantum physics. Against this, Beller demonstrates through close archival work that the founding physicists held shifting, contradictory, and often opportunistic philosophical positions; that the opposition (Einstein, Schrödinger, Bohm, de Broglie) was systematically caricatured and delegitimized; and that no genuine consensus ever existed among even the Copenhagen founders on such supposedly settled matters as indeterminism, wave-particle duality, or the indispensability of classical concepts. More broadly, Beller challenges Thomas Kuhn's theory of paradigms and scientific revolutions, arguing that Kuhn canonized the Copenhagen narrative into an overarching philosophy that illegitimately treats legitimate scientific disagreement as "deviance." She draws explicitly on Cushing's (1994) work on Bohmian mechanics as a viable alternative, and situates her approach alongside Fine's (1986) emphasis on local practices, Galison's (1997) intercalated subcultures, and Pickering's (1995) attention to practice, while insisting that dialogism recovers the cognitive and individual dimensions that these approaches sometimes lose.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Beller develops what she calls "dialogism"—a historiographical and epistemological framework indebted to Mikhail Bakhtin's literary theory of polyphony and addressivity—in which scientific creativity is understood as fundamentally communicative, emerging from ongoing responsiveness to the concerns of others. She mobilizes key concepts including "dialogical emergence" (scientific ideas forming through multilateral conversation), "polyphony" (multiple, often contradictory voices present within a single text or argument), "rhetorical consolidation" (the flattening of dialogical flux into monological narrative), and the crucial distinction between "need not" and "must not" (borrowed from Feynman) to analyze how local, contingent philosophical moves were illegitimately elevated into universal dogmas.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Beller conducts extremely close readings of primary sources—personal correspondence, notebooks, unpublished manuscripts, and published papers—drawn primarily from the Archive for the History of Quantum Physics (AHQP), supplemented by interview transcripts (including Kuhn's interviews with Heisenberg, Born, Jordan, and Klein). The temporal scope runs from the early 1920s through the 1950s, with the geographic focus on the Göttingen-Copenhagen axis and its interlocutors (Einstein in Berlin/Princeton, Schrödinger in various locations, Bohm in Princeton).

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Born's Probabilistic Interpretation as "Concepts in Flux" (Chapter 2).** Beller reconstructs the emergence of Born's probabilistic interpretation of the wave function—typically narrated as a straightforward response to the wave-particle dilemma from a firmly particle-oriented physicist—and shows it was far more fluid and contingent than the received account suggests. Born was initially deeply impressed by Schrödinger's wave mechanics, writing that he was drawn back "with flying banners" to the "clear conceptual structures of classical physics." His first collision paper could be read equally well under a wave or particle interpretation; Born himself said "my solution to the collision problem did not depend on an interpretation in terms of particles." The probabilistic interpretation was substantially a translation into particle language of Schrödinger's own idea that the ψ-function determines electron charge density, and Pauli's fusion of Schrödinger's "weight function" with Born's approach yielded the standard interpretation of the wave function as giving the probability of a specific configuration. Born's later recollections suppressed this debt to Schrödinger and his own initial openness to wave ontology, retrospectively casting the particle interpretation as having been present "all along." The standard narrative further obscures that Born's interpretation initially applied only to free particles; for bound systems—the more physically interesting case—the wave interpretation was more natural, as even Heisenberg and Born later conceded. This case study exemplifies how ideological reconstruction can transform a tentative, dialogically emerging interpretation into a seemingly inevitable discovery.

**The Dialogical Emergence of Heisenberg's Uncertainty Paper (Chapters 4–5).** Beller traces how Heisenberg's uncertainty principle did not spring from a single insight or a singular philosophical commitment but coalesced from at least five distinct dialogical strands. In dialogue with Schrödinger, Heisenberg was driven to demonstrate that his matrix mechanics could still provide some form of physical interpretation—the threat of Schrödinger's more intuitive wave mechanics pushed the entire Göttingen-Copenhagen group to reintroduce classical space-time concepts they had initially eliminated. In correspondence with Pauli, the time-energy uncertainty relation took shape through detailed exchange about stationary states and transition probabilities. Dialogue with Dirac introduced the geometric interpretation of commutation relations that yielded a more formal mathematical derivation. From Jordan came crucial insights about the statistical character of quantum theory. And from "lesser" scientists—Sentfleben and Campbell, who are virtually absent from standard histories—came essential ideas about causality, the meaning of time in atomic physics, and the relationship between determinism and observability. Campbell's 1926 article on "Time and Chance" posed the question of whether there was an intrinsic temporal structure within atomic processes and explicitly connected time with probability—ideas that found direct expression in Heisenberg's treatment. The resulting uncertainty paper is "polyphonic," containing multiple, sometimes contradictory voices: positivist and realist, causal and acausal, continuous and discontinuous commitments coexist within the same text. Beller demonstrates that the very ambiguity that makes the paper difficult to interpret is also what made it productive—it could speak to different audiences and respond to different challenges simultaneously.

**Bohr's Reply to Einstein-Podolsky-Rosen: Two Incompatible Voices (Chapter 7).** In what she describes as the "cut" between dialogical emergence and rhetorical consolidation, Beller (with Arthur Fine) provides a devastating analysis of Bohr's celebrated response to the EPR challenge. She identifies two distinct and incompatible argumentative voices in Bohr's reply. The first, older voice relies on the concept of physical disturbance—the idea that measurement physically disturbs the system, preventing simultaneous knowledge of conjugate variables. But Beller and Fine demonstrate that Bohr's physical realization of the EPR thought experiment actually involves an indirect mechanical disturbance (because changing from position to momentum measurement requires altering the physical arrangement of the two-slit diaphragm through which both particles pass), which means Bohr's setup violates the very EPR condition it was supposed to address—namely, that "no real change can take place in the second system in consequence of anything that may be done to the first system." The second, newer voice is operational and verificationist: concepts are defined by their conditions of measurement, so one cannot simultaneously speak of position and momentum. But Beller shows that this operational move undermines Bohr's own complementarity framework—if uncontrollable momentum exchange cannot in principle be measured, then from an operationalist standpoint it has no meaning, and uncertainty cannot "arise from" the physical interaction between object and apparatus. The "robust physical picture" Bohr conjures—wholeness, individuality, uncontrollable interaction—turns out upon scrutiny to be "the effect of a conjuring trick." Contra the widespread myth of Bohr's "triumph" over Einstein, Beller concludes that neither answer is satisfactory, and that what Bohr's response actually concealed was the most revolutionary implication of EPR: quantum entanglement and nonlocality.

**The Myth of Wave-Particle Complementarity and the Rhetoric of Inevitability (Chapters 9, 11).** Beller dismantles the standard pedagogical and philosophical narrative in which the "paradox" of wave-particle duality is presented as the foundational problem that Bohr's complementarity principle uniquely and inevitably resolves. She shows that the majority of working mathematical physicists—including Heisenberg himself—did not consider wave and particle characteristics irreconcilable. Heisenberg told Kuhn in a 1963 interview that he "never liked the 'dual' complementarity approach," viewing the need for two descriptions as merely "an indication of the inadequacy of words." He privately described Bohr's philosophy as: "If one begins with a statement 'there are waves and particles,' everything can be made contradiction-free." Jordan and Wigner's 1928 work on second quantization further demonstrated that one could use "either" wave or particle language, not necessarily "both." The apparent necessity of wave-particle complementarity rested on Bohr's "peculiar doctrine" of the indispensability of classical concepts—the claim that classical concepts, being "direct extensions of our intuition," constitute the only possible language for describing physical experience. Beller argues this doctrine was historically and philosophically inaccurate (ignoring the vast gap between Aristotelian intuition and Newtonian physics), and that it was rejected behind closed doors by Bohr's own closest collaborators. Heisenberg and Born supported it publicly as a cohesive strategy to present a united front, but it remained "a contingent composite of different philosophical strands, the public face on a hidden web of constantly shifting differences among its founders." The rhetorical technique was to disguise arguments of consistency (quantum mechanics can be interpreted without contradiction using complementary classical concepts) as arguments of inevitability (quantum mechanics must be interpreted this way, and no alternative is possible "in principle").

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"My approach is based on both admiration for the creation of scientific novelty and criticism toward the establishment of scientific dogmas." (p. xiv)

"This book analyzes the complex, multidirectional dialogical nature of scientific theorizing (part 1\) and the strategies by which this dialogical flux is flattened into a monological narrative (part 2)." (p. 3\)

"What is taken as objective quantum philosophy (and 'inevitable' at that) turns out to be ideology—where by ideology I mean a system of assertions that imply, from within, their own justice, truth, and self-evidence." (p. 11\)

"From the dialogical perspective, it is 'creative disagreement'—with oneself (doubt) or with others (lack of consensus)—that plays the crucial role in the advance of knowledge." (p. 3\)

"Under closer scrutiny the Copenhagen paradigm has neither coherence nor stability, despite the mass of rhetoric by Bohr and his followers asserting its 'inevitability.'" (p. 173\)

"Though scientists must presuppose reality, they need not believe in it. Often it is such conditional acceptance without commitment that creates the freedom necessary for scientific creativity." (p. 219\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Beller's book pairs powerfully with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (also on the reading list as \#31 in History of Science), which similarly examines how a particular experimental and philosophical framework achieved dominance through rhetorical and institutional strategies rather than pure epistemic superiority. Galison's *Image and Logic* (\#20 in History of Science, \#65 in History of Technology) provides a complementary account of how different subcultures in physics coordinate without paradigmatic consensus—Beller explicitly positions her "dialogism" against Galison's "trading zones," arguing that even a single strand of Galison's cable metaphor is "still too homogeneous." Kuhn's *Structure of Scientific Revolutions*, which Beller critiques at length, provides the obvious foil; her argument that Kuhn and Hanson canonized the Copenhagen ideology into universal philosophical categories makes this essential comparative reading.

One limitation is that Beller's dialogical framework, while richly applied to the quantum revolution, remains somewhat programmatic as a general historiographical method—the concluding chapter sketches rather than fully develops the epistemological claims. A second concern is that the intensity of Beller's critique of Bohr occasionally risks overcorrecting: she sometimes implies that all of Bohr's philosophical reasoning was merely strategic rhetoric, which underestimates the genuine (if confused) philosophical engagement that Bohr brought to interpretive questions.

First, she traces the concept of incommensurability—which Kuhn himself called "the central innovation" of *Structure*—back to Norwood Hanson's *Patterns of Discovery* (1958). Hanson's book appeared just before Kuhn's breakthrough year on *Structure* (1958–59), and Beller shows that Kuhn explicitly cited Hanson on Gestalt perception and theory-ladenness of observation. She then demonstrates that Hanson's book was, by his own admission, written entirely with quantum physics as its "lens"—all of his historical examples (Tycho vs. Kepler, Descartes vs. Beckman) were chosen because they illustrated the conceptual situation in microphysics. Hanson's final chapter is essentially a defense of the Copenhagen dogma, arguing the impossibility of any alternative to the orthodox interpretation. So the chain runs: Copenhagen ideology → Hanson's philosophy of perception and incommensurability → Kuhn's paradigms and revolutions.

Second, Beller draws a direct parallel between Heisenberg's concept of "closed theories" (developed in the late 1940s) and Kuhn's paradigms. Both resist modification by small changes. Both are holistic—their elements supposedly stand or fall together. Both demand that transitions come through wholesale jumps rather than gradual reform. Both require dogmatic commitment and discourage internal criticism. When Heisenberg read *Structure*, he reportedly responded: "Alright, but the new paradigm is a new closed theory." Beller's point is that Heisenberg's concept of a closed theory was not a neutral philosophical observation—it was an ideological tool, developed specifically to argue the finality of quantum mechanics and to delegitimize the opposition (Einstein, Schrödinger, Bohm). Kuhn's paradigm inherited this ideological function, even if Kuhn didn't intend it.

Third, and most consequentially for a generals exam: Beller argues that the relationship between Kuhn and the Copenhagen narrative is *circular*. The quantum revolution is cited as a prime example validating Kuhn's theory of paradigm shifts, while the Kuhnian framework in turn gives the Copenhagen narrative the appearance of objective philosophical inevitability. Historians who questioned whether Kuhnian concepts fit other cases (like the Copernican revolution) still assumed they worked for the quantum case—because the orthodox narrative told them so. This circularity made the Copenhagen interpretation appear "unassailable."

Beller's positive alternative is her "dialogism," which rejects several Kuhnian premises at once: the idea of binding consensus within paradigms, the notion that legitimate disagreement constitutes "deviance," the claim that dogmatic commitment is necessary for productive science, and the holism thesis that theoretical frameworks can only be changed wholesale. She argues instead that "creative disagreement" is what drives scientific advance, and that the quantum revolution itself—properly reconstructed—is the best evidence for this. Bohr, Heisenberg, Born, and Schrödinger never agreed on the basic philosophical tenets of quantum mechanics, yet this disagreement was generative rather than destructive. The appearance of consensus was manufactured after the fact through rhetorical strategies.

# **Steven Shapin and Simon Schaffer, *Leviathan and the Air-Pump: Hobbes, Boyle, and the Experimental Life* (1985)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Shapin and Schaffer argue that the 1660s dispute between Robert Boyle and Thomas Hobbes over the air-pump was not merely a disagreement about pneumatics but a fundamental contest over what should count as legitimate natural knowledge and how the community producing that knowledge should be organized. Their central claim is that "solutions to the problem of knowledge are solutions to the problem of social order": Boyle's experimental programme and Hobbes's demonstrative philosophy each proposed radically different epistemological frameworks that simultaneously encoded contrasting visions of political life, community governance, and the proper relationship between intellectual authority and the state.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Leviathan and the Air-Pump* intervenes against two deeply entrenched historiographic habits. The first is the "internalism-externalism" framework that had structured the history and sociology of science since the 1930s, in which scholars debated the relative influence of "internal" intellectual factors versus "external" social factors on scientific change. Shapin and Schaffer reject this framing entirely, arguing that knowledge and social order are not separate variables that influence one another but are co-constituted: to settle what counts as knowledge *is* to settle forms of social organization. The second target is the "Whig" tendency to treat Boyle's victory as self-explanatory—as the inevitable triumph of proper scientific method over obstinate philosophical dogmatism. This had led historians to dismiss Hobbes as a crank who "misunderstood" Boyle, and to erase his natural philosophy from the historical record almost entirely. Against figures like Marie Boas Hall (who claimed no rational person could resist Boyle's arguments) and Barbara Shapiro (who concluded there was "no serious opposition to the new philosophy"), Shapin and Schaffer adopt a symmetrical approach derived from the Edinburgh School's "Strong Programme" (Bloor, Barnes, Collins, Pinch), offering a "charitable interpretation" of Hobbes that reveals his objections as coherent, philosophically grounded, and rational within his own framework. The book also draws methodologically on Wittgenstein's concepts of "language-games" and "forms of life," treating the experimental programme not as a timeless method but as a historically contingent pattern of activity with specific social conventions. In doing so, the work instantiates what the sociology of scientific knowledge could look like when it abandoned the search for "social factors" influencing knowledge and instead treated knowledge-making as inherently social practice.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

The book draws on the sociology of scientific knowledge (SSK), particularly the Edinburgh Strong Programme's symmetry principle, combined with Wittgenstein's concepts of "language-game" and "form of life." Its key analytical innovation is the framework of **three technologies** of fact-making—material, literary, and social—and the concept of **virtual witnessing**, which together demonstrate how experimental knowledge is simultaneously a technical, rhetorical, and political achievement.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Shapin and Schaffer conduct close reading of published seventeenth-century natural philosophical texts, particularly Boyle's *New Experiments* (1660) and Hobbes's *Dialogus physicus de natura aeris* (1661, which Schaffer translated from Latin for the book's appendix, as no prior English translation existed). They also draw on correspondence (Boyle, Oldenburg, Huygens), Royal Society records, and parliamentary and ecclesiastical documents from the Restoration period. The geographic scope centers on England in the 1660s–1670s, with a significant chapter on Huygens's air-pump work in the Netherlands and France.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Three Technologies of Fact-Making.** Chapter 2 provides the book's most influential analytical contribution by dissecting how Boyle constituted experimental "matters of fact" through the intersection of three technologies. The *material technology* was the air-pump itself—an expensive, fragile, leak-prone machine whose physical integrity was essential to the credibility of the knowledge it produced. Boyle detailed his sealing methods (diachylon cement, salad oil, mixtures of pitch, rosin, and wood-ash) with extraordinary care, because the machine's tightness was both a physical and epistemological condition. The *literary technology* comprised Boyle's deliberately "prolix" writing style, his circumstantial experimental narratives, naturalistic engravings, professions of modesty, hedging language ("perhaps," "it seems," "it is not improbable"), and the separation of factual narrative from theoretical speculation on the printed page. All of this served to produce **virtual witnessing**—the generation in the reader's mind of such a vivid image of the experimental scene that it functioned as a substitute for being present. Boyle distinguished sharply between matters of fact (about which one could "speak confidently and positively") and causal explanations (which required tentative, modest language). The *social technology* included conventions about who could participate in experimental life, how disputes should be managed, what counted as proper behavior within the community, and the collective validation of facts through the multiplication of credible witnesses. Oxford professors were "more reliable witnesses than Oxfordshire peasants"—credibility tracked the social and moral accounting systems of Restoration England. Together, these three technologies constituted a "form of life" whose acceptance or rejection amounted to accepting or rejecting an entire way of organizing knowledge and community.

**Hobbes's Coherent Counter-Programme.** Chapters 3 and 4 recover Hobbes as a serious natural philosopher—not a crank but, alongside Descartes and Gassendi, one of the three most important mechanical philosophers of his era. Hobbes attacked Boyle's programme on multiple fronts. Physically, he argued the air-pump leaked: its receiver was always full of subtle matter, rendering its "matters of fact" artifacts of the machine's imperfection rather than discoveries about nature. Epistemologically, Hobbes insisted that genuine philosophy was a *causal* enterprise modeled on geometry, producing necessary demonstrations that compelled universal assent, not the probabilistic "moral certainty" Boyle offered. For Hobbes, Boyle's experimentalism was not philosophy at all—it produced mere opinion by aggregating individual testimonies rather than deriving truth through reason. Politically, Hobbes saw the Royal Society as a dangerous private confederacy: a self-governing intellectual community that, like the clerical corporations that had fueled the Civil War, claimed autonomous authority over knowledge. He asked pointedly, "Cannot anyone who wishes come?" and answered his own question: "The place where they meet is not public." Hobbes's unified philosophy—in which natural philosophy, epistemology, and civic philosophy were inseparable—demanded that the sovereign authority have final say over philosophical truth, just as over religious doctrine, because any autonomous group claiming privileged knowledge threatened civil peace.

**The Anomalous Suspension of Water and the Problem of Replication.** Chapter 6 examines what happened when Christiaan Huygens built his own air-pump in the Netherlands in 1661–1663 and produced a finding—the anomalous suspension of water in the Torricellian space—that appeared to invalidate Boyle's key explanatory framework (the "spring of the air"). This case reveals that despite Boyle's careful literary technology, no one was actually able to build a functioning air-pump without seeing the original machine in operation. Shapin and Schaffer document the tiny number of pumps successfully constructed in the 1660s (mapped across Europe) and show that replication was not a straightforward technical matter but was deeply dependent on tacit knowledge, craft skill, and social networks. Huygens's anomalous finding was managed through the conventions of the experimental community: rather than treating it as a refutation, experimentalists calibrated their response to the anomaly according to prior commitments about whether such a phenomenon could exist. The case demonstrates that the "successful working" of the air-pump was itself a judgment shaped by social conventions, not a transparent report on nature. It also shows how the experimental community's internal politics—its methods for managing disagreement without self-destruction—were themselves constitutive of what counted as factual.

**Natural Philosophy and the Restoration Settlement.** Chapter 7 situates the Boyle-Hobbes dispute within the broader political crisis of the English Restoration. After the Civil War and Interregnum, the problem of how to reconstitute social order was urgent and practical. Boyle's experimental community offered itself as a model polity: a space of managed freedom where free discourse did not breed civil war, where consensus could be generated without tyranny, and where authority was constitutionally limited rather than absolute. The Royal Society's publicists (especially Thomas Sprat) stressed that experimental philosophy demonstrated how to organize a peaceful society "between the extremes of tyranny and radical individualism." Hobbes, by contrast, argued that any autonomous intellectual community inevitably threatened civil order—just as clerical corporations had done. His philosophy demanded no boundaries between the natural, the human, and the social, and no dissent within it. The chapter shows that the experimental community's success was inseparable from the success of the Restoration settlement itself: experimental natural philosophy was legitimated not because it was self-evidently correct but because it served the political, religious, and cultural needs of Restoration society—offering solutions to theological doubt, practical utility, and above all, a model of orderly communal life.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Solutions to the problem of knowledge are solutions to the problem of social order." (p. 332\)

"We need to play the stranger, not to be the stranger. A genuine stranger is simply ignorant. We wish to adopt a calculated and an informed suspension of our taken-for-granted perceptions of experimental practice and its products." (p. 6\)

"As we come to recognize the conventional and artifactual status of our forms of knowing, we put ourselves in a position to realize that it is ourselves and not reality that is responsible for what we know. Knowledge, as much as the state, is the product of human actions. Hobbes was right." (p. 344\)

"The establishment of matters of fact in Boyle's experimental programme utilized three technologies: a material technology embedded in the construction and operation of the air-pump; a literary technology by means of which the phenomena produced by the pump were made known to those who were not direct witnesses; and a social technology that incorporated the conventions experimental philosophers should use in dealing with each other and considering knowledge-claims." (p. 25\)

"The form of life in which we make our scientific knowledge will stand or fall with the way we order our affairs in the state." (p. 344\)

"I dare speak confidently and positively of very few things, except of matters of fact." (Boyle, quoted p. 67\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*Leviathan and the Air-Pump* pairs naturally with Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life* (1979/1986), which shares its interest in the social construction of scientific facts but approaches the problem through contemporary ethnography rather than historical controversy—Shapin and Schaffer demonstrate that the historian can achieve what Collins argued required real-time ethnographic observation. It also connects productively with Daston and Galison's *Objectivity* (2007), which traces the longer history of epistemic virtues that Shapin and Schaffer identify in embryonic form in Boyle's modesty conventions and witnessing practices. Hasok Chang's *Inventing Temperature* (2004) offers a complementary study of how measurement and instrumentation produce knowledge through social and material negotiation rather than transparent access to nature.

The book's deliberate avoidance of the question "Why did Boyle win?" may leave readers wanting a more developed account of the mechanisms of intellectual victory and defeat. Additionally, the tight focus on the English context, while methodologically justified, limits engagement with how experimental practices developed differently in Continental settings—a gap the Huygens chapter only partially addresses.

# **Erika Lorraine Milam, *Creatures of Cain: The Hunt for Human Nature in Cold War America* (Princeton University Press, 2019\)**

---

### **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Milam argues that between 1955 and 1975, American conceptions of human nature underwent a dramatic transformation—from a postwar progressive vision emphasizing humanity's cooperative essence and unlimited future to a darker vision naturalizing violence, aggression, and territorial competition as the defining characteristics of the human animal. She demonstrates that the rise and fall of the "killer ape theory" was determined by two intertwined transformations: shifting evolutionary conceptions of humanity's essential nature, and changes in the texture of American intellectual life during the Cold War, in which "colloquial science" publications served as crucial vehicles for translating evolutionary theory into politically charged public discourse.

### **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Creatures of Cain* intervenes in several overlapping scholarly conversations. Against the now-standard narrative in which the sociobiology controversy of the mid-1970s appears as a sudden eruption, Milam demonstrates that the fierce debates over E. O. Wilson's *Sociobiology* (1975) were the culmination of a decade-long public conversation about the biological basis of human violence, not a novel rupture. Critics like Gould and Lewontin had already honed their objections in response to Ardrey, Lorenz, and Morris. Milam also challenges accounts that treat "popular" and "professional" science as distinct registers by introducing the concept of "colloquial science"—following Jim Secord's work on science as communication—to describe a mode of publication that was simultaneously consumed by lay readers, aspiring scientists, and established researchers across disciplines. This framework refuses the dichotomy between popularization and serious research, showing instead that best-selling paperbacks by nonacademic writers like Ardrey were debated in graduate seminars alongside peer-reviewed work. The book further engages with Donna Haraway's *Primate Visions* by extending its analysis of how gender and race structured primatological knowledge, but pushes beyond Haraway's framework by tracing how feminist challenges (the "Woman the Gatherer" hypothesis) and racial politics intersected with the killer ape debates in mutually constitutive ways. Milam's periodization—anchored in the transition from postwar liberal progressivism to the fractured political landscape of the early 1970s—also enriches the work of historians like Jamie Cohen-Cole on the Cold War human sciences by showing how evolutionary biology functioned as a site of political contestation from both the Left and the Right simultaneously.

### **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Milam employs a cultural and intellectual history of science approach attentive to the co-production of scientific knowledge and political culture, emphasizing how scientific authority was constructed through multiple media forms (books, films, television, educational curricula) and how the boundary between "professional" and "colloquial" scientific communication was porous, politically contested, and consequential for debates over human nature.

### **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

The book draws on an extensive archival base spanning over a dozen collections—including the papers of Robert Ardrey, Irven DeVore, Loren Eiseley, Margaret Mead, the Harry Frank Guggenheim Foundation, and the Man: A Course of Study project records—supplemented by fifteen oral history interviews with key participants (DeVore, Zihlman, Hrdy, Fox, Tiger, and others). Milam also analyzes films (Hollywood features, documentaries, MACOS classroom films, television specials), book reviews, magazine essays, and congressional records. The geographic scope is primarily the United States, with attention to field sites in East Africa, the Arctic, and the Kalahari, spanning 1945–1986.

### **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Man: A Course of Study (MACOS) — From Liberal Pedagogy to Culture War (Chapters 3, 12).** Jerome Bruner's NSF-funded fifth-grade social science curriculum, organized around the question "What is human about human beings?", provides Milam with one of the book's richest case studies. MACOS was designed to use ethnographic films of Netsilik Inuit life and baboon behavior to teach children to reason like anthropologists, fostering appreciation for cultural diversity and the essential unity of humanity. Milam traces its creation with extraordinary granularity: the anthropologist Douglas Oliver's original vision of a six-year progression through human cultures; the filmmaker Asen Balikci's painstaking reconstructions of traditional Netsilik life in Pelly Bay (where tragedy struck when fourteen community members died after a medical x-ray visit); Irven DeVore's playful baboon food-choice experiments in Kenya; and the fraught decision to exclude the Ju/'hoansi footage because depicting "partially naked, dark-skinned 'primitives'" was deemed politically unacceptable for American classrooms. The Netsilik were used instead because they were "sufficiently 'white' to be uncontroversial." By 1975, however, Republican congressman John Conlan attacked MACOS from the floor of the House as a threat to Christian morality and a waste of federal money, demonstrating how evolutionary accounts of human nature had become caught between Left critiques (that biological explanations denied political agency) and Right critiques (that cultural relativism undermined moral order). MACOS became a casualty of the very culture wars it had been designed to transcend.

**Robert Ardrey and the Killer Ape Theory (Chapters 4–6).** Milam reconstructs the intellectual genealogy connecting Raymond Dart's 1953 "Predatory Transition from Ape to Man" paper to Ardrey's bestselling translations for general audiences. Dart, working in apartheid South Africa (where he testified in racial reclassification trials using skull measurements), had argued that Australopithecines were weapon-wielding carnivores whose "bloodlust" separated them from other animals. Ardrey, a playwright who met Dart while on commission for *The Reporter* magazine, rendered this into vivid prose: "Far from the truth lay the antique assumption that man had fathered the weapon. The weapon, instead, had fathered man." Milam traces how Ardrey's *The Territorial Imperative* (1966) combined this paleoanthropological argument with ethological theories of territoriality from Vero Copner Wynne-Edwards, replacing fossil evidence with animal behavior to argue that male-male competition for territory was the engine of human social organization. She connects Ardrey's ideas to the multimillionaire Harry Frank Guggenheim, who read Ardrey alongside Lorenz and Morris and reshaped his foundation's mission to fund research on dominance and aggression—eventually installing Robin Fox and Lionel Tiger as research directors, creating an institutional pipeline that would help fund the emerging field of sociobiology.

**Woman the Gatherer and Feminist Challenges (Chapter 7).** Milam traces how female anthropologists and primatologists challenged man-the-hunter narratives not from outside the evolutionary framework but from within it. Sally Slocum's 1971 essay "Woman the Gatherer: Male Bias in Anthropology" reframed the question of human origins by asking what women were doing while men were supposedly hunting. Adrienne Zihlman and Nancy Tanner, both trained at Berkeley under Sherwood Washburn, collaborated to argue that females played a crucial role in the transition from ape to human, with gathering, tool use, and bipedalism driven by female adaptation to the savannah. Their paper appeared in the new feminist journal *Signs* because they did not believe an anthropology journal would publish it—a decision that strengthened feminist intellectual networks but separated their work from the disciplinary mainstream. Milam illuminates the asymmetric professional stakes: Ardrey was fêted by the Animal Behavior Society and celebrated by Fox and Tiger as an honorary member of their intellectual "tribe," while Elaine Morgan—who skewered man-the-hunter narratives as "Tarzanist" fantasies in *The Descent of Woman*—was permanently marginalized because she also endorsed the speculative "aquatic ape" hypothesis. Professional women could never afford to publicly align with Morgan, even when they embraced her critique.

**Hollywood, Violence, and the Collapse of the Hays Code (Chapter 11).** Milam traces how evolutionary ideas about innate human aggression permeated popular film in the late 1960s and early 1970s, creating a feedback loop between colloquial science and mass culture. Director Sam Peckinpah explicitly invoked Ardrey as "the only prophet we have" when defending the graphic violence in *Straw Dogs* (1971), and film critic Pauline Kael described the film as Peckinpah discovering "the territorial imperative." The famous opening sequence of Kubrick's *2001: A Space Odyssey* (1968)—in which an Australopithecus discovers bone-weapons and hurls a club into the air in a match-cut to a spaceship—was staged with direct consultation from paleoanthropologists. Milam demonstrates that these films did not merely reflect scientific ideas but actively amplified them, generating concern from both secular scientists who worried about public misunderstanding and conservative Christians who saw in them evidence of dangerous moral decline.

### **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"This book tells the story of how definitions of human nature came to grip the American public with such force and why purported scientific insights shifted, so dramatically and in such a short time, from seeing humanity as characterized by our unique capacity for reasoned cooperation to emphasizing, even lauding, our propensity to violence." (p. 3\)

"Far from the truth lay the antique assumption that man had fathered the weapon. The weapon, instead, had fathered man." — Robert Ardrey, *African Genesis*, quoted by Milam (p. 95\)

"In its broadest scope, *Creatures of Cain* demonstrates that understanding the historical fate of any scientific vision of human nature requires attending to the political and social concerns that endowed that vision with persuasive power (or undermined it)." (p. 14\)

"By the mid-1970s... a new generation of evolutionists who called themselves sociobiologists... defined human nature primarily through comparisons with animal behavior... By dispensing with the historical development of human nature as irrelevant to understanding the consequences of its final (i.e., current) form, sociobiological theories of what it meant to be human de-emphasized the sense of progress that had characterized postwar scientists' visions of humanity. Stripped of progress, stripped of cultural variation, humans became yet another species of animal." (p. 11\)

"Ongoing synergistic frictions between colloquial and professional conversations about human nature are a continuing legacy of the Cold War decades in which Americans turned to experts on animal behavior to answer fundamental questions about what it means to be human. Today, our long, contingent journey from ape to human no longer provokes fear; it provides hope." (p. 280\)

"Gene-itis has been a part of our Western European culture for more than a century. It is time to cure that disease, whether it appears in the guise of eugenics, racism, or sociobiology." — Sherwood Washburn, quoted by Milam (p. 253\)

### **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Milam's work pairs naturally with Donna Haraway's *Primate Visions* (1989), which pioneered the analysis of how gender, race, and colonialism structured primatological knowledge production—Milam extends this framework with richer archival depth and sharper periodization. The book also connects productively with Erickson et al., *How Reason Almost Lost Its Mind* (2013), which traces Cold War rationality across multiple disciplines; Milam's account of how evolutionary science became a vehicle for debating the political implications of human nature complements their analysis of formal decision theory and game theory. For urban and environmental history, Milam's treatment of John Calhoun's rodent overcrowding experiments and the environmental doomsday rhetoric of Ehrlich, Commoner, and Hardin connects to broader histories of postwar environmental politics. One limitation is that the book's tight focus on the American context, despite its subjects' global field sites, leaves underexplored how these same debates played out in European and African intellectual contexts where evolutionary science also carried political weight. Additionally, while Milam's "colloquial science" framework is analytically productive, the book's narrative occasionally risks treating the professional-popular distinction as simply dissolved rather than reconstituted in new forms—an issue she acknowledges but does not fully resolve.

# **Donna Haraway, *Primate Visions: Gender, Race, and Nature in the World of Modern Science* (1989)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Western primatology is a form of "simian orientalism"—a culturally and politically saturated storytelling practice in which the study of monkeys and apes has served as a theater for constructing narratives about human origins, nature, sex, race, and the boundaries between nature and culture. Haraway argues that the sciences of the Primate Order are simultaneously empirical investigations and mythic narratives structured by the dualisms of sex/gender and nature/culture, and that reading primatology as a contested narrative field (what she designates "SF"—the overlapping space of scientific fact and speculative fiction) reveals how gender, race, class, and colonial power have been written into the body of nature through the disciplined practices of modern science.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Primate Visions* intervenes in multiple scholarly conversations at once. Against the internalist histories of primatology that narrate the field as a progressive clarification of vision—from misty anecdote to sharp-eyed quantitative science—Haraway insists that scientific practice is itself a storytelling craft, historically specific and politically embedded. She engages critically with four "temptations" that structure science studies: the strong social constructivism of Bruno Latour and Steve Woolgar's *Laboratory Life*, which she finds powerful but insufficient because it cannot adjudicate between better and worse accounts; the Marxist standpoint epistemology tradition (drawing on Nancy Hartsock and the British Radical Science Journal), which she values for showing how race, sex, and class systematically determine knowledge, but which she finds limited by its privileging of labor as the master category; the empiricist realism of practicing scientists themselves; and the discourse-centered approach of Michel Foucault. Rather than choosing one framework, Haraway holds all four in productive tension, developing a reading practice that treats primatological texts as simultaneously scientific documents, cultural artifacts, political interventions, and mythic narratives. She is explicitly in dialogue with Edward Said's *Orientalism*, extending its logic to argue that western primatology constructs "the self from the raw material of the other." She also challenges the feminist science studies of the period by refusing a simple corrective model—the problem is not merely that bad (male, white) science needs to be replaced by good (feminist, anti-racist) science, but that the very categories through which nature is known are historically constructed and politically consequential, requiring genealogical excavation rather than simple substitution.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Haraway mobilizes a dense theoretical apparatus combining feminist theory, semiotics, post-structuralist discourse analysis, science and technology studies (especially social constructivism and actor-network theory), and postcolonial criticism. Her key original concepts include "simian orientalism" (western primatology as an appropriative discourse constructing the human self from the animal other, analogous to Said's Orientalism); the SF field (the overlapping narrative space of scientific fact and speculative fiction); and the insistence that scientific practice is a "story-telling practice—a rule-governed, constrained, historically changing craft of narrating the history of nature." She treats the primate body as "an intriguing kind of political discourse" and reads biological organisms as "icons (condensations) of the whole of the history and politics of the culture that constructed them."

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Haraway draws on an extraordinary range of sources: museum archives (especially the American Museum of Natural History), published scientific literature across primatology, physical anthropology, comparative psychology, and sociobiology, extensive interviews with primatologists (including Washburn, Altmann, Fedigan, Zihlman, Hrdy, Jolly, and others), popular media (National Geographic television specials, magazine articles, advertisements), institutional records, photographs, films, grant applications, and conference proceedings. The temporal scope spans from Linnaeus's eighteenth-century taxonomy through 1980s sociobiology and feminist primatology, with the geographic range encompassing the United States, Europe, Japan, Africa, South Asia, and Madagascar.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Teddy Bear Patriarchy: Carl Akeley and the American Museum of Natural History (Chapter 3).** This is perhaps the book's most celebrated set-piece. Haraway reads the African Hall of the American Museum of Natural History—opened in 1936 across from Central Park—as a civic ritual space where early twentieth-century anxieties about racial degeneration, urban decadence, immigration, and the crisis of white masculinity were resolved through the sacralized encounter between (white, male) viewer and taxidermied African wildlife. The taxidermist Carl Akeley killed and mounted the Giant of Karisimbi, a silverback gorilla, in 1921—the same year the Museum hosted the Second International Congress of Eugenics. Haraway shows how each diorama was composed according to organicist principles: a vigilant male, one or two females, a baby, arranged in a "natural" division of labor that simultaneously encoded racial hierarchy (the building's exterior featured bas-reliefs of Theodore Roosevelt flanked by an African and a Native American guide) and sexual hierarchy. The viewer enters alone into a darkened cathedral-like space, experiencing "individual communion with nature" through a visual technology—the glass-fronted diorama—that "forbids the body's entry, but the gaze invites his visual penetration." Taxidermy becomes "a politics of reproduction" in which the dead animal body is reborn into a perfected, permanent form that can heal the "sick vision of civilized man." The case brilliantly demonstrates how natural history display, conservation ideology, eugenics, colonial extraction, and gendered vision converge in a single institutional site.

**Harry Harlow and the Technology of Love (Chapter 9).** Haraway's analysis of Harry Harlow's surrogate mother experiments at the University of Wisconsin becomes a meditation on the laboratory as a site where metaphor is translated into hardware. Harlow's cloth and wire surrogate mothers—stripped-down mechanical substitutes for maternal bodies—were designed to test whether infant attachment was based on "contact comfort" rather than drive reduction from nursing. Haraway reads the entire laboratory apparatus through the lens of masculinist narrative and sadistic visual discipline: Harlow's own account of "seeing" the cloth surrogate mother as his reflection in an airplane window becomes an allegory of the scientist's self-birthing through the construction of a "bold and barren" female double. The Wisconsin General Test Apparatus (WGTA) positioned experimenter and monkey face-to-face through a one-way screen—asymmetrical vision as the architecture of knowledge production. The experiments that produced socially devastated, self-clutching isolated monkeys, and later "rejecting mothers" who could not care for their own young, became part of Cold War reproductive politics, translating controversial social issues about working women, maternal deprivation, and the engineering of affect into laboratory artifacts. Haraway shows how Harlow's burlesque prose style—his jocular announcement that fathers could replace mothers since they possessed "all the really essential equipment"—was not incidental but structurally related to the sadistic logic of the experimental apparatus itself.

**Sherwood Washburn and the New Physical Anthropology (Chapter 8).** Haraway traces how Washburn's postwar program to reconstruct physical anthropology around the modern evolutionary synthesis also reconstructed the category of "universal man." Working in the shadow of UNESCO's anti-racist statements of 1950-51, Washburn's "plan" deployed primate field studies, functional anatomy, and fossil interpretation to replace scientific racism with a universal human nature grounded in the "sharing way of life" of Man the Hunter. Haraway maps the entire network: the Berkeley doctoral students assigned to explore different "functional adaptive complexes" of the human body-mind, the pivotal 1966 "Man the Hunter" conference, and the "99% thesis" (that humans spent 99% of their evolutionary history as hunter-gatherers). She shows how this liberal, anti-racist universalism was nonetheless structured by a masculinist origin narrative in which hunting, tools, and male dynamism drove hominization, while gathering and female reproductive labor remained the unchanging "matrix." The subsequent destabilization of this framework came from multiple directions: sociobiology's replacement of the social group with the gene as the unit of selection, feminist challenges to the hunting hypothesis, and postcolonial critiques that saw universal man as a denial of cultural difference. The case of Richard Lee's \!Kung San project illustrates the fracture: initially conceived as a study of UNESCO's universal hunter-gatherer, Lee's observations of South African labor recruitment transformed Eden into historical space.

**Feminist Primatology as a Genre of Feminist Theory (Part Three, Chapters 11-16).** Haraway's sustained treatment of four women primatologists—Jeanne Altmann, Linda Fedigan, Adrienne Zihlman, and Sarah Blaffer Hrdy—constitutes a major argument that feminist discourse within primatology is not a correction applied from outside but a genre of feminist theory produced through the practice of science itself. Each scientist constructs a different kind of female primate subject: Altmann proposes an ergonomic self governed by time-energy budgets; Fedigan deconstructs narratives driven by anisogamy; Zihlman builds a liberal, flexible gatherer-subject; and Hrdy deploys a strategic, investing self within sociobiological frameworks. Haraway reads Zihlman's "woman the gatherer" hypothesis as a powerful but troubled universalism that risks reproducing colonial discourse even while challenging masculinist origin stories, while Hrdy's sociobiological feminism, rooted in possessive individualism and reproductive competition, paradoxically maps "the cultural logic of late capitalism" even as it empowers female agency. The section demonstrates that these scientists "practice their science, and thereby are part of a broad cultural reconstruction of what may count as nature"—dispersing and problematizing sexual difference through the details of their empirical work rather than through external critique.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Scientific practice may be considered a kind of story-telling practice—a rule-governed, constrained, historically changing craft of narrating the history of nature." (p. 4\)

"Simian orientalism means that western primatology has been about the construction of the self from the raw material of the other, the appropriation of nature in the production of culture, the ripening of the human from the soil of the animal, the clarity of white from the obscurity of color, the issue of man from the body of woman, the elaboration of gender from the resource of sex, the emergence of mind by the activation of body." (p. 11\)

"Biology, and primatology, are inherently political discourses, whose chief objects of knowledge, such as organisms and ecosystems, are icons (condensations) of the whole of the history and politics of the culture that constructed them for contemplation and manipulation. The primate body itself is an intriguing kind of political discourse." (p. 10\)

"The detached eye of objective science is an ideological fiction, and a powerful one. But it is a fiction that hides—and is designed to hide—how the powerful discourses of the natural sciences really work." (p. 13\)

"Taxidermy fulfills the fatal desire to represent, to be whole; it is a politics of reproduction." (p. 30\)

"Feminism, as well as primatology, is a story-telling practice." (p. 279, contextual reference from Part Three framing)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*Primate Visions* pairs productively with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (1985) as parallel demonstrations that scientific knowledge is constituted through specific social and material practices rather than through neutral observation of nature—though Haraway's attention to gender, race, and colonial power vastly expands the analytical terrain. It also connects powerfully to Daston and Galison's *Objectivity* (2007), which traces the historical construction of epistemic virtues in scientific image-making; Haraway's analysis of taxidermic vision, National Geographic specials, and laboratory visual technologies anticipates many of their themes about the relationship between seeing and knowing. Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life* (1986) is an explicit interlocutor—Haraway draws on their constructivism while insisting that it cannot account for the differential power of gender, race, and colonial position in shaping what counts as knowledge.

One limitation is that the book's theoretical density and deliberately heterogeneous prose style—mixing cultural criticism, semiotics, political theory, and close readings of scientific texts—can obscure rather than illuminate the empirical history of primatology for readers unfamiliar with the field. A second limitation, noted by some reviewers, is that Haraway's framework can make it difficult to distinguish between better and worse scientific accounts of primate behavior, since all are treated as culturally saturated narratives; her insistence that "one story is not as good as another" sits in some tension with the thoroughgoing constructivism of her analytical method.

# **Bruno Latour, *We Have Never Been Modern* (1993)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Latour argues that "modernity" rests on a constitutional settlement—emerging paradigmatically from the seventeenth-century confrontation between Boyle and Hobbes—that enforces a strict separation between Nature (the domain of nonhuman things, represented by scientists) and Society (the domain of human subjects, represented by political spokespersons), while simultaneously making this very separation invisible. This "modern Constitution" succeeads not by actually keeping nature and society apart but by allowing the clandestine proliferation of hybrids (entities that are simultaneously natural, social, and discursive) beneath an official ideology of purification. Since the work of purification has never actually described how collectives operate—since every scientific fact, technological artifact, and political institution is a "quasi-object" blending human and nonhuman elements—we have, in fact, never been modern.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Latour intervenes simultaneously against multiple scholarly and philosophical camps. Against the Edinburgh school's strong programme in the sociology of scientific knowledge (Bloor, Barnes, Shapin), he argues that social constructivism merely inverts the asymmetry it claims to correct: where naive realism treats Nature as explaining everything and society as irrelevant, the strong programme treats social interests as the master variable while leaving "society" itself unexamined. Even Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*—which Latour treats as the founding text for a comparative anthropology of science—falls into this trap, concluding "Hobbes was right" and allowing social explanation to remain a privileged, undeconstructed category. Against postmodernism (Lyotard, Baudrillard), Latour argues that deconstructionists correctly sense that something has gone wrong with the modern critical project but fail to diagnose the problem, instead doubling down on suspicion and irony while accepting modernity's framework of successive revolutions and radical breaks with the past. Against traditional philosophy of science, which locates scientific validity in the correspondence between representation and an external reality "out there," Latour relocates it "down there" on the laboratory bench, in the material practices of experimental systems and the extension of standardized networks. The new framework Latour proposes—what he calls "nonmodern" or "amodern"—deploys the methods of symmetrical anthropology to treat the sciences of Western modernity with the same analytical tools ethnographers use for non-Western societies, refusing the "Great Divide" between modern and premodern that has structured both anthropology and the philosophy of science.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Latour mobilizes actor-network theory (ANT), symmetrical anthropology, and Michel Serres's concept of the "quasi-object" to argue for an ontology in which entities are neither purely natural nor purely social but exist along a continuum of translation and mediation. Key concepts include: "purification" (the official work of separating nature from society), "translation" or "mediation" (the unofficial work of producing hybrids), "quasi-objects/quasi-subjects" (entities that are simultaneously real, narrated, and collective), and the "modern Constitution" (the implicit framework of guarantees that organizes the separation of powers between science and politics while enabling hybrid proliferation).

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

This is an explicitly speculative philosophical essay rather than an empirical study; Latour himself acknowledges its "very Gallic" character and notes that empirical support can be found in his other works and those of his colleagues. The primary analytical case is drawn from Shapin and Schaffer's historical reconstruction of the Boyle-Hobbes controversy (1660s England), which Latour reinterprets and extends. Latour also draws on ethnographic comparisons (especially Philippe Descola's work on the Achuar of Amazonia), the sociology of critical judgment (Boltanski and Thévenot), and René Girard's theory of the scapegoat mechanism.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Boyle-Hobbes Settlement as the Origin of the Modern Constitution (Chapter 2).** Latour's central historical case, drawn from Shapin and Schaffer, reconstructs the simultaneous invention of modern scientific practice and modern political theory in seventeenth-century England. Boyle created the laboratory as a space where "trustworthy witnesses" could observe artificially produced "matters of fact"—mobilizing legal and biblical traditions of testimony but applying them, for the first time, to the behavior of nonhuman entities (descending mercury columns, suffocating birds, collapsing marble cylinders in the transparent chamber of an air pump). Meanwhile, Hobbes created the modern theory of sovereignty in which naked, calculating citizens constitute the Leviathan through a social contract. The critical point is that both were simultaneously inventing the world we still inhabit: Boyle defined a political discourse from which politics was excluded (science), while Hobbes defined a scientific politics from which experimental science was excluded (the state). Together, they established the constitutional settlement in which "the representation of things through the intermediary of the laboratory is forever dissociated from the representation of citizens through the intermediary of the social contract." Latour notes the critical symmetry: the word "representation" is the same in both cases, but the Constitution renders the resemblance unthinkable. He pushes beyond Shapin and Schaffer by insisting that their own analysis unwittingly reproduces the asymmetry it reveals—they deconstruct Boyle's Nature but leave Hobbes's Society intact, using "power," "interest," and "politics" as unexamined analytical resources even though Hobbes invented those very concepts.

**The Newspaper as an Inventory of Hybrids (Chapter 1).** Latour opens with a brilliantly constructed set piece: a reading of a daily newspaper whose articles seamlessly connect "chemical reactions and political reactions," weaving together atmospheric chemistry, corporate strategy, heads of state, Third World development, and local elections in a single story about the ozone hole. The AIDS virus links sex, the unconscious, Africa, tissue cultures, DNA, and San Francisco. Frozen embryos, deforestation, whales with radio collars, classified slag heaps, the Pope and contraception—each story is a "hybrid article" that mixes science, politics, economy, law, religion, and technology. Yet the newspaper maintains section headings—Economy, Politics, Science, Culture—"as if there were nothing odd going on." The analytical disciplines do the same: they "slice the delicate network traced by the virus for you into tidy compartments where you will find only science, only economy, only social phenomena." This opening establishes Latour's central diagnostic: the modern world is constituted by the proliferation of hybrids that its official categories systematically deny.

**The Four Guarantees and the Paradox of Modern Power (Chapter 2).** Latour formalizes the modern Constitution as a set of interlocking guarantees that function as checks and balances. The first guarantee: Nature is transcendent—it exists independently of human construction. The second: Society is immanent—humans freely construct their own political order. But each guarantee secretly contains its opposite: Nature is also immanent (scientists fabricate facts in laboratories), and Society is also transcendent (the Leviathan, sustained by mobilized objects, surpasses its human creators). The third guarantee maintains an absolute separation between the two domains, ensuring that the contradictions within each are never visible simultaneously. The fourth guarantee positions a "crossed-out God" as absent arbiter—"totally absent but ensuring arbitration between the two branches of government." This constitutional machinery, Latour argues, made the moderns "invincible": they could appeal to Nature's transcendence to debunk social prejudice, then to Society's freedom to debunk naturalization, then to the separation of domains to deny any contradiction, and finally to the crossed-out God for spiritual consolation. "If you criticize them by saying that Nature is a world constructed by human hands, they will show you that it is transcendent... If you tell them that we are free and that our destiny is in our own hands, they will tell you that Society is transcendent and its laws infinitely surpass us." The beauty of the mechanism is that its six resources of critique appeared separate and even contradictory, making the unified system impossible to resist.

**Premoderns, Postmoderns, and the Proliferation Crisis (Chapters 3–5).** Latour develops a comparative anthropology of knowledge systems by contrasting the modern Constitution with "premodern" collectives. Citing Descola on the Achuar and Lévi-Strauss on "savage thought," he argues that non-Western cultures explicitly conceptualize the connections between natural and social orders—"every monster becomes visible and thinkable"—which has the effect of limiting the proliferation of hybrids. The modern Constitution achieves the opposite: by officially denying hybrids, it allows their unconstrained multiplication. "The less the moderns think they are blended, the more they blend." But this mechanism has reached its limit. The proliferation of quasi-objects—frozen embryos, ozone holes, gene synthesizers, sensor-equipped robots, psychotropic drugs—has overwhelmed the capacity of the constitutional framework to contain them. "The third estate ends up being too numerous to feel that it is faithfully represented either by the order of objects or by the order of subjects." This diagnosis leads to Latour's proposal for a "Parliament of Things"—an explicitly nonmodern political arrangement in which the production of hybrids becomes publicly visible and democratically regulated, and in which "natures are present, but with their representatives, scientists who speak in their name" while "societies are present, but with the objects that have been serving as their ballast from time immemorial."

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The hypothesis of this essay is that the word 'modern' designates two sets of entirely different practices which must remain distinct if they are to remain effective, but have recently begun to be confused. The first set of practices, by 'translation', creates mixtures between entirely new types of beings, hybrids of nature and culture. The second, by 'purification', creates two entirely distinct ontological zones: that of human beings on the one hand; that of nonhumans on the other." (p. 10–11)

"No one has ever been modern. Modernity has never begun. There has never been a modern world." (p. 47\)

"Native Americans were not mistaken when they accused the Whites of having forked tongues. By separating the relations of political power from the relations of scientific reasoning while continuing to shore up power with reason and reason with power, the moderns have always had two irons in the fire. They have become invincible." (p. 38\)

"Yes, the scientific facts are indeed constructed, but they cannot be reduced to the social dimension because this dimension is populated by objects mobilized to construct it. Yes, those objects are real but they look so much like social actors that they cannot be reduced to the reality 'out there' invented by the philosophers of science." (p. 6\)

"The less the moderns think they are blended, the more they blend. The more science is absolutely pure, the more it is intimately bound up with the fabric of society." (p. 43\)

"Natures are present, but with their representatives, scientists who speak in their name. Societies are present, but with the objects that have been serving as their ballast from time immemorial... The imbroglios and networks that had no place now have the whole place to themselves." (p. 144\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*We Have Never Been Modern* pairs most productively with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (1985), which supplies its primary historical case and which Latour simultaneously celebrates and critiques for reproducing the asymmetry it reveals. It connects directly to Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life* (1986) as an earlier empirical demonstration of fact-construction, and to Donna Haraway's "Cyborg Manifesto" (1985), which Latour cites as a kindred effort to theorize hybrid entities that dissolve the nature/culture boundary. On your technology list, it is in direct dialogue with the SCOT framework (Bijker, Hughes, and Pinch), which Latour regards as insufficiently symmetrical, and with Winner's "Do Artifacts Have Politics?" and the broader debate over technological determinism—Latour's framework dissolves the determinism question entirely by refusing the separation of technical and social.

The book's principal limitation is its own admission: it is "speculative" and "too crude," offering no sustained empirical case study of its own (the Boyle-Hobbes analysis is drawn from Shapin and Schaffer).
