Critics have also noted that the nonmodern Constitution and Parliament of Things remain schematic proposals rather than worked-out political or institutional programs—the book is far stronger as diagnosis than as prescription.

# **Harry Collins, *Changing Order: Replication and Induction in Scientific Practice* (1985)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Collins argues that experimental replication—science's foundational test for truth—cannot function as an objective, self-contained arbiter of knowledge claims because of what he calls the "experimenters' regress": since the competence of an experiment can only be judged by whether it produces the correct result, and the correct result is precisely what is in dispute, no purely experimental procedure can settle scientific controversies. The resolution of disputes instead depends on social processes—negotiations, alliances, rhetorical strategies, and institutional pressures—that are subsequently rendered invisible once a controversy closes and its outcome is retrospectively reconstructed as the inevitable product of proper scientific method.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Changing Order* intervenes primarily against the "canonical model" of science, which holds that experimental replication provides an algorithmic, rule-governed procedure for testing knowledge claims independently of the social contexts in which they are produced. Collins directly challenges Karl Popper's falsificationist epistemology, which depends on the assumption that experimental tests can definitively adjudicate between competing claims. He also engages with and extends Thomas Kuhn's framework by developing a more fine-grained vocabulary for what happens *during* scientific controversy—what Collins calls the "extraordinary" phase—as distinct from Kuhn's broad categories of normal and revolutionary science. The book positions itself within the "Bath School" of the sociology of scientific knowledge (SSK), alongside the Edinburgh School's "Strong Programme" associated with Barry Barnes and David Bloor, but takes a more empirically grounded, microsociological approach. Collins's framework—the Empirical Programme of Relativism (EPOR)—proposes three stages of analysis: demonstrating the interpretive flexibility of experimental results, examining the social mechanisms that close controversies, and relating these closure mechanisms to wider social structures. The work builds on Nelson Goodman's philosophical analysis of the "New Riddle of Induction" and Wittgenstein's arguments about rule-following, translating these philosophical puzzles into an empirical sociological research programme. Against rationalist philosophers of science like Imre Lakatos, Collins argues that no formal distinction between legitimate and illegitimate defenses of a theory can be drawn independently of social context.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Collins deploys methodological relativism as an analytical tool, treating the truth or falsity of scientific claims symmetrically in order to examine how knowledge is socially certified. His key conceptual innovations include the "experimenters' regress," the "core set" (the small group of scientists whose negotiations actually settle a controversy), and the notion of "tacit knowledge" as central to experimental skill—concepts he embeds within a broader network model of conceptual order drawn from Mary Hesse's philosophy and Wittgenstein's analysis of rule-following and "forms of life."

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Collins employs participant observation and semi-structured interviews with working scientists, conducted primarily during the early-to-mid 1970s. The fieldwork covers three case studies: TEA-laser builders in the UK (including Collins's own participation in laser construction), gravitational radiation researchers in the US and UK, and practitioners of parapsychological research. The temporal scope spans roughly 1970–1982, and the geographic focus is the Anglo-American scientific community, particularly physics laboratories.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The TEA-Laser and Tacit Knowledge (Chapter 3).** Collins's study of the replication of the Transversely Excited Atmospheric pressure CO₂ laser provides the book's crucial baseline case of "normal" science. The TEA-laser, first operated in 1968 at a Canadian defense laboratory, produced infrared radiation powerful enough to vaporize concrete. Collins traces the attempts of various British laboratories to replicate the device throughout the early 1970s and then, in extraordinary detail, follows laser physicist Bob Harrison's attempt in March 1979 to build a copy of his own working laser ("Jumbo") at Heriot-Watt University. Despite Harrison being the most knowledgeable person alive regarding his own laser design, and despite having a working model literally alongside his new build for comparison, the replication still required days of frustrating troubleshooting—exchanging components, testing hypotheses about failing circuits, and making intuitive leaps (such as removing a trigger wire on a hunch that produced the first discharge). Collins uses this case to demonstrate several propositions: that skill-like knowledge is "capricious" in its transfer (Proposition One); that it travels best through accomplished practitioners (Proposition Two); that experimental ability is invisible and cannot be fully articulated (Proposition Four); and most importantly, that the experimenters' regress does *not* arise here because there exists a universally agreed criterion of success—the laser either vaporizes material or it does not. Harrison's developing tacit knowledge is visible in what he had learned *not* to see as significant differences: thinner trigger wires, different electrode thicknesses, and varied connection methods that would have distracted a less experienced builder. Conversely, he had learned to see significance in things—like certain patterns of anode marking at earlier stages—that had no obvious logical priority. The case establishes that even "easy" replication is a social accomplishment rather than a mere following of instructions.

**Gravitational Radiation and the Experimenters' Regress (Chapter 4).** This is the book's centerpiece. Beginning in 1969, Joseph Weber of the University of Maryland claimed to detect gravitational radiation using massive aluminum bar antennae fitted with piezo-electric strain gauges. His claimed detection rate of approximately seven events per day was orders of magnitude higher than cosmological theory predicted possible—if extrapolated to an isotropic universe, the implied energy output would "burn up" the universe unreasonably quickly. Yet Weber produced increasingly ingenious supporting evidence: coincident signals on detectors separated by a thousand miles, and a 24-hour periodicity correlated with the Earth's orientation to the galaxy (the "sidereal correlation"). Collins interviewed scientists in the field in 1972 and again around 1975, documenting radical disagreements about the competence of various experiments. He presents devastating sets of quotations in which three different scientists evaluate the same experiment (W, X, Y, or Z) with assessments ranging from "very impressive" to "a bunch of shit" to "an out and out fraud." Collins catalogs an extraordinary list of variables—detector material, electronics, statistical techniques, calibration methods, signal frequency, burst length—each of which scientists invoked to explain discrepant results, but whose relative importance was itself contested. The experimenters' regress is fully visible here: a good gravity wave detector is one that gives correct results, but the correct results are precisely what is in question. The controversy's closure came not through a single decisive experiment but through a cumulative social process in which one particularly aggressive group (referred to as "Q") deliberately set out to "kill" Weber's claims. Q's team built a detector, obtained null results, published in a confrontational manner, and even circulated Irving Langmuir's paper on "pathological science" to Weber and other researchers. Collins shows that Q acted "as one might expect a scientist to act who realized that evidence and arguments alone are insufficient to settle unambiguously the existential status of a phenomenon." After Q's intervention, the field "crystallized": henceforward only negative results were accepted as legitimate, and any experiment producing positive results was, by that very fact, considered flawed. The case also demonstrates the failure of "tests of tests": attempts to calibrate gravity wave detectors using surrogate phenomena (electrostatic pulses, later a rotating bar) could not resolve the regress because the adequacy of the surrogate itself became contested.

**Plant Perception and Parapsychology (Chapter 5).** Collins examines Cleve Backster's 1966 claim that plants exhibit "primary perception"—an ability to respond emotionally to human thoughts and to the destruction of living organisms nearby—detected via a polygraph attached to plant leaves. Collins himself attempted to replicate one of these experiments (the "brine shrimp" test, in which automated killing of shrimp was supposed to produce measurable galvanic responses in nearby plants) and found null results, though his own experience of replication reproduced precisely the difficulties the book theorizes. He pairs this with a study of Random Number Generator experiments in parapsychology, where experimenters claimed that subjects could psychokinetically influence electronic random outputs. The structural parallels with the gravity wave case are striking: the same patterns of disagreement over experimental competence, the same inability of replication to settle matters definitively, and the same eventual "closure" through social rather than purely experimental means. Collins uses physicist John Taylor's attempts to test paranormal metal-bending to illustrate the strategy of "surrogate phenomena": Taylor argued that if paranormal forces existed they must be electromagnetic (since electromagnetism was the only known force that could explain them), tested for electromagnetic effects, found none, and declared the paranormal nonexistent. Collins shows that this "breaks" the experimenters' regress only for those who already accept the electromagnetic interpretation—essentially, only committed skeptics.

**The Scotophobin / Memory Transfer Controversy (Chapter 6).** Though treated more briefly, Collins draws on David Travis's analysis of the "memory transfer" debate to illustrate his concept of "creating contradiction." Researchers injected brain extracts from trained rats into untrained rats, finding apparent transfer of learned dark-avoidance behavior. The chemical was labeled "Scotophobin." Collins shows that the *same data* could be interpreted as either a routine discovery (one more behavior-modifying drug) or a revolutionary finding (a "memory molecule" contradicting the central dogma of molecular biology). Proponents initially chose the conservative interpretation to make their findings more palatable, while critics deliberately adopted the radical interpretation as a *reductio ad absurdum*. This case demonstrates that scientific findings do not carry fixed implications; their meaning depends on how they are positioned within the wider conceptual network, and both proponents and opponents make strategic choices about how much to "rattle the web."

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"What the correct outcome is depends upon whether there are gravity waves hitting the Earth in detectable fluxes. To find this out we must build a good gravity wave detector and have a look. But we won't know if we have built a good detector until we have tried it and obtained the correct outcome\! But we don't know what the correct outcome is until ... and so on ad infinitum." (p. 84\)

"It is not the regularity of the world that imposes itself on our senses but the regularity of our institutionalized beliefs that imposes itself on the world." (p. 148\)

"The core set 'launders' all these 'non-scientific' influences and 'non-scientific' debating tactics. It renders them invisible because, when the debate is over, all that is left is the conclusion that one result was replicable and one was not." (pp. 143–144)

"'Distance lends enchantment': the more distant in social space or time is the locus of creation of knowledge the more certain it is." (Proposition Eleven, p. 145\)

"Scientists do not act dishonourably when they engage in the debates typical of core sets; there is nothing else for them to do if a debate is ever to be settled and if new knowledge is ever to emerge from the dispute. There is no realm of ideal scientific behaviour. Such a realm—the canonical model of science—exists only in our imaginations." (p. 143\)

"The core set gives methodological propriety to social contingency." (p. 144\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*Changing Order* pairs most directly with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (1985), which appeared the same year and similarly shows how the conventions of experimental replication are socially constructed rather than self-evident, though Shapin and Schaffer work historically rather than ethnographically. It complements and contrasts with Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life* (1979/1986), which shares the microsociological focus on knowledge production in scientific settings but employs a more anthropological-constructivist vocabulary; Collins's emphasis on the *social* resolution of disputes (through the "core set") differs from Latour's later actor-network theory, which distributes agency to nonhuman actants. The book also connects productively to Pickering's *Constructing Quarks* (1984), which Collins explicitly draws upon for examples of how prior theoretical commitments shape the closure of experimental controversies.

One limitation is that Collins's case studies are all drawn from physics and parapsychology in the Anglo-American context; the generalizability of the experimenters' regress to other scientific cultures, disciplines (particularly the biological and social sciences), and non-Western contexts remains an open question. A second critique, most forcefully articulated by Langdon Winner in "Upon Opening the Black Box and Finding it Empty" (1993), is that Collins's symmetrical relativism, while analytically powerful, risks evacuating the capacity to make normative judgments about the quality of scientific knowledge—a concern particularly pressing when scientific expertise enters public policy arenas, though Collins himself addresses this in his Postscript and later work on expertise.

# **Andrew Pickering, *Constructing Quarks: A Sociological History of Particle Physics* (1984)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Pickering argues that the reality of quarks and gauge theory was not discovered through passive observation of nature but was actively *constructed* through the developing social and conceptual practice of the high-energy physics (HEP) community between the 1960s and 1980s. Scientific judgments about whether to accept experimental findings as facts, and whether to regard particular theories as plausible, were irreducible choices structured by "opportunism in context"—the match between available material and intellectual resources and the research opportunities generated by symbiotic traditions of experiment and theory. The quark-gauge theory worldview emerged not because nature compelled it, but because it sustained a uniquely powerful and self-reinforcing constellation of research traditions that displaced the fragmented practices of the "old physics."

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Constructing Quarks* is a landmark contribution to the "relativist-constructivist" programme in the sociology of scientific knowledge (SSK), applying to an entire subfield of modern physics the kind of analysis that had previously been conducted only on individual experiments or controversies. Pickering explicitly challenges what he calls the "scientist's account"—the retrospective, realist narrative in which the history of particle physics appears as the progressive uncovering of nature's pre-existing structure, with experiments serving as neutral arbiters between theories and quarks simply waiting to be found. This narrative, Pickering argues, is self-defeating for the historian: it uses the *outcome* of a historical process to explain the process itself, and it renders invisible the scientific judgments—concerning the acceptability of experimental data and the plausibility of theories—that actually drove conceptual change. Against the scientist's account, Pickering positions himself alongside Harry Collins, Trevor Pinch, and the Edinburgh school's "Strong Programme" (Barry Barnes, David Bloor, Steven Shapin), arguing that the historian must abstain from realism about theoretical entities in order to explain rather than merely ratify the decisions scientists made. He engages directly with Kuhn's concept of incommensurability, arguing that the transition from the "old physics" to the "new physics" provides a concrete historical instance of Kuhnian world-change—though, contrary to Kuhn's general model, the transition occurred without significant acrimony. The book also intervenes in philosophy of science debates about underdetermination (Quine, Duhem) and the theory-ladenness of observation, grounding these abstract arguments in detailed historical reconstruction.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Pickering develops a model of "opportunism in context," drawing on SSK's relativist-constructivist programme: research traditions grow through the symbiosis of experimental and theoretical practice, where each tradition provides justification and subject matter for the other. Key concepts include the "symbiosis of research traditions," the "tuning" of experimental techniques to produce phenomena of interest, "modelling" or "analogy" as the engine of theory construction, and the social production of "incommensurable" worldviews. The framework treats scientific judgments as genuinely open choices that become stabilized through their integration into self-reinforcing networks of practice rather than through correspondence with nature.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Pickering draws on the published technical and popular literature of high-energy physics, conference proceedings (especially the biennial "Rochester" conferences), interviews with particle physicists, and detailed reconstruction of specific experiments and theoretical developments. The temporal scope runs from the mid-1940s prehistory of HEP through 1980, with the core narrative focused on 1964–1980. The geographic scope is international, centered on CERN, SLAC, Fermilab, Brookhaven, and DESY, tracking a transatlantic community of experimenters and theorists.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Discovery of the Weak Neutral Current (Gargamelle, 1973).** This is the book's set-piece case study, analyzed in extended detail in Section 6.5. During the 1960s, neutrino experimenters at CERN adopted interpretive practices consistent with V-A theory, which predicted no neutral currents; apparent neutral-current events were attributed to "neutron background"—neutrons produced outside the detector's active volume that could mimic genuine neutrino events. The key problem was that neutrons, like neutrinos, are electrically neutral and leave no tracks in a bubble chamber, so distinguishing between a genuine neutral-current event and a neutron-background event required interpretive judgment, not direct observation. In the 1960s, the Gargamelle experimenters' predecessors dealt with this ambiguity through spatial and energy cuts: events occurring near the edges of the chamber or with low visible energy were excluded as likely background. These cuts were pragmatic—reasonable but not demonstrably correct—and they produced the result that neutral currents did not exist. When the Gargamelle experiment began in the early 1970s, however, the theoretical landscape had changed dramatically. 't Hooft's 1971 proof of the renormalizability of gauge theory, and Weinberg and Salam's electroweak model (augmented by the GIM mechanism), created intense theoretical demand for the neutral current to exist. CERN theorists directly contacted the Gargamelle experimenters to stress the importance of searching for neutral currents. The Gargamelle group adopted *different* interpretive procedures from their predecessors, applying less restrictive background cuts. They found approximately 100 neutral-current candidates among 290,000 photographs—events that the earlier interpretive framework would have attributed to background. Pickering's point is not that one set of interpretive practices was "right" and the other "wrong," but that the choice between them was irreducible—neither set could be conclusively validated—and was structured by the evolving context of theoretical practice. The neutral current came into existence as an empirical phenomenon through a simultaneous shift in both experimental interpretation and theoretical expectation.

**The November Revolution and Charm (1974–1976).** In November 1974, two teams simultaneously announced the discovery of a narrow, massive particle: the J (at Brookhaven, by Samuel Ting's group) and the psi (at SLAC's SPEAR collider, by Burton Richter's group). This "J-psi" particle was extraordinary: at 3.1 GeV it was the heaviest particle then known, yet it was also anomalously long-lived (its decay width was roughly 2000 times narrower than expected). An army of theorists advanced competing explanations, but the interpretation that triumphed was "charm"—the idea, proposed years earlier by the GIM mechanism, that a fourth quark flavor existed whose hidden-charm bound states (charmonium) would exhibit precisely such anomalous longevity. The charmonium model, developed principally by Appelquist and Politzer at Harvard, explained the psis' longevity through a "super-Zweig rule" derived from QCD's asymptotic freedom, and then explicitly recycled the theoretical machinery of atomic physics (positronium, non-relativistic quantum mechanics, energy-level diagrams) to predict additional hidden-charm states. Pickering uses this episode to demonstrate how theory development proceeded through modelling and analogy—the trick was to see hadrons as quark composites in just the way atoms were composites of nuclei and electrons—and how the charm interpretation triumphed not because it was uniquely demanded by data, but because it connected to an enormously productive resource base of existing theoretical techniques and sustained a community-wide symbiosis of experiment and theory.

**Three Transitional Biographies (Gaillard, De Rújula, Ellis).** In a chapter bridging the "old" and "new" physics, Pickering traces the career trajectories of three theorists—Mary Gaillard, Alvaro De Rújula, and John Ellis—who entered HEP in the 1960s before gauge theory existed as a research tradition, yet became some of charm's most ardent advocates during the November Revolution. All three began in the current-algebra tradition, which unlike the dominant constituent quark model and Regge traditions never lost touch with its field-theory ancestry. Their paths to gauge theory were structured by local contexts of day-to-day practice: De Rújula gained perturbative field-theory experience through a QED calculation at CERN and was then drawn into neutrino scattering and the parton model; Ellis moved from current algebra into the operator product expansion and then asymptotic freedom; Gaillard's work on weak decays positioned her at the intersection of current algebra, the GIM mechanism, and charm phenomenology. Pickering uses these micro-level biographies to show that the macro-level transformation of HEP—the "epidemic" of gauge theory—can be understood through the same opportunism-in-context model that explains routine developments within established traditions.

**Global Incommensurability: Old Physics versus New Physics.** In his concluding chapter, Pickering presents a synoptic contrast between the "old" and "new" physics as incommensurable worldviews. The old physics focused on the most common HEP phenomena: hadron resonances at low energies and soft-scattering at high energies, studied with hadron beams and conventional detectors, interpreted through the constituent quark model and Regge theory. The new physics emphasized rare phenomena: weak neutral currents, hard scattering, charm production, studied through electron-positron annihilation, lepton beams, and purpose-built detectors designed to filter out old-physics processes. The phenomenal worlds were disjoint not only in their theoretical interpretation but at the most basic level of laboratory hardware: old-physics experiments actively smothered the rare phenomena of interest to the new physics, while new-physics experiments were constructed to render old-physics processes invisible. Each worldview was self-contained and self-referential, with its theories confirmed in its own phenomenal domain and disconfirmed outside it. The gauge-theory toolkit of the late 1970s would have had nil phenomenological utility if transmitted back to the mid-1960s experimental context, and vice versa.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The view taken here is that the reality of quarks was the upshot of particle physicists' practice, and not the reverse: hence the title of the book, *Constructing Quarks*." (Preface, p. x)

"In this book, the view will be that agency belongs to actors not phenomena: scientists make their own history, they are not the passive mouthpieces of nature." (p. 8\)

"Opportunism in context is the theme which runs through my historical account. I seek to explain the dynamics of practice in terms of the contexts within which researchers find themselves, and the resources which they have available for the exploitation of those contexts." (p. 12\)

"The explanation of a genuine decision cannot be found in a statement of what that decision was." (p. 404\)

"Without analogy, there would have been no new physics." (p. 407\)

"Each phenomenological world was, then, part of a self-contained, self-referential package of theoretical and experimental practice. To attempt to choose between old- and new-physics theories on the basis of a common set of phenomena was impossible: the theories were integral parts of different worlds, and they were incommensurable." (p. 411\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*Constructing Quarks* pairs naturally with Collins's *Changing Order* (1985), which provides the micro-sociological laboratory studies of replication and experimenters' regress that Pickering's macro-level analysis presupposes, and with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (1985), which performs a parallel constructivist analysis for an earlier foundational episode in the history of experimental science. Galison's *Image and Logic* (1997) covers much of the same terrain of twentieth-century particle physics but rejects Pickering's strong constructivism in favor of a "trading zone" model that preserves a role for material constraint, making the two books productive foils. Latour and Woolgar's *Laboratory Life* (1979) provides the ethnographic complement—studying construction at the bench level rather than through published literature. Pickering's analysis of theory development through modelling and analogy also resonates with Warwick's *Masters of Theory* (2003) on the role of pedagogical traditions in shaping theoretical practice.

A central limitation is that Pickering's reliance on published literature and interviews, rather than ethnographic observation, means that his reconstruction of the actual process of experimental judgment and "tuning" necessarily remains somewhat schematic—he tells us that interpretive choices were made but cannot always show how they were experienced and negotiated in real time. Additionally, critics (notably the "epistemological chicken" debate with Ian Hacking and others) have charged that the strong constructivist position creates an uncomfortable symmetry: if the historian must abstain from realism about quarks, the resulting account can appear to leave no grounds for distinguishing between better and worse scientific practices, an implication Pickering acknowledges but does not fully resolve.

# **Ruth Rogaski, *Knowing Manchuria: Environments, the Senses, and Natural Knowledge on an Asian Borderland* (2022)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Rogaski argues that Manchuria was never a singular, coherent place but a composite of multiple environments—mountains, grasslands, wetlands, forests—made legible through competing "ways of knowing" deployed by successive empires (Qing, Russian, Japanese, Chinese Republican, PRC). By tracing how diverse actors produced "natural knowledge" of these specific locales from the seventeenth through the twentieth century, she demonstrates that the creation of knowledge about nature and the creation of political territorial claims over Manchuria were inseparable processes, each enabling and shaping the other. The shift from early modern modes of knowing that pursued enchantment and precision simultaneously to modern scientific regimes of atomized, penetrating vision accompanied—and underwritten—Manchuria's transformation from a land of sacred dragon veins into a site of fossil fuel extraction, industrial agriculture, and biological warfare.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Knowing Manchuria* intervenes in multiple scholarly conversations. Against the dominant "science and empire" framework that centers European actors exploring non-European peripheries, Rogaski shows how Asian empires—Qing, Japanese, Korean—independently deployed natural knowledge toward projects of territorial consolidation and resource extraction, creating "nested imperialisms" where the categories of imperial and indigenous constantly shifted. She builds on Mark Elliott's foundational work on Manchuria as a constructed place ("The Limits of Tartary") but pushes beyond his focus on cartography and administration to argue that Manchuria was made through embodied, sensory engagement with specific local environments, not merely through maps and bureaucratic fiat. Against environmental histories of Manchuria by David Bello and Jonathan Schlesinger that emphasize commodity extraction (furs, ginseng, pearls), Rogaski traces a deeper shift in the very meaning of what lay beneath the ground—from geomantic dragon veins to fossil fuel deposits—a "cultural reconceptualization of invisible energetic value." She engages the history of science's "spatial turn" (Livingstone, Kohler) but insists on attending to both the nature of knowledge and the nature of place simultaneously. By expanding her analytical category from "science" to the broader frame of "natural knowledge" (following Cameron Strang and James Delbourgo), she places poetry, geomancy, Chinese materia medica, shamanic practice, and modern bacteriology on the same analytical plane, evaluated through the common lens of "regimes of attention"—different modes of deploying the human senses to engage the environment.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Rogaski operates at the intersection of environmental history and history of science, deploying a spatial-sensory approach she frames through the concept of "natural knowledge"—an expansive category encompassing all forms of instrumental knowledge about nature, from shamanic ritual to bacteriological investigation. Key theoretical resources include Tim Ingold's "dwelling perspective" (embodied knowledge from skilled practice within environments), Richard White's spatial analysis ("if space is the question, movement is the answer"), Pamela Crossley's critique of "the slough of hybridity" for borderland identities, and Paul Carter's notion of "spatial history" as nonlinear, assembled, and resistant to nationalist narratives. She introduces the concepts of "visceral knowledge" (knowing nature through its effects on the body) and "visceral territory" (the interior of bodies as terrain for scientific investigation).

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Rogaski draws on a remarkably multilingual archive spanning Chinese poetry and local gazetteers, Korean travelogues, Manchu shaman songs, Japanese scientific publications, Russian botanical treatises, European Jesuit accounts, PRC state farm records, and sent-down youth memoirs. She supplements textual analysis with digital humanities tools—particularly GIS-assisted viewshed analysis to reconstruct historical lines of sight—and with her own embodied fieldwork at key sites across contemporary northeast China. The temporal scope runs from the seventeenth century to the 1970s, with each chapter anchored in a distinct locale within Manchuria's diverse environmental zones.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Lost Mountain: Umuna's 1677 Expedition to Find Mount Paektu/Changbai (Chapter 3).** In one of the book's most striking episodes, the Kangxi emperor orders his clansman Umuna to locate and visually verify the sacred Manchu ancestral mountain—Golmin Šanggiyan Alin, the Long White Mountain. Astonishingly, when Umuna reads the emperor's edict to Manchu troops at the frontier outpost of Ningguta, no one can say where the mountain is; everyone has only "seen it from a distance." Rogaski uses viewshed analysis to explain this paradox: the dense forests of the Changbai range render the 9,000-foot stratovolcano invisible from nearly every approach, unlike Japan's Fuji or Greece's Olympus. The mountain only reveals itself in the final miles of ascent. The expedition's success allowed the exiled poet Wu Zhaoqian to compose his *Long White Mountain Rhapsody*, which daringly repositioned this Manchu mountain as the paramount sacred peak of all Chinese civilization—dethroning Taishan and anchoring the Qing dynasty's legitimacy in both Manchu sacred geography and Han mytho-historical time. This case study brilliantly demonstrates how the production of empirical, sensory knowledge (seeing the mountain) was inseparable from projects of political legitimation and cultural incorporation.

**The Jehol Biota: From Dragon Veins to Fossil Fuels (Chapter 5).** Rogaski traces how the same landscape of southwestern Manchuria was "read" through radically different regimes of attention across two centuries. For the Qing court, Jehol was "where dragon veins meet"—a geomantic axis channeling sacred energy from the Long White Mountain across the empire. The Kangxi emperor had noted the region's curious "stone fish" (shi yu) embedded in shale, but regarded them as objects of aesthetic wonder, not scientific specimens. By the twentieth century, those same stone fish had become *Lycoptera*, index fossils that could signal the presence of coal deposits. Through the careers of the British paleontologist Arthur Smith Woodward, the American geologist Amadeus Grabau (who meticulously measured the whorls of fossilized snail shells to establish new species of *Campeloma*), and the Japanese scientist Endō Ryūji (whose military-driven search for oil in war-torn Jehol accidentally yielded *Monjurosuchus splendens*, the "Shining Crocodile of Old Manchukuo"), Rogaski demonstrates that every early discovery of what is now one of the world's most spectacular fossil sites was directly entangled with imperial competition over Manchuria's subterranean mineral wealth. The transition from sensing dragon veins to locating coal seams constitutes what Rogaski provocatively calls an "energy transition"—not from one fuel source to another, but a cultural reconceptualization of the invisible energetic value held within the earth.

**Plagueland: Wu Lien-teh and the Ecology of Yersinia pestis (Chapter 6).** Rogaski reconstructs how Chinese and Japanese scientists created knowledge of plague in the Manchurian-Mongolian grasslands through a multi-scalar visual project: satellite-level views of the grassland ecosystem, ground-level epidemiological mapping of death through towns connected by railway, subterranean study of the burrowing rodent colonies that harbor Yersinia pestis, and microscopic and autopsy-based investigations of the pathogen's journey through mammalian bodies. Wu Lien-teh's North Manchurian Plague Prevention Service conducted extraordinary (and ethically fraught) experiments: placing guinea pigs in buckets beside dying plague patients to test airborne transmission; strapping live marmots into iron muzzles and spraying them with plague-laden aerosol; and culturing the expectorate of apparently healthy quarantined humans, which nonetheless killed guinea pigs upon injection. This research created the concept of asymptomatic carriers and mapped the "visceral territory" of Manchuria—the interior of bodies as terrain that Yersinia pestis could occupy. Rogaski connects this work directly to Unit 731's biological warfare program, arguing that the Japanese military's plague research was not a rupture from "normal" science but an intensification of the same border-crossing logic that linked animal and human bodies as interconnected environments. The chapter opens hauntingly with the Japanese mining engineer Yamagata Miyuki, who catalogued Manchuria's songbirds with loving precision while Chinese miners labored in the coal pits below, treating rats as life-saving "gods" who warned of cave-ins—an intimate ecology of human-animal entanglement that had its monstrous inversion in the weaponization of plague.

**Reclaimed: Technology and Embodied Knowledge on the Sanjiang Plain (Chapter 8).** The book's final substantive chapter follows the transformation of China's largest freshwater wetland—roughly the size of Tennessee—into the nation's most productive farmland under successive waves of Mao-era mobilization. Rogaski frames this as a "workscape" in which knowledge of Manchuria's nature was produced through labor itself. The botanist Zhang Yuliang, sent to survey the land in the dead of winter (because Party authorities demanded urgency over scientific sense), found a waterlogged delta unsuitable for mechanized farming—warnings the state ignored. What followed was a half-century of struggle between machines and mud: American tractors that sank in the wetlands before 1949, Soviet tractors that required soldiers to physically drag them free in the 1950s, and finally the sent-down youth of the Cultural Revolution who, when machines failed, were literally inserted into the mechanisms of industrial farming—weeding soybeans by hand in rows calibrated to tractor dimensions, threshing wheat with sickles alongside combines. Rogaski demonstrates how knowledge of Manchuria's nature was ultimately produced through the "muscular, intensely physical sacrifices" of a generation, whose memories of this labor became the foundation for a national narrative—the "Beidahuang spirit"—that both celebrates and obscures the human cost of environmental transformation.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"This study frames the construction of knowledge about Manchuria's natures as a kinesthetic project, emphasizing the knowledge produced by the senses as humans moved through particular terrain." (p. 14\)

"If we focus on the dynamic spaces where the human body and the environment meet, it is possible to see all forms of knowledge as stemming from different 'regimes of attention'—different modes of deploying the human senses in engagement with the environment." (p. 14\)

"Through attention to locales and to those who transcended borders, I see a multiplicity of roads. This study does not flinch from histories of imperialism and conquest, but in response to those histories, it strives to reclaim the full measure of human ways of connecting with the environment." (p. 19\)

"Manchuria was an assemblage created by empires, its many natures forced to cohere." (p. 11\)

"As Manchuria joined the 'century of coal,' the land of the Qing dragon vein was probed and plundered by men in search of carbon-based energy to build modern nations and fuel modern armies." (p. 229\)

"If Manchuria today is a known space, its nature cataloged and understood, its landscape indigenized and made a central part of the national narrative of China, it is because of the memories of those who labored there." (p. 336\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Rogaski's work pairs productively with Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison's *Objectivity* (2007), which traces evolving visual regimes in science—Rogaski extends this analysis to non-Western and non-scientific modes of knowing, showing how "ways of seeing" nature were always politically constituted across multiple knowledge traditions. The book's attention to how extractive empires produce knowledge of environments resonates strongly with Gregg Mitman's *Empire of Rubber* (2021), another work on this reading list, though Rogaski's multi-imperial Asian frame decenters the Western/colonial binary. Paul Edwards' *The Closed World* (1996) offers a useful counterpoint in its analysis of how Cold War computational infrastructure produced knowledge of the globe, whereas Rogaski shows how embodied, sensory, and local knowledge persisted as essential (if often suppressed) modes of environmental understanding even within modern scientific regimes.

One limitation is that the book's deliberately disaggregated, multisited structure—each chapter set in a different locale and time period—occasionally sacrifices narrative momentum and makes the connections between chapters feel more thematic than causal. Additionally, while Rogaski persuasively recovers indigenous and non-elite "ways of knowing," the archival record inevitably privileges the perspectives of literate elites and scientific professionals, leaving the embodied knowledge of hunters, foragers, and laborers more inferred than documented.

# Week 5 — Technoscience

# **John Krige, *American Hegemony and the Postwar Reconstruction of Science in Europe* (MIT Press, 2006\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

The United States used its overwhelming postwar scientific and technological superiority—alongside economic leverage, political support, and philanthropic funding—to reshape European science in its own image as part of a broader project of informal empire-building. This "consensual hegemony" was *coproduced*: influential European scientific elites collaborated willingly (if not always enthusiastically) in adopting American models of research organization, because they desperately needed resources to rebuild and because they were given enough latitude to adapt U.S. proposals to local conditions.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Krige inserts the history of science into a conversation that diplomatic and political historians (Charles Maier, Melvyn Leffler, Geir Lundestad, John Lewis Gaddis) have long conducted about the nature of American power in postwar Europe—but which historians of science had almost entirely ignored. While scholars like Daniel Kevles and Ronald Doel had studied science's relationship to the national security state, they focused on superpower rivalry and largely left Western Europe out of the picture. Krige argues that they also failed to take seriously the *asymmetry of power* that structured scientific internationalism after 1945\. Doel and Joseph Manzione had noted that the U.S. "shared" science to promote American values, but Krige finds this language too anodyne: what was happening was not mere sharing but a deliberate attempt to reconfigure European scientific landscapes—reorienting research communities outward toward Anglo-American norms, breaking up what Americans saw as parochial and hierarchical continental traditions, and enlisting basic science as a tool of Cold War strategy. Krige also contributes to scholarship on foundations (building on Robert Kohler's "intimate partnership" model and Pnina Abir-Am's emphasis on asymmetric power) by showing how Rockefeller and Ford Foundation grants to European science functioned as instruments of hegemony, not simply patronage. Against historians who treat American support for European science as benevolent or neutral, Krige insists on reading it through the framework of informal empire—while equally insisting, against crude anti-American readings, that Europeans were active participants in shaping this hegemonic order, not passive victims of it.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Krige draws on the concept of "consensual hegemony" (Maier) and "coproduction" (borrowed from STS, particularly Sheila Jasanoff) to analyze how American power operated through persuasion, financial leverage, and shared values rather than coercion. He also engages Ann Stoler's concept of imperial formations as fluid, ambiguous "states of becoming" rather than fixed structures, and Jonathan Zeitlin's framework for understanding "Americanization" as a contested process of selective appropriation producing "innovative hybrids."

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Krige uses an impressive range of multi-national archival sources: the Rockefeller Foundation Archives, Ford Foundation Archives, NATO Archives (Brussels), CERN Archives (Geneva), the Niels Bohr Institute Archive (Copenhagen), the U.S. National Archives, the Library of Congress, the French National Archives, and the MIT Archives, among others. The book covers roughly 1945–1963, focused on Western Europe (especially France, Germany, Denmark, and Italy) and draws primarily on institutional correspondence, internal memos, diary entries of foundation officers, committee reports, and policy documents.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Rockefeller Foundation and the French CNRS (chapters 4–5).** This is the book's richest and most detailed case study. Warren Weaver, the Foundation's director of Natural Sciences, used the devastation of French science during the war as an opening to reshape the community along American lines: decentralized, competitive, English-speaking, and internationally oriented. His intermediary was Louis Rapkine, a Franco-Russian biologist who had helped French scientists during the war and who shuttled between Paris and New York, reassuring the French that the Foundation would respect their autonomy while simultaneously ensuring that Weaver's agenda would be accepted as if it were the French community's own idea. The Foundation's equipment grant ($250,000) was dispersed to provincial universities rather than concentrated in Paris—deliberately designed to break the centralized monopoly of the capital—and supplemented by conference grants that brought Anglo-American scientists to France to redirect French research toward "the most fruitful lines." Krige shows how this relationship exemplifies coproduced hegemony: the French desperately needed the equipment and welcomed the money, but the conditions attached to it served Weaver's vision of what French science should become. The case becomes even more revealing when the Cold War intervenes. In the Ephrussi affair, Weaver was forced to decide whether the geneticist Boris Ephrussi—who worked with Georges Teissier, a known Communist and sometime defender of Lysenkoist ideas—should continue to receive Rockefeller funding. Weaver's investigation uncovered the deep entanglement of Cold War politics with scientific patronage: foundation officers began recording the political affiliations of French scientists in their diaries, debating whether to share files with the FBI, and developing the epistemological argument that Communists *a priori* could not do objective science—neatly inverting responsibility for introducing political criteria into grant decisions.

**Isidor Rabi, CERN, and the Reintegration of Germany (chapter 3).** Columbia physicist I. I. Rabi used a 1950 UNESCO meeting in Florence to propose a European nuclear physics laboratory. Krige shows this was not simply an act of scientific generosity but a calculated move that served multiple U.S. interests. CERN would channel European physics into unclassified high-energy research (keeping Europeans busy with mesons rather than nuclear weapons), reintegrate West Germany into the Western scientific community on terms analogous to the Marshall Plan, include Tito's Yugoslavia to drive a wedge into the Soviet bloc, and standardize European experimental practices around American-style accelerator physics. The laboratory was deliberately modeled on Brookhaven National Laboratory, where governments (instead of universities) pooled resources. Crucially, Rabi promoted a laboratory *without* a nuclear reactor—specifically to undermine French-led plans for a joint European nuclear venture that would have included a pile with potential military applications.

**The Ford Foundation, Shepard Stone, and the Intellectual Cold War (chapter 6).** Stone, a Wilsonian internationalist who had served under U.S. High Commissioner James McCloy in occupied Germany, used Ford's rapidly expanding resources (assets of $492 million by 1950\) to wage an intellectual Cold War in Europe. His grants to CERN and the Niels Bohr Institute were explicitly designed to showcase Western scientific achievement, promote "Atlanticism," and combat neutralism among European intellectuals. The case of the Bohr Institute is particularly striking: Niels Bohr, who had famously championed open scientific internationalism, accepted Stone's stipulation that initial Ford fellowships at his institute should not go to scientists from Communist countries. Bohr even discussed the terms of the grant with Stone and the CIA. When U.S. policy shifted toward engagement with the Soviet Union and China, Bohr adjusted accordingly. Krige leaves open the unsettling question of whether Bohr shared these Cold War views, was being pragmatic, or was simply unable to refuse money his institute desperately needed.

**The Failed "European MIT" (chapter 7).** MIT president James Killian, working through the NATO Science Committee after the Sputnik shock of 1957, proposed creating an International Institute of Science and Technology (IIST) modeled on MIT—interdisciplinary, industry-linked, fusing engineering with science, and training "manpower for freedom." The project was intended as a "transforming institution" that would establish "the tradition of the future" and serve as a model not just for Europe but for the developing world. It was enthusiastically supported by small countries and by Italy but was ultimately killed by a combination of British and French resistance. The British feared it would drain their universities of top faculty just as national reforms were under way. The French, under de Gaulle, were willing to participate in an international institute only if the United States was excluded. This case powerfully illustrates the *limits* of consensual hegemony: once European economies recovered and national reform efforts gained momentum, it became easier—even necessary—to say no to American proposals that threatened indigenous development.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Given the 'basic inequality of resources' for science between the two sides of the Atlantic . . . is it not to be expected that any system of U.S.-European scientific and technological linkages established after the war were also part and parcel of an 'international structure analogous to empire'?" (p. 2\)

"The American empire that emerged was the negotiated outcome of a complex process in which European partners selectively appropriated and adapted features of the U.S. agenda and ambitions for the Continent and made them their own." (pp. 4–5)

"American assistance for the postwar reconstruction of science in Europe was not something that happened parallel to, and independently of, the construction of empire: it was part and parcel of the same project and cannot be fully understood without it." (p. 256\)

"The American hegemonic enterprise was always in danger of being undermined by a deep-seated internal contradiction. It was intended to make the world safe for democracy by containing the Communist threat, but many European intellectuals enrolled in the project felt that the threat was exaggerated and certainly not worth jettisoning some of their most time-honored values." (p. 261\)

"European science was not 'Americanized': this term is too cumbersome and too analytically crude to be of much use. But we can say that America's scientific accomplishments remained an omnipresent point of reference and a constant source of pressure for change in Europe." (p. 269\)

"As Rabi put it in an interview in 1973, 'You see it used to be very important to us to talk to the British. When the British declined in influence we lost somebody to talk to. . . . But if Europe ever became something, we'd have civilized people to talk to.'" (p. 270\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Krige pairs naturally with several works across all three reading lists. Jasanoff's *States of Knowledge* provides the theoretical vocabulary of "coproduction" that Krige adapts for his hegemony framework—but where Jasanoff focuses on the mutual constitution of science and social order within states, Krige extends it to international power relations. Light's *From Warfare to Welfare* covers the same postwar period from the American domestic side, showing how defense intellectuals brought Cold War methods into urban planning—the mirror image of Krige's story about exporting those methods to Europe. Erickson et al., *How Reason Almost Lost Its Mind*, overlaps directly on Cold War rationality and the NATO Science Committee, and the operations research chapters connect with Krige's chapter on Philip Morse's promotion of OR in NATO.

**Limitations:** The case study approach, as Krige himself acknowledges, does not produce an integrated narrative—each chapter illuminates a different facet of the hegemonic project without a clear through-line connecting them. More significantly, the book tells us far more about American intentions and institutional maneuvering than about how European scientific practices actually changed (or didn't) as a result—the "Americanization" question that the concluding chapter raises is suggestive but underdeveloped.

# **Eden Medina, *Cybernetic Revolutionaries: Technology and Politics in Allende's Chile* (MIT Press, 2011\)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Medina argues that Project Cybersyn—a real-time computer network for managing Chile's nationalized economy built between 1971 and 1973—reveals how technology and politics are mutually constitutive: the Allende government's socialist project shaped the design of the system, while the system's design in turn exposed the contradictions, class prejudices, and competing visions within Chile's revolutionary process. She demonstrates that embedding political values in technological systems is extraordinarily difficult because those values depend on fragile social and organizational arrangements that can easily be reconfigured to serve radically different ends.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Medina intervenes in several overlapping scholarly conversations. Against the history of computing's heavy focus on the United States and Western Europe, she insists that nations outside the economic and technological "center" had distinctive, innovative experiences with computers shaped by their specific political contexts—Chile built something in 1971–73 that neither Britain nor the Soviet Union accomplished. Against simplified narratives linking cybernetics exclusively to Cold War command-and-control (as in Paul Edwards's *Closed World*), she shows that cybernetics took radically different forms depending on national context, building on Ronald Kline's "disunity of cybernetics" thesis. She also pushes beyond the social construction of technology (SCOT) tradition: rather than revealing hidden politics within an ostensibly neutral technology, she starts from a situation where politics were explicit and ever-present, then uses the technology to "open the black box of politics" itself—showing how Project Cybersyn illuminates the internal fissures of Allende's coalition, the tension between revolution from above and revolution from below, and the persistence of class and gender hierarchies even within a revolutionary project. She positions herself alongside Gabrielle Hecht (*Radiance of France*), Edwards, and Ken Alder, scholars who have studied moments when governments explicitly used technology to advance political goals, but extends their framework to the Global South and to a case where the political project itself was deeply contested.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Medina develops the concept of "sociotechnical engineering"—the deliberate design of a technology *and* the social and organizational relationships surrounding it to uphold a particular configuration of power. She draws on Thomas Hughes's large technical systems (LTS) framework and the "seamless web" concept, Langdon Winner's question about the politics of artifacts, and Andrew Pickering's account of British cybernetics as "performative" rather than representational. Her key analytical move is showing that political values cannot be durably inscribed in hardware alone; they depend on maintaining specific social arrangements around the technology.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Medina draws on a remarkably diverse archive: sixteen boxes of Stafford Beer's papers at Liverpool John Moores University, personal archives of several project participants, Chilean government documents from CORFO and ECOM, over fifty interviews conducted across eight countries between 2001 and 2010, plus newspaper accounts, design drawings, computer printouts, and even folksong lyrics. The scope is Chile from roughly 1970–1973 (with extensions to Beer's earlier career and the post-coup afterlives of participants), with important British and transnational dimensions.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Operations Room and Its Chair.** The most vivid case study is the design of the Cybersyn operations room—a hexagonal space with seven fiberglass chairs arranged in a circle, screens projecting economic data via back-lit slides, and no paper or tables allowed. Beer and the Chilean industrial design team led by Gui Bonsiepe embedded political values at every turn: the circular seating prevented hierarchy; an odd number of chairs ensured no tied votes; geometric "big-hand" buttons replaced keyboards because keyboards were associated with female secretarial labor and would alienate male factory workers and politicians. Beer wanted the room to be accessible to workers, not a "sanctum sanctorum for a government elite." Yet these same design choices revealed the revolution's blind spots: the chair was designed for a masculine user who would "thump" buttons to emphasize a point, and the exclusion of keyboards implicitly excluded female clerical workers from the category of "worker." The room's futuristic appearance also concealed enormous hidden labor—four female graphic design students from the Catholic University drew every chart and graph displayed in the room by hand, with no plans for automation. The room projected an image of socialist modernity that was, in Medina's telling, both genuinely innovative and deeply dependent on invisible, gendered work.

**The October 1972 Strike.** When 40,000 truck owners and allied business associations struck to paralyze the Chilean economy in October 1972, the telex network originally built for Cybersyn became a lifeline for the Allende government. Fernando Flores proposed repurposing the network to create a command system linking the presidential palace to specialized centers for transportation, industry, energy, and supply. Gustavo Silva, working at the energy command center, used the telex to track oil distribution trucks in real time: "We knew exactly how many trucks we needed, so each time we lost one we were able to requisition another." The network connected the government's top-down command ("the revolution from above") to the improvisational factory-floor activity of workers maintaining production ("the revolution from below"), enabling the redistribution of fuel, raw materials, and spare parts across the country. The government survived the strike—a widely recognized turning point in the Allende period—in significant part because the Cybersyn network made rapid coordination possible. This case powerfully demonstrates Medina's claim that technologies can shape political history by making certain actions possible, even when those technologies are incomplete or repurposed from their original design.

**Worker Participation and the Factory Modeling Process.** Medina's account of how Cybersyn engineers modeled factory production processes is one of the book's sharpest critical passages. Beer instructed engineers to create quantified production models "with the help and the agreement of workers' committees." In practice, the young university-trained engineers—most on their first job—treated the modeling as a purely technical exercise. Tomás Kohn recalled that his team was "fairly arrogant" and suspected they "pissed off" several managers; they found it easier to frame the project as technocratic rather than political. Workers were largely excluded. The timing charts used to model the Easton Furniture factory closely resembled Taylorist time studies, the very management practice that Chilean workers had struck against in the 1960s. Medina observes that while the Allende government sought to empower workers, Cybersyn risked giving management control over labor through an abstract technological system rather than a visible shop floor manager with a stopwatch—echoing David Noble's argument in *Forces of Production* about numerical control technology. This case reveals how class prejudice and technocratic habit persisted within the revolutionary project and how the technical sophistication of a system can itself prevent the participation it claims to enable.

**International Readings and the "Big Brother" Problem.** When Beer went public with Cybersyn at the 1973 Goodman Lecture in Brighton and in *New Scientist*, the system was immediately read through Cold War lenses. Joseph Hanlon of *New Scientist* called Beer a "technological power broker" and the system "one of the most powerful weapons in history." Beer telexed his Chilean colleagues that invitations to build comparable systems came from Brazil and South Africa—both ruled by repressive governments—while accusations came from Britain and the United States. Medina uses this episode to demonstrate a core finding: the political values Beer tried to embed in Cybersyn depended on social and organizational safeguards (like the protocol giving factories time to self-correct before CORFO intervened), but these safeguards were fragile. Since CORFO staffers were notified of production anomalies from the outset, "dismantling one of the primary safeguards of that autonomy might have been as easy as having someone from the telex room walk down the hall." The same hardware, placed in a different political context, could easily serve authoritarian control. This case illustrates the central difficulty of sociotechnical engineering: political values live in organizational arrangements, not in the machines themselves.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"This book tells the history of two intersecting utopian visions, one political and one technological. The first was an attempt to implement socialist change peacefully and through existing democratic institutions. The second was an attempt to build a computer system for real-time economic control more than twenty years before the Internet became a feature of everyday life." (p. 1\)

"Technologies are historical texts. When we read them, we are able to read history." (p. 12\)

"I use the history of a technical system to open this black box of politics, just as I use politics to open this black box of technology." (p. 10\)

"It would have been much harder for workers to organize against an abstract technological system, or a factory model, than to stage a protest against a visible production manager holding a stopwatch." (p. 155\)

"Dismantling one of the primary safeguards of that autonomy might have been as easy as having someone from the telex room walk down the hall." (p. 198\)

"Although Allende believed that Chile would have a revolution with 'red wine and empanadas,' this assertion failed to account for the actual complexity that the process entailed. In the same way, the clean, futuristic appearance of the control room obscured the vast network of individuals, materials, expertise, and information required to make economic management appear simple." (p. 143\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Medina pairs naturally with Paul Edwards's *The Closed World* (the U.S. military-cybernetics complex she deliberately contrasts), David Noble's *Forces of Production* (the parallel between numerical control disempowering American machinists and Cybersyn's potential to disempower Chilean workers through abstraction), and Benjamin Peters's *How Not to Network a Nation* (the Soviet failure to build a national computer network for economic management, which makes Cybersyn's partial success all the more striking). Jennifer Light's *From Warfare to Welfare* is also a strong pairing, since Medina explicitly discusses how U.S. defense intellectuals' systems analysis migrated to urban planning—the same trajectory Light traces—and positions Chilean cybernetics as a fundamentally different approach to the same problem of managing complex social systems.

One limitation is that Medina acknowledges but cannot fully remedy the near-total absence of worker voices: no factory workers responded to her newspaper advertisement seeking memories of the project, and her account of what Cybersyn meant on the shop floor remains largely inferential. A second is that the book's rich detail on design and engineering sometimes comes at the cost of sustained engagement with Chilean political history—the internal dynamics of the Popular Unity coalition, for instance, are sketched rather than deeply analyzed, and readers unfamiliar with Chilean politics may find the political stakes somewhat abstract.

# **Jennifer S. Light, *From Warfare to Welfare: Defense Intellectuals and Urban Problems in Cold War America* (2003)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

During the three decades following World War II, civilian scientists and social scientists employed by the defense establishment—"defense intellectuals"—transferred military techniques and technologies (systems analysis, computer simulations, satellite reconnaissance, cable communications) to American city planning and management. While these collaborations created powerful professional networks and institutional alliances, they almost never delivered on their promises to improve urban life for ordinary city residents. The lasting significance of this episode lies not in technological success but in the creation and maintenance of an urban "power elite" whose technocratic framing of urban problems has persisted to the present day.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Light's book intervenes simultaneously in American urban history and the history of Cold War science and technology. Urban historians—Jackson, Sugrue, Teaford—had told the postwar story through the lenses of suburbanization, white flight, deindustrialization, and failed renewal programs, with almost no attention to the role of the military-industrial-academic complex in shaping how cities were administered. Historians of Cold War science—Leslie, Galison, Edwards—had traced the militarization of academic research and the rise of big science, but their accounts stopped at the edge of the city. Light inserts a missing chapter into both literatures. She argues that the standard narrative of American urban development requires a new set of actors: the defense intellectuals from RAND, SDC, MITRE, and aerospace firms who became advisers to city governments, and whose influence on how Americans *conceptualize* cities and their problems has outlasted any specific technology they transferred. Against conventional histories of "cybercities" that trace urban information systems to postmodern theory (Baudrillard, Boyer), Light argues these developments are better understood through the concrete history of military war games at RAND and MIT. She also pushes back against simple "militarization of everyday life" arguments—the effects on average city dwellers were negligible; what changed was the organizational and intellectual apparatus of urban governance itself.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Light draws on history of technology (technology transfer, social construction of technology), sociology of public problems (claims-making, problem definition), and Cold War studies (military-industrial-academic complex). Her key conceptual move is treating urban problem-solving as a site of *technology transfer* from military to civilian contexts, analyzing not just the movement of hardware but the migration of experts, institutional models, and rhetorical frameworks—above all, the cybernetic worldview that recast cities as information-processing systems amenable to command-and-control management.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Archival research in the records of RAND, the Los Angeles City Archives, and the New York City Municipal Archives (especially the John Lindsay collection), supplemented by trade publications (American City, Nation's Cities, Public Administration Review), federal program documents, conference proceedings, oral histories with participants (Harry Finger, Henry Rowen, Laurence Lynn), and evaluation research literature. Geographic scope centers on New York City and Los Angeles, with supporting cases in Pittsburgh, Dayton, and several California cities, from World War II through approximately 1975\.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Community Renewal Program in Pittsburgh (early 1960s).** Pittsburgh was the pioneering case of bringing military computer simulations to city administration. When Mayor Joseph Barr hired Calvin Hamilton to direct the planning department (Hamilton had worked on Project East River, a war game simulating atomic attack on New York City), Hamilton contracted out the analytic work to CONSAD Research Corporation, a think tank composed of former RAND, Lockheed, Hughes, and Douglas Aircraft employees who had relocated from Santa Monica to Pittsburgh. Under Wilbur Steger (former head of RAND's Logistics Simulation Laboratory) and Ira "Jack" Lowry, CONSAD built computer models of the city's housing stock, employment patterns, and demographics. But the simulations struggled with the fundamental problem of goal-setting: social welfare goals like "eradicating blight" could not be quantified with the precision that military objectives required. Multiple goals had to be reduced to a single "supergoal," and planners and model-builders clashed over what it should be. As Charles Leven observed, this clash of cultures between traditional urban professionals and the new systems experts made effective model-building nearly impossible. Pittsburgh became both the proof of concept and the cautionary tale for every city that followed.

**The New York City RAND Institute (1968–1975).** Mayor John Lindsay, determined to modernize city government, signed contracts with RAND in 1968 and then opened the New York City RAND Institute in 1969 as a joint city–think tank operation, explicitly modeled on RAND's relationship with the Air Force. The Institute's first director, Peter Szanton, was a former "whiz kid" from McNamara's Defense Department; Ira Lowry moved from Pittsburgh to head housing research; David McGarvey, who had worked on RAND's original dispersal team, ran health services. Staff circulated easily between defense and urban work—Robert Levine's publications ranged from *A Simulation Model of Air Force Maintenance Operations* (1958) to *Two Models of the Urban Crisis* (1970). Lindsay hoped the Institute would bring "the kind of streamlined, modern management thinking that Robert McNamara applied in the Pentagon." RAND consultants applied PPBS to police, fire, housing, and welfare operations. Some narrow efficiency gains were documented (fire department dispatch, police response times), but complex social welfare problems remained untouched. The Institute generated controversy: critics charged RAND consultants were overpaid and produced few visible results for residents; others feared surveillance and technocratic control. When Abraham Beame replaced Lindsay as mayor, the Institute was shut down in 1975, and most staff returned to Santa Monica.

**Remote Sensing and the Los Angeles Community Analysis Bureau (late 1960s–1970s).** After the Watts riots of 1965, Mayor Yorty created the Community Analysis Bureau (CAB) as a new city agency dedicated to applying military analytic tools to community renewal and urban blight. CAB staff explicitly described the agency in cybernetic terms—as a "thermostat" monitoring the city's temperature and adjusting policy accordingly. When NASA-funded researchers from Northwestern University demonstrated that color infrared aerial photography (using sensors designed for future satellite reconnaissance) could assess housing quality at the block level, CAB adopted these photointerpretation techniques for housing surveys. The bureau developed its own Land Use and Property Automated Management System (LUPAMS) using aerial survey data. But the hoped-for integration of satellite data never materialized: city governments had no use for satellite-resolution imagery, the costs of maintaining the systems outstripped municipal budgets, and the elaborate information infrastructure never delivered the promised transformation in community development. As CAB director Robert Joyce acknowledged, getting astronauts to the moon was a "comparatively simple task" next to solving "earthly problems" through aerospace innovations.

**Cable Television as Cold War Technology (1966–1975).** During the FCC's freeze on cable expansion into urban markets (1966–1972), an unlikely coalition—defense intellectuals at RAND, MITRE, and MIT; media activists from counterculture collectives like Raindance; civil rights advocates; and federal officials—converged around the vision of cable as a tool for urban community development. At its core was development theorist Ithiel de Sola Pool (MIT), whose earlier work on political stability and modernization in developing countries translated directly into his vision of cable as a technology for reducing ghetto alienation through two-way communication, minority narrowcasting, and decentralized social service delivery. MITRE developed TICCIT (Time-Shared, Interactive, Computer-Controlled Information Television) for a demonstration in Reston, Virginia. RAND produced extensive policy research on cable and community participation. The boundary between defense institutions and liberal activism proved remarkably permeable—Ralph Lee Smith of the Kerner Commission wrote for both the Nation and MITRE; Monroe Price consulted for both the United Church of Christ and RAND. Yet by the late 1970s, when cable operators assumed control of wiring cities, entertainment and profit displaced every vision of cable as a civic technology. Public-access channels, mandated during the freeze era, survived as artifacts of a specific historical moment—analogous, Light suggests, to the QWERTY keyboard persisting long after its original rationale disappeared.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"How and why are resources allocated time and again to support the adoption of technical and technological tools whose benefits remain unproven?" (p. vii)

"The lasting significance of this episode for U.S. urban history instead lies in its creation and maintenance of an urban 'power elite' whose influence on the ways Americans conceptualize cities and their problems has persisted to the present day." (p. 8\)

"The 'enemy' assumes many guises, including slum housing, poverty, and unequal opportunity . . . the City's existing housing and renewal programs, the extent to which they can be adapted to change, and both fiscal and human resources, represent our 'forces.'" (William Ballard, NYC Planning Commission, p. 65\)

"Any seeing sentient person . . . had to acknowledge that smoothly operating data flows within and among city departments . . . were not remedying the nation's complex urban problems." (Abe Gottlieb, Philadelphia city planner, p. 85\)

"I see many people who might otherwise be on the unemployment rolls engaged in organizing groups to promote technology transfer. . . . I still have the same old garbage truck, the same street sweeper that doesn't work." (William Donaldson, Cincinnati city manager, p. 154\)

"The rhetoric of involvement of the poor, combined with studied control of the poor, has led to ineffectiveness, to a substitution of rhetoric for basic social change." (Kenneth Clark and Jeannette Hopkins, p. 84\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Light's book pairs powerfully with Paul Edwards's *The Closed World* (1996), which traces how Cold War computing and cybernetic discourse shaped military thinking—Light effectively extends Edwards's framework from the Pentagon to the planning department. Eden Medina's *Cybernetic Revolutionaries* (2011) offers a comparative case of cybernetic governance in Allende's Chile, where similar questions about centralization, participation, and technocratic control played out in an entirely different political context. Margaret O'Mara's *Cities of Knowledge* (2005) shares Light's interest in Cold War institutions reshaping urban landscapes, though O'Mara focuses on the physical form of research parks rather than the transfer of management techniques to city governance.

Light's focus on NYC and LA, while compelling, means the book says relatively little about how these transfers played out (or didn't) in smaller cities, or how race shaped the reception of military techniques at the grassroots level beyond elite rhetoric. The book also acknowledges but does not deeply explore the question of *why* the pattern of adopting unproven technologies persisted—the institutional incentives are clear, but the political economy of failure (who benefited from continued investment in tools that didn't work) could be pushed further.

# **Sheila Jasanoff, ed., *States of Knowledge: The Co-Production of Science and Social Order* (Routledge, 2004\)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

The ways in which we know and represent the world are inseparable from the ways in which we choose to live in it. Scientific knowledge is neither a transparent mirror of nature nor an epiphenomenon of political interests; rather, natural and social orders are produced together through ongoing, mutually constitutive processes that Jasanoff calls "co-production." The book argues that understanding this simultaneous making of knowledge and social order — through identities, institutions, discourses, and representations — is indispensable for any serious analysis of power, culture, and political change.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*States of Knowledge* intervenes in several overlapping debates. Within STS, it pushes back against the "social construction" framing dominant since the Edinburgh school and the Strong Programme (Bloor, Collins), which risked giving causal primacy to "the social" over "the natural." It equally rejects technological and scientific determinism.
