The volume positions co-production as a synthesis that transcends the standoff of the 1990s "science wars" between realists and constructivists, offering a framework that is symmetrical — attending to how science shapes society just as much as the reverse. Against Latour's actor-network theory, which Jasanoff credits with usefully dissolving the nature/society boundary but faults for flattening out differences of power, human imagination, and cultural specificity, the volume insists on attending to inequality, national political cultures, and normative commitments. Jasanoff also challenges the traditional social sciences — particularly rational-choice political science and neoclassical economics — for lacking the vocabulary to analyze how knowledge-making and state-making are intertwined. The book explicitly bridges the gap between micro-level laboratory studies (the core of much STS work) and macro-level political and social theory, arguing that STS has been too timid about speaking to questions of power, governance, and democracy. The volume is in direct dialogue with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*, James Scott's *Seeing Like a State*, Benedict Anderson's *Imagined Communities*, Foucault's work on knowledge/power, and Mary Douglas's *How Institutions Think*.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

The book advances co-production as an "idiom" — not a rigid theory but an interpretive lens. Jasanoff delineates two strands: the *constitutive* (concerned with how new sociotechnical objects and ontological categories emerge and stabilize, drawing on metaphysics) and the *interactional* (concerned with how competing knowledge claims are resolved within existing social orders, drawing on epistemology). Co-production operates through four "ordering instruments": making identities, making institutions, making discourses, and making representations. These are the pathways by which natural and social orders become entangled and stabilized.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

This is an edited volume with twelve empirical chapters spanning the seventeenth century to the present, drawing on historical archives, ethnographic fieldwork, legal transcripts, institutional documents, and policy analysis. Case studies range geographically from early modern Europe to Cold War America, from colonial West Indies to contemporary Africa and the European Union. The methodological orientation is interpretive, qualitative, and contextual — committed to thick description over statistical generalization.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Clark Miller on the IPCC and global climate science (Ch. 3).** Miller shows how the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change simultaneously constructed a scientific object — climate as a global system rather than aggregated local weather — and a political order — the framework of global environmental governance. The IPCC's reframing of climate from a local weather problem into a planetary risk required building new institutions whose authority could credibly span the globe. The IPCC didn't just reflect pre-existing scientific consensus and then hand it to policymakers; it actively co-produced the science and the politics together, developing sophisticated boundary-drawing techniques to separate its "scientific" conclusions from "policy" recommendations. This boundary work, however, created its own vulnerabilities: when the Bush administration rejected the Kyoto Protocol in 2001, it could accept the IPCC's science while exercising sovereign prerogative to reject its political implications — precisely because the IPCC had been so effective at insisting on the science/policy divide. Miller's chapter also reveals the institutional politics behind the IPCC's founding, as the World Meteorological Organization, UNEP, and national governments fought over whether the panel should be a small expert body (like the ozone negotiations) or a government-controlled review process. This is an excellent case for showing how the scale at which nature is imagined (local weather vs. global climate system) necessarily implies a corresponding political order.

**John Carson on intelligence testing in France and America (Ch. 10).** Carson compares how France and the United States took the same instrument — the Binet intelligence test, developed in Paris around 1905 — and produced radically different scientific objects and social orders from it. In France, intelligence testing remained a clinical tool, limited to identifying pathological conditions, and was kept modest by a republican political culture that was uncomfortable with ranking citizens by innate mental capacity and by a rival tradition of clinical assessment that emphasized qualitative, holistic judgment. In the United States, Lewis Terman's Stanford-Binet revision (1916) transformed intelligence into a single quantifiable number — the IQ — claimed to be innate, hereditary, and predictive of life success. Army Alpha testing in World War I then scaled this instrument to 1.75 million soldiers, producing the sensational (and misleading) finding that the average American had a mental age of thirteen. After the war, intelligence testing became an industry embedded in schools, businesses, and the military, underwriting a meritocratic ideology that explained social hierarchy as the natural expression of differential cognitive capacity. Carson's point is that the "same" scientific knowledge — the measurement of intelligence — was co-produced with two different social orders: French republicanism, which resisted ranking citizens on a single hereditary scale, and American progressivism, which embraced precisely such ranking as the foundation of efficient, meritocratic governance. Neither outcome was determined by the science alone.

**Waterton and Wynne on the European Environment Agency (Ch. 5).** The EEA, founded in 1993, was tasked with providing "objective" environmental information for European policymaking — but not allowed to make policy recommendations itself. Waterton and Wynne show that this supposed boundary between knowledge and policy was impossible to maintain. The EEA found itself caught between two visions of Europe and two corresponding models of knowledge. The first was a centralist, modernist model: standardized data, harmonized across member states, feeding into a bureaucratic policy machine — Europe as a unified superstate. The second was more exploratory and decentralized: acknowledging ignorance and indeterminacy in scientific knowledge, engaging civil society and NGOs as legitimate participants in knowledge production, and embracing a more plural, heterogeneous vision of European identity. In the field of chemical risk assessment, the EEA began to open the "black boxes" of scientific risk assessment by reporting on the gaping holes in knowledge about the effects of thousands of human-made chemicals. By acknowledging what was *not* known, the EEA implicitly challenged the conventional assumption that risk assessment could provide definitive policy answers, and projected an alternative policy order in which civil society would play a larger role. The DGXI (the Commission's environment directorate) resisted this at every turn, trying to confine the EEA to providing basic "state of the environment" data that would not embarrass member states. The case elegantly demonstrates that the production of environmental information is never neutral — it always implies a corresponding vision of political order.

**William Storey on colonial agriculture in the West Indies (Ch. 6).** Storey traces how the Imperial Department of Agriculture for the West Indies was founded in the 1880s–1910s in response to the crisis of the sugar cane economy. The sugar beet industry in continental Europe, supported by state subsidies and scientific research, was undercutting Caribbean cane sugar. The British response involved creating new scientific institutions — botanical gardens, experimental stations, the department itself — that simultaneously addressed the agricultural crisis and constructed a new mode of imperial governance called "development." This was not laissez-faire; it represented a new interventionist approach that made British scientific knowledge more available to island farmers while also deepening imperial control. Storey shows how the biological characteristics of sugar cane itself — its need for intensive seasonal labor, rapid processing, tropical climate — had historically shaped the Plantation Complex and its dependence on coerced labor. The shift to scientific agriculture didn't dismantle these power relations; it reconfigured them around new forms of expertise and institutional authority, making the extractive colonial order appear natural, progressive, and scientifically rational. The chapter is a powerful example of how agricultural science, colonial identity (who counts as a "planter" versus a "peasant"), and imperial governance were all co-produced together.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Co-production is shorthand for the proposition that the ways in which we know and represent the world (both nature and society) are inseparable from the ways in which we choose to live in it." (p. 2–3)

"Scientific knowledge, in particular, is not a transcendent mirror of reality. It both embeds and is embedded in social practices, identities, norms, conventions, discourses, instruments and institutions — in short, in all the building blocks of what we term the social." (p. 3\)

"Solutions to the problem of knowledge are solutions to the problem of social order." (Shapin and Schaffer, quoted approvingly at p. 29\)

"Co-production is not about ideas alone; it is equally about concrete, physical things. It is not only about how people organize or express themselves, but also about what they value and how they assume responsibility for their inventions." (p. 6\)

"Co-production, in the view of contributors to this volume, should not be advanced as a fully fledged theory, claiming lawlike consistency and predictive power. It is far more an idiom — a way of interpreting and accounting for complex phenomena so as to avoid the strategic deletions and omissions of most other approaches in the social sciences." (p. 3\)

"By rejecting linearity, co-production stories undoubtedly complicate the 'why' questions that have formed the staple of much social science research; at the same time, they add to the agenda of the social sciences a series of 'how' questions that either have eluded systematic inquiry or else were rendered invisible by existing disciplinary framings." (p. 277\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

This book pairs most naturally with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* (1985), which is the direct intellectual ancestor of the interactional strand of co-production and which several contributors cite as foundational. It also pairs productively with Porter's *Trust in Numbers* (1995), which offers a complementary account of how quantification serves as a "technology of trust" that bridges the knowledge/governance divide — Porter's "mechanical objectivity" can be read as a specific mechanism of co-production. Latour's *We Have Never Been Modern* (1993) is the key interlocutor for the constitutive strand, though Jasanoff's framework deliberately corrects Latour's inattention to power asymmetries, national political culture, and normative commitments.

The volume's main limitation is one Jasanoff herself acknowledges: co-production is offered as an "idiom" rather than a theory, which gives it impressive flexibility but makes it difficult to know what would count as a disconfirming case. If everything is always co-produced, the framework risks becoming tautological — a descriptive vocabulary rather than an explanatory engine. Second, while the volume aspires to bridge micro-level STS work and macro-level social theory, the empirical chapters vary widely in how successfully they achieve this, and the volume remains weighted toward state-level and international institutions in the global North, with relatively thin attention to how co-production operates in contexts of radical inequality, authoritarian governance, or the global South beyond colonial settings.

# **Lynn K. Nyhart, *Modern Nature: The Rise of the Biological Perspective in Germany* (2009)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Nyhart argues that what early twentieth-century Germans called the "biological perspective"—a way of seeing organisms as living beings embedded in environments, dependent on both physical conditions and other organisms for survival—emerged not from elite university science but from the civic realm of museums, zoos, schools, and popular natural history. This perspective, which eventually became the conceptual foundation for German animal ecology, was developed and spread by socially mobile men from modest backgrounds who used overlapping reform movements (in taxidermy, museum display, school curricula, and biogeography) to carry a functionalist, community-centered view of nature into broad German culture between the 1850s and the 1920s.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

The book intervenes in the history of ecology by rejecting the standard genealogy that traces ecology's origins primarily through university-based science and Anglo-American traditions. English-language histories of ecology have typically centered on American and British figures and institutions, treating the discipline's emergence as an academic affair. Nyhart shows instead that German ecology—particularly animal ecology—grew out of a civic infrastructure of public museums, zoological gardens, and elementary school classrooms, developed by people who were largely marginal to the university system. This reframes the relationship between "popular" and "elite" science: rather than trickling down from universities to the public, the biological perspective traveled upward and outward from civic institutions into academic legitimacy. The book also intervenes in German cultural history, showing that the Heimat movement's emphasis on identity, place, and belonging had a significant natural-historical dimension that scholars like Celia Applegate and Alon Confino acknowledged but never examined in depth. It engages with long-standing questions in the sociology of science about the relationship between scientists' social positions and the content of their ideas—territory associated with Robert Merton, Steven Shapin, and the Edinburgh school—but insists on a more nuanced account in which social structure creates conditions of possibility for ideas without crudely determining them. And it pushes back against an Anglo-American orientation in the history of ecology that has treated wilderness-as-separate-from-humanity as the default framing, showing that the German version always included humans within nature.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Nyhart combines social history of science with cultural and institutional history, tracking how ideas travel through personal networks, career structures, and reform movements rather than through purely intellectual genealogies. Her key analytical move is to treat natural history reform movements as simultaneously intellectual and social phenomena, where concepts like the Lebensgemeinschaft gain traction not only because they are scientifically useful but because they resonate with broader social anxieties about community, identity, and place in a rapidly modernizing society.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

The book draws on an impressive range of German archival sources: museum correspondence and annual reports, personal diaries (especially Möbius's), school inspection records, teachers' periodicals, zoo records, popular science publications, and institutional archives from museums in Berlin, Hamburg, Kiel, Stuttgart, Bremen, and Altona. The geographic scope is Germany (with comparative gestures to the United States), and the temporal scope runs roughly from the 1850s to the 1920s, with heaviest focus on 1870–1910.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Karl Möbius and the Oyster Beds (Chapter 4).** This is the book's centerpiece. Möbius (1825–1908), born the son of a cartwright, was too poor for Gymnasium and trained as an elementary school teacher before clawing his way up through secondary school teaching in Hamburg and finally a university professorship at Kiel. His extraordinary social mobility is not incidental to the story—Nyhart argues it shaped the very content of his science. Commissioned by the Prussian agricultural ministry to investigate the collapsing oyster harvests along the Schleswig-Holstein coast, Möbius published *The Oyster and Oyster-Culture* (1877), in which he coined the term Biocönose (biocoenosis) to describe an oyster bed as "a community of living beings, a collection of species and a massing of individuals, which find here everything necessary for their growth and continuance." His key insight was that the vast number of oyster eggs produced bore no relationship to the number of mature oysters the community could sustain—overfishing had disrupted the delicate balance, and cockles and mussels had taken over the ecological niches. He compared the oysters to the buffalo of North America and the dodo of Mauritius, arguing that human intervention could destroy a biocoenosis irreversibly. Nyhart reads this concept as carrying deep social resonance: it emphasized functional relationships over ancestry, mirroring the aspirations of a liberal meritocracy, while also insisting that individuals exist within webs of dependence that are exceedingly difficult to change—a tension that "embodied" the central problem of modernizing German society.

**Friedrich Junge and the Village Pond Curriculum (Chapter 5).** Junge (1832–1905), head teacher at the Second Girls' Free School in Kiel, attended Möbius's evening lectures for teachers and transformed the living-community concept into a radical pedagogical program. His 1885 book *The Village Pond as a Living Community* proposed reorganizing elementary natural history teaching around the ecological community rather than the traditional Linnaean classification system. Instead of memorizing taxonomic categories, children aged ten to fourteen would study the pond near their village as an integrated whole—the relationships among frogs, insects, plants, water, and weather. This was explicitly tied to a social question: Junge argued that studying biological communities would help children answer their "deepest existential question: What is my place in society? Where do I fit in?" The curriculum spread rapidly through Germany's well-developed network of teachers' meetings and periodicals, becoming a flashpoint in battles between progressive and conservative pedagogy. It merged naturally with the Heimat movement, linking nature study to local identity and national belonging. By 1899, the Teachers' Union for Nature Study founded in Junge's wake had eleven thousand members; by 1915, over thirty-eight thousand. The biological community concept thus became the vehicle through which millions of German schoolchildren learned to see nature—and, by extension, their own place in society.

**Museum Reform and the "Biological Group" (Chapters 6–7).** Nyhart traces how natural history museums across Germany were transformed between the 1880s and 1910s through the "New Museum Idea," which aimed to redirect museums from scholarly repositories toward mass public education. Led by Möbius in Berlin and Karl Kraepelin in Hamburg, reformist curators split their collections into separate research and public display sections, and began creating "biological groups"—taxidermic mounts that posed animals together in lifelike positions within reconstructed habitats. Three museums receive close attention: the Bremen Museum für Natur-, Völker-, und Handelskunde (1896), which integrated natural history, ethnography, and trade into a single narrative of nature-culture relationships; the Altona City Museum (1901), which placed biological groups within a Heimatkunde framework linking local nature to regional identity; and the Berlin Museum für Meereskunde (1906), which presented marine ecosystems including a famous diorama of birdlife on a mudflat that prominently included the bow of a rowboat—a detail Nyhart reads as emblematic of the German refusal to separate nature from human presence. Each museum embedded biological groups within different larger schemes, but all shared the commitment to showing nature as a web of functional relationships rather than a cabinet of classified specimens.

**Comparative Divergence: German vs. American Nature (Chapter 10).** In the conclusion, Nyhart uses three sustained comparisons with American natural history to crystallize what was distinctive about the German biological perspective. First, the American habitat diorama—the spectacular, illusionistic re-creation of wilderness scenes—took off in the United States thanks to wealthy patrons who funded vertically integrated collecting expeditions (scientists and taxidermists traveled together), while German museums, lacking such patrons, assembled their biological groups from donated zoo animals and serendipitous acquisitions, producing more textbook-like displays rather than immersive wilderness scenes. Second, the career path from taxidermy to museum leadership was more open in America (William Hornaday moved from taxidermy to directing the Bronx Zoo), while in Germany rigid class and education barriers kept taxidermists from becoming curators, limiting their platform for nature advocacy. Third, American nature study became gendered feminine (87% of elementary teachers were women by 1921), while in Germany, where 80% of elementary teachers were men as late as 1911, love of nature was coded not as feminine sentimentalism but as a national character trait of all Germans.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Every oyster-bed is a community of living beings, a collection of species and a massing of individuals, which find here everything necessary for their growth and continuance, such as suitable soil, sufficient food, the requisite percentage of salt, and a temperature favorable to their development." (p. 152, quoting Möbius 1877/1883 translation)

"The biological perspective, I argue, thus embodied both the challenges and the opportunities presented by modern German society in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, as seen through its understanding of nature." (p. 4\)

"What is my place in society? Where do I fit in?" (p. 13, Nyhart paraphrasing Junge's argument for why children should study biological communities)

"The concept took hold because of its social resonances: it simultaneously captured both the importance of functional relationships (rather than those of heredity) central to a liberal meritocracy and a recognition that these functional relationships form a larger system of dependences binding individuals into relationships that, though not strictly fixed, are exceedingly difficult to change." (p. 160\)

"Nothing is as suited for tempering the divisions between the different stations of society as the truly human consciousness of belonging to a common earthly home." (p. 368, quoting the Heimat teacher Karl Gottlob Lutz, 1889\)

"The roots of German animal ecology, in particular, cannot be found in the world of the universities that for so long preoccupied historians of German science. They must be sought in the combination of attitudes, ideas, and institutions that emerged in the civic realm in the second half of the nineteenth century." (p. 34\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Nyhart's book pairs naturally with Robert Kohler's *Lords of the Fly* and his later *Landscapes and Labscapes* on the relationship between field and laboratory science, and with Donna Haraway's "Teddy Bear Patriarchy" essay (in *Primate Visions*) on natural history museums as sites where nature, culture, and politics are jointly produced. It also connects productively to Erika Milam's *Creatures of Cain* on how scientific conceptions of animal nature carry social freight, and to the Heimat and identity literature (Applegate, Confino) that Nyhart explicitly engages.

The book's main limitation is one Nyhart acknowledges: it traces the biological perspective almost entirely from the side of those who promoted it, with very little evidence about how museum visitors, schoolchildren, or general audiences actually received these ideas. The story is also heavily focused on northern Germany (Kiel, Hamburg, Bremen, Berlin) because that is where the personal networks of her central actors were densest, which means the picture may look different in southern or Catholic Germany.

# **How Reason Almost Lost Its Mind: The Strange Career of Cold War Rationality**

## **Paul Erickson, Judy L. Klein, Lorraine Daston, Rebecca Lemov, Thomas Sturm, and Michael D. Gordin (2013)**

---

### **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

During the Cold War (roughly 1945–early 1980s), American intellectuals across the human sciences undertook an unprecedented interdisciplinary campaign to articulate a new kind of *rationality*—formal, algorithmic, optimizing, and potentially mechanical—that could replace the slower, more deliberative forms of *reason* traditionally associated with good judgment. This new rationality was driven by the conviction that nuclear weapons had made the stakes of decision-making too catastrophic and the pace too fast for ordinary human reasoning. The book argues that what made this period distinctive was not any single theory but a vigorous, wide-ranging *debate* about rationality—fought across game theory, operations research, nuclear strategy, experimental psychology, and behavioral science—that briefly united otherwise disparate disciplines, institutions, and thinkers, and that collapsed when the concept of rationality itself splintered in the late 1970s and 1980s.

---

### **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Most prior scholarship on Cold War science treated game theory, operations research, nuclear strategy, and the behavioral sciences as separate disciplinary stories, occasionally linked by shared military funding or institutional settings like RAND. Erickson et al. argue instead that these were all participants in a single, connected *debate* about rationality—one with a shared ideal type (formal, algorithmic, optimizing, context-free), shared antagonists, and shared stakes. The book pushes back against two familiar narratives: first, the triumphalist account in which Cold War rationality was simply "applied" and proved its worth; second, the dismissive account (common in critical STS) that treats it as merely ideology or madness. Instead, the authors show that the most interesting intellectual action happened between proponents and critics, who shared a common starting point even as they stretched, revised, and ultimately fractured the ideal. The book is in dialogue with historians of the Cold War human sciences—particularly Hunter Heyck's work on Herbert Simon, Philip Mirowski on economics, and S.M. Amadae on rational choice theory—and with broader histories of Cold War science by Paul Edwards, Peter Galison, and Jessica Wang. The authors also position themselves against the popular Kahneman-and-Tversky story of "discovering" human irrationality, showing that the heuristics-and-biases program was itself a product of Cold War rationality, not an external critique of it.

---

### **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

The book constructs an "ideal type" (in Weber's sense) of Cold War rationality—formal, algorithmic, optimizing, mechanizable, context-independent—and then traces how this ideal was debated, modified, and eventually fragmented. The key conceptual move is the distinction between *reason* (mindful deliberation, judgment, wisdom, sensitivity to context) and *rationality* (rule-following, algorithmic procedure, formal optimization), arguing that Cold War thinkers deliberately expanded the domain of the latter at the expense of the former.

---

### **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Collectively authored intellectual history drawing on published works, archival papers (from RAND, Oskar Morgenstern's papers at Duke, Harvard's Department of Social Relations), conference transcripts (especially the 1964 Berkeley conference on strategic interaction), government documents, and popular media. Focused on the United States, roughly 1945–1985, with a comparative glance at the Soviet Union's reception of game theory.

---

### **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Project SCOOP, the Berlin Airlift, and the Diet Problem (Chapter 2).** In 1948, George Dantzig's Project for the Scientific Computation of Optimum Programs (Project SCOOP) at the Pentagon attempted to use linear programming to compute the optimal allocation of resources for the Berlin Airlift—the massive aerial supply operation to keep western Berlin fed during the Soviet blockade. The project promised to achieve through centralized mathematical planning what a competitive market would have achieved: the ideal combination of personnel, planes, fuel, and supplies. But the available punch-card calculators were hopelessly underpowered for the optimization mathematics. The only problem Project SCOOP could actually solve optimally was George Stigler's "diet problem"—computing the cheapest combination of foods to meet the nutritional needs of a seventy-kilogram urban economist. The actual airlift had to be managed with suboptimal protocols: Taylorist time-and-motion studies, standardized flight paths, planes landing at Tempelhof every three minutes. This gap between the aspiration of true optimization and the reality of computational limits became the founding condition for Herbert Simon's concept of "bounded rationality"—the idea that real decision-makers must "satisfice" (find good-enough solutions) rather than optimize. Simon developed this framework through further military-funded work at the Carnegie Institute of Technology, including production planning for a Pittsburgh paint factory, where computing limitations again forced researchers to substitute simple heuristics for ideal solutions. The case beautifully illustrates how the practical constraints of Cold War operations research generated fundamental theoretical concepts about the nature of rationality itself.

**Osgood's GRIT and the Cuban Missile Crisis (Chapter 3).** Charles Osgood, an experimental psychologist at the University of Illinois best known for developing the "semantic differential" technique for measuring meaning, stumbled into nuclear strategy in the late 1950s during a sabbatical at the Center for Advanced Study in the Behavioral Sciences. His proposal, GRIT (Graduated and Reciprocated Initiatives in Tension Reduction), was a de-escalation strategy built on a distinctive critique of Cold War rationality: Osgood argued that algorithmic models of nuclear strategy were *empirically inadequate* because they failed to account for the psychological constraints that shaped all human reasoning. His key insight was that the arms race was itself a "graduated and reciprocated" process—and therefore could be reversed by the same logic. Rather than trying to strip psychology out of decision-making (as hawks like Herman Kahn wanted), Osgood insisted that only by *recognizing* our psychological limitations—fear, cognitive dissonance, "Neanderthal" emotional responses—could we produce genuinely rational outcomes. GRIT functioned as both a strategy and a test: if you applied graduated de-escalation and your opponent reciprocated, you confirmed they were rational; if they didn't, you had learned something essential. The case shows how psychology entered the nuclear strategy conversation not simply to catalog irrationality but to propose a richer model of rationality that reincorporated emotion and self-awareness. Irving Janis's "groupthink" concept (developed from analysis of the Bay of Pigs, Pearl Harbor, Korea, and Vietnam) offered a contrasting approach: rather than harnessing psychology, Janis proposed *quarantining* its distortions through procedural reforms. Yet by defining irrationality as simply the failure to follow algorithmic rules, Janis inadvertently reinforced the very model of Cold War rationality he seemed to be criticizing.

**The Prisoner's Dilemma's Migration across Disciplines (Chapter 5).** The prisoner's dilemma was invented in January 1950 by RAND mathematicians Merrill Flood and Melvin Dresher, then given its famous story by Princeton mathematician Albert Tucker. For the RAND mathematicians, the game's significance was narrow: it was essentially a counterexample that exposed the inadequacy of John Nash's noncooperative equilibrium concept, since the "rational" Nash solution (mutual confession) produced an outcome worse for both players than cooperation. But game theory experiments at RAND stalled in the mid-1950s—partly because of the irresolvable tension between what the mathematics predicted and what experimental subjects actually did (they cooperated far more than the theory expected). The prisoner's dilemma then migrated to social psychology, where Anatol Rapoport at the University of Michigan ran thousands of iterated prisoner's dilemma experiments, explicitly arguing that the game's value lay in the *failure* of game theory to solve it—that failure opened a window into "real psychology": personality, trust, moral commitment. From psychology, the game traveled to evolutionary biology in the 1970s, where W.D. Hamilton reframed it as a model of natural selection among organisms that lacked any capacity for communication, memory, or conscious choice. Robert Trivers used Hamilton's framework to develop his theory of "reciprocal altruism," and Robert Axelrod's computer tournaments at Michigan showed that a simple "tit-for-tat" strategy—submitted by Rapoport himself—could produce cooperation without requiring intelligence, memory, or even consciousness. The case demonstrates how game theory's matrix notation served as a remarkably portable technology for coding behavior across wildly different contexts—from nuclear strategy to insect populations—and how each disciplinary migration subtly redefined what "rationality" meant.

**The Rationality Wars and Kahneman & Tversky (Chapter 6).** The chapter opens with the 1980–81 Polish crisis and the question of whether experts could rationally predict Soviet behavior, then pivots to the heuristics-and-biases research program of Daniel Kahneman and Amos Tversky. Their experiments—the Wason selection task, the conjunction fallacy (the "Linda problem," in which participants judged that "a Russian invasion of Poland AND a suspension of diplomatic relations" was more probable than a suspension of relations alone), prospect theory—seemed to demonstrate that humans systematically violated the formal rules of logic and probability. But the chapter's real argument is about what happened *next*: in 1981, philosopher L. Jonathan Cohen ignited the "rationality wars" by arguing that human irrationality *could not be demonstrated experimentally*, invoking Chomsky's distinction between competence and performance. Three major objections emerged: the heuristics-and-biases researchers had cherry-picked negative results while ignoring extensive evidence of adequate reasoning; the "illusions" could often be eliminated by changing how problems were framed (especially using deontic rather than descriptive conditionals); and the formal norms used as benchmarks were themselves contested—there was nothing sacrosanct about standard propositional logic or Bayesian probability as the definition of rational thinking. The result was the fragmentation of the concept of rationality itself: the options for what counted as rational or irrational had multiplied and diverged to the point of incoherence. This fragmentation, combined with the end of the Cold War, dissolved the interdisciplinary debate that had given Cold War rationality its energy.

---

### **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"In that implied gap between reason and rationality lay the novelty of Cold War rationality." (p. 2\)

"The aim of this book is to make the label 'Cold War rationality' stick." (p. 3\)

"The hurtling pace of technological advances, especially but not exclusively in the realm of weaponry, had, they believed, overwhelmed the 'Stone Age mentality' of so-called *Homo sapiens*, a mindset that had shown itself unable to manage automobiles wisely, much less nuclear bombs." (p. 16\)

"The potentially rich contributions of game theory to psychology will derive from the failure of game theory rather than from its successes." —Anatol Rapoport (p. 144\)

"The irrationality most damaging to Cold War rationality was not the passions or malevolence or caprice; it was inconsistency, two (or more) souls within a single breast competing for mastery to choose and decide." (p. 49\)

"For approximately forty years, the history of rationality was forged in flight plans for feeding West Berlin, tense deliberations of the Cuban Missile Crisis, war games played between generals and strategists, 'situations' devised by social scientists in Micronesia and at Harvard, and in other contexts seldom frequented by academic philosophers." (p. 188\)

---

### **7\. CONNECTIONS**

This book pairs directly with Paul Edwards's *The Closed World* (which covers the same Cold War institutional landscape but focuses on computers and discourse rather than rationality as a concept), Eden Medina's *Cybernetic Revolutionaries* (which tells a parallel story about cybernetic rationality deployed in Allende's Chile rather than the American context), and Porter's *Trust in Numbers* (which provides the longer backstory of how quantification and formal rules became substitutes for personal judgment). It also connects powerfully to Ronald Kline's *The Cybernetics Moment*, which covers overlapping figures (Wiener, von Neumann, Simon) through the lens of information theory rather than rationality per se.

The book's collectively authored format occasionally produces unevenness: some chapters (especially on operations research and the prisoner's dilemma) are tightly argued, while the chapter on the behavioral sciences "situation" sometimes strains to fit its material into the book's larger arc. The Soviet comparison in the introduction, while suggestive, remains underdeveloped—the authors acknowledge that American-style Cold War rationality had "no precise Soviet mirror image," but the asymmetry is never fully explored.

# Week 6 — Biology

# **Theodore M. Porter, *Trust in Numbers: The Pursuit of Objectivity in Science and Public Life* (1995)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Porter argues that quantification is "a technology of distance"—a set of strategies for communicating and making decisions across boundaries of locality, community, and personal acquaintance. The demand for rigorous quantitative methods in science and public life does not arise primarily from their ability to capture truth about nature, but from their capacity to minimize the need for personal trust. The drive toward mechanical objectivity—strict adherence to explicit rules rather than expert judgment—reflects not the strength of the communities that adopt it, but their *weakness and vulnerability* to outside scrutiny.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Porter intervenes against two dominant narratives about quantification. The first, favored by apologists, treats the spread of numbers into public decision-making as the straightforward result of quantification's success in the natural sciences—a trickle-down story from physics to policy. The second, favored by critics (especially on the left), treats quantification as ideology, a tool of domination wielded by powerful elites. Porter rejects both. He argues instead that we should "point the arrow of explanation in the opposite direction": by understanding the political and social pressures that made quantification indispensable in government, business, and social research, we can learn something new about why it took the forms it did even in the natural sciences. His key move is to connect the history of science to the history of democratic political culture, particularly the culture of systematic distrust that characterizes the American state. He draws on and extends the work of Lorraine Daston on scientific objectivity, Harry Collins on tacit knowledge and replication, Steven Shapin on trust in science, and the sociology of scientific knowledge more broadly. But where Shapin, Collins, and the microsociologists emphasize the local, trust-dependent character of knowledge production, Porter asks the complementary question: how has scientific knowledge been made to travel? His answer—through quantification, standardization, and the deliberate suppression of personal judgment—reframes objectivity as a social and political achievement rather than an epistemic one.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Porter develops a political sociology of objectivity, treating quantification as a "technology of trust" that substitutes impersonal rules for personal judgment. He distinguishes between three forms of objectivity—disciplinary (consensus among specialists), mechanical (following explicit rules), and what he calls the "moral" or political form (fairness, impartiality)—and shows how the tension between disciplinary and mechanical objectivity drives the historical dynamics of quantification. His central theoretical claim is that "the seemingly relentless push for objectivity and impersonality in science is not quite universal, and must be understood partly as an adaptation to institutional disunity and permeable disciplinary boundaries."

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Porter combines conceptual argument with archival case studies drawn from France, Britain, and the United States, spanning roughly 1830 to 1970\. His primary research draws on the archives of the French Corps des Ponts et Chaussées, the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers (including the National Archives and the Office of History at Fort Belvoir), and published records of congressional hearings. The more theoretical chapters synthesize a vast secondary literature across history of science, sociology of knowledge, political history, and the philosophy of science.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**U.S. Army Corps of Engineers and cost-benefit analysis (Ch. 7).** This is the book's centerpiece. Porter traces how the Corps developed its system of calculating costs and benefits for flood control and navigation projects from the 1920s through the 1960s. In the early period, the Corps' numbers were accepted almost entirely on institutional authority—congressional committees were "dazzlingly uninquisitive" about economic methodology, and senators praised the Corps as incorruptible without examining its calculations. Cost-benefit ratios of 1.03 provoked no alarm; when asked about complex formulas, Senator Prescott Bush simply replied "All right." The real pressure to standardize and formalize cost-benefit methods came not from within, but from powerful opponents—electric utility companies, railroads, and rival federal agencies like the Bureau of Reclamation and the Soil Conservation Service. When the Corps faced bureaucratic competitors who could produce their own numbers, informal judgment was no longer defensible. The agencies tried to negotiate shared standards; when negotiation failed, they were "compelled to try to ground their makeshift techniques in economic rationality." The result: cost-benefit analysis was transformed from a collection of local bureaucratic practices into a supposedly universal methodology. Porter's punchline: "The regime of calculation was imposed not by all-powerful experts, but by relatively weak and divided ones."

**French Corps des Ponts et Chaussées (Ch. 6).** Porter uses the French engineering corps as the mirror image of the American case. The Ponts engineers were part of a genuine administrative elite—graduates of the École Polytechnique, embedded in a unified ruling class that moved fluidly between government departments. Because they operated within a culture of personal trust and bureaucratic autonomy, they never developed the rigid cost-benefit standards the Americans did. Engineers like Dupuit, Navier, and Cheysson practiced economic calculation as a flexible tool of administration, not as a defense against hostile outsiders. Dupuit even pioneered the concept of diminishing marginal utility, but his economics was designed for administrative use, not for producing publicly defensible numbers. The French could rely on expert judgment because their expert community was secure; the Americans could not, because theirs was not.

**Accounting and the SEC (Ch. 5).** Porter traces how American accounting shifted from reliance on expert judgment to rigid standardized rules in response to the Securities and Exchange Commission after the Depression. British actuaries of the nineteenth century provide the contrast: they successfully resisted standardization because their small, elite professional community commanded enough trust to rely on informed discretion. American accountants could not sustain this model. After the stock market crash, investor confidence could only be restored through enforceable reporting rules. The American Institute of Accountants established its own standardization mechanisms—the Committee on Accounting Procedure, the Accounting Principles Board, the Financial Accounting Standards Board—that functioned almost like government agencies. The profession's internal debate over whether objectivity meant "following rules" or "reaching truth" was, Porter argues, not an abstract philosophical question but a fight over professional autonomy. The SEC's notorious Depression-era ruling that corporate book value be based on original cost of assets—widely regarded by accountants as a sacrifice of accuracy for enforceability—exemplifies how mechanical objectivity is driven by political need rather than epistemic virtue.

**Inferential statistics in medicine and psychology (Ch. 8).** Porter shows that statistical inference did not migrate down the hierarchy of science from physics and mathematics; it was "seized most readily by weaker disciplines, such as psychology and medical research, and indeed by their relatively applied subfields." The controlled clinical trial became standard in medicine not because doctors wanted it—most resisted the "considerable sacrifice" of merging their clinical individuality into group investigations—but because regulatory authorities (especially the FDA after the Kefauver Bill of 1962\) demanded publicly defensible evidence. Austin Bradford Hill's double-blind methodology was designed to "neutralize the effects of expert discretion without disbarring it." In psychology, the adoption of statistical significance testing served a similar function: it provided a publicly defensible standard in a discipline with weak boundaries and low social authority, allowing researchers to claim objectivity without the kind of elite disciplinary consensus that physicists could take for granted.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Quantification is a technology of distance." (p. ix, Preface)

"Reliance on numbers and quantitative manipulation minimizes the need for intimate knowledge and personal trust." (p. ix, Preface)

"Quantification is a way of making decisions without seeming to decide. Objectivity lends authority to officials who have very little of their own." (p. 8\)

"The drive to supplant personal judgment by quantitative rules reflects weakness and vulnerability. I interpret it as a response to conditions of distrust attending the absence of a secure and autonomous community." (p. xi, Preface)

"In science, as in political and administrative affairs, objectivity names a set of strategies for dealing with distance and distrust." (p. x, Preface)

"Much of what passes for scientific method is a contrivance of weak communities, partly in response to the vulnerability of science to pressures from outside." (p. 12\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Porter pairs most directly with Daston and Galison's *Objectivity* (2007), which traces changing regimes of scientific self-discipline through the history of scientific atlases—where Porter emphasizes institutional vulnerability, Daston and Galison foreground epistemic virtues and the moral training of the scientific self. MacKenzie's *Mechanizing Proof* (2001) and *Inventing Accuracy* (1990) share Porter's interest in how quantification and formal method serve social functions beyond truth-finding—MacKenzie shows how mathematical proof was mechanized in part to settle disputes where trust had broken down, much as Porter describes for cost-benefit analysis. Jasanoff's *States of Knowledge* (2004) extends Porter's political analysis by showing how different national political cultures produce different relationships between expertise, quantification, and democratic authority.

# **Sarah Franklin, *Dolly Mixtures: The Remaking of Genealogy* (Duke University Press, 2007\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Franklin argues that Dolly the sheep — the first mammal cloned from an adult cell — can only be understood through the deep historical genealogies that made her creation possible, including centuries of British sheep breeding, pastoral capitalism, colonial expansion, and the intertwining of agricultural innovation with the life sciences. Rather than asking whether cloning is ethical, Franklin treats Dolly as a "biocultural entity" whose thick genealogy reveals how biological reproduction, capital accumulation, national identity, and imperial power have always been entangled through the bodies of sheep — and are now being reconfigured through stem cell science, tissue engineering, and "the age of biological control."

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Franklin positions *Dolly Mixtures* against two dominant approaches to cloning: the bioethical framework (is cloning moral?) and the technophilic celebration of scientific novelty. Both, she argues, are presentist — they either freeze the question in a future-oriented ethical dilemma or treat Dolly as a rupture without precedent. Franklin insists on a genealogical method instead, demonstrating that Dolly is continuous with longstanding practices of reshaping animal bodies for economic and national purposes. The book draws heavily on Donna Haraway's cyborg/companion species framework, Marilyn Strathern's work on kinship and assisted reproduction, Harriet Ritvo's histories of animal breeding and classification, and Charis Thompson's concept of "ontological choreography" in reproductive technologies. Against Latourian frameworks of networks and assemblages, Franklin deliberately retains the older, more conservative idiom of "genealogy" and "social order" to emphasize structural continuity — how the forms of order that made Dolly possible have profoundly shaped the past and will shape the future. She also engages Patrick Wolfe's postcolonial theory of "frontier effects" to argue that Australian settler colonialism was literally trodden in by selectively bred sheep, and that the genealogies linking Dolly to IVF, stem cells, and regenerative medicine run directly through Australia's pastoral economy.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Cultural anthropology of science and reproduction, drawing on feminist science studies (Haraway), kinship theory (Strathern), postcolonial criticism (Wolfe), and the history of biology. Key concepts Franklin mobilizes include "biocultural entity," "the age of biological control" (from Wilmut), "breed wealth," "protocapital," "de-differentiation" as both a biological and conceptual process, "genealogical orientation," and the "conditional biological" — the idea that biology has become plastic, reversible, and fundamentally contingent rather than fixed.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Interdisciplinary and deliberately eclectic: interviews with Roslin Institute scientists (Ian Wilmut, Bill Ritchie); laboratory ethnography of Britain's first stem cell lines at King's College London; extensive use of nineteenth- and twentieth-century sheep-breeding literature (Youatt, Trow-Smith, Ryder); colonial Australian archival sources; newspaper coverage of the 2001 foot-and-mouth epidemic; etymological analysis of terms like "stock," "colony," "clone," and "frontier"; and close readings of scientific publications, parliamentary reports (House of Lords stem cell inquiry), and popular media representations of Dolly. Geographic scope spans Britain, Australia, and the global biotechnology industry; temporal scope runs from Neolithic sheep domestication through the early 2000s.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The etymology and semantics of "stock."** One of the book's most original moves is a sustained close reading of the Oxford English Dictionary entry for "stock," which runs thirty-four pages and encompasses over two hundred definitions. Franklin traces how "stock" binds together meanings of trunk/stem (biological origin), fund/capital (economic value), tool/instrument (utility), and lineage/breed (genealogy). The OED editors themselves describe the financial definitions as "obscure" and "blended" — specifically and exclusively British developments in which the biological and the economic became inseparable. Franklin uses this to argue that "livestock" is not a metaphor but a literal description of how animal bodies became instruments of capital accumulation: at once living tools, movable property, and reproductive funds. The word contains, in compressed form, the entire history connecting sheep breeding, industrial capitalism, and what she calls "life stock" — stem cell lines banked as biological capital for future therapeutic use. The parallel between sheep as "bioreactors" producing human proteins in their milk and stem cells as cultivated, propagated lines of biological value is not analogy but historical descent.

**The British system of stratified sheep breeding.** Franklin describes in detail how Britain's uniquely diverse sheep population — more breeds than any other country — is organized through a complex national system of cross-breeding that moves animals from the harsh northern hills down to richer southern pastures through successive crosses. Hardy Scottish Blackface ewes are bred with long-wool rams to produce "mules," who in turn are crossed with lowland terminal sires to produce fat lambs for market. The system depends on maintaining breed diversity, seasonal market cycles, regional fairs, and constant movement of animals — extracting maximum value from marginal land by passing it down through successive crosses. Franklin argues this system is the direct ancestor of the stem cell economy: both depend on cultivating biological diversity, maintaining "lines" of descent, banking reproductive material, and strategically controlling biological reproduction to maximize output. When stem cell researchers describe their work with metaphors of "seeding," "passaging," "cultivating," and "propagation," they are not borrowing agricultural language — they are continuing agricultural practice.

**Sheep and Australian settler colonialism.** Drawing on Patrick Wolfe's concept of the "frontier effect" and Frederick Jackson Turner's frontier thesis, Franklin traces how selectively bred Merino sheep served as literal agents of colonial occupation in Australia. The "white tide" of sheep sweeping westward from Sydney consumed indigenous flora, destroyed Aboriginal subsistence ecology, and converted pasturage into export commodities — all while establishing the economic foundations of the Australian nation-state. She examines John Macarthur's famous 1803 *Statement of Improvement* to Parliament, in which he leveraged a single hank of wool into five thousand acres of prime farmland, as a case study in how "breed wealth" and speculative capital were inseparable from the first moments of Australian colonial economy. The frontier was "trodden in" by sheep, and the mythologies of Australian national character — the stoic bushman, the pastoral pioneer — are genealogically descended from ovine settlement patterns. Franklin extends this to show how Australian sheep research later produced key techniques for IVF (particularly ovulation induction), which in turn led to embryonic cell culture, which in turn enabled Dolly — so that the genealogical line connecting British imperial sheep breeding to twenty-first-century regenerative medicine runs directly through Australia's colonial pastoral economy.

**The 2001 foot-and-mouth epidemic as the opposite of biological control.** Franklin's chapter on "Death" analyzes the British foot-and-mouth crisis through close readings of newspaper coverage, showing how the epidemic revealed the two sides of "biological control." The disease itself is harmless to sheep and virtually never affects humans — it is only "lethal" because the global market divides between disease-free and disease-affected countries, making it economically intolerable. The British government's "scorched earth" culling policy destroyed nearly four million animals, including irreplaceable lines of heritage breeding stock, using the most ancient methods (slaughter and burning) while Dolly sat quarantined nearby, embodying the most sophisticated biological technologies ever applied to a sheep. Franklin reads this juxtaposition as demonstrating that increased biological control has no necessary relationship to the reduction of its opposite: "Like increased security, increased control produces new risks and may aggravate susceptibility to older ones." She compares newspaper treatments across the political spectrum — the *Daily Telegraph's* dignified, humanless photograph of Cheviot sheep standing heroically on a plain versus the *Guardian's* confused, fish-eye-lens distortion of a lamb — to show how the "affective economies" of sheep-human relationships structure national politics, rural identity, and the meaning of domestication itself.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Dolly is in this sense both a frontier and a horizon — a relational someplace and no place signaling future possibility and direction." (p. 5\)

"What Wilmut describes as 'the age of biological control' . . . not only refers to a set of possibilities nonexistent before Dolly . . . but to the loss of the idea that there is anything like a biological barrier or limit beyond which humans cannot go." (pp. 32–33)

"The appearance of capital as an independent and leading force in agriculture does not take place all at once and generally, but gradually and in particular lines of production. It encompasses at first, not agriculture proper, but such branches of production as cattle breeding, especially sheep-raising." (Marx, *Capital*, quoted on p. 46\)

"Like increased security, increased control produces new risks and may aggravate susceptibility to older ones." (p. 191\)

"It is likely to be a future in which we continue to have more in common than we think with sheep. This is, of course, the reason why the remaking of Dolly's genealogy matters — because it remakes our own." (p. 207\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Franklin's argument about the deep entanglement of agricultural innovation, capital, and the life sciences connects directly to Krementsov's *Stalinist Science* (where state control of biological research shapes what counts as legitimate science), to Haraway's *Primate Visions* (already on the HoS list, and one of Franklin's primary interlocutors on how animal-human relationships structure scientific knowledge), and to Cronon's *Nature's Metropolis* (where the Chicago commodity markets similarly transform living organisms into fungible units of capital — Cronon's grain elevators and Franklin's stem cell banks perform analogous work of abstraction). The colonial chapter pairs powerfully with any discussion of ecological imperialism or the relationship between livestock economies and settler colonialism.

**Limitations:** The book's deliberately eclectic, "sheepish" method — following sheep wherever they lead — sometimes produces chapters that feel more like curated assemblages of suggestive connections than sustained historical arguments. The etymological analyses, while often brilliant, occasionally risk conflating linguistic resemblance with causal relationship. Franklin herself acknowledges these weaknesses with disarming honesty ("like Dolly herself, it has been overfed"), but the result is a text that is more generative as a source of concepts and framings than as a tightly controlled historical narrative.

# **David Serlin, *Replaceable You: Engineering the Body in Postwar America* (University of Chicago Press, 2004\)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Serlin argues that in the late 1940s and early 1950s, medical procedures used to rehabilitate or alter the human body—prosthetics, plastic surgery, hormone therapy, and sex reassignment—became entangled with the rhetoric of Americanism, Cold War nationalism, and consumer culture. Bodily transformation was not merely a private medical act but served as an allegory for national rehabilitation, enabling individuals with nonnormative bodies to insert themselves into a patriotic narrative in which physical reconstruction made one more visibly and tangibly "American." The book treats the postwar body as a site where nationalist politics, gender normativity, racial ideology, and medical consumerism converged.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Serlin intervenes in several overlapping literatures. Against the dominant historiography of Cold War culture—represented by Paul Boyer's *By the Bomb's Early Light*, Elaine Tyler May's *Homeward Bound*, Alan Nadel's *Containment Culture*, and Stephen Whitfield's *The Culture of the Cold War*—Serlin shifts the terrain from the familiar registers of domestic ideology and geopolitical anxiety to the physical body itself as a locus of Cold War politics. Where these scholars focused on family structure, media, and containment discourse, Serlin shows how medical technology literally inscribed Cold War values onto flesh and bone. He also pushes against the standard "preliberation shame / postliberation freedom" periodization, arguing that the 1950s were not simply an era of enforced conformity and secrecy but one in which some Americans aggressively pursued medical procedures to reconcile private selves with public identities. In disability studies, he challenges the tendency to use "prosthesis" as an abstracted postmodern metaphor (à la Kathleen Woodward and cyborg theory), insisting on the material history of actual prostheses and the people who wore them. The book draws on Sander Gilman, Rosemarie Garland Thomson, Elizabeth Haiken, and Siobhan Somerville while carving out new ground at the intersection of disability studies, queer studies, and Cold War cultural history.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Serlin operates at the intersection of cultural history, disability studies, gender and sexuality studies, and STS. His central concept is what he calls "medical consumerism"—the postwar transformation of medical procedures into amenities of a modern, meritocratic social order that Americans could privately consume as tools of self-realization and civic participation. He borrows from Du Bois's "double consciousness" to theorize the experience of physically or sexually nonnormative individuals, and from Stallybrass and White's concept of "displaced abjection" to explain how exposing others' deviance permitted individuals to secure their own normative status.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Serlin uses an eclectic mix of archival materials, popular media, and visual culture: Veterans Administration records, prosthetics research files from Walter Reed and NYU, the Henry Dreyfuss papers at Cooper-Hewitt, popular magazines (*Fortune*, *Ebony*, *Saturday Review*, *Collier's*), comic strips, television transcripts (*This Is Your Life*), personal letters (Jorgensen's correspondence), Quaker organizational records, film, photography, and medical literature. The geographic scope is primarily the United States with important transnational moments in Hiroshima and Copenhagen. The temporal scope centers on roughly 1945–1960.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Henry Dreyfuss's Prosthetic Hand (Chapter 1):** In 1948–50, industrial designer Henry Dreyfuss—who also designed Honeywell thermostats, Bell telephones, and military hardware—created a prosthetic hand for the Veterans Administration. The stainless steel split-hook device, still in use decades later, was photographed forming a signature in beautiful longhand, with its mechanics hidden tastefully beneath an Oxford-cloth shirtsleeve. Serlin reads this as the pivotal artifact of a shift in American masculinity: unlike earlier prostheses marketed to blue-collar workers and shown operating farm machinery, the Dreyfuss hand was pitched to a new class of white-collar corporate workers. It was "a whole new hand for a whole new kind of labor." Serlin calls this "the other arms race"—not the Cold War competition with the Soviets, but the competition between blue-collar and white-collar models of masculine rehabilitation. The Dreyfuss hand obeyed the aesthetic principles of industrial design (no visible screws, no exposed seams, no distracting colors) and appeared alongside consumer products in Dreyfuss's manifesto *Designing for People*. It represented the consumerist ethos of the 1950s: "even amputees could sign their lives away through credit debt." Meanwhile, Norbert Wiener's cybernetically controlled "Liberty Limb"—designed to bring self-sufficiency to amputees—was never commercially viable in the U.S. Its cybernetic principles were instead appropriated for industrial robotics, and the USSR exhibited the world's first commercially available myoelectric arm at the 1958 Brussels World's Fair, an irony Serlin calls one of "the crushing ironies of postwar labor."

**The Hiroshima Maidens (Chapter 2):** In 1955, twenty-five young Japanese women scarred by the atomic bombing of Hiroshima were brought to the United States for reconstructive plastic surgery at Mount Sinai Hospital in New York, organized by Reverend Kiyoshi Tanimoto and *Saturday Review* editor Norman Cousins. Serlin sets this story against the rejection of Isamu Noguchi's cenotaph design for Hiroshima's Peace Memorial Park—both events illustrating contradictory strategies for memorializing and repairing atomic damage. The Maidens were housed with Quaker families in wealthy New York suburbs (Scarsdale, Darien, Upper Montclair), and Serlin recovers the remarkable personal assessment lists compiled by Quaker organizer Ida Day, which sorted the women by intelligence, marriageability, class background, and personality ("Not lovable. Superiority air, objectionable to others"). These lists reveal how even well-intentioned Americans imposed normative categories of gender, class, and assimilability onto the Maidens. Serlin also examines the women's appearance on the television show *This Is Your Life*, where producers hid the Maidens behind translucent screens because they deemed the women's faces too disturbing for television audiences—the same episode that ambushed Tanimoto with a confrontation with Lt. Robert Lewis, co-pilot of the Enola Gay. The Mount Sinai surgeons carefully distinguished their work as "ballpark surgery" (reconstructive, not cosmetic), while newspaper headlines announced "Disfigured Jap Girls to Get Facelifting."

**Gladys Bentley and Hormone Therapy (Chapter 3):** Gladys Bentley (1907–60) was a pianist and performer who openly identified as a lesbian during the Harlem Renaissance, known for wearing men's clothing and marrying her white girlfriend in a public ceremony. By the late 1940s, Bentley claimed in a 1952 *Ebony* article ("I Am a Woman Again") that estrogen therapy had "corrected" her homosexuality and made her a well-adjusted heterosexual woman. Serlin situates Bentley's story within the racial politics of endocrinology, showing how early twentieth-century hormone science was entangled with eugenic thinking that correlated glandular function with racial "fitness." He traces how endocrinology's "glandular vision of life" enabled physicians to pathologize both homosexuality and racial difference as forms of biological immaturity. Bentley's embrace of heterosexual femininity through hormone therapy, Serlin argues, reflected the pressures on middle-class African Americans in the pre-civil rights era to define themselves through consensus culture rather than resistance. He connects Bentley to gynecologist Robert Wilson's characterization of estradiol (derived from pregnant mares' urine) as the "Cadillac of hormones"—a product for sophisticated consumers seeking to maintain proper femininity. Serlin pushes back against scholars who insist on reclaiming Bentley solely as a "potent Black lesbian icon," arguing that her later life deserves "the dignity of biographical complexity" rather than having its reactionary politics explained away.

**Christine Jorgensen's Rise and Fall (Chapter 4):** When the *New York Daily News* ran the headline "EX-GI BECOMES BLONDE BEAUTY" on December 1, 1952, Christine Jorgensen became the first internationally known transsexual figure. Serlin traces a precise six-month arc: from December 1952 to May 1953, Jorgensen was celebrated as "the most talked-about girl in the world," her patriotic GI background and glamorous femininity making her compatible with Cold War nationalism. The media circulated her personal letters, fashion photographs, and references to a boyfriend—all normalizing her within conventional femininity. But when endocrinologists and surgeons publicly revealed that Danish doctors had not created a biological woman but had merely performed castration and hormone treatments, Jorgensen was recast as pathological: not a medical miracle but a fraud. Her "gender betrayal" was treated as unpatriotic. Serlin reads her trajectory through what Stallybrass and White call "displaced abjection"—the same logic that drove Roy Cohn to persecute the Rosenbergs while concealing his own homosexuality. Jorgensen's story demonstrates how the postwar closet could open briefly when medical procedures aligned with nationalist narratives of transformation and progress, but slammed shut when the body's material reality failed to match the ideological story being told about it.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"In the late 1940s and early 1950s, medical procedures enabled some Americans to experience their bodies, in material form, as both public expressions of national conscience and individual expressions of personal identity." (p. 3\)

"Bodily transformation also obliged Americans to confront the social and moral opprobrium with which they typically greeted the idea of the unnatural. What, after all, did the so-called natural body mean if an engineered body approximated one's sense of self far more persuasively than the body one was born into?" (p. 5\)

"War's rehabilitation engineering may well become the social engineering of the future." (*Popular Science*, 1945, quoted p. 12\)

"This, then, was 'the other arms race' of the postwar period: not the technological cum political competition with the Soviet Union but the competition between white-collar masculinity (as symbolized by the Dreyfuss arm) and that of the blue-collar worker who formerly had proved his worthiness and aptitude through the labor he accomplished after completing rehabilitation." (p. 54–55)

"The production staff had also requested that Chyssa Tanimoto wear a kimono and wooden slippers during her appearance on the program, even though she normally wore modern Western apparel. It must have seemed necessary to extinguish the actual details of Chyssa's modern experience for the sake of transforming her and the Maidens into Oriental spectacles." (p. 90\)

"Do we want so much to claim her as a sister that we are willing to disregard and even distort how Bentley understood her own life? Surely as important as canonizing her as a role model for African American lesbians or for African Americans in general is bestowing on Bentley the dignity of biographical complexity." (p. 158\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Serlin pairs well with Jennifer Light's *From Warfare to Welfare*, which similarly traces how Cold War defense expertise was repurposed for domestic social engineering, though Light focuses on urban planning rather than bodies. The book also pairs productively with Donna Haraway's "Cyborg Manifesto" and Ruth Oldenziel's *Making Technology Masculine*: Serlin provides the material, historical grounding for the body-machine interface that Haraway theorizes abstractly, while his analysis of prosthetics and masculinity complements Oldenziel's account of how technology became gendered in America. For the hormone and transsexuality chapters, Krementsov's *Stalinist Science* offers an interesting Cold War counterpoint on how medicine served state ideology.

Limitations: The book's four-chapter structure, organized around discrete case studies, sometimes sacrifices sustained analytical depth for breadth—the connections between prosthetics, plastic surgery, hormones, and sex reassignment are asserted more than demonstrated through a unified causal argument. The book also focuses almost exclusively on how nonnormative individuals sought to conform to dominant ideals, with less attention to those who resisted or subverted the medical establishment's normalizing imperatives.

# **Elizabeth Hennessy, *On the Backs of Tortoises: Darwin, the Galápagos, and the Fate of an Evolutionary Eden* (Yale University Press, 2019\)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Hennessy argues that the Galápagos Islands are not, and never were, a pristine "natural laboratory of evolution" isolated from human history, but rather a place whose nature has been fundamentally shaped by five centuries of entanglement among tortoises, sailors, colonists, naturalists, conservationists, tourists, and the Ecuadorian state. The dominant "geographical imagination" of the archipelago as an evolutionary Eden—a vision traced from Darwin through mid-twentieth-century conservation campaigns—has paradoxically undermined conservation by enforcing a false separation of nature and culture that delegitimizes human inhabitants and obscures the political economies that continue to reshape island ecologies.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Hennessy intervenes in environmental history, the history of science, and critical conservation studies by challenging what she calls the "tortoise problem"—the impossible, un-Darwinian desire to restore a prehuman baseline in the name of saving evolution. She pushes back against a long tradition, from Julian Huxley's mid-century rhetoric to contemporary socio-ecological systems (SES) models, that treats the Galápagos as a place where nature can be separated from society and managed by scientific experts. Drawing on political ecology (Roderick Neumann, Arturo Escobar), multispecies ethnography (Donna Haraway, Thom van Dooren), and postcolonial science studies (Mary Louise Pratt), she argues that conservation biology's baseline thinking—evaluating the present against a pre-1535 "Eden"—is itself a product of imperial history, not a neutral scientific standard. She is in direct conversation with Christophe Grenier's *Conservación Contra Natura*, which first identified the contradiction between conservation's desire for island isolation and its dependence on global tourism. She also engages with William Cronon's critique of wilderness ideology, historian Edward Larson's history of the Galápagos, and the SES frameworks of González et al. that proposed rethinking the islands as a socio-ecological system. Hennessy credits the SES model for acknowledging human presence but criticizes its systems-theory approach for reducing contingent, political histories to neat feedback loops and declensionist narratives.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Political ecology, multispecies ethnography, and critical geography. Hennessy's central conceptual apparatus is the "geographical imagination"—borrowed from David Harvey and adapted to show how particular visions of the Galápagos (as storehouse, natural laboratory, evolutionary Eden) are social and political productions with material consequences for landscapes, species, and people. She also deploys Foucault's concept of "biopower" to describe conservation's management of which species are "made live" (endemic tortoises) and which are "made die" (introduced goats, rats); van Dooren's "violent-care" to capture the ethical ambiguity of killing hundreds of thousands of animals to save others; and Isabelle Stengers's reframing of the "turtles all the way down" problem as a call to ask not what the turtles rest on but "with whom they lived."

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Hennessy draws on twelve years of fieldwork in the Galápagos (2007–2018), including ethnographic interviews with park guards, farmers, fishers, scientists, and tourism workers; archival research at the California Academy of Sciences, the British Natural History Museum, the Charles Darwin Research Station, UNESCO, the American Museum of Natural History, and the Smithsonian; published travel narratives and ships' logs from the sixteenth through twentieth centuries; and participatory fieldwork volunteering at the tortoise breeding center and accompanying ecological transect surveys. Geographic scope spans the Galápagos, Ecuador, London, San Francisco, and New York; temporal scope runs from the 1535 "discovery" by Berlanga through contemporary conservation debates.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The two Darwins and the consumption of tortoises.** Hennessy sets up a sustained contrast between the young Darwin who ate tortoise soup on Santiago in 1835, camping with political-exile tortoise hunters, and the gray-bearded "patron saint" Darwin memorialized in statues throughout the archipelago. She shows that the Beagle's visit was not a voyage of nature appreciation but an imperial hydrographic survey mission, and that Darwin secured his berth not as the ship's naturalist but as a gentleman companion for Captain FitzRoy—himself following charts made by James Colnett for the British whaling company Enderby & Sons. Darwin missed key evolutionary clues (Vice Governor Lawson told him the tortoises differed by island, but Darwin "did not for some time pay sufficient attention"), ate his way through the archipelago rather than collecting tortoise specimens, and brought home only four tiny pets. This case study demolishes the myth that the Galápagos produced an evolutionary epiphany and reframes the archipelago's significance as emerging through decades of post-voyage museum work, not through fieldwork revelation. The deeper point is that contemporary tourism's aesthetic "consumption" of tortoises—through cameras, souvenirs, cruise tickets—rests on the same political economy of extraction as the whaling era, just with different commodities.

**The California Academy of Sciences expedition (1905–06) and the making of species.** Hennessy traces how the fifteen tortoise species were not simply "discovered" but brought into being through specific social practices of collection, preservation, and museum classification. She follows Specimen 8109—the holotype of *Chelonoidis darwini*—from its capture on Santiago by collector Rollo Beck through skinning, preservation in arsenic soap, shipment to San Francisco, and formal description by John Van Denburgh.
