She also profiles Walter Rothschild, the eccentric banking heir who funded Beck's earlier expeditions and accumulated 144 giant tortoise specimens in his private museum at Tring, riding tortoises on his estate grounds. The case shows that species taxonomy was embedded in imperial power, personal obsession, and industrial-scale collecting. Species names, as historian Gordon McOuat argued, "are about power, about possession, about capital and the status of naturalists." Hennessy makes the striking point that the very practices of collection that made the tortoises legible as evidence of evolution also contributed to their decline—Beck found machete-hacked carapaces and near-extinct populations where oil collectors had recently worked.

**The founding of the Darwin Station and the "making" of a natural laboratory (1959–1964).** Hennessy reconstructs the mid-century alliance of Cold War science, transnational conservation institutions (UNESCO, IUCN), and the Ecuadorian state that created the Galápagos National Park and Charles Darwin Research Station in 1959\. She shows this was not simply about protecting nature but about forging a particular geographical imagination: one that cast the islands as a "living museum of evolution" and "monument to Charles Darwin." The 1964 Galápagos International Scientific Project arrived on a US military training vessel, was supported by US Navy helicopters, and threw a champagne party where an Ecuadorian general signed a 25-year scientific advisory agreement after riding a giant tortoise. Hennessy traces how this vision succeeded by absorbing competing imaginations of the archipelago—as agricultural frontier, island prison, Crusoe-style escape, and military outpost—into a single framework. But the laboratory ideal carried the seeds of its own crisis: its dependence on nature tourism opened the islands to flows of people, capital, and introduced species that undermined the very isolation conservationists sought to protect.

**Diego, captive breeding, and the violent-care of "restoring evolution."** Hennessy uses the celebrity tortoise Diego—a "supermacho" stud who sired nearly a thousand offspring after being returned from the San Diego Zoo in 1977—as an entry point into the ethical complexities of modern conservation. She details the daily labor of the breeding center (feeding, cleaning, nest excavation, egg incubation) from her own experience as a volunteer, including the discomfiting moment when she was asked to discard eggs from crosses between species that conservationists deemed genetically undesirable. She pairs this with the massive eradication campaigns against introduced species—the killing of over 160,000 goats on Isabela using helicopters, GPS tracking, and semiautomatic rifles in "Project Isabela." Drawing on Foucault, she frames this as a conservationist "biopower" that determines which species deserve to live and which must die, all in service of a baseline that is itself a historical artifact. The hybrid tortoises discovered on Volcán Wolf in 2008—crosses between Pinta and Floreana species and native Isabela tortoises, likely mixed by the very sailors conservationists condemn—offer the most poignant complication: the continued existence of "extinct" species is itself a product of the socio-natural history that nearly destroyed them.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The Galápagos have a tortoise problem. I do not mean a problem with saving the endangered animals themselves, which conservationists have been quite successful at addressing. Instead I mean a problem with what the giant tortoises represent." (p. 27\)

"The idea that the Galápagos archipelago is an evolutionary Eden is a 'geographical imagination'—a powerful way of understanding and engaging with particular places that reflects social, class, and political positions and has profound material effects." (p. 30\)

"It is not giant tortoises, but entanglement, all the way down in the Galápagos." (p. 260\)

"Conserving life, then, cannot be driven by nostalgia for an inaccessible past world, but must be inspired by hope for a future that is as diverse, as resplendent, and as just as possible." (p. 261\)

"The continued existence of these species is a product of the very socio-natural history that nearly caused their extinction." (p. 250\)

"The major oversight of mid-twentieth-century conservationists was that they failed to allow for the continued existence of the Galápagos human society, the members of which did not understand themselves to be living in a laboratory." (p. 259\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Hennessy pairs naturally with William Cronon's *Nature's Metropolis* (and his "Trouble with Wilderness" essay) for their shared insistence that "nature" is a historical and political production rather than a stable baseline; with Donna Haraway's work on multispecies entanglement and the politics of scientific knowledge-making; and with Gregg Mitman's *Empire of Rubber* for its attention to how extractive economies, scientific fieldwork, and conservation are mutually constitutive rather than opposed. On the history of science side, the book resonates strongly with Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison's *Objectivity* for its attention to how scientific practices (specimen-making, taxonomy, classification) produce their objects rather than simply describing them.

The book's main limitation is that despite its critique of the nature/culture divide, the voices and perspectives of ordinary Galápagos residents—fishers, farmers, recent migrants—remain somewhat thin compared to the richly detailed accounts of scientists, conservationists, and historical naturalists. The political economy of Ecuadorian state formation and its relationship to the archipelago is gestured at but never deeply developed, leaving an asymmetry between the careful treatment of northern scientific institutions and the more cursory handling of Latin American politics.

# **James A. Secord, *Victorian Sensation: The Extraordinary Publication, Reception, and Secret Authorship of Vestiges of the Natural History of Creation* (2000)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Secord argues that the most abstract ideas about nature should be understood first and foremost as material objects of commerce, situated in specific settings for reading. By reconstructing the full range of readers and readings of *Vestiges of the Natural History of Creation* (1844)—the anonymously published evolutionary bestseller that preceded Darwin's *Origin* by fifteen years—Secord demonstrates that what historians have called "the Darwinian Revolution" must be recast as an episode in the industrialization of communication and the transformation of reading audiences. The meaning of evolutionary ideas was not determined by their scientific content but was produced through the material circumstances of how, where, and by whom they were read.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

*Victorian Sensation* directly challenges the overwhelming centrality of Darwin and the *Origin of Species* in histories of evolutionary thought. In the standard narrative, *Vestiges* is dismissed as a "popular" work and a failed precursor—interesting only insofar as it prepared the ground for Darwin's real science. Secord argues that this framing is anachronistic, projecting back onto the 1840s the professional/popular divide that only emerged in the second half of the nineteenth century. To call *Vestiges* "popular" or its author an "amateur" in the 1840s is to apply categories that the very debates around the book helped to create. Secord is also intervening in the history of science more broadly, pushing against both intellectual history (which tends to treat ideas as disembodied) and the "common context" approach of Robert Young and others (which mapped scientific ideas onto political ideologies like competitive capitalism). Drawing on the history of the book—particularly the work of Roger Chartier, Robert Darnton, and D. F. McKenzie—Secord insists that meaning is produced through reading practices, and that texts, their material forms, and their audiences cannot be separated. He explicitly calls for the tools of literary critics, cultural historians, and historians of the book to be brought together in a way that had not been attempted for a nonfiction work in the industrial era.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Secord combines the history of the book (Chartier, Darnton, McKenzie) with cultural history and reception theory (Jauss, Gadamer) to treat *Vestiges* as a "cultural tracer"—a single work followed through all its uses and manifestations across Victorian society. Key concepts include the material form of texts as integral to their meaning, reading as a socially situated practice rather than a private intellectual act, and the "horizon of expectations" that writers project for implied readers.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Secord draws on an extraordinary breadth of archival sources: private diaries, personal letters, annotated copies, publishers' records, periodical reviews, newspaper editorials, pamphlets, lectures, and the publishing correspondence between Chambers, his intermediary Alexander Ireland, and the publisher John Churchill. The geographic scope covers Britain and its empire, with deep local studies of London's West End, Liverpool, Edinburgh, Cambridge, Oxford, and provincial towns like Halifax. The temporal scope runs roughly from the 1830s to the 1890s, with the core period being 1844–1860.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**Mary Smith reading by candlelight in Carlisle (1850).** Smith, the daughter of an Oxfordshire shoemaker and an unpaid schoolteacher, was "intensely anxious" to read *Vestiges*—the book that "most excited the wonder and curiosity of the reading world." When a copy arrived as a loan to her employer, she secretly borrowed it and sat up until daybreak finishing it at her bedroom window. Smith's account perfectly illustrates Secord's argument about reading as a situated, material, embodied act: her access to the book was constrained by class, gender, employment, and the physical arrangement of domestic space. She read clandestinely, at night, by candlelight—her encounter with cosmic evolution was inseparable from the practical circumstances of her life.

**Thomas Archer Hirst's journal in Halifax (1848).** Hirst, an eighteen-year-old surveyor's apprentice in a Yorkshire textile town, kept an extraordinarily detailed diary of his reading of *Vestiges*. He studied it for nearly a month, copying long passages, reading reviews and rebuttals, and recording debates with friends at taverns, the Mechanics' Institution, and his Mutual Improvement Society. Secord uses Hirst to show how reading functioned within a local culture of working- and middle-class self-improvement—not as a private encounter with ideas, but as a social process embedded in civic institutions and personal relationships. Hirst is later known as a member of the X-Club, the inner circle of Victorian scientific naturalism, but Secord insists on reading his experience forward from Halifax rather than backward from London.

**The Liverpool newspaper wars (1845–1846).** Liverpool—overcrowded, sectarian, riven by conflicts between Unitarians and evangelical Anglicans—produced the most prolonged newspaper debate about *Vestiges* anywhere in Britain. The Mechanics' Institution (Unitarian, classical facade) and the Collegiate Institution (Anglican, Tudor gothic) embodied competing visions of science's relationship to religion in literal stone. Secord uses the Liverpool case to demonstrate that reading was geographically specific: the same book provoked fundamentally different controversies in different cities, depending on local religious politics, class structure, and institutional culture. It was through Liverpool's newspapers—not London's—that readers across the country first learned that men of science condemned *Vestiges*.

**Aristocratic salon conversations in London's West End (early 1845).** The first 1,750 copies of *Vestiges* targeted an elite readership, and the book became the talk of London high Society through conversation at parties, clubs, and dinners. Secord reconstructs these interactions from diaries and letters—Lady Amelia Murray discussing cosmic evolution at Lady Noel Byron's party, Disraeli reporting to his sister that the book "is convulsing the world, anonymous." Secord notes that there are no histories of the Victorian soiree, no analyses of male conversation in clubs, and no accounts of how talk at aristocratic gatherings shaped intellectual fashion—despite the uniquely rich archival sources. He uses this case to argue that fashionable conversation, not published reviews, initially determined the book's reception and that the early editions were aimed at the aristocracy, not the commercial middle classes with which *Vestiges* later became associated.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"The most abstract ideas about nature should be approached first and foremost as material objects of commerce and situated in specific settings for reading." (p. 4\)

"What once made sense as the 'Darwinian Revolution' must be recast as an episode in the industrialization of communication and the transformation of reading audiences." (p. 4\)

"Every act of reading is an act of forgetting: the experience of reading is a palimpsest, in which each text partially covers those that came before. Those books that allow us to forget the most are accorded the authority of the classic." (p. 515\)

"The statement 'Darwin published his Origin of Species in 1859' is false. Authors are not publishers, texts are not books, and books are not the interpretative property of authors." (p. 518\)

"The mass-communications industry never created a passive, homogeneous audience: it stereotyped books and newspapers, not readers." (p. 525\)

"A nostalgic obsession with gentility has blinded historians to the fact that for most practitioners science was a demanding, even desperate way of making money." (p. 439\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*Victorian Sensation* pairs naturally with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump*, which similarly argues that the credibility of scientific knowledge depends on social arrangements (gentlemanly trust, virtual witnessing) rather than on the inherent truth of experimental results—though Secord extends this analysis into the age of industrial print culture. It also connects directly to Daston and Galison's *Objectivity*, which traces how epistemic virtues shift over time; Secord's account of gentlemanly science and the emergence of commercial expertise covers the same transitional period from a different angle. For the History of Technology list, the book resonates with the work on communication networks (Carey, Kern, John, Hochfelder) and with Williams's concept of "mobile privatisation"—*Vestiges* was read on trains, in private bedrooms, in clubs, making it a case study in how new communication technologies restructured the relationship between public and private reading.

Secord's commitment to exhaustive local reconstruction can sometimes overwhelm the reader with detail at the expense of a sharper analytic throughline; the sheer volume of individual reading cases occasionally makes it difficult to see the larger argument in motion. The book also has relatively little to say about readers outside Britain—the American reception is gestured at but never given the deep treatment that Liverpool or Halifax receive.

# **Nick Hopwood, *Haeckel's Embryos: Images, Evolution, and Fraud* (University of Chicago Press, 2015\)**

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Hopwood argues that the long career of Ernst Haeckel's comparative embryo illustrations—from their creation in 1868 through three major fraud controversies and into the genomics age—reveals how copying, far from being a derivative or passive process, is creative, contested, and consequential in the history of scientific knowledge. By tracking how one set of images was produced, reproduced, reworked, debated, and canonized across nearly two centuries, he shows that the fates of scientific pictures are determined less by their accuracy or the intentions of their makers than by the shifting conditions of publishing, pedagogy, disciplinary politics, and public culture through which they travel.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Hopwood intervenes against two dominant tendencies in the history of science. The first is the habit of treating scientific images as mere illustrations of ideas—transparent windows onto nature or vehicles for theory—rather than as material objects with their own complex histories of production, reproduction, and reception. Against this, he insists that images have social lives: their meanings change as they are copied into new contexts, and their survival depends on publishing economics, printing technology, copyright law, and institutional gatekeeping as much as on scientific accuracy. The second tendency is the individualist, hero-or-villain approach to scientific fraud, exemplified by the way Haeckel has been repeatedly tried and retried as either a deliberate forger or an innocent schematizer. Hopwood draws on the sociological insight of Jan Sapp and Reinhard Gursch that fraud is itself socially constructed—that controversies serve to confirm or refute attributions of fraud depending on broader assessments of a scientist's reputation and the political stakes of the moment. Where Stephen Jay Gould attributed the persistence of Haeckel's images to "mindless recycling," Hopwood shows that their survival required active decisions by publishers, textbook authors, and curriculum committees, and that the images were repeatedly reworked, recontextualized, and even rehabilitated. The book is in explicit dialogue with David Kaiser's work on Feynman diagrams, Lorraine Daston and Peter Galison's *Objectivity*, and the broader visual turn in the history of science associated with scholars like Martin Rudwick and James Secord.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Hopwood combines the history of scientific illustration and visual culture with a social history of knowledge communication that attends to the material practices of printing, copying, and publishing. His key analytical concept is "the shock of the copy"—the idea that images' repeated involvement in innovation, not merely their longevity, is what makes their histories surprising and consequential. He also draws on the sociology of scientific knowledge (particularly around fraud and trust) and on reception history that treats consumption as analytically equal to production.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

Hopwood conducted archival research across multiple countries, most importantly at the Ernst Haeckel House in Jena, where he discovered the original drawings hidden since 1868, and at collections in Basel, London, Cambridge, and the United States. He draws on correspondence between Haeckel and his publishers, previously unexploited manuscripts, print-run data, reviews in newspapers and magazines, textbook surveys across three national traditions (German, British, American), and interviews with late-twentieth-century scientists involved in the 1997 controversy. The temporal scope runs from roughly the 1750s (when developmental series first became common) through 2010, with geographic focus on German-speaking Europe, Britain, and the United States.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The making of the first grid (1868–1874).** Hopwood reconstructs step by step how Haeckel pieced together his comparative embryo plates for the *Natürliche Schöpfungsgeschichte* and then the *Anthropogenie*. Using newly discovered original drawings from the Jena archive, he shows that critics' most damning charge—that Haeckel had the same woodblock printed three times and labeled as three different species—was technically accurate, was swiftly corrected, and was slowly admitted. But the more debatable accusation, that Haeckel had "tendentiously schematized" from well-known originals, requires contextualizing his drawing practices within the visual conventions of mid-nineteenth-century university embryology courses, where schematic simplification was standard. The grid itself—with its columns for species and rows for developmental stages—was an invention of comparative seeing, an "aid to comparative seeing like the pairs, tableaus, series, arrays, and overlays which helped eyes detect similarities and differences." Its power lay in its visual format, not merely in the specific drawings that filled its cells.

**The "Forbidden Fruit" and German school culture (1870s–1900s).** Some of the book's most vivid material concerns the reception of Haeckel's embryos among young people in Imperial Germany. Grammar-school boys formed "secret leagues" to read the banned books in back rooms of pubs. Wilhelm Bölsche, later Germany's most successful science writer, was initiated into evolutionary thinking this way as a teenager in Cologne. The young Carl Rabl "read the book with true devotion day and night" at his Austrian gymnasium despite a priest's warnings, and went on to become a professional embryologist who sent Haeckel corrected drawings. The ban on Darwinism from German schools—a product of the Kulturkampf-era collision between science and religion—gave these images the allure of forbidden knowledge. Meanwhile, progressive schoolteacher Hermann Müller in Lippstadt lent pupils Haeckel's books and provoked a national controversy when his students allegedly shouted "Carbon\!" to correct a religion teacher quoting the Gospel of John. The pictures pitted "enthralled schoolchildren against outraged priests, student Darwinists against expert professors, young women against prudish parents, and itinerant freethinkers against defenders of Christian faith."

**The Normal Plates project and the creation of visual standards (1880s–1910s).** Haeckel's most persistent critic, the anatomist Wilhelm His, did not merely attack the drawings but used the controversy as a spur to reform human embryology entirely. His developed new sectioning and modeling techniques, established the first "normal plate" for ordering human embryos, and launched an international project—the *Normal Plates on the Development of the Vertebrates*—that effectively turned each of Haeckel's species columns into a comprehensive monograph. These normal plates and the staging systems derived from them are still used in developmental biology today. Hopwood's central irony is that Haeckel's contested images provoked the creation of the very visual standards against which they were subsequently judged inadequate. The most authoritative images in embryology were created "in part in reaction against these, the most contested."

**The 1997 fraud controversy and the Discovery Institute.** In 1997, the London-based embryologist Michael Richardson published a paper arguing that vertebrate embryos do not look as similar as Haeckel depicted, accompanied by new comparative photographs. Richardson then went to the press, telling the Times of London that Haeckel's drawings were "one of the worst cases of scientific fraud." *Science* magazine headlined the story "Fraud Rediscovered." The neo-creationist Discovery Institute seized the opportunity, drafting warning stickers for high-school textbooks and pressuring publishers to remove the figures. Yet the "discovery" was old news to any historically informed biologist—the accusations were nearly as old as the pictures themselves. The irony of this iconoclasm was that forcing the images out of textbooks made them more globally available than ever, as creationists and the Internet circulated them as "ruined idols"—"emblems of evolutionist perfidy." Then in 2010, a version of the 136-year-old grid appeared on the cover of *Nature*, constructed from 66,579 images of *Drosophila* gene expression patterns, illustrating new genomic evidence for the phylotypic stage. The pictures Hopwood assumed were "finally on their way out" proved to be involved in visual innovation once again.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Copying, the epitome of the unoriginal, has been creative, contested, and consequential." (p. 7\)

"The 'first public embryo spectacle' starred Haeckel's illustrations, which entered ordinary homes and stimulated influential research." (p. 26\)

"'Development' ... is from now on the magic word through which we shall solve all of the riddles around us, or can at least reach the path to their solution." (p. 69, quoting Haeckel)

"'Generations of biology students may have been misled,' reported Science magazine." (p. 286, quoting *Science* 1997\)

"'The shock of the copy,' like 'the shock of the old,' is not that scientific images last for long periods or even that they may reach furthest a century after they were first produced, but their repeated involvement in innovation." (p. 301\)

"We accept a picture less because we believe individual scientists or artists to whom we rarely have independent access than because a periodical, publisher, channel, or URL inspires trust." (p. 300\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

Hopwood's book pairs naturally with Daston and Galison's *Objectivity* (2007), which provides the broader framework for understanding shifting visual epistemologies—mechanical objectivity, trained judgment—within which Haeckel's schematic drawings became problematic. It also connects powerfully to Kaiser's *Drawing Theories Apart* (2005), which similarly tracks how a visual tool (Feynman diagrams) was adopted, adapted, and perpetuated through networks of practitioners rather than through any simple correspondence with nature. For the history of technology reading list, it resonates with the themes of Bowker and Star's *Sorting Things Out* on how classification systems become invisible infrastructure, and with Edgerton's *The Shock of the Old* on how use and maintenance—rather than invention—determine the real history of technology.

One limitation is that the book's focus on German, British, and American contexts means that the circulation of Haeckel's images in colonial and postcolonial settings—where evolutionary illustrations carried particular ideological weight—receives only passing attention. Additionally, while Hopwood is excellent on the institutional and publishing conditions that sustained the images, he sometimes moves so carefully through the details of each controversy that the book's analytical arguments can be hard to extract from the narrative.

# **Paul Rabinow, *Making PCR: A Story of Biotechnology* (1996)**

---

## **1\. CENTRAL ARGUMENT**

Rabinow argues that the polymerase chain reaction was not the product of a lone genius but emerged from a contingent ensemble of subjects, sites, and objects — a specific corporate milieu (Cetus Corporation in the 1980s), a cast of scientists and technicians with distinct vocational dispositions, and a set of technical elements that had to be recombined and made to work experimentally before PCR existed as a scientific entity. The book insists that concept, experimental system, and institutional context are inseparable: PCR did not become PCR until a team turned Kary Mullis's idea into a demonstrably functioning tool.

## **2\. HISTORIOGRAPHIC INTERVENTION**

Rabinow positions himself against two symmetrical "corruption stories" that dominate accounts of late-twentieth-century bioscience. The first is a nostalgia narrative: that molecular biology was once a pure, cooperative, Mertonian enterprise corrupted by commercialization and profit. The second is a cynical inversion from the sociology of scientific knowledge (SSK): that because Mertonian norms were never perfectly realized, science was never anything more than self-interested power play. Both, Rabinow argues, are "Manichaean commonplaces" that obscure more than they reveal. Drawing explicitly on Steven Shapin's *A Social History of Truth* — and pushing past it — Rabinow argues that evaluations of character, trust, and virtue remain constitutive of laboratory practice but have been fundamentally reconfigured in the biotech milieu, no longer operating within the gentlemanly culture Shapin describes. Against the "lone genius" narrative that the Nobel Prize committee and science journalism reinforced by awarding Mullis alone, Rabinow documents the collective, institutional, and deeply contingent character of the invention. The book is also in direct conversation with Max Weber's "Science as a Vocation" and John Dewey's pragmatist epistemology, using both to frame what a scientific life looks like when the boundaries between university and industry, pure and applied, are being renegotiated. Rabinow's approach — an anthropologist studying scientists ethnographically — extends the laboratory studies tradition of Latour, Knorr-Cetina, and others while criticizing their relative silence on science as a vocation and a form of life regulation.

## **3\. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK**

Rabinow works within an anthropological and Foucauldian framework, treating biotechnology as a distinctive "configuration" of scientific, technical, economic, legal, and cultural elements. His key analytic categories are the *milieu* (the institutional space that makes certain kinds of work possible), the *subject* (the kinds of persons fashioned by and for scientific practice), and the *object* (the technical invention itself as it moves from concept to experimental system to tool). He mobilizes Weber's concept of *Beruf* (vocation/calling) and Dewey's linking of experiment and experience to analyze what it means to do science in a for-profit setting.

## **4\. METHODOLOGY & SOURCES**

The book is an ethnographic study based primarily on extended oral history interviews with all the major actors at Cetus Corporation — scientists, technicians, and business leaders — conducted between 1990 and 1994\. Rabinow supplements these with internal Cetus memos, annual reports, lab notebooks, patent filings, published scientific papers, and the Du Pont v. Cetus trial record. The geographic scope is the San Francisco Bay Area biotech world of the late 1970s through the late 1980s, with contextual attention to the national regulatory, legal, and funding environment.

## **5\. KEY CASE STUDIES**

**The Monterey Retreat (June 1984).** At Cetus's annual scientific meeting, Mullis presented PCR on a poster that was largely ignored by colleagues and the company's distinguished scientific advisory board. The same night, Mullis got into a physical altercation with another scientist, made abusive phone calls to Tom White at 3 a.m., and had to be escorted to the beach by hotel security. This episode crystallizes one of the book's central tensions: the relationship between personal credibility and scientific credibility. Mullis's declining reputation — driven by romantic entanglements in the lab, threats of violence, and a track record of grandiose claims without data — meant that his genuinely revolutionary concept was dismissed. White and Price debated firing him. They ultimately decided to keep him, but only barely, and only because White believed PCR deserved a real experimental test. The case shows that character judgments did not merely accompany scientific evaluation; they *constituted* it. Trust and distrust shaped whose ideas got resources and attention.

**The Beta-Globin Model System and the OR Assay.** Erlich's Human Genetics group, unable to develop a "solve-it-all-in-one-kit" diagnostic approach, reversed strategy and chose the well-studied beta-globin mutation (underlying sickle-cell anemia) as a model system. This choice was pivotal: it gave the PCR group a known target against which to test amplification and specificity. Randy Saiki's observation — "Everyone was trying to amplify the signal; no one was thinking about ways of amplifying the target" — captures the conceptual inversion that made PCR transformative. The extended experimental labor of making the oligomer restriction assay work, and then combining it with PCR, demonstrates Rabinow's argument that invention is not a moment of insight but a drawn-out process of making a concept function experimentally. The months of drudgery — manually transferring tubes between near-boiling and 37°C water baths, cycle after cycle — reveal PCR before automation as embodied, artisanal labor.

**The Taq Polymerase Breakthrough.** The introduction of the heat-stable enzyme from *Thermus aquaticus* was the last major piece that transformed PCR from a laborious procedure into a practical machine. Mullis proposed using a thermostable polymerase but refused to do the purification himself; Gelfand and Susanne Stoffel completed the work in about three weeks. The first experiment with Taq on human genomic DNA for beta globin produced a single clean band — "It worked like a charm," Gelfand recalled. The enzyme was so much more specific than the Klenow fragment it replaced that, as Gelfand put it, "We didn't realize how 'bad' Klenow had been because Klenow was so much better than anything before it." This case study demonstrates how a crucial technical contribution — one that made PCR commercially viable — came not from the conceptual inventor but from a team of enzyme specialists working in a shared institutional space. It also illustrates Rabinow's argument about the convertibility of symbolic and material capital: Mullis received a $10,000 bonus (the largest ever at Cetus), while Erlich, Saiki, and others received the nominal $1 for their patent contributions, and Hoffmann-La Roche eventually paid $300 million for the PCR rights.

**The HIV Detection Project.** John Sninsky and Shirley Kwok's effort to develop a PCR-based test for HIV illustrates PCR's extraordinary versatility and the social dynamics of technician-led innovation. Kwok, who had risen from an entry-level screening position without a PhD, became project leader and eventually director of Infectious Diseases. When the team decoded their first set of blinded AIDS blood samples and correctly identified the single positive, "we were dancing up and down." But the project also revealed PCR's most dangerous feature: contamination. Amplification was so powerful that even trace amounts of previously amplified product produced false positives. Kwok's colleagues initially blamed her technique — "You're just sloppy" — before the group recognized that the technology's very power demanded entirely new spatial organization of laboratory work, including dedicated clean hoods and rigorous separation of pre- and post-amplification areas. The case shows how a tool can reshape the material and social organization of the laboratory itself.

## **6\. ESSENTIAL QUOTES**

"Everyone was trying to amplify the signal; no one was thinking about ways of amplifying the target." — Randy Saiki (p. 84\)

"Once PCR had been worked out, i.e. developed, only then was it useful." — Henry Erlich (p. 7\)

"It was so simple it didn't take a lot of explanation. Once they understood it they thought there must be some reason that it didn't work." — Corey Levenson (p. 98\)

"He was naive enough not to expect it to work. He knew just enough molecular biology to understand how he might do it but not so much that he would be discouraged from trying." — Randy Saiki (p. 160\)

"I define practices as norms in context and in process." — Paul Rabinow (p. 14\)

"Being a simple little thing PCR tends to work its way into many studies. Everyone thinks of their own little twist to put on it to make it work for their own particular problem." — Kary Mullis (p. 168\)

## **7\. CONNECTIONS**

*Making PCR* pairs naturally with Shapin and Schaffer's *Leviathan and the Air-Pump* on the relationship between personal credibility and scientific knowledge-making, and with Collins's *Changing Order* on the experimenters' regress — the problem of knowing when an experiment has "worked" when the result is itself what's in question. It also connects productively to Neff's *Venture Labor* on risk-bearing in innovative industries, and to Galison's *Image and Logic* on the material culture of experimental practice and the role of trading zones between different scientific communities.

One limitation is that Rabinow's ethnographic focus on Cetus insiders provides little perspective from outside the company — competitors, regulators, or the scientific community that eventually adopted PCR. A second is that the philosophical framing through Weber, Nietzsche, and Dewey, while intellectually ambitious, sometimes sits awkwardly alongside the ethnographic material and can feel like a separate book grafted onto the case study.

Sure. The book reads almost like a ensemble drama set at a single company, so here's the arc.

**The Setting:** Cetus Corporation, founded in 1971 in Berkeley, was the first recombinant DNA startup company. By the early 1980s it was transitioning from old-school fermentation work (improving vitamin B₁₂ yields, etc.) into recombinant DNA technology, under intense pressure from venture capitalists and competitors like Genentech to produce commercially viable products. The company was chaotic — too many projects, unclear decision-making authority, and constant tension between business leadership wanting products and scientists wanting to do good science.

**The Cast:**

**Tom White** is arguably the book's moral center. A Berkeley-trained biochemist who did the Peace Corps in Liberia to avoid Vietnam, he joined Cetus in 1978 when it was considered career suicide for a serious scientist to go to industry. He became head of Recombinant Molecular Research in 1981 and spent years trying to impose organizational rationality on Cetus — writing memos demanding clear authority structures, instituting internal seminars, centralizing hiring. He's the one who hired Mullis, protected him from being fired, insisted PCR get a real experimental test, assembled the team that made it work, and then negotiated Mullis's departure when things became untenable. Rabinow clearly admires him — he frames White through Weber's concept of vocation as someone who combined scientific integrity with pragmatic institutional management.

**Kary Mullis** is the charismatic, erratic inventor of the PCR concept. A Berkeley PhD in biochemistry (under the tolerant J.P. Neilands), he'd bounced through a series of jobs — pediatric cardiology in Kansas, managing a bakery in Berkeley, isolating endorphins at UCSF — before White got him hired at Cetus in 1979 to make oligonucleotides. He was brilliant at improving synthesis speed but terrible at institutional life: affairs with lab mates that turned into public scenes, threats of violence, grandiose claims without data, contempt for proof. His conceptual breakthrough came on a drive to his Mendocino cabin in spring 1983 — he realized that by using two primers bracketing a DNA target and repeating cycles of heating, cooling, and polymerase extension, you could exponentially amplify a specific DNA fragment. But he then did essentially nothing experimentally for months. His first experiments (September–December 1983\) all failed. His August 1983 seminar presentation was met with heckling and walkouts. His credibility was so low — "on page 141, I'm curing cancer," as he noted of his own notebook — that almost no one took the idea seriously. He left Cetus in September 1986 with a $10,000 bonus and won the Nobel Prize in 1993\.

**Henry Erlich** is the senior scientist who ran the Human Genetics group. He'd come to Cetus from Stanford, drawn by the possibility of pursuing his passion for the HLA (immune system) genetics while also developing commercial diagnostics. His group had been working on the beta-globin/sickle-cell system as a model for genetic diagnostics, developing the "oligomer restriction" assay — useful work but limited by the fundamental sensitivity problem. Erlich co-directed the PCR group that White assembled in summer 1984, provided the critical analytical criteria for evaluating whether PCR was actually working, and later led the application of PCR to HLA typing, which Rabinow presents as the real scientific payoff of PCR. In the book's conclusion, Erlich is the most openly angry about the Nobel going to Mullis alone, arguing that Mullis "never really got it to work" and that the Nobel certified a false creation myth.

**Randy Saiki** was the technician (no PhD) in Erlich's group who executed almost every experiment in the first PCR publication. Rabinow portrays him as the consummate bench scientist — respected across the company, technically superb, quietly effective. When Arnheim and Erlich decided the project needed someone who could reliably produce clean, publishable data, they assigned Saiki full-time. His earlier observation that "everyone was trying to amplify the signal; no one was thinking about ways of amplifying the target" captures the conceptual shift that PCR represented. In the conclusion, he's more weary than angry about the credit question — he sees it as a tedious game — but insists that without the team, Mullis would have dropped PCR and moved on to something else.

**Norman Arnheim** was a tenured professor from Stony Brook who took a sabbatical at Cetus in 1983–84 — the first tenured professor to do a full sabbatical at the company. He co-directed the PCR group with Erlich beginning in summer 1984\. His role was partly supervisory (White asked him to monitor what Mullis was doing) and partly experimental (he suggested key strategies like the "blocking oligonucleotide" approach). He was initially skeptical of Mullis's data and pushed for rigorous controls, which Mullis resented as intrusive.

**Stephen Scharf** was the technician who, on November 15, 1984, produced the first convincing experimental demonstration that PCR worked — the "IT WORKS\!" entry in his lab notebook. He did more of the grueling manual labor of early PCR than anyone (transferring tubes between water baths, cycle after cycle). Rabinow uses him to illustrate the ambiguous status of technicians: everyone acknowledged his skill, but he was denied the status, pay, and access (he wasn't allowed to attend the Monterey conference) that his contributions warranted.

**David Gelfand** was a Cetus veteran who had headed the recombinant DNA division since 1976\. His most consequential contribution came later: when Mullis proposed using a heat-stable polymerase but refused to do the purification himself, and no protein chemist at Cetus would spare the time, Gelfand and **Susanne Stoffel** purified the Taq polymerase in about three weeks. That enzyme transformed PCR from a laborious manual process into something approaching a machine — the first Taq experiment produced a single clean band from human genomic DNA, a quantum leap over the messy results with the Klenow enzyme.

**Fred Faloona** was Mullis's loyal assistant, a technician without a BA who occupied the bottom of the scientific hierarchy. His low status and friendly personality let him function as a go-between, carrying reagents, results, and news between Mullis's operation and the PCR group without provoking territorial conflicts. The praise and blame went to Mullis; Faloona was largely invisible.

**John Sninsky and Shirley Kwok** represent the next phase. Sninsky arrived in 1984 and proposed using PCR to detect HIV, which marketing dismissed because AIDS was "restricted to homosexuals" and therefore not a significant commercial opportunity. Kwok, who had started at Cetus as an entry-level technician screening antibiotic isolates, became the project leader. Their HIV work demonstrated PCR's extraordinary power — they correctly identified a single positive in a blinded set of ten blood samples — but also revealed the contamination problem that would haunt PCR for years. Kwok's trajectory from entry-level tech to director of Infectious Diseases is one of Rabinow's examples of how Cetus's flatter hierarchy enabled careers that would have been impossible in academia.

**Robert Fildes** appears as a background antagonist of sorts — the CEO brought in to impose commercial discipline, who was "adamantly opposed to wasting resources on this non-therapeutic project" and refused to fund Taq purification as late as 1990\.

**The Plot Arc:** Concept (spring 1983\) → failed experiments and institutional skepticism (fall 1983–summer 1984\) → White assembles the PCR group and assigns Arnheim and Erlich to supervise (summer 1984\) → Scharf's breakthrough demonstration (November 1984\) → months of systematic optimization by Saiki, Scharf, and the team (winter 1984–spring 1985\) → first publication (1985) → Taq purification by Gelfand and Stoffel transforms the technology (1986) → Mullis leaves Cetus (September 1986\) → Hoffmann-La Roche buys rights for $300 million → Mullis wins Nobel Prize alone (1993), which the Cetus scientists view as certifying a false creation myth.

The whole thing reads as an argument that invention is a *process* distributed across persons and institutions, not a *moment* located in one mind on a country road.

# My Essays

# Week 1 – Foundations

## **Week 1 — Generals Write-Up**

**Matthew Jordan**

A first impression after working through these nine books: there are so many ways to do the history of science, and in particular the history of the period we often classify as the “scientific revolution”. So many possible prime movers—artisans’ contact with intellectuals, changing discourses around the wonders or exploitation of nature, new social practices for knowledge creation in closed-off labs or public dissections or Caribbean ritual practices, the shifting role of religion—and so many things worth explaining: new worldviews, world pictures, epistemes, relationships to nature, and the body, and the purpose of craft, and the interplay of tools, knowledge, markets, and ideology.

In short, any idea I might have had that there was any straightforward story to tell—about this or indeed any period in history—was immediately vanquished. I will explain them each one-by-one then draw out some common themes and observations across them all.

I began with Foucault’s *The Order of Things* (1966). The central message, as I read it, was the following: it is not merely factual knowledge that changes from one generation to the next; it is the organization and structure of knowledge itself, the kinds of knowing that are even possible by any given epoch’s central guiding epistemic foundation or *episteme*. The study of “man” only became possible around 1800 with the advent of the modern episteme, from which the “human sciences” (economics, psychology, linguistics, sociology, etc.) emerged. But this formulation, too, might be temporary, “​​like a face drawn in sand at the edge of the sea."

Prior to our modern era, the governing episteme of the Renaissance was Resemblance, or similarity—knowledge is organized by similitude. In the classical age, knowledge was Representation—a time of ordering and taxonomy. Our modern episteme is characterized by theoretical, non-representational principles like “life” in biology or “labor” in economics; invisible forces that limit man’s potential. Foucault’s argument is rooted in foundational texts from each age in natural history, grammar, philosophy, science and politics. These are read for what they reveal about the underlying epistemic structure of the age, rather than for their actual substantive arguments.

Katherine Park’s *Secrets of Women* (2006) has a double-edged title: it refers to both “knowledge women possess” and “knowledge about women’s bodies men must uncover”. Park shows that dissection itself was far more complex than first appears, and was actually part of a diverse set of religious and familial practices, far outside of university settings: embalming, autopsies, *sectio in mortua* (extraction of fetuses from women who died in childbirth), the inspection of holy bodies, preserving nuns’ bodies for veneration. The uterus became emblematic of these “secrets of women”—the site of human generation and therefore the greatest mystery. The actors in this book (inhabitants of northern Italian cities from the mid-thirteenth to mid-sixteenth century) understood their bodies in terms of family & kinship & religion. Medicine was a distant concept.

The book contrasts knowledge that was shared privately between women—about anatomy, sexuality, pregnancy, childbirth, and health— with private knowledge that had to be obtained by trained medical experts often using practices like dissection that emerged in non-academic settings. Two traditions of knowledge: one oral, domestic, and female, the other literate, public, and male. The latter eventually “wins out”—this is "the masculine birth of gynecology": women's authority over their own bodily knowledge supplanted by knowledge generated in institutions that women were not even permitted to attend.

Carolyn Merchant’s *The Death of Nature* (1980) makes the bold argument that the transformation we call the “scientific revolution” was in fact the replacement of an organic, female-centered conception of nature as a living, nurturing mother, with a mechanistic conception of nature as dead, inert matter subject to domination and exploitation. Merchant reviews the worldviews and language of central figures like Francis Bacon, Thomas Hobbes, and Rene Descartes, and shows that their language and philosophies draw from discourses of, say, mining, witch hunting, and mechanical technology. Nature, through their texts, becomes predictable, rule-governed, a resource for extraction, and therefore justifies deforestation & drainage of fens.

Quite explicit in Merchant’s argument is that metaphor, language, and imagery play a very active role in shaping thought & action. The description of nature as a nurturing mother vs. a machine are not merely discursive; they inform worldviews, and in turn, shape our collective relationship with ecosystems. The source base therefore includes a lot of sources of metaphor and imagery, such a the poetry of John Donne,[^1] along with standard primary texts from Bacon, Boyle, Newton, Leibniz, Hobbes, Anne Conway, etc. This is also a kind of environmental & technological history, with its focus on the history of mining, fen drainage, and forest management.

*The Body of the Artisan* (2004) by Pamela Smith suggests another location where new kinds of scientific knowledge are to be found: in the studio of the artisan. Painters, sculptors, goldsmith, potters, and other makers possess a unique artisanal epistemology, an embedded set of tacit knowledge, that enabled them to make deeply “scientific” claims, generating insights about plants, animals, metals, and more. The book is filled to bursting with unbelievable images of paintings, sketches, glassware, engravings, and other artworks, which Smith argues were part of a process of acquiring very legitimate forms of scientific expertise. Some of it’s freaky as heck.

Reading slightly outside of the book itself, Smith’s book is in the tradition of the Zilsel Thesis, which argues that the Scientific Revolution was enabled by the contact of craftspeople with academically trained rational thinkers. Smith takes this claim one step further, arguing that in many cases the craftspeople did not need the elite academic thinkers in order to make radical new claims about the natural world. For Smith’s artisans, knowledge did not come from books, “knowledge was produced through bodily labor and accumulated in the body through repeated practice”. 

![][image3]![][image4]

All of these previous works about knowledge and expertise have been situated in Europe. For the first non-Western perspective on this list, we turn to Pablo Gómez’s *The Experiential Caribbean* (2017). Gómez argues that Black Caribbean ritual practitioners (*Mohanes*, meaning a “master of sorcerers”) led their own epistemological revolution in the 17th century, developing new kinds of knowledge about bodies that broke from previous traditions. An example is Bernardo Macaya, who purported to control the weather, performed diagnostic ceremonies, maintained a sophisticated altar suitable for multiethnic Caribbean visitors, and who was feared by many despite having arrived to Panama as a slave from the Congo.

A key focus is the idea of “experiential”: these practitioners were making knowledge claims and performing acts based on sensory experience, as opposed to the appeal of authority or dogma. “​​Caribbean Mohanes were less interested in reinforcing particular paradigms and dogmas in a place in which they could not be ascertained or understood, and more interested in gaining power from the viscerally felt nature of the astonishing”. I haven’t gotten to it yet in this write-up, but in this sense *The Experiential Caribbean* and *Wonders and the Order of Nature* make a nice pair.

“There was no such thing as the scientific revolution, and this is a book about it” is the now-classic opening to Shapin’s *The Scientific Revolution* (1996). This is a fairly different book than the rest on this list because it’s not really operating from primary source texts. It’s a kind of “historiographic update” on arguments around the idea of the Scientific Revolution, planting a stake in the ground against the notion articulated midcentury by Alexandre Koyré, Herbert Butterfield, and A. Rupert Hall that there was a single, discontinuous “revolution” whose leading figures (Galileo, Bacon, Newton) we can point to as the originators of “both the modern world and of the modern mentality”.

Instead, Shapin argues, science was and always is historically situated, contingent, messy, and politically fraught. Several assumptions about the Scientific Revolution are in fact untrue, and Shapin dismantles them one by one. It was not a wholesale rejection of the past (plenty of continuities with medieval thought), experience did not triumph over authority (social trust & testimony played a major role), science was not opposed to religion (it was often religion’s “Hand-maiden”). The book also serves to “SSK”-ify the story of the scientific revolution, showing that science is not about internal ideas vs. external social factors: "There is as much society inside the scientist's laboratory, and internal to the development of scientific knowledge, as there is outside." Along the way, Shapin “re-reads” the narrative of Galileo, Bacon, Descartes, and Boyle, situating sun spots, the empirical program, the air pump, and the mechanical program in a broader historical context.

Daniela Bleichmar’s *Visible Empire: Botanical Expeditions and Visual Culture in the Hispanic Enlightenment* (2012) probably does not belong on this list, because it’s from a totally different time period, but that’s ok. The basic argument is that Spanish natural history expeditions were “visualization projects” designed to make nature visible through illustrations. The point is to de-center the primacy of textual knowledge—in this sense it has much in common with *The Body of the Artisan* and the production of wares. 

A question the book invites is exemplified in the Cavanilles-Ruiz controversy. Who gets to take credit for the authorship of a new plant: the person who travels to see it (Ruiz), or the person whose illustrations make the plant visible for wider audiences (Cavanilles)? Cavanilles: “The author is not the one who gathers plants and seeds and ships them without due examination: the true author of a plant is only the one who makes it known to the public”. This book feels like it must be read alongside *Objectivity* which very clearly informs the argument here.

*What is the History of Knowledge* (2015) is also a very different kind of book—a survey of an emerging field-in-progress. It’s also unusual because of its size: 125 pages, written for the “What is History?” series (Burke previously wrote *What is Cultural History* (2004)). The idea is that “science” is an incomplete category, representing a particular narrow and quite new Western worldview that does not take into account the situatedness and contingency of knowledge, and also the many different kinds of knowledge worthy of systematic study. 

Beyond science, there are many other ways of turning information (raw) into knowledge (cooked). Tactical knowledge, bureaucratic knowledge, traditional Indigenous knowledge, knowledge embedded in cultures, tacit knowledge, bodily knowledge—all of these count as worthy of study in the history of knowledge.

Finally, *Wonders and the Order of Nature, 1150–1750* (1998) by Lorraine Daston & Katharine Park. The argument is fairly straightforward: wonders—objects or phenomena that mark the “outermost limits of the natural”—were a core part of the natural order from the Middle Ages to the Enlightenment. By the mid-18th century, however, marvels disappeared from elite discourses, and came to be viewed as vulgar, for three reasons. First metaphysical: nature came to be viewed as regular and uniform. Second, political: wonders were viewed as powerful, portentious. Protestants and Catholics alike tamped down on the culture of portents. Finally aesthetic: art moved towards verisimilitude, theater moved towards probability. This cultural shift made wonders less salient.

Medieval and early modern thinkers distinguished three domains: Natural (orginary), preternatural, and supernatural (caused directly by God). Wonders fell in the middle, eliciting both curiosity and delight. Naturalists’ stance towards wonders, as sites of “cognitive passion”, was "undulatory, continuous, sometimes cyclical". The book historicizes passion itself, tracing the way that scientists and previous thinkers responded to wonders like monstrous births (conjoined twins), glowing objects, bioluminiscence, colorful minerals, magnetic objects, animal tusks, geographic marvels (volcanoes), astrological anomalies, automata, unusual cloud formations, and much more. Much like *The Body of the Artisan*, this book is filled to the brim with examples of unusual animals, people, and objects.

Having now summarized these texts, I offer some stray and not particularly well-developed thoughts on these works as a whole.

One immediate thread I noticed is a focus on *language* as the locus for evaluating historical change. Foucault and Merchant in particular use texts not for their substantive content, but for the *way* their subject matter is being discussed, and then use the language to argue for the existence of an entirely different a priori manner of seeing the world. This seems extremely challenging to prove\! I am curious to learn more about what is informing this particular idea in historical and philosophical scholarship. Was something in the water in the ‘80s that led people to lean into this mode of argument?

Another central theme, and this somewhat goes without saying, is the de-centering of certain triumphalist or linear or “context-free” narratives about science. I will confess that after reading all of these works, those “traditional narratives” feel like boogeymen or straw men—if every book is tearing them down, why must they be attacked so vociferously. Is it worth giving these “traditional texts” a try to see what all the fuss was about? 

That said, I did appreciate the sheer range of settings and contexts in which new kinds of knowledge was located throughout these works: Park placed it in women’s familial and devotional practices. Gomez places it in Caribbean ritual practitioners. Smith places it in the workshop of the artisan. Bleichmar (in a different periodization). There is also such a wonderfully vast range of knowledge-makers: Mohanes, artisans, nuns, philosophers, botanic illustrators. Each provides their own vantage to the “history of knowledge” Burke describes.

Another thought I had while reading is this. What’s more radical: taking the existing periodization and claiming it’s about something totally different (e.g. Merchant), inventing a new periodization entirely (Foucault), or trying to describe continuities that evade the traditional periodization (Daston & Park)?

# Week 2 — Subjectivities Essay

## **Week 2 — History of Science Generals Write-Up**

**Matthew Jordan**

This week’s readings were about what it means to be, or to become a person who does science. They all collectively emphasize that science is a kind of transformation of the self. To be a scientist is to become the kind of person who can see a certain way, do a particular kind of calculation, self-fashion to impress the court, or join a disciplinary community and fight for its political status. Even if we accept that scientists are not disembodied perfectly rational truth seekers—and who believes that? Did anyone really ever?[^2]—it still requires some work to explain exactly how scientists come to become the kinds of localized, embodied, disciplinary bound, socially embedded people they are. That’s what these books do.

*Objectivity* by Daston & Gallison is the conceptual anchor. It’s a history of many different conceptions of "objectivity" and the kinds of people scientists were required to be in order to exhibit them. The 18th century was about idealized depiction. Wise sages ignored inconsistencies, removed imperfections, and depicted nature at its purest. In the 1830s, a new standard emerged (coincident with, not overtaking, the previous): mechanical depiction. The ideal observer now needed to suppress the will, ignore the ego, and depict the world exactly as it actually is. A century later, trained judgement became the new norm: experts learned to glean insights from raw images or data. Each of these modes of objectivity required a new kind of self: the sage, the workers, the trained expert. How one knows is bound up with what kind of person one becomes.[^3]  
![][image5]![][image6]![][image7]

The next two books—which I will confess had me cheering in my seat as a physicist-turned-historian—are about the ways that ideas in physics get transmitted from discoverer to practitioner, from teacher to student. The first, David Kaiser’s *Drawing Theories Apart* is about the “dispersion” of Feynman diagrams in two senses: the way this tool of calculation spread throughout the physics world, and the way it scattered and changed along the way. As Kaiser says, Feynman diagrams were “adapted as they were adopted”. Kaiser’s key point is that theoretical physics is ultimately a craft practice that depends on tacit skill transmission. You cannot learn the “paper tools” of Feynman diagram calculation by simply reading papers any more than you can learn to build physical machines unapprenticed. The incredible achievemnt of the book is following the Feynman diagrams between people and scientific papers, and showing how their use followed predictable chains of in-person contact: Freeman Dyson set off a “postdoc cascade” during his years at the IAS, and people who had come into contact with Dyson, who spread to Cornell, or Berkeley, or Chicago, were more likely to use the diagrams in their own work. And when people outside of those networks did use the diagrams—for instance in Japan or the Soviet Union—they took on a different form that reflected the local context.

A very similar point is made in Warwick’s *Masters of Theory*, which instead of being about a new technique (Feynman Diagrams), is about the general mathematical toolkit that all physicists (or indeed many kinds of scientists) must master. His inquiry centers on Cambridge University’s Mathematical Tripos, considered at the time (and perhaps still today?) the most rigorous mathematical education in the world. Coming top of one’s class (“Senior Wrangler”) is essentially the highest academic achievement in the world. Warwick shows that the mathematical aptitudes held by these people are not acquired through isolated individual study, but rather through a rigorous communal program of training, with particular skills, methods, and techniques passed directly from teachers to students. We can even follow particular scientists’ work (like Poynting) and show how the skills they acquired from particular teachers at Cambridge enabled them to solve problems later in their career. I was particularly taken by Edward Routh, a clear expert pedagogue, who trained 80% of the top-three wranglers every year through an extremely arduous program of supervised study. I also loved the example of how Maxwell’s *Treatise* was taught and interpreted differently, and how it took many years to make it “teachable” (echoing Kaiser and Feynman diagrams).[^4]

I will observe that *Masters of Theory* and *Drawing Theories Apart* strike me as remarkably similar. They seem to be part of a kind of “turn to pedagogy” within the theoretical sciences—it feel that the field became very interested in tacit knowledge transmission. I was familiar with the work of Harry Collins, but that started way back in the ‘70s. I’m curious to fill in the gaps here between Collins and then these early 2000s books about physics. What was going on in between?

The next two books are less about the transmission of ideas, but more about the fashioning of individuals as scientific selves. Jan Golinski’s *The Experimental Self* asks: what does it mean to become a scientist when no such job exists? Humphrey Davy “self-fashioned” into a particular kind of scientific practitioner by assuming many overlapping personae: enthusiast, experimentalist, philosopher, traveller. The main image of Davy that comes across in the book is that of someone who is unafraid be his completely own kind of person,[^5] one who can move fluidly between social circles, and who clearly has not been “disciplined” or “professionalized”—a stark contrast to the pupils in *Masters of Theory*. In his younger days, Davy used himself and his friends as experimental subjects, inhaling nitrous oxide, sometimes mixed with alcohol, and wrote phenomenological accounts of the results. Davy’s personal identity was an inseparable part of his scientific work: when one is experimenting upon oneself, there is no boundary between objective witness and emotional participant. As he aged, however, his status as a dispassionate truth-seeking discoverer and philosopher hardened. He mobilized these identities to ensure his rival inventors did not get credit for the safety lamp because they were doing their work for financial credit.

A similar biography is Biagioli’s *Galileo, Courtier*, which similarly shows how Galileo moved between social spaces—the court, the university, the system of elite royal patronage—to self-fashion as a particular kind of thinker and scholar. Biagioli’s argument is that Galileo’s courtly role did not just fund, but directly shaped, his scientific output. For instance, Galileo’s naming of the moons of Jupiter after Cosimo II of Medici was not just an act of flattery, it was a recognition of the court’s iconography and the dynasty’s own self-mythology. There was a kind of exchange of legitimacy: Galileo established the relationship between the court and the heavens, and the court responded in kind by legitimating his role as “Philosopher” (a title he would not have been granted in a university setting). Biagioli emphasizes that the Copernican revolution was not merely about a shift to heliocentrism; it also required a reorganization of disciplinary hierarchies too.The court also informed Galileo’s writing style: polemical rather than measured, sarcastic rather than sincere. He was appealing to the spectacle of the court, a participant in an intellectual duel, fighting to be a “favorite” rather than making a disembodied appeal for the truth. But like Davy, Galileo’s career in the court was defined by his construction of the self: “Disputes—like competitive gift-exchanges—were processes of self-fashioning.”   
We move now from individuals to communities. How did the physics community establish itself as a unique professional collective? How did it separate itself from chemistry, despite both ostensibly studying the same kinds of questions about atoms? These are the questions that drive Nye’s *Before Big Science* and Kevles’ *The Physicists.* Kevles’ central claim is that the American physics community has been driven since its earliest days in the Civil War era by a desire for autonomy, resources, and recognition. The central (essential?) tension is that between scientific elitism—the physics community governing its own resource allocation—and democratic accountability. One of the major insights, perhaps counter-intuitive for the time, is that physicists are political maneuverers and institutional entrepreneurs; they act according to their community’s self-interest and try to secure resources with minimal oversight. Among Kevles’ key ideas are *best science elitism* and *political elitism*: the idea that funding should go to the best scientists, and that physicists (not democratically elected politicians, “philistines in and out of the government") should be responsible for the distribution of that funding. Throughout the book, we see physicists’ strong relationship to corporate, military, & defence spending.[^6] Defense has been very friendly to physicists: the military gets cutting-edge science at a reasonable price; the physicists get minimal accountability or oversight. To make this relationship happen, the physicists have needed to repeatedly persuade the funding apparatus that pure research serves the country’s best interest. This is essentially the argument that Vannevar Bush made in *Science, The Endless Frontier*, calling for a foundation governed by part-time scientists insulated from political control.[^7]

Mary Jo Nye’s *Before Big Science* is similarly about the physics community, but instead of highlighting its relationship to the federal government or military, it highlights its relationship to a highly related scientific field: chemistry. The core argument is that the period 1800-1940 saw these two fields developing distinct but intertwined identities, institutions, and methodologies, even though they emerged from the same traditions of natural philosophy. Two insights in particular from the book stood out to me. The first was Justus von Liebig’s chemical laboratory as a “knowledge factory”. This seemed like the invention of the Principal Investigator model: a hierarchical system where the head of the lab is mainly focused on research and advanced proteges, while the older students instructed junior ones. The other was the different ways different scientists conceptualized the “atom”. The atom could be defined by its atomic weight, by its momentum & energy, or by its source of particular radiation. Each kind of investigator—physical chemist, spectroscopist, meteorological theorist, physicist—had a different concept of the atom’s most salient characteristics. The chemical atom and the physical atom ultimately were not unified until Einstein and then later quantum theory. Frankly I understand very little of the science here and really feel the need to get into it more. This book felt much less “arugment-driven” to me, and more a kind of recounting of the way chemistry and physics got their own distinctive disciplinary identities, while also pointing out continuities between pre-WWI and post-WWII physics, to show that “big science” was not some massive unprecedented break with the past.

A major part of the formation of any community is the agreement on a shared language, and Michael Gordin’s *Scientific Babel* is about how English came to be the lingua franca of global science. English gradually rose to prominence because of a series of contingent and geopolitical-infused events: abandoning Latin, nationalist conflicts in Europe in WWI, disillusionment with Soviet Russia in the Cold War, and the failure of invented or machine-translated languages to enable communication across barriers. A crucial theoretical point here is that there is no “language of science” that transcends human language, and in fact science often changes as it is translated into different local contexts—a point made in this week’s reading most saliently by *Drawing Theories Apart*. In the work of Kaiser, Warwick, and Gordin, we see the direct person-to-person work required to translate science to different audiences, illustrated in this case by Lavoisier learning about Priestly’s work via intermediaries, or the priority dispute in Russian vs. German over the periodic table. I was also shocked to learn about the use of an IBM computer for machine translation two full years before the Dartmouth Conference that is typically said to have inaugurated the field of AI. This is of course also not the last time a massive computer demonstration has been more theater than true test of scientific achievement.

The final book here felt totally disconnected from the rest conceptually, but it might very well have been my favorite. Alice Lovejoy’s *Tales of Militant Chemistry* is about treating film as something less like film and more like weapons & explosives. Film production is a mass chemical manufacturing process, much like plastics, pesticides, painkillers, and poisonous gasses. Tennessee Eastman (Kodak’s chemical engineering division) developed techniques for making "safe" cellulose acetate film, which led directly to poison gas, explosives, and the atomic bomb. And the most important thing to happen to chemical engineering companies like Eastman in the 20th century was the Manhattan Project, which was—quite underratedly, I’d say, relative to the mass popular focus on the physics happening at Los Alamos—a concerted effort by all of the nation's industrial capacity to refine uranium. The US Army Corps of Engineers recruited the nation's top manufacturing companies (Union Carbide, Standard Oil, DuPont, General Electric, Monsanto, Raytheon) to this mass construction effort, and this included many of the workers of Tennessee Eastman. Many of the women in particular worked on the Calutron at the Y-12 production facility at Oak Ridge. All the while, Agfa Wolfen's wartime film production depended on concentration camp prisoners. The same factories producing film for Nazi propaganda were sites of enslaved labor.   
![][image8]

Some final concluding thoughts and questions. Clear themes emerged across these readings: scientific selves (Davy, Galileo), the central role of person-to-person contact in the transmission of new theories (Maxwell, Feynman, Priestly, etc.), the unexpected role of meteorology as a generator of results in physics (comes up in both *Objectivity* and *Before Big Science*), the deep social embeddedness of chemistry and physics in matters of war (comes up in *The Physicists*, *Tales of Militant Chemistry*, and *Before Big Science*). I loved the idea that you could tell where someone was trained based on how they solved Feynman diagrams or taught Maxwell’s equations. I also loved the emphasis on entrepreneurship (not necessarily using that word), both of the self (to use Foucault terminology), but also of major institutions (NSF, NRC, Liebig's laboratory). Scientists are often “founders”, and I think this is one way I can bridge my interest in the history of technology & “startups” with the history of science as such.

But as with last week, I cannot help but wonder: what exactly are the narratives these books are pushing against? Who is it, exactly, who believes that scientists are dispassionate disembodied transmitters of information? In what world are theories simply handed down on high, in perfect form, from sage physicist to accepting community to learned student? No scientist believes this is the way they work, and no one looking at a scientist at work would ever claim this is how things are done. So how, then, did such “idealized” images of the concept of a science come to become so widespread? It’s clear that a great deal of branding work by scientists and the people who write about them has gone into displaying a particular public image of the scientist as a particular kind of person; I am very interested indeed in works that explore this aspect of things (the book on Davy certainly touches on this).

# Week 3 — Practices

## **Week 3 — History of Science Generals Write-Up**

**Matthew Jordan**

This week contained some readings that are among my favorite things I’ve ever read in the history of science, some things I found totally impenetrable, and some things that were total non-sequiturs. I enjoyed it all thoroughly. Whereas last week was about the production of scientists as people—their training, their disciplinary formation, their relationship to the state and their teachers and each other—this week was more about the production of scientific knowledge. What do scientists actually do all day? What tools are they using? How do they organize themselves in their labs and journals and particle accelerator experiments? How do experimental results get interpreted, written about, and eventually agreed upon by an all-too-human community of investigators? These are the general questions that came up in this week’s readings.  
	I’ll start with the book I spent the most time with: Peter Galison’s *Image & Logic*. It is surely clichéd as a former physics student now training as a historian to say that I’m a big Peter Galison fan, but sometimes the greats are the greats for a reason. This book is a behemoth, but its overarching theoretical framework is fairly straightforward. Contra Kuhn, whose notion of “paradigms” and “scientific revolutions” takes scientific knowledge to be a unified set of theories building up brick-by-brick, Galison proposes that the scientific community, specifically in his case physics, is in fact made up of many distinct traditions—theorists, experimentalists, mathematicians, machine-makers—and each of them operates at its own distinctive pace. Theory can change while experiment remains stagnant; machines can be invented with no change to theory for a couple decades. Machines are not “reified theorems”; if anything, they are haphazard tools that are often unrelated to the frontier of physics, drawing inspiration from anything from meteorology to gambling, but which can produce outsized impact on what even counts as knowledge in physics. The book also argues against the idea floated in *Leviathan & The Air Pump* that, through Boyle and Hobbes, some question about the nature of the experimental method was resolved for the next 300 years. Rather, says Galison, the nature of the experimental method is a perpetual negotiation between the strict inner laboratory (the actual site of the experiment), the wider outer laboratory, the nature of new machines, and the negotiation of evidence from images, measurements, simulations, and other kinds of measurements.[^8]   
The book also has a thesis about how, when physicists across different traditions—image and logic, theory versus experiment, computational versus mathematical—come together, they generate new intermediary languages, creoles, to be able to communicate across cultural lines. I found this argument a bit weaker, and I found it quite interesting that the blurbs on the back of the book all seemed to think that this notion of “trading zones” was going to be the central take-away from this book, a linguistic contribution at the level of “paradigm”, and as far as I am aware, that has not been the case.  
Two additional miscellaneous observations about *Image & Logic*. First, I was delighted to see the return of Kodak as the provider of the film that was used for nuclear emulsion experiments. Second, I was particularly taken by the chapter on Monte Carlo methods and simulations as a totally new genre of inquiry (*tertium quid*) that could not be easily slotted into theory or experiment.
