The idea of randomness itself had to be re-invented for the computer: simulated random numbers producing a simulation of reality, which in turn gets aggregated to form a prediction about the behavior of neutrons.   
It’s hard to follow up a tome like *Image & Logic*, but frankly I was equally taken by the conceptual apparatus deployed in *Inventing Temperature*. Chang’s primary philosophical intervention is to do away with any notion of science as a progressive enterprise that builds up from a solid foundational or axiomatic base. Science is full of circular logics: how can you verify the temperature of mercury when your measurement device uses mercury itself to take temperatures? How can you calibrate any measurement device without an “objective” measurement for the very quantity you’re trying to capture? We cannot build our way into some solid ground truth. What we can do, however, is epistemic iteration: gradually refine our standards, make new attempts, and create mutually reinforcing knowledge claims without ever being truly certain that our starting point was correct. Even an idea as simple as “the boiling point of water being 100ºC” is one that involves centuries of interpretive flexibility, uncertainty, consensus-building, and tinkering with devices. “Boiling”, “water”, and “100ºC” are all contested categories: impurities, supersaturation, the quantity of bubbles or steam, and the measurement of temperature all produce uncertainties. I was also quite moved by Chang’s “principle of respect”: the idea that the at-present consensus on a particular scientific topic “embodies considerable achievement that may be very difficult to match if one starts from another basis”, and so one ought to use it as a starting point, even if one has many issues with the current formulation. One unexpected connection here is the central importance of meteorology: John Aitken is an important character in *Inventing Temperature*, just as he is in *Image & Logic*. Meteorology played an outsized and, to my eye, highly underrated role in the history of science.  
Moving back in (historiographic) time and to a rather different kind of science, we have *Laboratory Life*, which much like Image & Logic is clearly a foundational text for a reason. It really does seem that, amid debates in Edinburgh over SSK and the strong programme, Latour showed up basically out of nowhere, ready to follow his actors around and inaugurate an entire adjacent (competing?) school in the anthropology of science. *Laboratory Life* has a premise that rhymes with a quote I drew out from Shapin’s *Scientific Revolution*: there is as much “society” inside the lab as outside it. It also rhymes quite a bit with Chang’s epistemic iteration and coherentism: in Latour & Woolgar’s laboratory, facts achieve their solid factful-ness through the accumulation of investments that make alternatives costly to raise or even propose. This book also sets the stage for Latour’s idea of a “parliament of things” (though not by that term), and the idea, later fleshed out in actor network theory, that objects, theories, instruments, and writing all form a seamless web of “actants” (again, a terminology not present here) that cannot be separated into cognitive and social realms. Latour & Woolgar also presage Klein’s later idea of “paper tools” by talking about “inscription devices” as the means of turning material substances into written documents that can then be used in laboratory arguments. An additional key idea is splitting and inversion: the process where statements become separated from their process of production, and appear as pre-existing objects “out there”.[^9] One crucial point that becomes apparent in *Laboratory Life* is that particular methods & techniques don’t hold weight outside of the particular contexts they are wielded in and the particular individuals doing the wielding. Statistical techniques used in one context to prove a claim can be used to disprove it in a near-identical situation. There is also a chapter dedicated to the motivations of one particular scientist (Dietrich), whose decisions are motivated by the accumulation not of material rewards, but of credibility in his field: credentials, access to data, and access to information that might prove pertinent to his professional movings.[^10]  
This fits right into *Lords of the Fly*, which treats these kinds of credibility-acquiring processes as a core part of the “moral economy” of scientific life. Kohler deploys moral economy to refer to the movement of tools, information, and relationships that constitute the delicate balance of (lowercase) laboratory life. In this particular case, the laboratory is Thomas Hunt Morgan’s fly room at Columbia University, where fruit flies were turned from an object of study to a standard laboratory instrument of their own, a mass-produced technology for understanding genetics and heritability. In this sense, *Lords of the Fly* is, much like *Image & Logic*, a story about the role of machines in the history of science, although in this case, the “machine” is a very particular kind of actant, the fruit fly *drosophila*, placed in conditions to reproduce in a “breeder reactor” virtuous cycle.[^11] This work also draws on the great Thomas Hughes, arguing that the production of *drosophila* is a kind of large socio-technical system, albeit one very different in nature than electrification. Especially interesting in the book are the chapters dedicated to the fly room. We essentially get the full org chart: Morgan as the boss, students (“boys”) competing for funding, women often systematically excluded, trade networks of drosophila to other labs on the condition that they not scoop publications. A well-regulated exchange network of information, tools, and practices.  
Moving now to another book about the exchange of constructed tools across laboratories to create a scientific exchange network: Angela Creager’s *Life Atomic* moves away from the highly theoretical and towards the highly material role of radioisotopes in postwar biology and medicine. Much like Kohler’s flies, Creager’s isotopes are a crucial technology that forms the basis of postwar exchange networks, and in a kind of meta-scientific move, the isotopes themselves can be traced through those networks, followed as they cross the civilian-military border. The very same isotopes, for instance, that were used for radiological warfare were also used for cancer research and ecological studies, making them a classic case of a dual-use technology. *Life Atomic* overlaps considerably with *Image & Logic*, but shows how many of the tools described in Galison’s laboratories ended up in totally unrelated contexts. At the RadLab in Berkeley for instance, biochemists used nuclear carbon-14 from Oak Ridge to uncover the exact chemical process of photosynthesis in a way that had previously been impossible with other carbon isotopes. Bacteriophages were investigated with phosphorus-32 from Oak Ridge; iodine-131 was used to develop new tools for diabetes in Veterans Administration hospitals; isotopes were followed around rivers and forest ecosystems, in all cases blurring the boundary between military-funded and civilian science.  
Jumping now back to the highly theoretical, we have Rheinberger’s *Towards a History of Epistemic Things*. Once again using a case study from biology—like Kohler, Latour, and Creager—this time protein synthesis at Mass General, Rheinberger argues that the fundamental “unit” of scientific research is the “experimental system”: the hybrid arrangement of epistemic things (ideas, theories) and technical things (lab equipment, machines). Science does not make “discoveries” but rather generates “unprecedented events” that cannot be anticipated by the researchers themselves. This work contains traces of many I’ve touched on already: the hybrid merging of epistemic and technical things evokes Latour & Woolgar[^12], but the separate treatment of experiment and theory in the same space evokes Galison, while the “system” itself as an object to investigate calls to Kohler’s “moral economy”. We once again, and this is always music to my ear, see the laboratory system as a kind of large scale socio-technical system: the experimental system is a “machine for constructing the future”. One thing this book makes salient is that scientists often make their core innovations “accidentally”, and then they or other scientists later re-conceptualize their work in light of future knowledge. Zamecnik and Watson’s meeting, for instance, was basically unfruitful, but Zamecnik later reached his hand back into history to frame the encounter as being about “messages” and “code”, which was not how people talked about DNA at the time. The past was made legible retroactively by using a set of words and concepts that were only available *after* the key discoveries had been made.  
The final three works, which I will only have time to gloss over very quickly, take many of the ideas from these previous works and move them to very different geographies: the contemporary government database records center in Islamabad, the medical centers of the mid-century Gulag in Russia, the rubber plantation in Liberia. Each has their own angle on how scientific knowledge is constructed. In *The Gulag Doctors*, we see two fundamentally opposing aims: the penal authority of camp commanders whose job it is to extract labour, and the medical knowledge of doctors and nurses trying to heal patients. Healthcare professionals held dual loyalties: to medicine (as a practice and knowledge-generating discipline) and to the Gulag system itself. Like many books that takes us inside of horrendous or oppressive conditions, part of its job is to humanize and give agency to the people involved, and to show that prisoners never passively accepted the conditions of their subjugation, such as in the case of psychiatrist Lev Sokolovsky, who studied psychosis in the camps. The chapter on the morgue also evoked *Secrets of Women*, perhaps in a perverse funhouse mirror sense: the autopsy as a contested site, for the acquisition of real medical expertise, but also the outcome of a brutal system.  
Continuing the theme of “Brutal system”, we have Mitman’s *Empire of Rubber*, a history of the American company Firestone Rubber’s operations in Liberia. Firestone ran what was essentially a corporate slave trade, establishing a plantation, local labour networks that amounted to human trafficking, and extracting profits from the sale of rubber that looked very much like the Atlantic slave trade. Globalization brought this extractive system of working conditions from the antebellum south to Liberia. I was most interested in the case studies of Black thought leaders, industrialists, and pro-globalization advocates like W.E.B. DuBois or Lester Walton, who were caught between the desire for American capitalism to help spur the kind of dynamic market activity that can generate massive wealth for many, and the need to defend Liberia’s sovereignty as a Black republic.  
Finally, Hasmi’s *Making Reliable Persons*, which is about the complexities of identity claims in Pakistan. The national biometric database, which is framed as a kind of techno-optimist’s rational dream, in fact reinforces the country’s traditional logic that prizes genealogy, rather than biometric data, as the authoritative source of one’s identity claim. Pakistanis interfacing with NADRA needed to be able to establish clear evidence of their ancestors, which would then be part of computerized “kin calculations”, to be able to file citizenship claims.[^13]

A final conclusion to wrap things up. I appreciate the overarching emphasis, across basically all of these books, on the *material* aspects of the construction of laboratory knowledge. The fruit fly, the bubble chamber, the radioisotope, the thermometer. These are physical tools that are constructed in order to make arguments and generate knowledge. The “social construction of knowledge”, it seems to me, is far less of a boogeyman (as it was in the Science Wars) when it’s clear that a huge part of the “construction” at hand is the highly contingent, highly politicized, very clearly non-algorithmic, unsystematic, seat-of-the-pants building of tools. No wonder then that the theories generated by those tools have those very same constructed properties\!

# Week 4 — Social Construction

## **Week 4 — History of Science Generals Write-Up**

**Matthew Jordan**

We begin this week’s “Thomas Kuhn is over party” where we left off last time, with Beller’s truly brilliant, underrated, and frankly inspired *Quantum Dialogue*, published just after *Image & Logic* and arguing, as have Galison and many other authors we’ve encountered, that Kuhn’s model of paradigms is not only incomplete but also circularly self-reinforcing in a way that calls it into high suspicion. This book is an intellectual history of the quantum revolution, showing in painstaking detail, through physicists’ published papers and private letters how they developed their ideas in dialogue with each other. If other historians have taken the lab, the scientific society, the court, the machine, or the classroom as the primary object of their investigation, Beller’s focus is text itself. Which physicists were corresponding with whom? What were they reading, and how did those texts creep into their own writings? How did they use rhetoric to make open disputes seem settled or to re-write their own messy thought process into inevitabilities?   
	Two things in particular stood out to me. The first is the analysis of Kuhn’s *Structure* itself. Beller’s contention—which as far as I can tell seems rock-solid—is that *Structure* is full of ideas about physics that are borrowed, whether on purpose or perhaps by accident, from the writings of the group of scientists who espoused the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum mechanics (e.g. Bohr, Heisenberg). Kuhn cites philosophers of science whose ideas about Gestalt perception and theory-ladenness are borrowed from quantum mechanics, and borrows Heisenberg’s idea of “closed theories”, and, most incriminatingly, Kuhn uses the quantum revolution as an *example* of a type of scientific revolution that validates his thesis, when in fact the quantum revolution is the *framework* that the thesis was built on in the first place.[^14] Beller’s answer to Kuhn’s paradigms is the idea of “dialogism”: the idea that creative disagreement is what pushes science forward. On this account Bohr, Heisenberg, Born, and Schrödinger never actually agreed on the basic philosophical principles of quantum mechanics, but their disagreement in the form of dialogue was itself the generative force that gave rise to innovative ideas. The idea of consensus, however, was manufactured after the fact to make it seem like there was an inevitable outcome that all of this work was pointing towards.  
Why had I never heard of this book? Is it not well known? Has it been lost? And is this simply what “intellectual history” is? Why does it feel different? I can’t quite place what feels so unique about her approach, but it strikes me as uniquely lucid and totally singular.[^15]  
The next few books were ones I (we?) have read before, whether in last year’s class or elsewhere. The most classic of course remains *Leviathan and the Air Pump*, which is, like many of these works, a classic for a reason. Steven Shapin once again reigns supreme in the one-liners: “solutions to the problem of knowledge are solutions to the problem of social order”. (And of course the mic-dropping “Hobbes was right”.) A few things struck me on this re-reading. The first is the extent to which theories about anything are in fact theories about everything. It’s not that all observation is theory-laden, it’s that all theories are everything—politics, culture, epistemology, religion, governance—laden. The second, very related, is the extent to which this book is framed as a kind of solution to the internalism-externalism debate. There is no separation between the “internal” ideas of the lab and the “external” ideas of politics; to settle what counts as knowledge is to settle forms of social organization. I also really admired the extent to which Shapin and Shaffer really earnestly do pursue the Strong Programme to its fullest extent: they never allow us to believe that Boyle is “right”. Of course, I agree with Galison’s critique that this Boyle-Hobbes controversy did not permanently settle the question of experimental scientific knowledge, but I’m sure the authors of this book do as well. It doesn’t seem they are arguing that this is the “definitive” resolution to the matter of what counts as experimental knowledge (but I’m also probably reading with too broad a brush).  
Per my comment last week about how everything eventually comes down to the history of technology, we yet again in *Leviathan and the Air Pump* have a case of technology as a central actor. S\&S point to “three technologies of fact-making”: material, literary, and social. The primary “technology” is the air-pump itself, which the book portrays as an error-prone, leaky machine, but which was an essential tool for establishing credibility. Boyle’s “literary technology” was his prose stylings, his hedging, his lengthy descriptions, his vivid conjuring of scenes in the reader’s eye, enabling them to engage in “virtual witnessing” of the events in the lab. The “social technology” is about who was permitted in the lab, whose opinions on Boyle’s experiment counted, whose witnessing was credible. Collectively this is not just a set of claims about the nature of the vacuum, it’s a “form of life”.[^16] Hobbes’ critique, then, isn’t strictly about the vacuum pump in itself, but about the entire system of social organization that Boyle is espousing here. As I was reading Hobbes’ critique, I was reminded of the “logic” people’s critique of the “image” people: “anything can happen once”.  
Moving onto Harry Collins, whose papers I’ve read and who comes up in every single one of these books as practically the metonym for “tacit knowledge”. And reading him now at length: I get it. Collins’ argument is extremely sharp and incisive. The problem is as follows: the gold standard of scientific proof is replication. But how does one know that one is properly replicating another person’s experiment? The competence of the experimenter or the functioning of the apparatus can only be determined by obtaining the correct result, but the “correct result” is precisely what is in contention.[^17] (We’ve discussed this circularity when talking about Chang as well.) So, replication cannot in fact resolve experimental disputes. What actually resolves disputes is social processes: negotiation, alliances, rhetoric, institutional pressures. These are then washed away in the final analysis and the experiment is narrativized as having taken place according to strict scientific methods.[^18] Collins’ proposal is the Empirical Programme of Relativism, which takes as its central focus the opening of interpretive flexibility and the closure of controversies, contra Popper’s “falsification” or Lakatos’ “research programs”, and adding color to Kuhn’s “revolutions”. Collins’ idea of the “core set” strikes me as quite compatible with Beller’s idea of dialogue as the core generator of productive disagreement, though I would be curious to understand more about places if or when these two approaches disagree. Something else I am interested in with Collins: he seems to have attached himself to the gravitational wave people pretty early in his career, and in their existence as a small scientific community. Does this still happen? Do people simply decide to start following emergent scientific communities and write about them, regardless of the potential outcome of their work? Does Collins just happen to have gotten lucky that gravitational waves were eventually detected? Did he make a very sound gamble?  
Collins draws a lot on Andrew Pickering, whose *Constructing Quarks* we turn to next. Pickering, like all the authors we’ve considered thus far, is not particularly enchanted by the idea that objective reality determines the content of physics theories (“In this book, the view will be that agency belongs to actors not phenomena: scientists make their own history, they are not the passive mouthpieces of nature”.) Rather, it is “opportunism”: the right mix of social milieu, available information, and rhetorical finesse that enables certain ideas to gain purchase within the physics community at certain times. I get the sense that this is a fairly landmark book—it took ideas that were only really applied to small case studies (e.g. a single lab in Laboratory Life, a single instrument in Collins’ earlier TEA Set papers) and applied them to an entire field of high-energy physics. The actual physics here is quite complicated, and frankly I found it difficult to follow even as a person with the requisite training. But in all of the case studies, the point becomes clear: there is a constant negotiation at play between theory, practice, and social relations. Experimenters might change their approach in response to some theory not because the theory is “true” per se, but because the theoreticians put various social pressures on the experimenters to adapt to this new way. In so doing, experimentalists might discover something new, something unexpected, using methods that are hard to judge as “right” or “wrong”. Much like Chang’s “coherentism”, or his “epistemic iteration”, these theories become validated not by sitting atop some axiomatic foundation, but because of their interweaving within a web of other knowledge claims. It also strikes me that this book is a rare one that chronicles the physics of the 1960s-80s, enabling Picklering to deploy first-hand accounts to re-construct the events as they happened. But this is frankly rare in the historiography: histories of physics tend to fade out after the 1960s, as the field becomes diffuse and no longer the “center of gravity”. Which makes me wonder, then: who is writing the histories of, say, string theory, an unbelievably socially complex field that many now consider, after 40 years, to be a bubble that has finally burst? I would also like to think through a bit more what the theoretical differences are between this relativist-constructivist account and Galison’s framework in *Image & Logic*. To me these two texts are not incompatible per se, but I suspect a more careful reading would find differences.  
We conclude these highly theoretical sociologist men texts with Latour’s classic *We Have Never Been Modern*, which is also a re-visit. The basic idea is that we have ostensibly separated Nature (science, nonhuman) and Society (human subjects, politicians), but in reality every scientific fact, technological artifact, and political institution is a Nature-Society hybrid, a quasi-object that blends the scientific and the political, the human and the non-human. Thus, try as we might to separate these two realms, we never have and never will—ergo, We Have Never Been Modern.   
Latour is explicitly critical of the Strong Programme because it doesn’t go far enough: naive views of science try to explain nature in terms of nature; SSK tries to explain nature in terms of society, but it leaves “society” itself unexamined (“Hobbes was right”). His solution is actor-network theory, and quasi-objects,....and actants…….and the “modern Constitution”...and a bunch else besides.[^19] We Have Never Been Modern is, I somehow forgot, largely *about* Leviathan and the Air-Pump, and how Boyle & Hobbes originated the “Modern Constitution”, which excludes society from nature and vice-versa. But this modern constitution simply leads to a proliferation of *more* hybrids, and the more we deny their existence, the more they spread: “The less the moderns think they are blended, the more they blend.” We’ve reached a tipping point where there are simply too many quasi-objects for Latour’s liking: frozen embryos, ozone holes, gene synthesizers, sensor-equipped robots, psychotropic drugs. The solution is a “Parliament of Things”. We need to stop symmetrizing Nature and Society and simply put *everything* into the mix. Scientists, politicians, citizens, nonhumans, actants of all kinds, together in a vast negotiation, with no dichotomies as we’ve traditionally conceived of them.  
We can now move to two texts that pair very productively: Haraway’s *Primate Visions*, and Milam’s *Creatures of Cain*. *Primate Visions* was another re-visit for me, and this book really is quite brilliant. It feels totally fresh and inimitable. On this reading, I was particularly attuned to the way Haraway is focused on the theatrics of science.[^20] For Haraway, the sciences of primates are at once an empirical investigation and a kind of spectacle, the formation and perpetuation of mythic narratives. If we consider primatology as contested both as scientific field and speculative fiction, we can see how science has shaped the stories we tell ourselves about gender, sex, race, class, and colonial power. This is the first text to explicitly focus on the *storytelling* nature of scientific work. Beller is attuned to discourse, but she does not frame it quite as the creation of stories. Haraway’s most masterful stroke is in holding primalogical texts, videos, films, and field recordings as scientific documents, cultural artifacts, political statements, and mythic stories at the same time (similarly evoking S\&S’s “three technologies”). Haraway is also mobilizing every imaginable theoretical toolkit in the book: feminist theory, semiotics, post-structuralism, Marxist analysis, SSK, social constructivism, actor-network theory, postcolonial criticism. There’s Edward Said and there’s Bruno Latour and there’s Sandra Harding all rolled into one.  
The thesis of *Primate Visions* is irrefutable through its sheer breadth of examples. We are transported from gorilla dioramas recapitulating stereotypical gender roles in the American Museum of Natural History to experiments between monkeys and “wire mothers” at the University of Wisconsin that become the basis for Cold War Reproductive politics; from the surreal spectacle of Ham the chimpanzee in space to the work of women sociobiologists and anthropologists who recapitulate gendered stereotypes and “the logic of late capitalism” through their ostensibly progressive scientific work; from the National Geographic-mediated spectacle around Jane Goodall, whose distinctively feminine mode of inquiry legitimated her work and helped portray Gombe as a kind of Edenic, primitive, “back to nature” space, to the tragic story of Dian Fossey, whose murder symbolizes a “White woman \[sacrificing\] herself in Edenic wilderness to defend endangered innocence”. Throughout the book, apes are understood as part of global commodity flows (e.g. between Tanzania and Yale), as stand-ins for ideal visions of masculinity or femininity, as laboratory objects meant to inform the measurement of human intelligence. *Primate Visions* is totally singular. I cannot really think of another history book that so deftly makes the strange familiar and the familiar strange. That’s probably because *Primate Visions* is less about “doing history” in the conventional sense, and more about marshalling hundreds of pages of examples from primatology, human-ape interactions, and the mediating role of the camera, and dissecting each one, bit-by-bit, until one is left with a deeply eerie sense about the whole enterprise.  
Finally, staying on the ape theme, we conclude with *Creatures of Cain*. Much like *Primate Visions*, it uses the study of apes as a lens through which to understand broader claims about humans. In this case, the central question is: how did scientists’ conception of human nature change in the Cold War era? Are we fundamentally cooperative or destructive? Naturally friendly or naturally violent? The study of nonhuman animals proved a fertile ground on which to adjudicate these fundamentally human questions. If we can find evidence, for instance, that apes are fundamentally bellicose (as in the “killer ape theory”, first proposed in the 1950s), then we can conclude that we, too, are wired for violence.   
If there is any connection between this book and the rest of the ones on this week’s list, it’s that the question of “human nature” is both a scientific one—the domain of evolutionary biology, psychology, anthropology, and genetics—and a political one, and these two motivations have always been interwoven. After the war, scientists and educators and politicians alike sought a new kind of story about human nature that would emphasize our fundamental shared humanity. Scientists supplied this story, a “non-teleological and progressive vision” of humanity, emphasizing our shared evolutionary lineage and the importance of cultural evolution over biological evolution—a rejection of earlier eugenic thinking and an affirmation of antiracist ideals. The “Man: A Course of Study” curriculum reflected these priorities, teaching children to appreciate cultural diversity and the unity of humankind. By the 1970s, however, MACOS had become a casualty of a culture war, attacked from both the left (biological explanations \= bad), and right (cultural relativism \= bad). The immediate postwar harmony had turned stale by the 1960s. A new vision of human nature came to meet the moment. The killer ape theory was proposed by Raymond Dart, an Australian anatomist working in South Africa who testified in apartheid racial reclassification trials, and who argued that Australopithecines were weapon-wielding carnivores. His ideas were popularized by the playwright, screenwriter, and science writer Robert Ardrey: “Far from the truth lay the antique assumption that man had fathered the weapon. The weapon, instead, had fathered man”. Ardrey’s books proved enormously influential and inspired, among other things, Harry Frank Guggenheim to invest heavily in emerging researchers studying male aggression and intrasexual competition as one of the founding principles of human social organization.[^21] This set the stage for the later emergence of “sociobiology”.[^22]  
Milam also introduces an aspect of science that all of the works we’ve considered in this generals field thus far ignore: namely, the communication of scientific ideas to non-specialist audiences. “Colloquial science” is her term for the sophisticated mode of scientific writing that was consumed by lay readers, aspiring scientists, and researchers across disciplines (in contrast to “popular science”, which has some connotations of “dumbing down” for general audiences). Works of colloquial science, like the nonfiction books of Robert Ardrey (or EO Wilson, or Stephen Jay Gould, or Richard Lewontin) were not mere popularizations of already-accepted ideas; they were in fact the battleground on which new ideas were contested, and were read by graduate students in seminars as much as by non-expert readers. These ideas also made their way into film, where they were amplified into mass cultural events—consider for instance the iconic opening scene of *2001: A Space Odyssey*, which was staged with direct consultation from paleoanthropologists.[^23]

# Week 6 — Biology

## **Week 6 — History of Science Generals Write-Up**

**Matthew Jordan**

Let me begin by drawing out some high-level themes that recurred through many of these books. I felt that most of these books were, in their own way, in dialogue with both *Objectivity* and *Primate Visions*. *Objectivity* is a clear through-line: from Porter’s analysis of quantification as a defensive use of mechanical objectivity, to Secord’s exploration of shifting epistemic virtues, to most obviously Hopwood’s analysis of Haeckel’s images—themselves a key case study in Daston & Galison’s own book—these works are about how numbers, images, and museum collection and depiction practices shape scientific knowledge. *Primate Visions* is also everywhere in these texts; many of them feel like “doing *Primate Visions* for X”, where X can be sheep (Franklin), tortoises (Hennessey), or body transformation procedures (Serlin).[^24]  
Another theme, which I was probably primed to see based on our conversation last week and our discussion around *Creatures of Cain*, was the role of information exchange between expert and non-expert readers through boundary-crossing publications—colloquial science. It pops up everywhere in these texts. Most obvious is Secord’s *Victorian Sensation*, which is about the reception of one particular scientific text among a wide range of audience. But it seems that all of these texts in their own way involve “popular media” in some way or another. Dolly the Sheep was a mass sensation; body enhancement and transformation practices are both biological and highly social; the Galápagos had to be constructed as a kind of “Eden” in the public imagination and turned into a tourist spot; Haeckel’s images were engaged with by biologists, students, and the lay public alike, and became the subject of much debate by canonical colloquial science writers like Stephen Jay Gould.  
I cannot help but wonder if there’s something about biology that makes it uniquely connected to the popular imagination. I’m of two minds about this. Physics, too, is connected to the popular imagination. One could imagine many of our texts engaging with observatories, planetariums, Carl Sagan, NOVA, Neil deGrasse Tyson, and much else besides. There is clearly a “popular culture” of physics. But—and maybe this is a selection bias on the part of our list—it seems that the texts we’ve focused on are squarely centered in the laboratory or the classroom. The entire SSK project, or the work of Latour, is about exposing that there is just as much politics inside the lab as outside of it. This seems to be taken as a much more of a given when it comes to evolutionary theory, or embryology, or cloning, and these texts therefore feel at liberty to analyze these scientific ideas in the context not only of “politics” in the sense of “negotiations of power relations”, but in a much richer way, taking into account popular media, television, reception history of texts, industrial manufacturing, and more.   
That said, maybe there is just something about biology as a field that makes it more “accessible” to public scrutiny. Readers are interested in gender-affirming surgeries and images of embryos and exotic animals, in a way that bubble chambers and Feynman diagrams and thermometers cannot match. Or maybe these issues have been “socialized” as interesting, which is itself part of the tension these texts explore. I look forward to talking about this more.  
Perhaps the only text that doesn’t quite fit into the lineages I’m drawing out is *Making PCR*, which is less driven by case-studies and is more a conventional narrative history, with a narrow well-defined set of characters and time period. If anything, it most resembles *Image & Logic*. What is unique about it is that it’s about frontier science in an almost entirely corporate environment. In this sense, it most resembles *Tales of Militant Chemistry*, which as far as I can recall is the only other book we’ve read that mostly takes place in a corporation. This has led me to reflect on the many different “scientific sites” we have explored. The academic laboratory and classroom are naturally at the fore. Last week we spoke a great deal about postwar think tanks, while governments, courts, and military institutions have been omnipresent. But alas companies are rarely foregrounded. Even *Tales of Militant Chemistry* is not about the corporation and its internal characters; it’s more about situating Kodak within a broader military context. I also look forward to talk about this\!

Let me know go book-by-book as usual and summarize the key insights I took from each of them. I’ll start with *Trust in Numbers*, which remains a landmark work. We once again see “technology” being deployed to describe a non-machine innovation: quantification as a “technology of distance”. Porter’s contention is that numbers are useful precisely when trust is low. Numbers are fundamentally *defensive*: by counting and measuring, by tracking efficiency, by running cost-benefit analyses and adhering to strict standards, institutions guard themselves from external attacks and give themselves a kind of fabricated legitimacy they wouldn’t have needed if they were not under threat. Porter illustrates this point by comparing the US Army Corps of Engineers to the French Corps des Ponts et Chaussées. The latter were embedded in a ruling elite, made up of graduates of the same prestigious universities, within a culture of high personal trust. As a result, they never developed rigorous cost-benefit standards, unlike the Americans, who faced pressure from electric utility companies, railroads, and rival federal agencies like the Bureau of Reclamation and the Soil Conservation Service. When the Corps faced bureaucratic competitors who could produce their own numbers, they had no choice but to turn to numbers too. Porter’s punchline: “The regime of calculation was imposed not by all-powerful experts, but by relatively weak and divided ones.”   
Embedded in this narrative is an augment about the American state as a whole: America is a deeply fragmented country, made up of many different kinds of elites, spread out over great distance. It is no wonder, then, that American accountants developed sophisticated techniques of standardization that came to prioritize “following rules” over “telling truth”. The goal of numbers in actuarial work was not epistemic virtue; it was setting enforceable norms in a fractured nation that did not have the embedded trust of small elite communities. Most interesting to me in the book was Porter’s discussion of statistics, which he observes moved “up” the hierarchy of science, from “weaker disciplines” like sociology, psychology, and medicine, up to “stronger” disciplines like physics. Psychology’s low public authority is the *reason* for its insistence on statistical significance testing; same with medicine and the double-blind control trial. These methods allowed them to claim the kind of scientific objectivity that physicists get to take for granted.  
Sarah Franklin’s *Dolly Mixtures*—what I think of as *Primate Visions* for sheep—is a very big swing. The goal is to argue that the history of sheep breeding recapitulates (wink to Haeckel) the history of colonialism, and that the cloning of Dolly the sheet can only be understood by situating it with histories of pastoral capitalism. For Franklin, “is cloning moral?” is the wrong question; the correct approach is to show that all sorts of morally fraught genealogies—reproduction, capital, national pride, and imperial power—are all wrought up in the body of the sheep. Dolly is ultimately a “biocultural entity” that resists any kind of straightforward classification or analysis.   
The number of kinds of history she pulls on are multifaceted. They range from linguistic/etymological analysis to studies of British sheep breeding to sociological analyses of the foot-in-mouth epistemic to the long history of Australian colonial pastoral economy. She is highly attuned to language: one chapter deals with the etymology of the word “stock” and all its meanings: trunk/stem (biology), fund/capital (economic value), tool/instrument (utility), and lineage/breed (genealogy). “Livestock” is therefore an apt term for animal bodies as instruments of capital accumulation. The best chapter chronicles the entire system of British sheep breeding in its great complexity: a highly sophisticated system of cross-breeding, creating sheep fit for different climatic zones or market sale via crosses. This system is dependent on the constant movement of animals and management of species, which mirrors exactly the stem cell economy. Both depend on cultivating biological diversity, maintaining "lines" of descent, banking reproductive material, and strategically controlling biological reproduction to maximize output.[^25]  
Next is David Serlin’s *Replaceable You*, which, like *Dolly Mixtures*, is more an assemblage of disjoint case studies meant to prove an overall point than a coherent historical narrative. The core argument is that postwar practices of body modification—prosthetics, gender-affirming surgery, plastic surgery, hormone therapy—played a major role in discourses about American pride, Cold War nationalism, and new conceptions of masculinity and femininity. Bodily transformation was a kind of allegory for national rehabilitation, with many returning home with nonnormative bodies, or using the postwar period as an opportunity to push the limits of normative bodily practices. The postwar period was not simply a time of enforced conformity. Serlin’s book is an attempt to supplement the image of postwar suburban conformity with one of many Americans using surgical or hormonal techniques to align their private selves with their public identity. This book is, I should say, drawing on literatures that are quite far afield from our usual fare: it’s more in the vein of disability studies, queer studies, and STS than our typical.   
Several chapters make this point from different perspectives. Henry Dreyfuss, an industrial designer best known for thermostats and telephones, also designed a prosthetic hand for the Veterans Administration. Unlike previous prosthetics, which were marketed to blue-collar workers and were shown doing manual labor or farm work, this was an “upscale” device, “a whole new hand for a whole new kind of labor”, designed to hide its mechanical features. This was (cheekily) “the other arms race”, one not about Cold War destruction, but about shifting definitions of masculinity, where “even amputees could sign their lives away through credit debt”.[^26] Another case study considers Christine Jorgensen, a GI who transitioned postwar and was one of the first recognized recipients of sex reassignment surgery. This transition turned Jorgensen into a celebrity: from a masculine role in the Army, to a highly feminized "Blonde Beauty”, she was rapidly normalized within a “normal” 1950s femininity. However, the details of her surgical procedure and hormone intake was viewed as a kind of “gender betrayal”, not just deceitful but unpatriotic. In both of these case studies, Serlin is demonstrating that these surgical or prosthetic operations are speaking directly to shifting values about what it means to be a man, a woman, or an American.  
Next—and I should say I am writing about these books in the somewhat arbitrary order I read them—is Hennessy’s *On the Backs of Tortoises*. I appreciated reading this and *Victorian Sensation* to finally engage with some Darwin literature, which I know is enormous but have never really dived into personally. Hennessy’s primary claim is that the Galapagos were never an untrammeled natural laboratory from which Darwin derived his evolutionary theory. That simple story is wrong for two reasons. First, much of Darwin’s crucial work happened far after his visit to the archipelago; to conceive of Darwin as “discovering” evolution there would be anachronistic. Second and more importantly, the Galapagos were always a highly contested space among many actors: tortoises, sailors, colonists, naturalists, conservationists, tourists, and the Ecuadorian state. The islands are not an “evolutionary Eden”, but rather a contested and constructed place. This conception of the Galapagos has a long history. From Julian Huxley to the present day, the islands have been conceived of as a place where nature can be separated from society and managed by scientific experts. But the problem with this worldview is that it positions itself against a baseline “Eden” that never existed. The book is extremely impressive in its research base, including most saliently twelve years of fieldwork.  
Some brief notes on the content of the book itself. The Beagle’s journey was an imperial hydrographic survey mission, using charts made for British whaling missions—hardly a neutral scientific journey. Darwin appears to have done very little rigorous scientific work on the island, and hardly collected any specimens. This highlights the point that the islands’ significance emerged through post-voyage museum work, not through Darwin’s actual fieldwork. Darwin’s name was evoked a century later in the Charles Darwin Research Station, a Cold War collaboration that serves as both conservation research enterprise and nature tourism site. This opened the islands to flows of people, capital, species that undermined the very isolation conservationists sought to protect. Time and again, this work shows that several different goals—conservation, isolation, tourism, species identification and taxonomization, the use of tortoises as tools of propaganda and branding—are all at odds with each other.  
James Secord’s *Victorian Sensation* is a kind of reception studies of one book: *Vestiges of the Natural History of Creation,* which opened the British public to the idea of evolutionary theory fifteen years before the publication of *Origin of Species*. Secord’s claim is that the so-called “Darwinian revolution” is just as much about a transformation in reading audiences and communication of scientific ideas as it is a transformation in evolutionary thinking per se. In some sense, the medium is the message: the reception of evolutionary ideas was determined by the way they were published and the reading publics that were mobilized to engage with them. This book speaks the most to the idea of “colloquial science”, and exists in a time before there is a meaningful difference between “professional” and “popular” literature. The book works through the experiences of several readers, like Mary Smith, the daughter of an Oxforshire shoemaker, who read the book clandestinely by candlelight, or Thomas Archer Hirst, a surveyor’s apprentice, who kept a detailed diary of his reading. *Victorian Sensation* also works through other spaces in which the book was heavily contested: Liverpudlian newspapers, Aristocratic salons in London’s West End. The crucial point in all of these case studies is that it is the circumstances of the reading public’s contact with this work that most determined their conception of it.  
Finally, Hopwood’s *Haeckel’s Embryos* is a history of Haeckel’s work and its legacy up to the present day. These illustrations have been rife with controversy from the moment of their creation in 1868 well into the age of genomics, and they represent the ways that the copying, reproduction, and reworking of images are not neutral practices.[^27] The fate of particular scientific images lies not in their accuracy alone, but in the conditions of their publishing, their role as pedagogical tools, the way they fit into disciplinary politics, and the public reaction to their publication. As we’ve seen in Daston & Gallison, images are not transparent reflections of reality; they have their own material histories, and Hopwood builds on the Haeckel-His chapter of *Objectivity* to reconstruct the full history of these embryo illustrations, which features different species on the vertical axis, and different evolutionary stages on the horizontal. This grid was itself a kind of innovation: an “aid to comparative seeing like the pairs, tableaus, series, arrays, and overlays which helped eyes detect similarities and differences”. These images were read in secret in German schools, which at the time had banned Darwinist ideas, giving them the allure of forbidden knowledge. Hopwood also makes the point that “fraud” itself is socially constructed, and that accusations of fraud often serve broader political ends. I also really appreciated the point that Stephen Jay Gould’s claim that these images were “mindlessly recycled” isn’t as straightforward as it seems. At each stage, the images were deliberately remade, repurposed, corrected, and refigured to meet different ends—textbook covers, book inserts, illustrative historical examples.

[^1]:   Licence my roving hands, and let them go,

[^2]:  One thing that repeatedly comes up for me in these readings that I would love to talk through at some point: it seems that so many of the texts we read are fighting against the notion of science as procedural, algorithmic, linearly progressive, done by perfectly disembodied rational actors. My question is: who believes this? One need only spend 15 minutes talking to any scientist, or observing the way a lab works in practice, or eavesdropping on scientists’ lunch conversation to understand that this isn’t how things work. I almost wonder if this traditional depiction of science is a kind of “truth-to-nature” objectivity. The philosophers and historians writing this way are ignoring all of the abnormalities and asymmetries in favor of the “metaphysical universal truth” of how science works. Whereas the people who take a more sociological approach starting in the ‘70s are more “mechanical objectivity”—they want to suppress the ego (though I’m not sure SSK is an ego-suppressed group) and see science as it is really done.

[^3]:  A key feature of *Objectivity*, per our discussions last week, is that it provides another alternative to the traditional 17th century scientific revolution story. It complicates both the idea of a “hard break” from the past and the idea that there is one kind of person called a “scientist” with one kind of “objective pursuit” of nature.

[^4]:  Reminded me of one of my favorite books, Bruce J. Hunt’s *The Maxwellians*.

[^5]:  “New kind of guy just dropped” as we’d say online.

[^6]:  I wonder how this squares with conversations around the “neoliberal university”, or with some sense I get that private or corporate funding for science is somehow a new thing. It doesn’t strike me as new at all. It seems that physicists are very content to go where the funding goes. The continued existence of their research is, as far as I can tell, much more important to them than where the money happens to be coming from (as long as the money lets them do what they want unimpeded).

[^7]:  I remember reading Dorothy Nelkin, who when writing about the Science Wars argued that what was really happening was the breakdown of this agreement between science and the state. Physicists could no longer meet their end of the bargain, and so it became clear that the arrangement that they had—unfettered funding with zero oversight in exchange for military-ready pathbreaking advances in science—no longer made sense.

[^8]:  There’s a lot of *Objectivity* in *Image & Logic*. The traces of the basic thesis—especially the rise of trained judgement as a mode of objectivity in the early 20th century—are all there.

[^9]:  This concept incidentally evoked the example of Poynting’s lecture in Masters of Theory, where Poynting’s final talk had stripped away all of the technique he had learned at Cambridge and just provided his final result ex nihilo as if generated from nothing.

[^10]:   To end things off here, I just want to take a few choice quotes from the book: “The result of the construction of a fact is that it appears unconstructed by anyone; the result of rhetorical persuasion in the agonistic field is that participants are convinced that they have not been convinced” (240). “​​The set of statements considered too costly to modify constitute what is referred to as reality” (243). Good stuff. 

[^11]:  Candidly I don’t quite have enough of a grasp of genetics to understand the organ-series to chromosome mapping. I understood the broader point but do wish I could sit with the science more.

[^12]:  More like “We have never been Kuhnian” am I right

[^13]:  I am reading Jon Agar’s “What did computers do?” for my history of technology generals and this is very conceptually similar: in basically all cases, the computer reinforces and legibilizes and accelerates and formalizes what was already present.

[^14]:  I also read Beller’s amazing 1998 article responding to Sokal and showing that many of Sokal’s own critiques of postmodernist theory were actually espoused in near-identical terms by Bohr, Einstein, Heisenberg, Schrodinger, and others in their debates about the nature of reality, the relationship between objectivity and observation, the potential integration of particle-wave duality with Hegelian dialectic. It’s really a masterstroke; I’m stunned I hadn’t seen it sooner.

[^15]:  I love this quote from the start of the book: “My approach is based on both admiration for the creation of scientific novelty and criticism toward the establishment of scientific dogmas”.

[^16]:  One of my favorite pieces in the history of technology is “Technologies as Forms of Life” by Langdon Winner, where he proposes the idea of “technological somnambulism”, which is one of the stickier concepts for me.

[^17]:  “What the correct outcome is depends upon whether there are gravity waves hitting the Earth in detectable fluxes. To find this out we must build a good gravity wave detector and have a look. But we won't know if we have built a good detector until we have tried it and obtained the correct outcome\! But we don't know what the correct outcome is until ... and so on ad infinitum.”

[^18]:  This “back-propagation” of narrative has come up in basically all of these texts, and many of last week’s as well. It seems to be a huge through-line. Collins calls it “laundering” at one point.

[^19]:  Sometimes you just gotta throw all your ideas at the wall and hope something sticks.

[^20]:  I was just reading Mike Davis’ *City of Quartz* for Urban History generals, so I am thinking a lot at the moment about the way that places like LA are represented, narrativized, and sold in the imagination to the public. The same is certainly true here of apes in *Primate Visions*.

[^21]:  I almost lost it at the names “Lionel Tiger” and “Robin Fox” being people who study animals.

[^22]:  I have a very strong personal interest in this topic as a person who studied evolutionary psychology for several years and was under the rhetorical sway of Steven Pinker for many years as an undergraduate. What this book made very clear to me was just how radical Wilson’s “inversion” was: instead of assuming humans are cooperative and asking why violence occurs, Wilson assumed competition and needed to explain altruism.
